Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> At nightfall of the Fourteenth one checks leavened matter by candle light. A place into which no leavened matter is introduced does not need checking. And where<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli and Maimonides read ולמה “why” instead of ובמה “where”.</i> did they say two rows in the cellar<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The storage place for wine or oil amphoras. While this is not a place where one usually stores leavened matter it is possible that the person who is asked to bring a barrel of wine or oil for household use is eating some bread and deposits it temporarily near the entrance door. Therefore it needs checking except for a wholesaler’s cellar.</i>? A place where one introduces leavened matter. The House of Shammai are saying two rows in the front of the entire cellar<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The amphoras are arranged in a rectangular pattern. The House of Shammai requires checking of two rows in all directions accessible from the door. Since usually one starts arranging the amphoras starting at two walls meeting at an angle, the outer rows at the other two sides of the rectangular arrangement have to be checked.</i>, but the House of Hillel are saying the outermost two rows which are the uppermost<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Only the rows facing the door have to be checked. If the amphoras also are stacked on top of one another, the two top rows facing the door have to be checked.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “At nightfall of the fourteenth,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:17-18" href="/Exodus.12.17-18"><i>Ex.</i> 12:17–18</a>.</i>: <i>You shall guard the mazzot, because on this same day I took out your multitudes from</i><i>the Land of Egypt. On the first, on the fourteenth of the month, in the evening, you shall eat mazzot,</i> etc. Where do we hold<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">What means <i>on the first</i>? Does it mean on the first month, or on the first day of the holidays?</i>? If for eating <i>mazzah,</i> is it not already written, <i>seven days you shall eat mazzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex</i>. 12:15</a>.</i>? Or if to say that one starts on the Fourteenth, is it not written, <i>until the twenty-first of the month</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:17-18" href="/Exodus.12.17-18"><i>Ex.</i> 12:17–18</a>.</i>? But if it is not needed as a reference to the eating of <i>mazzah,</i> take it as a reference to the elimination of leavened matter<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As usual, the argument partially refers to parts of the verse which are not quoted in the text. It says in 12:15, <i>seven days you shall eat mazzot, only on the first day you shall eliminate sour dough from your houses.</i> No calendar date has been given. One could read v. 17 to state that <i>mazzot</i> have to be eaten and leavened matter eliminated on the 14<sup>th</sup>. This is impossible since the same verse states that the seventh day is the 21<sup>st</sup>. Therefore the obligation to eat <i>mazzot</i> starts in the evening preceding the 15<sup>th</sup>. Since it is accepted doctrine that the calendar day starts at nightfall of the preceding night, the reference to the 14<sup>th</sup> cannot possibly refer to eating, but must give the date of the elimination of leavened matter for which the search in the preceding night is a preparation.</i>.
Why by candlelight? Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, because a candle permits checking everywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In holes and crevices which are dark in daytime (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 8a" href="/Pesachim.8a">Babli 8a</a>). The “candle” mentioned here is a small clay vessel filled with oil.</i>. Why in the night? Rebbi Yose said, because checking with a candle is exact only during night time<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">As stated later, candle light is not effective during daytime.</i>. Rebbi Mana did not say so: <i>And guard this day for your generations as an eternal law</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:17-18" href="/Exodus.12.17-18"><i>Ex.</i> 12:17–18</a>.</i>, see to it that day and night be guarded<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The entire day should be dedicated to the elimination of leavened matter.</i>. Then should one not start on the Thirteenth that day and night be guarded? He also agrees with the statement of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">One cannot say that R. Mana disagrees with R. Yose; he adds to his statement.</i>. Then should one start in the evening of the Thirteenth? If so, he could even check on the beginning of the month<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since checking two weeks in advance would be useless, so is checking a full day in advance. Since checking should be done at nighttime, the only remaining date is the evening of the 14<sup>th</sup> of Nisan (preceding the day).</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah said that Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac asked: May one check by the light of torches? What is his problem? Because their light is unsteady<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the light is unsteady, it does not serve in searching nooks and crannies.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac is consistent since Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, because a candle permits checking everywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote בודק כל שהוא כל שהוא “checking extremely well everywhere.” The second כל שהוא was deleted by the corrector; probably incorrectly.</i>. [<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta <i>Pisha</i> 1:1</a>; corrector’s addition.</i> Even though it is not proof of the matter, it is an allusion to the matter: <i>It shall be on that day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Misquote from memory.</i><i>that I shall search Jerusalem by candles</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zephaniah 1:12" href="/Zephaniah.1.12"><i>Zeph.</i> 1:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 7b-8a" href="/Pesachim.7b-8a">Babli 7b–8a</a>.</i><i>.</i> But some want to say,] one can understand it from the following: <i>A candle of the Eternal is a person’s soul, it searches all rooms of the body</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 20:27" href="/Proverbs.20.27"><i>Prov</i>. 20:27</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 7b-8a" href="/Pesachim.7b-8a">Babli 7b–8a</a>.</i><i>.</i>
Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah implies that checking during daytime is called checking, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah says, one checks at nightfall of the Fourteenth, and on the Fourteenth, and at the moment of elimination.” Not only Rebbi Jehudah but even the rabbis, as we have stated: “If he did not check at nightfall of the Fourteenth he shall check on the Fourteenth.”
Is it obligatory to check by candlelight<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">When checking during daytime, even following the rabbis.</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 7b" href="/Pesachim.7b">Babli 7b</a>.</i> “One checks neither by sunlight, nor by moonlight, nor by starlight, but by candlelight.” One understands “neither by moonlight, nor by starlight,” but “neither by sunlight”? Is there any sun in the night? This implies, even during daytime one has to check by candlelight; not only in a house without light, but even in a house which has light, even during daytime, it is necessary to check by candlelight.
May one<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This translation of צריך follows S. Liebermann.</i> check dark passages<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Roofed public spaces.</i> purposely<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As the parallel expression in the next paragraph shows, a word “during daytime” is missing here.</i> by candlelight? The words of the rabbis imply that not as it radiates at night it radiates during the day, <sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 31:12" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.31.12"><i>Gen. r.</i> 31:12</a>, a much better text. As given here, the story is Babylonian and there is no reason why they should be fleeing, and why the earth under the place (under the open sky) where the twice-yearly assembly of scholars was held should have been tunnelled. In <i>Gen. rabba</i> the name is <i>Rebbi</i> Huna, the place is Tiberias, and they were hiding from soldiers.</i> as Rav Huna said, when we were fleeing to these catacombs of the great assembly they were lighting candles for us. When they were faint, we knew that it was day; when they were bright we knew that it was night. This parallels what Rebbi Aḥawa bar Ze`ira<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The son of R. Zeˋira. In <i>Gen. r.</i> “R. Phineas in the name of R. Zeˋira”. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 108b" href="/Sanhedrin.108b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 108b</a> is a similar statement of R. Joḥanan.</i> said, when Noah entered the Ark he brought with him precious stones and pearls. When they were dull, he knew that it was daytime; when they were shiny he knew that it was nighttime.Why? There are animals eating at daytime and there are animals eating at nighttime. But is there not written, <i>make a skylight to the ark</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:16" href="/Genesis.6.16"><i>Gen.</i> 6:16</a>.</i>? Following him who said that the stars were not operative in the year of the flood<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 25:2" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.25.2"><i>Gen. r.</i> 25:2</a>, interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 8:22" href="/Genesis.8.22"><i>Gen.</i>8:22</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Do synagogues and houses of study need checking? What is his problem? Since one brings<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In general it is forbidden to bring food into a synagogue or study hall proper, but for a meal accompanying the public declaration of the new month or the additional month one brought food there, bread and vegetables (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 70b" href="/Sanhedrin.70b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 70b</a>) or grapes and bread (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.4.4">Yerushalmi <i>Berakhot</i>6:4, Note 149</a>).</i> there for intercalations<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In a responsum of R. Nissim of Kairawan (<i>Teshuvot Hegaonim Lyck,</i> #105) and the Frankfurt ms. of <i>Or zarua</i> II §453, עיבריות. Even the word spelled with א is interpreted as if written with ע, “intercalations”; J. N. Epstein, <i>Tarbiz</i> 5 (1933–34) p. 263. In the printed editions of the Yerushalmi, following a correction on the margin of the ms., “on Sabbaths” (cf. R. Nissim <i>loc. cit.,</i> p. 34).</i> and on the days of the New Moon. It should be obvious for him<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">That one has to check, even if the place was not usually used for public meals.</i>, but what really is his problem? Since they are very well illuminated, may one check them purposely during daytime by candlelight<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">And not in the evening of the 14<sup>th</sup>, when people are occupied in their own houses. Since no answer is given, theˋ answer is negative.</i>?
Rebbi Yose asked: The courtyards in Jerusalem where one eats the bread of thanksgiving offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Which have an obligatory component of leavened bread, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:13" href="/Leviticus.7.13"><i>Lev.</i> 7:13</a>.</i> and the wafers of a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Part of the offering of the <i>nazir</i> at the fulfillment of his vow, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:15" href="/Numbers.6.15"><i>Num.</i> 6:15</a>. These are unleavened.</i><i>,</i> do they need checking? Are they not checked anyhow for leftovers<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since any leftovers from any well-being offering, including the thanksgiving offering, must be burned (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:17" href="/Leviticus.7.17"><i>Lev.</i>7:17</a>), every day of the year these localities are treated the way every other dwelling is treated on Passover Eve.</i>? It should be parallel to what Rebbi Zachariah the son-in-law of Rebbi Levi stated: A menstruating woman washes her hair and combs<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">She becomes pure by immersing <i>all</i> her body in water. Therefore it is imperative that not two hairs cling together; before immersion she has to make sure that this does not happen.</i>. A priestly woman does not wash her hair and comb<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The wife, unmarried daughter, or slave of a Cohen who participate with the Cohen in eating heave and other sancta. As long as the rules of purity could be kept, these women, together with the Cohen, have to immerse themselves every day close to sundown (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Berakhot 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Berakhot.1.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 1:1, Note 3</a>). Therefore washing the hair is not necessary. Quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Niddah 66b" href="/Tosafot_on_Niddah.66b">Tosaphot <i>Niddah</i> 66b</a>.</i>. A menstruating priestly woman washes her hair and combs, not to differentiate between one menstruating woman and another. So also here, not to differentiate between elimination and elimination. Rabbi Gamliel bar Inyany stated before Rebbi Mana: A menstruating woman who interrupts for seven days washes her hair and combs. A priestly woman who immerses herself every day does not wash her hair and comb<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">From the comparison of the menstruating and the priestly women one cannot infer anything since the menstruating priestly woman is excluded from the common meal of <i>sancta</i> for the period of her impurity. The statement which exempts her from washing her hair applies only to pure priestly women.</i>. He told him, it is necessary for one who watches one day for a day<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the rules of purity could be observed, a woman in the household of a Cohen was obligated to reduce the period of her impurity to a minimum. The method by which any other woman could avoid showing her blood to a rabbinic authority to ascertain the status of her impurity (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.4.1.1"><i>Niddah</i> 4:1 Note 3</a>) is not available to the priestly woman. It is rabbinic doctrine that after one menstruation, the next is possible only after 11 days.(Mishnah <i>Niddah</i> 4:7). If she loses blood on one of these days, she does not follow the rules of <i>niddah</i> but those of <i>zavah.</i> For the first two occurrences in one period she is impure only for 24 hours. The statement about the impure priestly woman is needed to decree that even an interruption of one day without immersion triggers the obligation of washing the hair.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 8a" href="/Pesachim.8a">Babli 8a</a>.</i>“Holes in the house, the upper and the lower ones, and the veranda<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Any additional structure having a sloping roof.</i>, and the ditch<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">חדות is the same as דות in the Tosephta.</i>, and the upper floor<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Used only for storage but not as dwelling.</i>, and the roof of the olive press<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the reading of the Tosephta, גג הבית “the roof of the house” is the correct one (as it is quoted as reading of the Yerushalmi by R. Isaac Ibn Ghiat). A flat roof is exposed to the wind and to foraging birds; one may assume that no leavened matter stays there for long.</i>, and the roof of the tower<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This has a sloping roof on which nothing stays.</i>, the storage room of straw, and the cattle stable, and the storage room of wood, and the storage room of provisions, wine storage, and oil storage, and produce storage, do not need checking.” One understands the upper ones<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If “upper” means “higher than the reach of a person”, one need not expect that leavened matter was deposited there. But very low lying holes are at the reach of children.</i>. The lower ones? Does this mean that they did not worry about things falling down? Rebbi Yose said, explain it if both were close to the wall<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Holes at the outside of the wall.</i>, whether higher than ten or lower than ten [hand-breadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The text is confirmed by a quote in <i>Tur Orah Hayyim</i> §433; it does not make any sense since 10 hand-breadths is a height very accessible to all uses. The statement is rejected by all authorities who quote it.</i>. Did we not state “the ditch”? Does this not mean that they did not worry about things falling down? The toddler is afraid to go there. But should one not be afraid that leavened matter rolled there? Explain it by a ditch which has a rim. “And the roof of the olive press, and the roof of the tower.” That is, if they are three hand-breadths high; but if they are not three hand-breadths high they are like ground. And if they were not used for leavened matter, but if they were used for leavened matter even if it is very high it needs checking. Rebbi Mana said, this is correct. Would we say that this papyrus scaffold which is very high should not need checking<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 14:3" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).14.3">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 14(15):3</a> mentions an instrument used to remove bread from the papyrus scaffold where it is stored. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.3.1"><i>Kilaim</i> 6:3 Note 38</a>.</i>?
It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 8a" href="/Pesachim.8a">Babli 8a</a>.</i>“Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, a couch which was separated<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The couch was separated from the floor, i. e., it was on high legs which left room for storage under the bed.</i> in the house but wood and stones are stored under it, one checks at the outside but does not have to check on the inside.” Because wood and stones are stored under it, but if no wood and stones are stored under it, one has to check on the inside. Does this mean that they did (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The word was written by the scribe but correctly deleted by the corrector.</i> worry about things falling down? I am saying that (a child exerted himself and) [an unruly child]<sup class="footnote-marker">53*</sup><i class="footnote">Versions of (scribe) and [corrector.]</i> brought leavened matter there<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Just as the first part of the Tosephta, quoted in the preceding paragraph, does not prove that the earlier generations were not afraid of bread accidentally falling down without being picked up, so this part does not prove the opposite conclusion.</i>.
A wine cellar needs checking, an oil cellar does not need checking<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 8a" href="/Pesachim.8a">Babli 8a</a>.</i>. What is the difference between wine and oil? Wine has no staying power, oil has staying power<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Wine is drawn from the amphora at least once a day; fresh oil is needed only from time to time.</i>. Storage<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Wholesale storage for commercial purposes.</i>, whether of wine or oil, does not need checking. What is a cellar? Anything used with bread in the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Any place where bread is introduced during the year needs checking. Some Medieval authors, e.g. Meïri (ed. J. Klein, Jerusalem 1964, p. 39, Notes 468–469) read here an additional sentence: “Storage is what is not used with bread in the courtyard.”</i>. There exists a cellar which is like storage and storage which is like a cellar. A cellar in which he is ashamed to eat is like storage; storage where he is not ashamed to eat is like a cellar. <sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">This does not belong here; because of the similarity in language it is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.6.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:7, Notes 71–72</a>.</i> There is a courtyard which is like an alley and an alley which is like a courtyard. A courtyard which is used by the public is like an alley, and an alley which is not used by the public is like a courtyard. But should one not be afraid that animal drivers introduce leavened matter into it<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This refers back to storage areas. While the producer of the wine will not introduce bread into his storage area, what can be said about the teamster who will transport the wine for sale at other places?</i>? Animal drivers usually do not introduce leavened matter into it but sweets since they help in checking the quality of the wine<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If the teamster is transporting for his own account, he will want to check the quality of the wine he is buying; for that he eats taste enhancing candy, not taste dulling bread.</i>.
“If somebody leaves to travel by sea, before thirty days he does not have to check; within thirty [days] he has to check”<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a> (in the formulation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6a" href="/Pesachim.6a">Babli 6a</a>).</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6a" href="/Pesachim.6a">Babli 6a</a> rules in everything the exact opposite of the Yerushalmi, which holds that everybody is obligated at all times to see to it that his property contains no leavened matter on Passover, wherever it be situated and wherever he spends the holiday.</i> That is to say, if he intends to return. But if he does not intend to return, even before thirty days he has to check. If there is a doubt; but if it is certain then even from New Year’s Day. Rebbi Abba said, but even if he intends to return he has to check, maybe he will change his mind and not return.
Everybody is trustworthy about elimination of leavened matter, even women, even slaves. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira: There is no “even” women. Women intrinsically are trustworthy; since they are slow they check everything very carefully. Samaritans any time when they prepare their <i>mazzah</i> with Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">If the Samaritan calendar has Passover on the same day as the rabbinic one.</i> are trustworthy about checking for leavened matter; if they do not prepare their <i>mazzah</i> with Jews they are not trustworthy about checking for leavened matter. Rebbi Yose said, this means in the houses, but they are suspect in courtyards since they explain that <i>it should not be found in your houses,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:19" href="/Exodus.12.19"><i>Ex.</i> 12:19</a>.</i> does not apply to your courtyards.
It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 10a" href="/Gittin.10a">Babli <i>Gittin</i> 10a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 76a" href="/Kiddushin.76a"><i>Qiddushin</i> 76a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 4a" href="/Chullin.4a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 4a</a>.</i> Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, any commandment which the Samaritans keep they are much more exact with them than Jews. Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This author cannot be R. Simeon ben Yoḥai, but he might be R. Simeon ben Eleazar <i>(Avodah zarah</i> 5:4, p. 447).</i> said, that means earlier when they were concentrated in their villages. But today, when they have no commandments nor remainders of commandments they are suspect and degenerate.
One asks about practices of Passover at Passover, practices of Pentecost at Pentecost, practices of Tabernacles at Tabernacles<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4a" href="/Megillah.4a">Babli <i>Megillah</i> 4a</a>. <i>Sifry Num.</i> 66.</i>. In the House of Assembly one asks thirty days in advance; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, two weeks<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6a" href="/Pesachim.6a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 6a</a>.</i>. The statement of Rebbi Joḥanan follows the rabbis, that of the colleagues Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of the rabbis, because Moses stands on the First Passover and explains to them the rules of Second Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of the Second Passover to be celebrated on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Iyar by people impure on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan were promulgated on the 13<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:9-14" href="/Numbers.9.9-14"><i>Num.</i>9:9–14</a>.</i>. That of the colleagues follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, because Moses stands on the day of the New Moon and explains to them the rules of Passover<sup>71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:1-4" href="/Numbers.9.1-4"><i>Num.</i> 9:1–4</a>.</i>.
“Where<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli and Maimonides read ולמה “why” instead of ובמה “where”.</i> did they say two rows in the cellar<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The storage place for wine or oil amphoras. While this is not a place where one usually stores leavened matter it is possible that the person who is asked to bring a barrel of wine or oil for household use is eating some bread and deposits it temporarily near the entrance door. Therefore it needs checking except for a wholesaler’s cellar.</i>,” etc. Rav Huna in the name of Rav: He peels it like a Gamma. Bar Qappara stated, he peels it like two Gamma<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">He checks the outer rows in the cellar in two directions orthogonal to one another, in the shape of Ã. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 8b" href="/Pesachim.8b">Babli 8b</a> in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>. If it was situated in the middle of the room. In the opinion of Rav Huna he strips it of one garment, in the opinion of Bar Qappara he strips it of two garments<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">He has to check the lower row and one vertical row and the upper row and, depending on the interpretation given later, either another vertical row or a horizontal row inwards.</i>. If it<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The storage room. Each step defines a new front row.</i> was built in steps, he strips it by many Gamma. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Ḥizqiah: Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan. The outermost row which is the uppermost which sees both door and beam of the roof, [and] the one inside of it<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If the amphoras are stored at least two layers high, one has to check the two rows close to the entrance of the top layer.</i>. It was stated: the one below it. They wanted to say that he who says, the one inside of it, so much more that one below it. He who says, the one below it, therefore not the one inside of it<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Only the front rows in all layers have to be checked.</i>. If half a row was used, it is obvious that what was used has inside of it and below it. That which was not used has inside of it and below it<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Even if during the year wine was taken only from one side of one row, once part of a row must be checked, the entire row must be checked.</i>. That is to say, if they are spaced<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">According to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 10:5" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.10.5">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 10:5</a>, amphoras are spaced if on two opposite sides there is one hand-breadth of free space.</i>. But if they are tightly packed he passes the light over them<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s text. The scribe’s הנייר ולווי “the paper and appurtenances” is unintelligible.</i> and it is enough. Does this mean that they did worry about things falling down? Rebbi Phineas from Yafo<sup class="footnote-marker">80*</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing more is known about him.</i> said, I am saying that the servant introduced leavened matter when he placed them.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not suspect<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">After a room has been searched for leavened matter, one does not fear that an animal brought such matter back into the room; no place has to be searched more than once.</i> that a mole dragged it from room to room or from place to place<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In one courtyard.</i>; if that were the case, also from courtyard to courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Since not all people search their property at the same time, animals could find leavened matter somewhere at all times and no search could ever be completed.</i> and from town to town without end.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not suspect,” etc. Rebbi Jonah said, so the Mishnah should have been stated: From town to town and from courtyard to courtyard, from place to place and from room to room. If you suspect from town to town, you will suspect from courtyard to courtyard. If you suspect from courtyard to courtyard, you will suspect from place to place. If you suspect from place to place, you will suspect from room to room. Rebbi Yose said, even following the Mishnah it is correct. “One does not suspect that a mole pulled it from room to room or from place to place; if that were the case, also from courtyard to courtyard and from town to town without end.” For if you do not suspect from room to room, not so much more you suspect from place to place? If you do not suspect from place to place, not so much more from courtyard to courtyard? If you do not suspect from courtyard to courtyard, not so much more from town to town? All of Israel can check their leavened matter together<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> is a consequence of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>. One really should be worried that animals could transport food from one place to another. But since everybody checks at the same time, after sundown of the 13<sup>th</sup> of Nisan, there is no danger of later contamination.<br>This paragraph is the corrector’s text; the scribe’s text “if you do not suspect from town to town you certainly do mot worry from courtyard to courtyard, etc.“ has all arguments the wrong way.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah says, one checks at nightfall of the Fourteenth, and on the mourning of the Fourteenth, and at the time of elimination<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the Yerushalmi, R. Jehudah requires three checks for every place. Rav Joseph in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 10b" href="/Pesachim.10b">Babli (10b</a>) reads the conjunctions in R. Jehudah’s statement as “or”. The time of elimination is given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>. But the Sages say, he who did not check at nightfall of the Fourteenth has to check on the Fourteenth; he who did not check on the Fourteenth has to check on the holiday. He who did not check on the holiday has to check after the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since leavened matter which was in the possession of a Jew during Passover is forbidden for all usufruct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.2.1">Mishnah 2:2</a>), such matter if it was not eliminated before the holiday must be eliminated afterwards.</i>. And what he leaves he should deposit at a guarded place so that he would not be obligated to repeat checking<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Any bread and leavened matter to be used on the morning of the Fourteenth must be secured after the checking in the evening since if some of it would be missing in the morning the entire checking procedure would have to be repeated.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Jehudah says,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of Rebbi Jehudah, corresponding to the three times that in the Torah is written, <i>leavening shall not be seen as yours</i><sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Twice in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:7" href="/Exodus.13.7"><i>Ex</i>. 13:7</a>, once in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:4" href="/Deuteronomy.16.4"><i>Deut.</i> 16:4</a>.</i>. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex</i>. 12:15</a>.</i>, <i>you shall remove leavening from your houses?</i> That is a positive commandment. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:19" href="/Exodus.12.19"><i>Ex</i>. 12:19</a>.</i>, <i>for seven days leavening shall not be found in your houses?</i> Rebbi Yose said, since they are mutually needed<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the sequel.</i>, they are as one. <i>It should not be seen as yours,</i> I would have said, if somebody<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile’s leavened matter, which is exempt from Jewish law. But if it is a deposit, i. e., that the Jew is responsible for damages, it becomes the Jew’s property for the rules of Passover.</i> deposited with him it should be permitted. The verse says, <i>it shall not be found in your houses.</i> If <i>it shall not be found in your houses,</i> if he gave him a separate place<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile’s leavened matter stored at a separate place for which the Jewish owner of the place accepts no responsibility may stay there over Passover. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6a" href="/Pesachim.6a">Babli 6a</a>.</i> it should be forbidden. The verse says, <i>it shall not be seen as yours.</i> How is this? If he deposited with him it is forbidden; if he gave him a separate place it is permitted.
The argument of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">It is not spelled out what other opinion of R. Jehudah is in conflict with his statement in the Mishnah. It seems that while he is stringent in the requirement of triple checking before the holiday he is lenient in not requiring anything afterwards if checking was not done by the time of elimination.</i>. As we have stated, “Rebbi Jehudah says, one checks at nightfall of the Fourteenth, and on the mourning of the Fourteenth, and at the time of elimination<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:17-18" href="/Exodus.12.17-18"><i>Ex.</i> 12:17–18</a>.</i>. But the Sages say, if he did not check at nightfall of the Fourteenth, he has to check on the Fourteenth.” Does he have to check three times? Since at a time before its term of elimination you are saying that he has to check three times, at the moment of the term of elimination not so much more? <sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses was deleted by the corrector and therefore is not found in the printed texts. It is a necessary component of the argument.</i>(No, it need not be so. If he did not check on the Fourteenth, he has to check on the holiday. Does he have to check three times? Since at a time before its term of elimination you are saying that he has to check three times, at the moment of the term of elimination not so much more?) No, it need not be so. If he did not check on the holiday, he has to check after the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The answer to the question was that certainly R. Jehudah would agree with the Sages that checking during or after the holidays is required if it was not done beforehand (a position rejected by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 10b-11a" href="/Pesachim.10b-11a">Babli 10b–11a</a>.) The only question is whether in these cases also R. Jehudah requires triple checking. Since he does not spell out his position, an attempt to determine this by inferences is unsuccessful. Since practice does not follow R. Jehudah, a final answer is not given.</i>.
“And what he leaves he should deposit at a guarded place so that he would not be obligated to repeat checking<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Any bread and leavened matter to be used on the morning of the Fourteenth must be secured after the checking in the evening since if some of it would be missing in the morning the entire checking procedure would have to be repeated.</i>.” What does he do? He covers it with a vessel. If he covered it with a vessel but did not find it<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If the bread was covered, we assume that a human took it. If it was uncovered, one assumes that an animal took it. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 9b" href="/Pesachim.9b">Babli 9b</a> one supposes in both cases that an animal took it; this also seems to be the underlying hypothesis in the Yerushalmi. Rabbenu Yeruham (Part 1, Path 5, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 39a" href="/Pesachim.39a">39a</a>) reads in the first case אימא שד נטלתו “I would say, a spirit took it” but from Ravia §444 (vol. 2, p.68) it seems that for שד “spirit” one should read שיד “that a hand”, meaning a human hand. Raviah notes that his own copy of the Yerushalmi does not contain the passage. In any case the reading of R. Yeruham is suspect since אימא is Babylonian Aramaic based on Accadic, for standard אימר, תימר.</i>. I am saying that a hand took it. If he did not cover it with a vessel and did not find it, this house<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since a standard house had one room, it might be better to translate “a room”.</i> needs checking. Or the other houses would not need checking? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.3.3">Tosephta <i>Tahorot</i> 3:5</a>.</i>: “If the volume of an olive from a cadaver was lost in a house; it was searched but not found. The house is pure; if it will be found, the house is retroactively impure.” This can teach about that and that can teach about this. This can teach about that, there is no difference between “was lost” and “what he leaves”<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">This is the original assertion that the rules of Passover Eve can be compared to the rules of impurity.</i>. And that can teach about this, only this house needs to be checked. Following Rebbi Jehudah even this house should not need to be checked, as we may hear from the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 9a" href="/Pesachim.9a">Babli 9a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 16:6" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.16.6">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 16:13</a>.</i> “Rebbi Jehudah said, it happened that the slave girl of a discharged veteran<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>missicus,</i> cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.6.1"><i>Bava qamma</i> 10:6, Notes 60</a>.</i> at Rimmon threw a still birth into a cistern. A Cohen came and looked to know what she had thrown down. The case came before the Sages and they declared him pure<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">By bending over the opening of the cistern the Cohen would become impure by “tent impurity” if the body still was there.</i> for moles and martens usually drag it away.” It runs after flesh, it does not run after bread<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 9a" href="/Pesachim.9a">Babli 9a</a>.</i>. And even if you are saying that it runs after flesh and bread, it drags away flesh and eats it; it drags away bread and stores it. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: One does not suspect that a mole dragged it, but following Rebbi Jehudah one suspects it<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah cannot accept <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, otherwise he would accept single checking.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Meïr says, one eats<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Leavened food on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan.</i> all during the fifth [hour]<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">According to most opinions. the standard day is counted from sunrise to sundown and is divided into 12 hours. At the spring equinox the day starts at 6 am local time; then the fourth hour is 9–10 am, the fifth 10–11 am and the start of the sixth is 11 am.</i> and burns at the start of the sixth. Rebbi Jehudah says, one eats the entire fourth [hour], suspends<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">One neither eats nor burns.</i> during the fifths, and burns at the start of the sixth.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Meïr says, one eats all during the fifth [hour],” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The text is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 5:3:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.5.3.2-5"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 5:3</a> where in general it is agreed that in a criminal case where it is necessary to determine the time of an alleged crime a difference of two hours in the testimony of different witnesses invalidates the testimonies; R. Jehudah applies this also to noontime but the anonymous Tanna (supposed to be R. Meïr) disagrees and allows discrepancies up to two hours in this case.<br>The scribe of the Leiden ms., after the text translated here in the first 5 sentences, wrote: “one repeats from <i>Sanhedrin</i> until the sun never starts setting’.” The corrector who prepared the ms. for the Venice printer added the omitted portion; his text differs from <i>Sanhedrin</i> by both an addition and a lacuna. It is impossible to decide whether the corrector’s text be copied from a different ms. or represents the corrector’s emendations of the <i>Sanhedrin</i> text. In neither text is the use of references “here” and “there” (either <i>Pesaḥim</i> or <i>Sanhedrin)</i> completely consistent.</i> Rebbi Meïr says, from noontime on it<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of leavened matter on Passover Eve.</i> is from their words; Rebbi Jehudah says, from noontime on it is biblical. What is Rebbi Meïr’s reason? <i>Only on the first day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex.</i> 12:15</a>: <i>Seven days you shall eat maẓzot; only on the first day you shall eliminate sour dough from your houses …</i> אַךְ might also be translated as “certainly”.</i><i>,</i> that is the fifteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:14" href="/Exodus.12.14"><i>Ex.</i> 12:14</a> states: <i>This day shall be a remembrance for you; you shall keep it as a holiday of pilgrimage for the Eternal … Num.</i> 28:15–16 require that the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan be <i>pesaḥ</i> for the Eternal; starting from the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> for seven days it is the holiday of <i>mazzot.</i> Since <i>pesaḥ</i> (i. e., the day of the slaughter of the <i>pesaḥ</i> sacrifice) is connected inextricably with the holiday of <i>mazzot,</i> the reference in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:14" href="/Numbers.28.14">v. 14</a> to the “first day” is intrinsically ambiguous, whether it refer to <i>pesaḥ</i> or to the holiday.</i>. I could think at nightfall; the verse says <i>only,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">A similar argument is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 4b" href="/Pesachim.4b">Babli, 4b</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i> (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 28), <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon b. Iohai</i> (ed. Epstein-Melamed p. 17).</i><i>.</i> How is this? Give it one hour before sundown<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the only biblical requirement is that all leavened matter be completely disposed of before the holiday at sundown.</i>. What is Rebbi Jehudah’s reason? <i>Only on the first day,</i> that is the fourteenth. I could think the entire day; the verse says <i>only.</i> How is that? Split the day, half for leavened matter, half for <i>mazzah.</i> Rebbi Meïr’s argument seems inverted. There, he said <i>only</i> to add; here he said <i>only</i> to diminish<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a>: …<i>no work shall be done [on the holidays],</i> only <i>what may be eaten by any soul, it alone may be made by you.</i> Everybody agrees that food may be prepared on a holiday. According to R. Meïr (i. e., the anonymous opinion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 1:8</a>) <i>only</i> food may be prepared, not preparations necessary for the preparation of food. According to R. Jehudah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.2-5"><i>Megillah</i> 1:8</a>), anything that in the end leads to preparation of food is permitted on a holiday. R. Meïr reads <i>only</i> as a restriction in v. 16 and as an addition in v. 15!</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon said, he diminished, he excludes leavened matter. Rebbi Meïr says, <i>do not eat leavened matter with it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:3" href="/Deuteronomy.16.3"><i>Deut.</i> 16:3</a>, referring to the <i>pesaḥ</i> sacrifice which is slaughtered on the afternoon of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> and eaten in the night of the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>, while it is eaten. Rebbi Jehudah says, <i>do not eat leavened matter with it,</i> while it is prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In the afternoon of the 14<sup>th</sup>. This supports R. Jehudah’s contention that leavened matter is biblically forbidden in the afternoon of the 14<sup>th</sup>; <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 130.</i>.
Rebbi Jehudah has [both a positive and a negative commandment concerning its eating<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">“It” here refers to <i>maẓzah.</i></i>, a positive and a negative commandment concerning its<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">“It” here refers to leavened matter.</i> removal. A positive commandment concerning its eating, <i>seven days you shall eat unleavened bread with it,</i> but not leavened. Any prohibition which is implied by a positive commandment has the status of positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">It is not an indictable offense; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 1:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.1.5.4"><i>Bikkurim</i> 1:5, Note 103</a>. If a positive commandment is in conflict with a negative one (a prohibition), the positive is stronger. But an obligation which is both positive and negative is stronger than anything else.</i>. A negative commandment concerning its eating, <i>do not eat leavened for it.</i> A positive commandment concerning its removal, <i>you shall remove sour dough</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex.</i> 12:15</a>: <i>Seven days you shall eat maẓzot; only on the first day you shall eliminate sour dough from your houses …</i> אַךְ might also be translated as “certainly”.</i><i>.</i> A negative commandment concerning its removal, <i>for seven days sour dough shall not be found in your houses</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:19" href="/Exodus.12.19"><i>Ex.</i> 12:19</a>.</i>.
Now Rebbi Meïr says, after noontime it is forbidden because of their words. The seventh hour is forbidden because of a fence. Why the sixth? Because of a fence<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Meïr, the biblical prohibition of leavened matter starts at the 11<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> hour (5 pm local time). The earlier afternoon hours, including the 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, are rabbinically forbidden as a “fence around the law”. Then it is difficult to understand why leavened matter has to be burned <i>at the start</i> of the 6<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, extending the rabbinic prohibition for another hour as a fence around the fence, a practice generally rejected.</i>. Is there a fence around a fence? But the sixth hour may be confounded with the seventh<sup>123</sup><i class="footnote">It is not a fence around a fence but consistent with the opinion of the Sages (R. Meïr) in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 5:3:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.5.3.2-5"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 5:3</a>. In a society without watches the difference between 11am and 12am is not generally recognized; a prohibition enforced after noontime must practically be enforced starting from 11am.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of the paragraph is missing in <i>Sanhedrin.</i></i> Now Rebbi Jehudah says, after the fifth hour it is forbidden because of their words; the sixth hour is forbidden because of a fence. Why the fifth? Because of a fence. Is there a fence around a fence? But the fifth hour may be confounded with the seventh.
Rebbi Jehudah’s argument seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to <i>Sanhedrin.</i> R. Jehudah agrees that while without watches people cannot distinguish between two morning or two afternoon hours, he explicitly agrees that people distinguish between fifth (10–11am) and seventh (12am–1pm).</i>, he says that the fifth cannot be confounded with the seventh. Rebbi Yose said, there the matter is given over to the court, and the court is diligent. But here the matter is given over to women who are slow. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sanhedrin</i> the Mishnah does not require the court to accept any testimonies where the witnesses differ widely in determining the time of a crime; R. Jehudah admonishes the court under certain circumstances to investigate whether the witnesses do not in reality testify about the same time; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 12b" href="/Pesachim.12b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 12b</a>.</i> it is a matter between the beginning of the fifth and the end of the seventh hour. Here it is between the end of the fifth and the start of the seventh<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Pesaḥim</i> the period of doubt is little more than 60 min., in <i>Sanhedrin</i> close to 180 min. The apparent inconsistency is due to the informal use of “hour.”</i>. It also was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 12b" href="/Pesachim.12b">Babli 12b</a>.</i>: At the start of the fifth hour the sun is in the East, and at the end of the [seventh] hour the sun is in the West. The sun never starts setting before the end of the seventh hour.]
Rav said, the words of Rebbi Meïr: He who preliminarily marries with leavened matter starting from the sixth hour did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1, Note 73. A preliminary marriage can be contracted in different ways; one of them is delivering something of value to the bride. While leavened matter for R. Jehudah is prohibited only after noontime of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan, since it is rabbinically forbidden after the start of the fifth hour it no longer has any monetary value. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 7a" href="/Pesachim.7a">Babli 7a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Huna said, this is correct. Would Cordovanian<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <i>loc. cit.,</i> קורדניתא “Kurdish”. According to Rashi and R. Hananel they are very hard; to be used they have to be soaked in water and then are considered leavened and forbidden for ususfruct on Passover.</i> wheat in the desert be any good on the holiday? A person deposited a double sack<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δισάκκιον, τό. A different version is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 13a" href="/Pesachim.13a">Babli 13a</a>.</i> of bread with the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya. Rebbi Yose ben Rabbi Abun said, he was Joḥanan from Ḥiqoq. He went and asked Rebbi who told him, it should be sold under judicial supervision at the time of elimination<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The owner did not collect his property before Passover Eve. To avoid it becoming worthless, it has to be sold to Gentiles and the proceeds kept in trust for the owner.</i>. A person deposited a jar of <i>kutaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">A kind of yogurt made in Babylonia from milk, salt, and mouldy bread as source of the bacteria. This is leavened matter.</i> with Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi. He went and asked Rav who told him, it should be sold under judicial supervision at the time of elimination. What is the time of elimination<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">When is the time to sell leavened matter whose owner did not collect it in time?</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, in the morning<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The entire morning of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, the fifth [hour] following Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Babli’s decision in the name of Rebbi, 13a.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi Jeremiah said it correctly. Did they not say to touch them in order to sell them only to return a lost article to its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">In general, an unpaid keeper is not responsible for losses unless he manipulates the deposit without authorization. Without incurring financial responsibility he only can sell by direction of the court in order to avoid major financial loss to the depositor.</i>? In the fifth [hour] following Rebbi Jehudah it is not worth anything.
The colleagues said, in the fifth [hour] following Rebbi Jehudah if he dedicated it it is dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">In order to avoid having to burn the leavened matter, he dedicates it to the Temple to be sold and the proceeds used for the Temple’s needs. Temple property is not subject to the prohibition of leavened matter since it is not <i>yours</i> as mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:7" href="/Exodus.13.7"><i>Ex.</i>13:7</a>.</i>; if he designated it as heave it is no heave. If he dedicated it it is dedicated, dedication of money’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Even for R. Jehudah the biblical prohibition of usufruct only starts on midday of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>; during the fifth hour the matter is worth money even for Jews, since it may be used as cattle feed.</i>. If he designated it as heave it is no heave, since heave was given only to be consumed<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Heave is a <i>sanctum</i> to be given to the Cohen to be consumed in purity; nothing pure that cannot be consumed may become heave.</i>. Rebbi Yose told them, the opposite is reasonable. If he dedicated it it is not dedicated; if he designated it as heave it is heave. If he dedicated it it is not dedicated, since one does not redeem <i>sancta</i> to feed to the dogs<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 29a" href="/Pesachim.29a">Babli 29a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 11b" href="/Shevuot.11b"><i>Ševuot</i> 11b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 15a" href="/Bekhorot.15a"><i>Bekhorot</i> 15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 17a" href="/Temurah.17a"><i>Temurah</i> 17a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 30b" href="/Temurah.30b">30b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 31a" href="/Temurah.31a">31a</a>; Yerushalmi <i>Maˋaser Šeni</i> 2:5 Note 82.</i>. If he designated it as heave it is heave, since it is pure by the words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Since by biblical standards it is food until 12 am, a rabbinic prohibition cannot invalidate a biblically valid dedication. This cannot be compared with the case of preliminary marriage since there money’s worth is required, which was eliminated by rabbinic usage.</i>. You are the one who decided that it has to be burned.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In addition, Rebbi Jehudah said, two disqualified loaves of thanksgiving offering<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">At Jerusalem in Temple times, the Temple was the timekeeper and signalled to the people the times to dispose of leavened matter. A thanksgiving offering requires the addition of leavened bread <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:13" href="/Leviticus.7.13"><i>(Lev.</i> 7:13</a>). Since the bread was kept until the time its consumption was forbidden, one used loaves which could not be eaten, either because they were impure or because they were from the preceding day’s offering. A thanksgiving offering must be consumed on the day it is offered <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:15" href="/Leviticus.7.15"><i>(Lev.</i> 7:15</a>), this applies not only to the meat but also to the accompanying cereal.</i> were lying on the roof of the stoa<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The stoa surrounding the Temple Mount. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 13b" href="/Pesachim.13b">Babli 13b</a></i>. As long as they were lying there, everybody was eating. If one was removed one suspends, neither does one eat nor burn. If both were removed, everybody started to burn.<br>Rabban Gamliel says, profane food is eaten the entire fourth [hour] and heave the entire fifth; and one burns at the start of the sixth.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: They were qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The breads exhibited as a sign. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 13b" href="/Pesachim.13b">Babli 13b</a>.</i>. And why does he treat them as disqualified? Not to slaughter the sacrifice for them<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">What means <i>on the first</i>? Does it mean on the first month, or on the first day of the holidays?</i>. But do they not become <i>sancta</i> only by the slaughter? They should be redeemed and be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">They were dedicated as bread accompanying a thanksgiving sacrifice; their holiness is that of a gift to the Temple, not that of a sacrifice. Once the sacrifice was slaughtered and the blood sprinkled on the altar, the bread becomes intrinsically holy and its status equals that of sacrificial meat which cannot be redeemed. This implies that any time before the slaughter, the bread can be redeemed to regain profane status and other bread dedicated in its stead. The bread cannot become disqualified if the corresponding sacrifice is not slaughtered.</i>! Rebbi Ḥanania said, he has profane [bread] before him which he could not eat and you are saying, they should be redeemed and be eaten? Rebbi [Ḥanina]<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">The name is missing in the ms. and the printed editions but retained in the quote of this passage by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Pesachim 13b:9:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Pesachim.13b.9.1">Tosaphot (13b <i>s.v.</i> אלא</a>).</i> said, they were disqualified. Since they are in a hurry to bring their thanksgiving sacrifices because of the leavened bread with the sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi assumes that thanksgiving sacrifices may be offered on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>. These are family sacrifices and in this case must be eaten as long as the bread may be eaten. Since no sacrifices may be offered before the daily morning sacrifice, the time to consume an entire animal is very short. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 13b" href="/Pesachim.13b">Babli 13b</a> holds that no thanksgiving sacrifice is accepted on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> and refers R. Yannai’s statement to bread dedicated for such a sacrifice which then cannot be used for sacrificial purposes.</i>, it is impossible that not the blood of one of them be spilled<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">An argument accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 13b" href="/Pesachim.13b">Babli 13b</a> as minority opinion of R. Simeon ben Eleazar.</i> and it becomes disqualified.
It was stated: Two cows were ploughing [in Jerusalem]<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 14a" href="/Pesachim.14a">Babli 14a</a>. In this version they were ploughing on Mount Olivet and could be seen everywhere in Jerusalem. One had to stop eating leavened bread when only one cow was seen.</i>. But is Jerusalem not a place where they used not to work on the Fourteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> is a semi-holiday. As noted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.1.1">Mishnah 4:1</a>, whether work in the morning was permitted or forbidden was a matter of local custom.</i>? They were looking as if ploughing. There are Tannaim who stated: Two lights were burning. There are Tannaim who stated: Two sheets<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Two large linen sheets displayed as flags.</i>. Rebbi Phineas said, they do not disagree. He who said, two cows, two lights, on a weekday. He who said, two sheets, on the Sabbath.
Rebbi Ḥananiah asked before Rebbi Mana, should they not insitute trumpet blowing? If you say so, it would turn out that [they would say, maybe] they blow for the daily sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">As required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:10" href="/Numbers.10.10"><i>Num.</i> 10:10</a>.</i>[, and they would be led astray]. He retorted, but did we not state: “Three to separate people from work, and three to separate between weekday and holiness”<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 5:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.5.5.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 5:5</a>. Every Friday afternoon after the daily sacrifice trumpets were blown to announce the coming of the Sabbath.</i>? He answered him, there every single Friday they are blowing, one will not err. But here it is once in a long time; if you say so, it would turn out that they would say, maybe they blow for the daily sacrifice, and they would be led astray.
Why<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Why may heave, which is a <i>sanctum</i> and may be eaten only by pure Cohanim, be eaten an additional hour?</i>? Because of its holiness or because its eaters are few? What is the difference between them? The breads of the thanksgiving sacrifice. If you are saying because its eaters are few, the eaters of these are many<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Of the 10 breads accompanying a thanksgiving sacrifice, 9 are eaten by the offerer and his family, only one by a Cohen.</i>. If you are saying because of its holiness, these are holy. Rebbi Nathan says, they<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees with R. Jehudah that two breads were displayed as a sign that leavened matter may be eaten. He disagrees with him on two points. First, that the breads were pure <i>sancta,</i> and that they were lifted together and eaten, exactly one hour before leavening had to be burned.</i> were taken when qualified, (that there be) [one waits]<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe’s text (in parentheses) is as good as the corrector’s [in brackets]/</i> one hour when they can be eaten, one suspends but does not burn. Could you say because its eaters are few<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are only two, they could be eaten by the person lifting them.</i>? No, because of its holiness; here also because of its holiness.
Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi said, it seems that practice has to follow Rabban Gamliel since he says it similar to both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr and R. Jehudah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i> There came Rebbi Abun, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Josadaq: practice follows Rabban Gamliel since what he says is similar to both of them.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥananiah, the executive officer of the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">The organizer of the Temple service; one of the few permanent positions in the Temple service.</i>, says, the Cohanim never refrained from burning meat which became impure by derivative impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:22" href="/Numbers.19.22"><i>Num.</i> 19:22</a>) <i>anything an impure person touches will become impure,</i> it is clear that biblical original impurity induces biblical derivative impurity. In both cases, sacrificial meat which became impure must be burned. The question is raised in the Halakhah, how many stages of derivative impurity are implied by biblical law and how many are purely rabbinical. If biblically impure meat would be burned together with rabbinically impure (but biblically pure) meat, the latter could become more impure before being destroyed.</i> with meat which became impure by original impurity even though they added impurity to it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:6:2-8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.6.2-8.4">Mishnah 7</a>, even though this is noted only later.</i> Bar Qappara said, original impurity is a word from the Torah, derivative impurity is of their words<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the terms used by R. Ḥananiah in the Mishnah. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 15" href="/Pesachim.15">Babli, 15a/15b</a>, the attributions are switched. In the Yerushalmi the attributions are confirmed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 8:3:2-4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.8.3.2-4.1"><i>Šeqalim</i> 8:5</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, both these and those are word of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, biblically pure food, even if it may no longer be eaten or sacrificed, may not be destroyed.</i>. In the opinion of Bar Qappara it is understandable<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Ḥananiah contains essential information, that one may cause impurity to food which is biblically pure in order to burn it together with biblically impure food. While it is a matter of dispute whether this applies to food in a profane setting, it is agreed that it is prohibited to increase impurity in a sacral setting.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, original impurity induces first degree derivative. Derivative impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Derivative impurity in the first degree such as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:22" href="/Numbers.19.22"><i>Num.</i> 19:22</a>. The biblical rule that for material susceptible to impurity contact with derivative impurity in the first degree induces derivative impurity in the second degree is derived from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:19" href="/Leviticus.7.19"><i>Lev.</i>7:19</a>. Since there is no verse which implies the possibility of biblical derivative impurity in the third degree, derivative impurity in the second degree biblically should be characterized as <i>disqualified for sacral use</i> rather than <i>impure.</i></i> induces second degree derivative. Second degree impurity which touched first degree one remains in its place, second degree, since third degree which touches first degree becomes second degree<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 14a" href="/Pesachim.14a">Babli 14a</a>. Then R. Ḥananiah’s statement is trivial and unnecessary.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a>.</i>: “The House of Shammai say, one does not burn pure meat with impure meat, but the House of Hillel permit.” In the opinion of Bar Qappara it is understandable. One burns what is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Derivative impurity in the second degree.</i> by the Torah with what is disqualified as impure<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Original impurity or derivative impurity in the first degree.</i> by the Torah. And it was necessary to let hear, impurity by their words with impurity by the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the terms used by R. Ḥananiah in the Mishnah. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 15" href="/Pesachim.15">Babli, 15a/15b</a>, the attributions are switched. In the Yerushalmi the attributions are confirmed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 8:3:2-4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.8.3.2-4.1"><i>Šeqalim</i> 8:5</a>.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, if one burns disqualified by the Torah together with impurity by the Torah, so much more impurity by the Torah together with impurity by the Torah. Rebbi Ḥananiah, the executive officer of the Cohanim, did state it in the name of the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Joḥanan, while the statement of R. Ḥananiah is trivial, it is important as summarizing the consensus of the Houses of Shammai and Hillel.</i>. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose: The opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan is understandable, for Rebbi Joḥanan said, for six doubts one was permanently suspending, but in Usha they decided burning for them<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 15b" href="/Shabbat.15b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 15b</a>.</i>. Was Rebbi Ḥananiah, the executive officer of the Cohanim, not before Usha<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">In Usha, the place of R. Jehudah, a loyal supporter of the Roman government, the Rabbinate was reconstituted by the third generation Tannaim after the death of Hadrian and the suspension of the latter’s anti-Jewish decrees. The executive officer of the Cohanim must have served in the Temple; he therefore belongs to the first generation of Tannaim.</i>? Before Usha there was no burning for their words<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinically impure <i>sancta</i> neither could be used nor destroyed.</i>. He told him, explain it if it became impure in a glass vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical impurity applies only to kinds of vessels and implements mentioned in the rules of impurity: earthenware and metal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:21" href="/Leviticus.6.21"><i>Lev.</i> 6:21</a>), leather and textiles (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:49" href="/Leviticus.13.49"><i>Lev.</i> 13:49</a>), bone and wood (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 31:20" href="/Numbers.31.20"><i>Num.</i> 31:20</a>). Other vessels cannot become impure; to this fact one attributes the great number of stone vessels found on archeological sites of the Second Temple period. (Similarly intrinsically pure vessels made of cow dung have not survived for the archeological record.) The impurity of glass vessels, unknown to Moses, is purely rabbinical. One has to take the information that the impurity of glass ware “was decided” by the heads of the Synhedrion to mean that they codified the rules which were popularly observed before. Glass vessels are compared to earthenware vessels since the former are made from sand, the latter from clay. They are also compared to metal vessels since if broken they can be melted down and made into new vessels.</i>. He retorted, even if you are saying that it became impure in a glass vessel, did not Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Abuna say in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi or Rav Jeremiah bar Abba, the first generation Babylonian Amora. The main place of the statement is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 8:11:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.8.11.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 8:11, Notes 98–105</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 14b" href="/Shabbat.14b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 14b</a>.</i>, Yose ben Yoˋezer from Sereda and Yose ben Joḥanan from Jerusalem decided impurity for Gentile land and glass ware<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">The land outside the Holy Land was always being considered impure (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 7:17" href="/Amos.7.17"><i>Am.</i>7:17</a>). What they decided was that earth of Gentile places, within or outside the Holy Land, is considered infected with the impurity of rotting human bodies. While it is in dispute whether Gentile corpses induce tent impurity, it is universally accepted that they transmit impurity by touching.</i>. [Rebbi Jehuda said, Jehudah ben Tabbai and Simeon ben Šetah decided on metal vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">The possible impurity of metal vessels is biblical (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.6.3">Note 178</a>). Following the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 16b" href="/Shabbat.16b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 16b</a>, what they decreed was that metal impure by the impurity of the dead should not become pure by being melted down, but only by sprinkling with water containing ashes of the Red Cow.</i>. Hillel and Shammai decided on purity of hands<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">They codified the rules that hands which were not all the time consciously guarded from impurity after being washed are impure in the second degree and, therefore, impart impurity to fluids, heave, and sacrifices but not to solid profane food (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Notes 136–137</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 14b" href="/Shabbat.14b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 14b</a>). Corrector’s addition from <i>Ketubot.</i></i>.] Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi or Rav Jeremiah bar Abba, the first generation Babylonian Amora.
