The main place of the statement is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 8:11:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.8.11.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 8:11, Notes 98–105</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 14b" href="/Shabbat.14b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 14b</a>.</i> was of the opinion that for Gentile land there is suspension and for glass ware there is suspension. Rebbi Yose was of the opinion that for Gentile lands there is suspension, for glassware burning<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">That it was decided that glassware has the biblical status of metalware.</i>. The following are the six doubts<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 4:5" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.4.5">Mishnah <i>Taharot</i> 4:5</a>.</i>: “On the doubt of a <i>bet happeras</i><sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">A place known to contain a grave whose position is no longer known or ascertainable. If heave was transported over this place, it might have become impure by tent impurity.</i>, on the doubt of Gentile land, on the doubt of clothing of the vulgar<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">A person not known to observe the rules of impurity. His clothing may be impure either by contact with his impure wife in her period or because he suffers from gonorrhea.<br>The quote here is lacunary; there is missing the possible impurity of vessels found in the public domain.</i>, on the doubt of spittle<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">Which might be the source of biblical original impurity as body fluid of a woman menstruating or suffering from flux or a male sufferer from gonorrhea.</i>, on the doubt of human urine which is separate from animal urine<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Urine was used industrially and as a household chemical. Animal urine is pure; human urine may be a source of biblical original impurity as body fluid of a person whose impurity is caused by his own body (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.6.3">Note 187</a>).</i>, on certain touch which is a doubt of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">If the fact that it touched the object in question is not in doubt; the only doubt is whether that object transmits impurity or not.</i> on these one burns heave.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Aqiba added and said, the Cohanim never refrained from burning oil which became disqualified by a <i>ṭevul-yom</i><sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">A person who is cleansed from impurity by immersion in a <i>miqweh,</i> who is no longer impure but prevented from touching <i>sancta</i> before the sundown following his immersion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev.</i> 22:7</a>).</i> in a lamp impure by the impurity of the dead even though they added impurity to it<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The oil was heave which became disqualified from being consumed by the touch of a <i>ṭevul-yom</i> but is biblically pure. By being filled into an impure lamp it becomes biblically impure.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> 7<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that in this tradition the statement of R. Aqiba in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:6:2-8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.6.2-8.4">Mishnah 7</a> was a separate Mishnah.</i>: In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan there<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">“Here” refers to the statement of R. Aqiba, “there” to that of R. Ḥananiah, the executive officer of the Cohanim (Notes 166,167).</i> one burns biblical impurity with biblical impurity. He<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba compared to R. Ḥananiah.</i> comes to add biblical disqualification with biblical impurity. In the opinion of Bar Qappara there<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">“Here” refers to the statement of R. Aqiba, “there” to that of R. Ḥananiah, the executive officer of the Cohanim (Notes 166,167).</i> one burns rabbinical impurity with biblical impurity. Here, biblical disqualification with biblical impurity only comes to diminish<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">This is unlikely.</i>. Explain it if he was <i>ṭevul yom</i> from a <i>bet happeras</i> which is rabbinical<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of Bar Qappara, the rabbinic impurity mentioned by R. Ḥananiah is secondary or tertiary impurity derived from biblical original impurity whereas R. Aqiba adds rabbinic impurity which has no biblical source.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah, the executive officer of the Cohanim, did state it in the name of the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Joḥanan, while the statement of R. Ḥananiah is trivial, it is important as summarizing the consensus of the Houses of Shammai and Hillel.</i>.
Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose: Rebbi Aqiba follows his opinion, since Rebbi Aqiba said, <i>shall be impure,</i> shall make impure by word of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:2:2-11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.2.2-11"><i>Sotah</i> 5:2</a>, R. Aqiba takes טמא as a transitive verb. Therefore he reads the expression יטמא as “will transmit impurity”, in contrast to טָמֵא “is passively impure.” This applies not only to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:22" href="/Numbers.19.22"><i>Num.</i>19:22</a> but also to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:32-35" href="/Leviticus.11.32-35"><i>Lev.</i> 11:32–35</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, even though Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:2:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.2.11"><i>Sotah</i> 5:2 Notes 103–106</a>. The position of R. Ismael on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:22" href="/Numbers.19.22"><i>Num.</i> 19:22</a> is not known from other sources.</i> does not accept <i>shall be impure,</i> shall make impure, for foodstuffs, he accepts <i>shall be impure,</i> shall make impure, for implements.
There are Tannaim who state “impurity of the dead”. There are Tannaim who state “by the impurity of the dead”<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">This is about the wording of R. Aqiba’s statement. A dead human body is the source of original impurity. A person or vessel touching the dead becomes originally impure, carrying “the impurity of the dead”.<br>A vessel touching such a person or vessel becomes impure in the first derivative degree “by the impurity of the dead”.</i>. He who said “impurity of the dead”, about vessels that have to be rinsed<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Impure clay vessels cannot be purified, but become pure only as potsherds or otherwise impossible to use as containers. The expression “vessels to be rinsed” comes from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:21" href="/Leviticus.6.21"><i>Lev.</i> 6:21</a> where it characterized metal pots. In rabbinic usage, the expression is applied to all vessels that can be purified; here it applies to wooden vessels since metal vessels are excluded in the next sentence.<br>The separate treatment of metal in cases of impurity of the dead is based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:16" href="/Numbers.19.16"><i>Num.</i> 19:16</a> where in the expression “slain by the sword” the mention of “sword” seems to be superfluous in the context and therefore one concludes that the sword, and by extension any metal, acquires the super-impurity of the dead to impart original impurity to its contents and anything it comes in contact with (<i>Sifry Num.</i> 127). The contents of the wooden vessel are impure in the second degree but those in the metal vessel in the first.</i>. He who said “by the impurity of the dead”, about metal vessels. What is the reason? <i>Any open vessel</i> etc. <i>is impure</i><sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:15" href="/Numbers.19.15"><i>Num.</i> 19:15</a>, referring mainly to clay vessels but also to all other non-metallic containers (<i>Sifry Num.</i> 126).</i><i>.</i> [It is] impure but does not become a source of impurity to transmit impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:2:2-11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.2.2-11"><i>Sotah</i> 5:2</a>, R. Aqiba takes טמא as a transitive verb. Therefore he reads the expression יטמא as “will transmit impurity”, in contrast to טָמֵא “is passively impure.” This applies not only to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:22" href="/Numbers.19.22"><i>Num.</i>19:22</a> but also to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:32-35" href="/Leviticus.11.32-35"><i>Lev.</i> 11:32–35</a>.</i>

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Meïr said, from their words we learned that one burns pure heave together with impure on Pesaḥ<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">The 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan where pure leavened heave becomes rabbinically forbidden as food at the start of the sixth hour and must be disposed of before its possession becomes biblically (for the majority) or rabbinically (for R. Meïr) forbidden at noontime.</i>. Rebbi Yose said to him, this is not the implication<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥananiah and R. Aqiba stated their positions only about (biblically or rabbinically) impure food, not about pure heave which becomes forbidden for an unrelated reason.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua agree that each of these is burned separately. Where did they disagree? On the suspended<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Heave whose status of purity is in doubt, which cannot be used either as pure or impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Terumot 8:7" href="/Mishnah_Terumot.8.7">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 8:7, Note 180</a>).</i> and impure ones, where Rebbi Eliezer says each one should be burned separately while Rebbi Joshua said, both together.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is “on Pesaḥ”? On the Fourteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">In biblical Hebrew <i>“Pesaḥ”</i> denotes the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan, the day of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifice <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:5" href="/Leviticus.23.5"><i>(Lev.</i> 23:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:16" href="/Deuteronomy.28.16"><i>Deut.</i> 28:16</a>). In popular language <i>“Pesaḥ”</i> denotes the festival of unleavened bread, the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>–21<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup>. The Mishnah uses the biblical expression.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, from the words<sup>204</sup><i class="footnote">Explaining what R. Meïr in the Mishnah is referring to.</i> of Rebbi Aqiba and Rebbi Ḥananiah the executive officer of the Cohanim. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, from the words of Rebbi Eliezer and the words of Rebbi Joshua. Rebbi Ze`ira said before Rebbi Yasa: The opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan is understandable<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann here endorses the opinion of earlier commentators who want to emend the text and switch the names of RR. Joḥanan and Simeon ben Laqish, since otherwise how could the latter ask where RR. Eliezer and Joshua enter the discussion? But it seems that with the two classical commentaries one may explain the text as it stands. According to R. Joḥanan, R. Meïr refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:6:2-8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.6.2-8.4">Mishnah 7</a>. Since RR. Eliezer and Joshua are mentioned only in the argument of R. Yose, the statement quoted at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.8.1">Mishnah 8</a> cannot be the one to which R. Meïr refers; it must be Mishnah <i>Terumot</i>8:7, quoted later in the Halakhah, which on the face of it deals with an unrelated subject, whether it is better to keep heave from becoming certainly impure or see to it that it be usable in conformity with the rules (Notes 216 ff.).</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, where enter Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua in the discussion? He told him, there are [different] Tannaim<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">There are Tannaim who state <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:6:2-8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.6.2-8.4">Mishnah 7</a> in the name of R. Eliezer and R. Joshua.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Everybody agrees that on the Sixteenth one burns pure and impure heave [together]<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">If leavened heave was discovered during the holiday it must be burned. If unleavened heave became impure during the holiday, it must be burned, in both cases by biblical decree. But <i>sancta</i> other than Temple sacrifices may not be burned on the holiday; the burning must be on the first of the intermediate days where work is permitted.</i>.
Rebbi Yasa asked: Why does Rebbi Yose say, this is not the implication? Rebbi Yasa had heard that Rebbi Joḥanan said, “from the words of Rebbi Aqiba and Rebbi Ḥananiah the executive officer of the Cohanim,” and he had heard that Bar Qappara said, “original impurity is a word from the Torah, derivative impurity is of their words<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the terms used by R. Ḥananiah in the Mishnah. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 15" href="/Pesachim.15">Babli, 15a/15b</a>, the attributions are switched. In the Yerushalmi the attributions are confirmed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 8:3:2-4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.8.3.2-4.1"><i>Šeqalim</i> 8:5</a>.</i>.” But he had not heard that Rebbi Joḥanan had said, “both these and those are words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, biblically pure food, even if it may no longer be eaten or sacrificed, may not be destroyed.</i>.” Therefore, he was asking: Just as it is permitted to burn what is (rabbinically) [biblically] forbidden with what is biblically impure, since one burns what is (rabbinically) [biblically] impure with what is biblically impure, so it should be permitted to burn what is (biblically) [rabbinically] forbidden with what is biblically impure since one burns what is (biblically) [rabbinically] impure with what is biblically impure. But there must be a difference between prohibition and impurity. And Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Everybody agrees that on the Sixteenth one burns pure and impure heave [together]<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">If leavened heave was discovered during the holiday it must be burned. If unleavened heave became impure during the holiday, it must be burned, in both cases by biblical decree. But <i>sancta</i> other than Temple sacrifices may not be burned on the holiday; the burning must be on the first of the intermediate days where work is permitted.</i>. Just as you did not make a difference for us between biblical prohibition and biblical impurity since one burns what is biblically impure with what is biblically impure, so you should not make a difference for us between what is rabbinically forbidden with what is biblically impure since one burns what is rabbinically impure with what is biblically impure. But it must be that both these and those are words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">Since the statement of R. Joḥanan about the 16<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> implies that it does not apply on the 14th, it is inconsistent with the statement of Bar Qappara. R. Yasa concluded that R. Joḥanan must disagree with Bar Qappara without having heard his statement. Then the correct text is the scribe’s (in parentheses), not the corrector’s the the printed texts’ [in brackets] since for R. Joḥanan Mishnah 7 refers to biblical impurities.</i>. There came Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba from Tyre and said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: both these and those are words of the Torah, and I<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yasa.</i> said, it is correct. There<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">“Here” refers to the statement of R. Aqiba, “there” to that of R. Ḥananiah, the executive officer of the Cohanim (Notes 166,167).</i> (rabbinic) [biblical] impurity with biblical impurity but here<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba compared to R. Ḥananiah.</i> biblical disqualification with biblical impurity. Therefore Rebbi Yose said, this is not the implication<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the reason given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.8.1">Note 201</a>, if R. Joḥanan’s statement about the 16<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> is correct, R. Yose will not accept R. Meïr’s treatment of rabbinic and biblical impurity as equivalent.</i>. The position of Rebbi Joḥanan is difficult for Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Joḥanan is an Amora and R. Meïr a Tanna, the statement rather should be that R. Joḥanan’s position is inconsistent with R. Meïr’s.</i>; therefore Rebbi Yose said, this is not the implication. Bar Qappara must argue like Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, as Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, from the words of Rebbi Eliezer and the words of Rebbi Joshua. Then the opinion of Bar Qappara is difficult for Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">Bar Qappara allows burning of rabbinically impure sacrificial meat with biblically impure even though momentarily the rabbinically impure will become biblically impure. In Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 8:7, discussed in the next paragraph, R. Joshua prescribes making possibly rabbinically impure heave biblically impure. Since the first generation R. Joshua is an overriding authority, it is difficult to see how the third generation R. Yose could disagree.</i> based on what Rebbi Meïr said, “from their words we learned.” Rebbi Abbin said, Rebbi Meïr is consistent<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Meïr the disputes between Bar Qappara and R. Joḥanan and R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish are irrelevant. Even if R. Ḥananiah speaks only about biblical impurities, it makes no difference for R. Meïr who applies all biblical stringencies to rabbinic prohibitions.</i> since Rebbi Meïr is restrictive in their words as in words of the Torah. Where do we find that Rebbi Meïr is restrictive in their words as in words of the Torah? Rebbi Ḥinena said, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Niddah</i> 6:13.</i>: “A woman who sees a stain is out of order and worries because of flux[, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, nothing in stains implies flux<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">In biblical rules (which are difficult for rabbinic women to follow, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.4.1.1"><i>Niddah</i> Chapter 4, Note 3</a>) there is a big difference between menstrual discharges (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:15-24" href="/Leviticus.19.15-24"><i>Lev.</i> 19:15–24</a>) and non-menstrual flux (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:25-30" href="/Leviticus.19.25-30"><i>Lev.</i> 19:25–30</a>). In rabbinic theory, there are 11 days after the end of a menstrual period in which no discharges are menstrual. If a woman discovers a stain on an undergarment, it is rabbinically considered evidence of a discharge. Since this is a purely rabbinic rule, she is “out of order” in that the 11 day rule cannot be applied. In addition, if the undergarment was worn for three days and the stain is large enough to possibly be the result of three discharges, for R. Meïr all rules spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:28-30" href="/Leviticus.19.28-30"><i>vv</i>. 19:28–30</a> do apply even though there is no biblical impurity.</i>].”
There<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 8:7. The text from here to the end of the Chapter is from <i>Terumot</i> 8:8–9 (Notes 180–235,<b>ת</b>); it cannot be understood without <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.8.1">Mishnaiot 8:8–9</a> which are not reproduced here.</i>, we have stated: An amphora of heave about which a doubt of impurity arose, Rebbi Eliezer says, if it was at a vulnerable place, he should move it to a hidden place. If it was uncovered, he should cover it. Rebbi Joshua says, if it was at a hidden place, he should move it to a vulnerable place. If it was covered, he should uncover it. Rabban Gamliel says, he should not change anything<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">If it were certainly impure, heave wine could be used to settle dust on dirt floors, heave oil or grain as fuel. If there only is a doubt, the heave is called “suspended”. It cannot be used as impure since it might be pure, nor as pure since it might be impure. R. Eliezer holds that one has to treat this heave according to all rules of holiness. R. Joshua holds that one has to make sure that the heave be used somehow. Since it never will be usable as pure heave, one has to make sure it will be usable impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Bekhorot 33b" href="/Rashi_on_Bekhorot.33b">Rashi in <i>Bekhorot</i> 33b</a>).</i>.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, from the opinion of all three of them: It is forbidden to burn suspended [heave]<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">Since neither of them proposes burning the heave as an alternative.</i>.
The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: The first amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 8:7 is consistent with the position of R. Yose in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:8:2-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.8.2-13"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:8</a>. Since R. Joshua only permits to put suspended heave in a situation where there is great probability that it accidentally will become impure but does not allow actively to make it impure, it follows that he will not allow pure heave to be made impure in the process of burning, as prescribed by R. Meïr.</i> following Rebbi Yose, the second<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">The amphora which is the topic of Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 8:10. If there is a choice of either making heave impure or letting it go to waste, R. Joshua permits to make it impure. This parallels the position of R. Meïr in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:8:2-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.8.2-13"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:8</a>, who permits making heave impure in the process of required destruction. Since that Mishnah is not quoted here, it is clear that the origin of the text is in <i>Terumot.</i></i> following Rebbi Meïr. The colleagues say, the first amphora following Rebbi Yose but Rebbi Meïr will not agree; the second following Rebbi Meïr but Rebbi Yose will not agree. Rebbi Yose told them, be careful what you are saying. The first amphora following Rebbi Yose, but following Rebbi Meïr one burns and following Rebbi Simeon one burns. Then Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Simeon should form a majority against Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">Even though R. Yose (ben Halafta) is a greater authority than either R. Meïr or R. Simeon, they together should be a majority to determine practice.</i> and one should burn. In addition, we understand from the following: we see the rabbis, if a case comes before them, they say, go and suspend<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">In the last generation of Amoraim practice still followed R. Yose.</i>. Where do we find that Rebbi Simeon says, one burns? It is what we have stated: “Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua agree that each of these is burned separately.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon stated this<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosefta 1:5</a>. While this does not prove that R. Simeon agrees with the position of R. Meïr about <i>sancta,</i> it establishes that he agrees with him about heave. The translation follows the spelling in <i>Terumot</i>, שנייה.</i>. If you want to say that Rebbi Meïr does not recognize suspension<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">But treats heave of doubtful status as impure.</i>, have we not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta 3:10</a>, about leavened heave to be disposed of if the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan is a Sabbath.</i>: “Suspended (pure)<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">With the text in a later paragraph and in <i>Terumot</i> one has to read: “or impure”.</i> heave is burned on Sabbath eve when it gets dark, the words of Rebbi Meïr; but the Sages say, at its appointed time<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">The 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan.</i>.” Rebbi Ezra<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">This reading, R. Ezra (fifth generation) sitting before R. Mana II, is preferable to that of <i>Terumot,</i> R. Zeˋira (third generation) before R. Mana I (early second generation).</i> said before Rebbi Mana, explain it that it is suspended because he does not intend to ask about it. He said to him, so also said Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">Fifth generation, the teacher of R. Mana II.</i>: All we are occupied with here is with suspended [heave] about which he does not intend to ask<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">Since designating any produce as heave is a dedication, it has the status of a vow and as such is subject to annulment by an ordained rabbi or a court. If the vow is annulled, the original produce reverts to profane status where secondary impurity is excluded; in most cases this means that it will automatically revert to purity. Then heave must be given from some other produce of the same harvest. Since all problems arising for suspended heave can be easily resolved by asking for annulment, it is clear that the Mishnah must assume that this way out is not chosen.</i>; but suspended [heave] about which he intends to ask is pure. It was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">A related text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:18" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.18">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i>7:18</a>.</i>: “Suspended heave about which they said, is it pure? It is impure. If he said, I am keeping it in order to ask about it, it is pure.” What about it? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it if the doubt arose at sundown. Then one cannot infer anything<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no time to burn anything, the opinion of the Sages must be followed even by their opponents. This does not prove anything about the position of R. Meïr if the heave already was suspended in the morning.<br>A different treatment in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 20b" href="/Pesachim.20b">Babli, 20b</a>.</i>.
And you<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">Continuation of the attack of R. Yose the Amora against the statement of the colleagues.</i> are saying, the first amphora following Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Meïr will not agree<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">While this R. Yose’s argument in both texts it is clear from the following that the discussion is about the second of their statements, that R. Yose cannot agree with the ruling of Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 8:8 and 8:10 about a barrel of pure heave wine which is breaking on top of a container of impure wine.</i>? But did we not state:<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">“Lifting” is explained in <i>Terumot</i> Chapter 5. If heave falls into a container of profane produce of the same kind as the heave and the volume of the profane matter is less than 101 times that of the heave, the entire matter is forbidden to non-Cohanim. But if it is at least that amount, a volume equal to that of the heave which was lost can be lifted from the mixture and declared as replacement heave. Then the particles of the original heave in the mixture revert to profane status and become permitted to lay persons.<br>Mishnaiot <i>Terumot</i> 8:8,10: “If an amphora broke in the upper part of a wine press whose lower part was impure, R. Eliezer and R. Joshua agree that if one can save a <i>quartarius</i> from it, he shall save that in purity.… Rebbi Joshua said, this is not a case for which I have been warned not to make it impure …”</i> When has this been said? Regarding a vat which contains enough to lift it. But for a vat which does not contain enough to lift it, it is forbidden to make any amount impure. And if it follows Rebbi Meïr, whether it is a vat which contains enough to lift it or a vat which does not contain enough to lift it, is it forbidden to make any amount impure?<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Meïr permits to bring impurity to anything that later automatically would become impure. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 21a" href="/Pesachim.21a">Babli 21a</a>.</i> In addition, from what we have stated: “Rebbi Yose said, this is not the implication,” nobody says “this is not” unless he agrees with the premise. How is that? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there in order to care for the property of Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Negaim 12:5" href="/Mishnah_Negaim.12.5">Mishnah <i>Negaˋim</i> 12:5</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 20b" href="/Pesachim.20b">Babli 20b</a>.</i>; here what do you have? Does the Jew not lose money even here? For he needs to burn wood separately<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">This text is corrupt. The correct version is in <i>Terumot:</i> For he needs wood to burn each lot separately.</i> and this separately? They worried about a big loss, they did not worry about a small loss. Rebbi Ḥananiah said before Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Terumot:</i> R. Yose ben R. Abun said.</i>, explain it following him who said, from the words of Rebbi Aqiba and Rebbi Ḥanina the executive officer of the Cohanim; and one cannot infer anything<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">There is no inference to be drawn from the Mishnah in <i>Pesaḥim</i> which according to R. Meïr speaks about impurity and disqualification and in <i>Terumot</i> dealing with impurity and purity. R. Yose’s second objection to the colleagues is not proven.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Ila, both in the name of Rebbi Eleazar. The first amphora following Rebbi Yose, but the second both following Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Ezra said before Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">This reading, R. Ezra (fifth generation) sitting before R. Mana II, is preferable to that of <i>Terumot,</i> R. Zeˋira (third generation) before R. Mana I (early second generation).</i>: Does this not disagree with Rebbi Yose? He told him, if not following Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Terumot</i> the names Yose and Meïr are systematically switched. For the interpretation there is no difference since, as stated in the next sentence, the two rabbis agree in this matter. Nevertheless it seems that the text in <i>Terumot</i> is more reliable since the remainder of the discussion concentrates on determining R. Meïr’s position.</i>, we may say not following Rebbi Meïr since we find that Rebbi Yose is burning suspended [heave] in all cases. Rebbi Mana said, I went to Caesarea and heard Rebbi Zeriqan in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: Rebbi Meïr is burning suspended [heave] in all cases. I asked him, even in a case where it is suspended by word of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">If we hold that the rule which states that doubts in cases of biblical commandments have to be decided restrictively is itself biblical, then suspended heave would have to be considered impure by biblical standards and immediately used as fuel.</i>? He told me, if I explain it that it became impure in Gentiles’ dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">Since Gentiles are suspected of burying their stillbirths in the dirt floors of their houses, their dwellings in the Holy Land are considered possibly impure by “tent impurity” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.2.2"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 6 Note 25</a>). Practice disregards the opinion of R. Simeon who denied the possibility of “tent impurity” for Gentiles.</i>, what do you have in your hand? It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 18:3" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.18.3">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i>18:7</a>.</i>: “For Gentiles’ dwelling one suspends, Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, one burns.” Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Zeˋura: Rebbi Meïr burns suspended [heave] any day of the year, and so it was stated: “Suspended [and]<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">Added from the text in <i>Terumot,</i> necessary by the context.</i> impure heave are burned on Sabbath eve when it gets dark, the words of Rebbi Meïr; but the Sages say, at its appointed time<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">The 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan.</i>.” Should he not burn in the morning? Explain it that he was lazy and did not burn. You should know that this is so, since it was stated “impure”<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">Since impure heave is used as fuel, there is no reason why it should not be used to burn leavened matter in the morning. Since in most years Passover Eve is not a Sabbath and one is obligated to burn in the morning, if Friday is the 13<sup>th</sup> of Nisan the rule is that in this case also one burns in the morning.</i>. Not because he was lazy and did not burn? Rebbi Abba Mari the brother of Rebbi Yose said, explain it that the impurity came to it at that moment and one cannot infer anything.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon and Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">The order appearing in the text of <i>Terumot,</i> R. Joshua (first generation), R. Simeon (third generation) is preferable.</i> both said the same thing. Rebbi Ila said, Rebbi Simeon in <i>Bekhorot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 5:2" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.5.2">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 5:2</a>, quoted in the next paragraph. While a firstling calf or lamb has to be treated as a sacrifice, R. Simeon permits any surgical operation if the health of the animal requires it, even if it is clear that by the operation the animal will become disqualified as sacrifice.</i> and Rebbi Joshua in <i>Terumot,</i> neither of them will agree with the other. Rebbi Zeˋira said, it is reasonable that Rebbi Simeon agrees with Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">Talmudic style requires the addition found in <i>Terumot:</i> “But R. Joshua will not agree with R. Simeon”. R. Joshua (<i>Terumot</i>8:8–10) permits to cause impurity to heave in order to save food; R. Simeon permits to make a blemish on a firstling in order to save its life. But the Mishnah in <i>Bekhorot</i> states that a firstling with this intentionally induced blemish may not be slaughtered; it cannot become food for anybody. Therefore it is not necessary to conclude that R. Joshua agrees with R. Simeon. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 33b" href="/Bekhorot.33b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 33b</a>.</i>. [Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said to Rebbi Zeˋira: In your opinion, since you say that Rebbi Simeon agrees with Rebbi Joshua, did we not state: How may one burn suspended [heave] with impure one]<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is neither in the Mishnah nor in <i>Terumot;</i> the question is correct as objection of R. Eliezer to R. Joshua.</i>? “Rebbi Eliezer agrees with Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">While the Mishnah states, “R. Eliezer and R. Joshua agree”, the formulation here is more to the point.</i> that each of these is burned separately.” Should one not burn both together? It is pure by words of the Torah. You are the one who decided on burning. In any case, would it not become disqualified by inattention<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">Heave must be eaten in purity, which can be guaranteed only if the heave is guarded at all times or at least kept at a place locked away from possible impurities (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 34a" href="/Pesachim.34a">Babli 34a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 7:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.7.2.2"><i>Šeqalim</i> 7:2</a>). Since on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan heave cannot be eaten after 10 a.m., there is no need to watch it any longer and, by being released from supervision, it should become disqualified immediately even by biblical standards.</i>? Did nor Rebbi Joḥanan say, inattention is a word of the Torah; blood affliction following Rebbi Simeon is Torah, the second amphora following Rebbi Meïr is Torah? It is not so<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">Even if disqualified for other reasons, it still has to be watched; the prior objection is baseless.</i>; but he watches it that it should not come in contact with other pure things. Rebbi Isaac, the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya the scribe, objected: Think of it, if it was put on coals<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">If one starts the fire to burn the leavened matter, guarding against impurity certainly is unnecessary. The answer is that this argument is irrelevant since we are dealing with the time before the fire was started.</i>. He told him, after it was put there<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">If one starts the fire to burn the leavened matter, guarding against impurity certainly is unnecessary. The answer is that this argument is irrelevant since we are dealing with the time before the fire was started.</i>. Rebbi Mana said to Rebbi Shammai: You are saying that Rebbi Simeon agrees with Rebbi Joshua. Even Rebbi Joshua does not agree with Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">The positions of R. Joshua in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.7.2-4"><i>Pesaḥim</i>1:7</a> and <i>Terumot</i> 8:8–10 do not necessarily coincide.</i>. He told him, these are Tannaim. There Rebbi Meïr in the name of Rebbi Joshua, here Rebbi Simeon in the name of Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to fully reconstruct the original position of R. Joshua since the only knowledge we have of his statements is through the interpretation of third generation Amoraim.</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 5:2" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.5.2">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 5:2</a> [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Section 7 10" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Section_7.10"><i>Sifra Emor Parashah</i> 7(10</a>)]. Since a firstling is a sacrifice by birth, it has to be treated according to the rules of sacrifices. The only difference is that a dedicated sacrifice which develops a blemish must be redeemed to become profane whereas a firstling in the hands of a Cohen which develops a blemish automatically becomes profane and may be eaten by everybody and in impurity. Therefore, Cohanim are suspected to induce blemishes on the firstlings in their possession.</i>: “A firstling afflicted by blood, even if it is going to die, cannot be bled, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. But the Sages say, it should be bled, only he should not intend to cause a blemish. If it did cause a blemish, it may not be slaughtered because of it. Rebbi Simeon says, he should bleed it even if he makes a blemish.”
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Rebbi Jehudah parallels Rabban Gamliel, the rabbis parallel Rebbi Eliezer, Rebbi Simeon parallels Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">The opinions stated in Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 8:7 (“the first amphora.”) The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 35b" href="/Bekhorot.35b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 35b</a> identifies the position of the Sages with that of R. Joshua (i. e., the operative opinion in <i>Bekhorot</i> with that in <i>Terumot</i>) while the Yerushalmi implies that practice should follow R. Simeon.</i>. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Simeon: “He should bleed it even if he intends to make a blemish.” This parallels another opinion of Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">His opinion about the “second amphora” where he permits to induce impurity on most of the heave in order to save a small portion in purity.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said before Rebbi Zeˋira: Explain about <i>sancta</i> for which he is liable following Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is missing in <i>Terumot.</i> The verse is formulated for all sacrifices which develop a blemish; it is more reasonable to apply it to the vast majority of dedicated sacrifices which have to be redeemed rather than to firstlings for which change to semi-profane status is automatic.<br>If somebody dedicates a specific animal and it later develops a blemish, there is no sacrifice. But if he vows “a sacrifice” and then dedicates a particular animal which later develops a blemish, he has to redeem the animal by an unblemished replacement.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. The reason of Rebbi Jehudah: <i>You shall not eat it, on the earth you shall you shall spill it like water</i><sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:24" href="/Deuteronomy.12.24"><i>Deut.</i> 12:24</a>.</i><i>.</i> I did permit you its blood only for spilling. Rebbi Abba Mari the brother of Rebbi Yose said, is not about disqualified sancta written so, <i>you shall not eat it, on the earth you shall spill it like water</i><sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">One would have expected R. Jehudah to use <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:23" href="/Deuteronomy.15.23"><i>Deut.</i> 15:23</a>, speaking of the firstling: <i>Only its blood you shall not eat, spill it onto the ground like water.</i> The verse used gives the rules for animals which develop blemishes after being dedicated. This also includes firstlings.</i>? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, it was said for preparation<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">Produce in the ground is impervious to impurity. It remains so after harvesting as long as it stays completely dry. Blood is one of the fluids that prepare dry food to accept impurity (cf. <i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Notes 136,141). Since the rules for preparation are spelled out for water (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:38" href="/Leviticus.11.38"><i>Lev.</i> 11:38</a>), other fluids have this property of water only if there is a biblical verse which compares them to water. <i>Sifry Deut.</i> #71.</i>. Just as the water prepares, so the blood shall prepare.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, both of them explain the same verse<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:21" href="/Leviticus.22.21"><i>Lev</i>. 22:21</a>.</i>: <i>Perfect it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">Any animal sacrifice.</i><i>shall be for goodwill; any blemish shall not be on it.</i> Rebbi Simeon explains: As long as it is for goodwill, you may not induce a blemish. If it is no longer for goodwill, you may induce a blemish. But the Sages say, even if it is all blemishes, you may not add a blemish<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">As Rashi explains in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 35b" href="/Bekhorot.35b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 35b</a>, the Sages hold that R. Simeon would be justified if the verse read “<i>no blemish</i> shall be on it.” But the involved language, <i>any blemish,</i> forbids the imposition of a new blemish on existing blemishes.</i>.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Any time one is permitted to eat one feeds it<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Leavened matter on the 14th of Nisan which has to be eliminated before noontime.</i> to domestic or wild animals or birds, or sells it to a Non-Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since only leavened matter in the possession of a Jew has to be eliminated, the Gentile may keep the leavened matter bought from the Jew and also use it during Passover time.</i>, and one may have usufruct from it<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">For example one may use the matter as fuel and its ashes as fertilizer.</i>. If its time has passed<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">After noontime on the 14th, even though according to the majority opinion the biblical prohibition of usufruct only starts shortly before sundown, there is a rabbinic prohibition of usufruct for the entire afternoon.</i> one may not have usufruct from it, nor use it to heat an oven or a cooking stove. Rebbi Jehudah says, leavened matter may only be eliminated by burning. But the Sages say, he makes crumbs and scatters them in the wind or throws it into the Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, which also is the reading of the Munich ms. of the Babli, R. Hananel, some texts of Alfasi, and Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah, the Sages disagree with R. Jehudah. The Mishnah in the printed Babli, starting with the <i>editio princeps,</i> reads “But the Sages say, he <i>also</i> may make crumbs and …” This determines current practice following R. Jehudah, even though <i>also</i> is missing in the Mishnah quote in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 28a" href="/Pesachim.28a">Babli Halakhah (28a</a>). Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 28a" href="/Pesachim.28a"><i>Diqduqe Soferim Pesaḥim</i> p. 28a Note 1</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Any time” etc. Rebbi Immi said, who is the Tanna of “any time when he is permitted to eat he is permitted to feed, when it is forbidden to the eater he is forbidden to feed”? Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 21a" href="/Pesachim.21a">Babli 21a</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:4</a> R. Meïr permits to eat up to the time one has to dispose of the leftovers, in contrast to R. Jehudah who forbids to eat starting one hour before the leavened matter will be forbidden. The latter could not have formulated “any time”.</i>. But following Rebbi Jehudah, in the fifth hour even though he is forbidden to eat he is permitted to feed. Rebbi Abba objected: Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.5.1">Mishnah 3:5</a>, presumed to be R. Meïr’s.</i>, “sour dough has to be burned but one who eats it is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:19" href="/Exodus.12.19"><i>Ex.</i> 12:19</a> contains two statements. 1° leavened matter may not be in a Jew’s possession on Passover, and 2°, it is a deadly sin, punishable by extirpation, to eat leavened matter during the holiday. The Tanna of the Mishnah holds that the second statement is not applicable to matters commonly considered to be inedible.</i>,” and did not Rav Ḥuna say in the name of Rebbi, one may feed it to the dogs<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> excludes feeding to animals.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, did we state “any leavened matter”, not “time<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.1">Mishnah 2:1</a> is formulated to apply only to the 14th of Nisan. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.5.1">Mishnah 3:5</a>, referring to Nisan 15–21, does not contradict the earlier Mishnah.</i>”? Who is the Tanna of “time”, Rebbi Meïr.
Rebbi Bun bar Ḥiyya said before Rebbi Zeˋira: This<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the Mishnah, “if its time has passed one may not have usufruct from it,” seems to imply that any use from which the owner does not derive usufruct is permitted.</i> implies that one is permitted to feed it to ownerless animals. Rebbi Jeremiah objected, did we not state “he crumbles it”? Rebbi Jeremiah was of the opinion, loaf sized<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the Mishnah as requiring only that loaves should not be disposed of whole, but may be disposed of in sizeable pieces. If these are thrown to the wind, the probability is great that a bird or a squirrel will pick them up; the reference to <i>his</i> usufruct seems to be unnecessary.</i>. Rebbi Yose said to him, it only mentioned “he crumbles it”, that when he crumbled it it was nullified<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah requires that any leftovers be reduced to tiny crumbs which are of no use. These crumbs do no longer qualify as food; the leavened matter is considered disposed off even if not thrown to the wind.</i>. But the following says it, <i>leavend matter may not be eaten today</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:3-4" href="/Exodus.13.3-4"><i>Ex.</i> 13:3–4</a>: <i>Moses said to the people, remember this day on which you left Egypt, the House of Slavery, for with a strong hand did the Eternal remove you from there, and leavened matter shall not be eaten. Today you are leaving, in the Spring month.</i> Since the text is written without commas and periods, the period implied by the oral tradition of separation of verses may be disregarded. A different interpretation of the same reading is in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael ad loc.</i> (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 62 line 2), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 28b" href="/Pesachim.28b">Babli 28b</a>.</i><i>,</i> even by the dogs. This includes to forbid it for usufruct. Where do we hold? If for his own dog, the prohibition of usufruct had been stated<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The passive voice in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:3" href="/Exodus.13.3"><i>Ex.</i> 13:3</a> implies that the identity of the eater is irrelevant, against the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.3">Note 11</a>.</i>. But we must hold for the dog [of others]. This implies that one is prohibited from feeding it to ownerless animals.
<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The text from here to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.10">Note 64</a> also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:1:3-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.1.3-9"><i>Orlah</i> 3:1, Notes 10–44</a>, <b>ע</b>. It seems that the origin of the text is in <i>Pesaḥim</i> since only here the verses are quoted in full and an important sentence is missing in <b>ע</b>. In the Babli, the parallel is 21b–23a.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In the printed editions of the Babli, R. Eleazar is not mentioned, but his name appears in the Munich ms.</i>: Everywhere it is written <i>do not eat, do not eat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Plural.</i><i>, it shall not be eaten,</i> you understand a prohibition of usufruct included in the prohibition of eating unless the verse is explicit and explains to you as it did explain about limbs of a living animal and a carcass. What did it explain about limbs of a living animal? <i>Flesh torn in the field you shall not eat; throw it to the dog</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex.</i> 22:30</a>. Why is it necessary to permit torn limbs as dog food? R. Eleazar argues that this shows that without such permission the limb would be forbidden for all usufruct.</i><i>.</i> And what did it explain about a carcass? <i>Do not eat any carcass; to the sojourner in your gates you shall give it and he may eat it, or sell to the stranger.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut.</i> 14:21</a>.</i> Ḥizqiah stated a disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 21b" href="/Pesachim.21b">Babli 21b</a>, Ḥizqiah accepts the statement of R. Eleazar only for the passive formulation; later (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.9">Note 48</a>) this is clarified to be the position of Ḥizqiah and R. Joḥanan in a second version. In this first version, Ḥizqiah must hold that an inference from a verse is only valid if there is no second verse leading to the same result. The theoretical basis is the recognition that the legal texts in the Torah are incomplete and sometimes contradictory as a system. In addition, it is held that words do not change their meaning in legal contexts. Therefore, a mechanism of translation of the Torah text into a coherent and reasonably complete system must exist. The rule quoted by Ḥizqiah is one of the translation rules; cf. H. Guggenheimer, Logical Problems in Jewish Tradition, in: Ph. Longworth (ed.), <i>Confrontations with Judaism</i> (London 1966) pp. 171–196.<br>Since here the mention of the torn limb and the carcass both lead to the same argument, either one of them would be superfluous and, therefore, both must be needed for other inferences. The argument of R. Eleazar is refuted.</i>. What does one forbid to the dog<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The dog is not a human and is not obliged by any rules.</i>?
But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:23" href="/Leviticus.7.23"><i>Lev.</i> 7:23</a>. This paragraph discusses verses which present difficulties for R. Eleazar.</i>: <i>Any fat of cattle, sheep, or goats you shall not eat</i>? Do you not have to understand the prohibition of usufruct from the prohibition of eating? There is a difference, for it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:23" href="/Leviticus.7.23"><i>Lev.</i> 7:23</a>. In the opinion of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 23a" href="/Pesachim.23a">Babli 23a</a>, the verse is needed to permit any use of profane fat since otherwise one would argue that since fat is forbidden for humans but required for the altar, fat of animals unfit for the altar should be permitted for use in the Temple but forbidden for profane use. In the <i>Sifra Ṣaw (Parasha</i> 10), the argument of the Babli is attributed to R. Yose the Galilean; R. Aqiba concludes that fat of domesticated animals is not food nor subject to the impurity of food.<br>In the opinion of the Yerushalmi, since some fat is permitted for unrestricted use, no fat can be forbidden for usufruct in the absence of an explicit verse. For Ḥizqiah, this is a third verse that could be used for R. Eleazar’s argument; nobody will contest that three parallel verses invalidate the argument. In the second version of Ḥizqiah’s position (below, after <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.9">Note 49</a>), he needs the verse to permit use of fat for work on Temple property.</i>: <i>But fat of a carcass and fat of a torn animal may be used for any work, only it may not be eaten</i>. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:16" href="/Deuteronomy.12.16"><i>Deut.</i> 12:16</a>.</i>: <i>Only the blood you may not eat</i>? Do you not have to understand the prohibition of usufruct from the prohibition of eating? There is a difference, for it is written: <i>You shall pour it on the ground like water</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 22b" href="/Pesachim.22b">Babli 22b</a> deduces from here that animal blood is a fluid which prepares for impurity only if it is spilled on the ground (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Note 136</a>). The argument of the Yerushalmi, and an argument that animal blood prepares for impurity in all cases, is in <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 73 and later here, in the second version of Ḥizqiah. (Preparation for impurity is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1-2"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Notes 136–141</a>.)</i><i>.</i> Since water is permitted for use, so blood shall be permitted for use. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 32:33" href="/Genesis.32.33"><i>Gen.</i> 32:33</a>.</i>: <i>Therefore, the Children of Israel do not eat the sinew of the sciatic nerve?</i> Rebbi Abbahu said, I explained it by the sinew of a carcass<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is more explicit in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 22a" href="/Pesachim.22a">Babli 22a</a>. R. Abbahu holds that when carcass and tom meat was permitted for the sojourner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.9">Note 53</a>) and the pagan, the entire animal was permitted, including the fat. Then the last paragraph of Note 24 establishes that the schiatic sinew cannot be forbidden for usufruct.</i>. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:14" href="/Leviticus.23.14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:14</a>.</i>: <i>Bread, parched or fresh grains you shall not eat until this very day?</i> Rebbi Abba Mari, the brother of Rebbi Yose, said there is a difference since the verse fixed a time for it. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:42" href="/Leviticus.11.42"><i>Lev.</i> 11:42</a>.</i>: <i>Do not eat them for they are abominations?</i> Rebbi [Mana]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <i>Orlah,</i> missing here.</i> said, that excludes their prohibition of usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>splenium,</i> Greek σπληνίον, τό, “pad, wound dressing.”</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He who makes a wound dressing<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>splenium,</i> Greek σπληνίον, τό, “pad, wound dressing.”</i> from a stoned ox<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 24b" href="/Pesachim.24b">Babli 24b</a> explains that one might use fat from the stoned ox to cover a wound.<br>The ox was stoned by order of the court because it killed humans (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28-29" href="/Exodus.21.28-29"><i>Ex</i>. 21:28–29</a>). Its meat is forbidden for usufruct as explained in the sequel. Cf. <i>Mekhilta dR.Ismael Mišpaṭim</i>10 (p. 282).</i> or from leftover sour matter after Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah had stated that leavened matter becomes prohibited for all usufruct in the afternoon of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan, after the holiday it cannot become permitted again.</i> cannot be whipped since its prohibition is not clear. For vineyard <i>kilaim</i> he is whipped since Rebbi Ḥanina said<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut.</i> 22:9</a>. For this derivation, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.1.3"><i>Kilaim</i> 8:1, Note 6</a>.</i>: <i>Lest it be sanctified,</i> lest fire should be kindled. For <i>ˋorlah</i> it is problematic. A prescriptive commandment to removal is written<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23" href="/Leviticus.19.23">Lev. 19:23</a> requires that the (budding) fruit is treated as “foreskin” and the foreskin has to be removed, one may take the verse as prescribing the removal of any <i>ˋorlah</i> fruit.</i>, a prohibition to eat is written<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Last two words of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23" href="/Leviticus.19.23"><i>Lev.</i> 19:23</a>. Since R. Joḥanan reads לֹ֥א יֵֽאָכֵֽל as prohibition of eating, not of usufruct, he follows his teacher Ḥizqiah in rejecting the argument of R. Eleazar.</i>, [“do not eat”]<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">An incorrect and unnecessary addition by the corrector, not part of the original ms.</i>, a prohibition to remove it is not written<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Non-fulfillment of a prescriptive commandment is not prosecutable.</i>.
In a <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> as stated here is not found in any other source except the Yerushalmi parallels in <i>Orlah</i> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 5:12:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.5.12.3"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 5:12 (Note 155</a>). In <i>Mekhilta Mišpaṭim</i> 19; quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 22b" href="/Pesachim.22b">Babli 22b</a>, <i>Qiddušin</i> 56b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 41a" href="/Bava_Kamma.41a"><i>Bava Qamma</i> 41a</a> the text explicitly notes that, since “its meat shall not be eaten” is included in the statement of the sentence to be passed by the court, only after judgment is rendered does slaughter become ineffective. This may also be he rule implied by the Yerushalmi Targum to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i>21:28</a>: וְלָא יִתְנְכַס לְמֵיכוּל יַת בִּשְׂרֵיהּ “it should not be slaughtered to make its flesh edible.” Since the Babli follows R. Eleazar, no discussion of the prohibition of usufruct is necessary.</i> one disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: What does one understand from what has been said<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex</i>. 21:28</a>.</i>: <i>the ox shall certainly be stoned?</i> Do we not know that its meat is forbidden as food<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">As carcass meat.</i>? Then why does the verse say, <i>its meat shall not be eaten?</i> To tell you that just as it is forbidden as food so it is forbidden for usufruct. What does Rebbi Joḥanan do with this? He explained it if the owners slaughtered it before sentence was pronounced<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This statement directly contradicts the position of the Babli. R. Joḥanan will hold that the prescriptive commandment to stone the ox after judgment has been passed automatically makes any slaughter invalid; that would not need a proof from the verse.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira asked before Rebbi Abbahu: Here you say so, there you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">He states contradictory theses, whether or not prohibition as food implies prohibition of usufruct.</i>? He said to him, here in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, there in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan.
The rabbis of Caesarea, Rabbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Nowhere do you understand a prohibition of usufruct included in the prohibition of eating if it is written <i>do not eat, do not eat. It may not be eaten (f.). it may not be eaten (m.),</i> you understand a prohibition of usufruct included in the prohibition of eating<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the statement of R. Eleazar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.4">Note 17</a>) it is asserted that if the prohibition of food is in the active voice it does not imply prohibition of usufruct. Still the passive voice does imply prohibition of usufruct.</i>. The paradigm for all cases is<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:23" href="/Leviticus.6.23"><i>Lev.</i> 6:23</a>.</i>: <i>Any purification offering of whose blood was brought into the Tent of Meeting to purify the sanctuary shall not be eaten, in fire it shall be burned</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">It is shown that the passive voice implies prohibition of usufruct, since it is the only such case where the inference is valid according to everybody. The verse is understood (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 8 5" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_8.5"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 8(5</a>), quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 82a" href="/Zevachim.82a">Babli <i>Zebaḥim</i> 82a</a>, Yerushalmi <i>Pesaḥim</i>7:9, fol. 35a] following a punctuation which differs from the masoretic one: <i>Any purification offering, some of whose blood was brought into the Tent of Meeting to purify, in the Sanctuary it shall not be eaten, in fire it shall be burned.</i> This is a possible reading since purification offerings may be eaten only in the Sanctuary. Then “Sanctuary” is taken also to refer to the last clause, (in the sanctuary) <i>in fire it shall be burned.</i> This excludes all sacred and profane usufruct after purification.</i>. Ḥizqiah stated support for Rebbi Joḥanan: If one understands what has been said<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:23" href="/Leviticus.7.23"><i>Lev.</i> 7:23</a>.</i>: <i>Any fat of cattle, sheep, or goats you shall not eat,</i> why has it been said: <i>but fat of a carcass and fat of a torn animal may be used for any work?</i> Even for the work of Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:23" href="/Leviticus.7.23"><i>Lev.</i> 7:23</a>. In the opinion of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 23a" href="/Pesachim.23a">Babli 23a</a>, the verse is needed to permit any use of profane fat since otherwise one would argue that since fat is forbidden for humans but required for the altar, fat of animals unfit for the altar should be permitted for use in the Temple but forbidden for profane use. In the <i>Sifra Ṣaw (Parasha</i> 10), the argument of the Babli is attributed to R. Yose the Galilean; R. Aqiba concludes that fat of domesticated animals is not food nor subject to the impurity of food.<br>In the opinion of the Yerushalmi, since some fat is permitted for unrestricted use, no fat can be forbidden for usufruct in the absence of an explicit verse. For Ḥizqiah, this is a third verse that could be used for R. Eleazar’s argument; nobody will contest that three parallel verses invalidate the argument. In the second version of Ḥizqiah’s position (below, after <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.9">Note 49</a>), he needs the verse to permit use of fat for work on Temple property.</i>. If one understands what has been said<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:16" href="/Deuteronomy.12.16"><i>Deut.</i> 12:16</a>.</i>: <i>But the blood you shall not eat,</i> why has it been said,<i>you shall pour it on the ground like water?</i> As water prepares<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Preparation for impurity is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1-2"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Notes 136–141</a>.</i>, so blood prepares. If one understands what has been said<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut.</i> 14:21</a>.</i>: <i>Do not eat any carcass;</i> why has it been said, <i>to the sojourner in your gates you shall give it and he may eat it?</i> It serves to tell you that the resident sojourner may eat carcass meat<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The resident sojourner, in order to receive the full protection of the law, only has to follow the “precepts of the descendants of Noe”, to abstain from idolatry, murder, incest and adultery, eating limbs tom from a living animal, blasphemy, robbery, and anarchy.</i>. If one understands what has been said<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex.</i> 22:30</a>.</i>: <i>Flesh torn in the field you shall not eat,</i> why does the verse say, <i>throw it to the dog?</i> This you throw to the dog but you do not throw profane meat slaughtered in the Temple precinct<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 22a" href="/Pesachim.22a">Babli 22a</a> this is quoted as the opinion of R. Meïr. It is forbidden to slaughter anything but sacrifices in the Temple precinct, <i>Lev</i>.17:4.</i>.
A <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Bo</i> 16, p. 51. <i>Tanḥuma Bo</i> 11</i> supports both of them. The <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement between R. Eleazar and R. Joḥanan is an old tannaitic disagreement between Rabbis Josia and Isaac of the fourth tannaitic generation.</i>. <i>Sour bread shall not be eaten</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:3" href="/Exodus.13.3"><i>Ex.</i> 13:3</a>.</i><i>,</i> to make the feeder equal to the eater<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The person who serves sour matter to a Jew on Passover is guilty as if he ate it, to be punished by extirpation. If the server acts intentionally and the eater unintentionally, the server alone is punishable.</i>. And you say for this, or is it only to forbid its usufruct? Since it says:<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:3" href="/Deuteronomy.16.3"><i>Deut.</i> 16:3</a>.</i> <i>You shall not eat sour bread with it,</i> we learned that usufruct is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">This is the position of R. Eleazar.</i>. Therefore, why does the verse say, <i>sour bread shall not be eaten?</i> To make the feeder equal to the eater, the words of Rebbi Josia. Rebbi Isaac says, this is unnecessary. Since for crawling things, a minor prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Eating forbidden living things is punished by whipping by the earthly court; but eating sour matter on Passover is punished by Heaven with extirpation.</i>, He made the feeder equal to the eater<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Šemini Pereq</i> 5(1). R. Abraham ben David in his commentary notes that this is not the position of the Babli; he does not refer to the Yerushalmi.</i>; regarding sour bread which is a major prohibition it should only be logical that we consider the feeder to be equal to the eater. Therefore, why does the verse say, sour bread shall not be eaten? The verse serves only to forbid its usufruct. Since it is written, <i>sour bread shall not be eaten,</i> therefore from <i>you shall not eat</i> one cannot infer anything. This supports Rebbi Joḥanan <sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Here ends the parallel with <i>ˋOrlah</i> 3:1.</i>.
“Nor use it to heat an oven or a cooking stove.” If he transgressed and heated, it shall come like the following<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Avodah zarah</i> 3:14, dealing with using wood from an <i>Asherah</i> to heat a clay stove which was not finished in a kiln. It would become hardened by being exposed to the fire kindled in them. Therefore a new oven would be finished by the <i>Ashera</i> wood; it must be destroyed. The text might be a quote not of this Mishnah but of Tosephta <i>Orlah</i> 7 dealing with the same situation if the fuel is <i>orlah</i> fruit (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.3.2"><i>Orlah</i> 3:3 Note 114</a>) which is quoted <i>in extenso</i> in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 26b" href="/Pesachim.26b">Babli 26b</a>.</i>: “If it was new it must be destroyed<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">Prepared to be used with the meat. Since both this and <i>haroset</i> are moist, leavening would be caused by the addition of flour.</i>, if it was old it must be cooled down.”
It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah says, leavened matter may only be eliminated by burning.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The first and last paragraphs in this section are also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 27b-28a" href="/Pesachim.27b-28a">Babli, 27b–28a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Yoḥay ad Ex.</i> 12:15, pp. 17–18</a>..</i> It is an argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> Since <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices which were offered with the intention that the meat be eaten out of place or time; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:7" href="/Leviticus.19.7"><i>Lev.</i> 19:7</a>.</i> and leftover<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrificial meat left over after the time allotted for its consumption, depending on the kind of sacrifice either day and night or two daytimes with the night in between.</i>, which are not under an injunction not to be seen and not to be found, only may be disposed of by burning, for leavened matter which is under an injunction not to be seen and not to be found, it is only logical that it only may be disposed by burning. They told Rebbi Jehudah, any argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> which you argue in the beginning as a restriction but it turns out in the end to be a leniency, is no argument <i>de minore ad majus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The premise that there be a case of major and minor is disproved. Cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Logical Problems in Jewish Tradition,</i> in: <i>Confrontations with Judaism,</i> Ph. Longworth, ed., London 1966, pp. 171–196.</i><i>.</i> It would imply that if he does not find fire he could sit and not dispose of it. The Torah said<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex.</i> 12:15</a>.</i>, <i>eliminate sour dough from your houses.</i>
Similarly said Rebbi Jehudah, <i>if a woman carries seed and gives birth to a male</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:1" href="/Leviticus.13.1"><i>Lev.</i> 13:1</a>. <i>Sifra TazriaˋIntroduction</i> (5); another version of <i>Sifra</i> in <i>Midrash Haggadol Lev.,</i> ed A. Steinsalz, Jerusalem 1976, pp. 313–314.</i><i>.</i> What does the verse imply? Since it is said, <i>she shall be impure for seven days,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:2" href="/Leviticus.13.2"><i>Lev.</i> 13:2</a>.</i><i>and on the eighth day one shall circumcise,</i> I understand that a live birth makes his mother impure by the impurity of birth. From where that a stillbirth makes his mother impure by the impurity of birth? Rebbi Jehudah said, I am presenting an argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> Since a live birth, who does not make impure for seven days his mother, and those who come with him<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">According to <i>Pene Mosheh,</i> this refers to multiple births if one of the fetuses is dead.</i>, [or who comes with his mother,]<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Unnecessary addition by the corrector.</i> into a tent<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A live birth causes his mother to be impure for seven days; after this time she may remove her impurity at any time by immersion in a <i>miqweh.</i> A stillbirth causes impurity of the dead not only by touch but also by being under the same “tent” and requires the purification rite of the ashes of the Red Cow described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num.</i> 19</a>.</i>, the stillbirth who makes impure for seven days his mother, and those who come with him, [or who comes with his mother,]<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Unnecessary addition by the corrector.</i> into a tent, it is only logical that he should make his mother impure by the impurity of birth.
