They told Rebbi Jehudah, any argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> which you argue in the beginning as a restriction but it turns out in the end to be a leniency is no argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> As a consequence, since a live birth purifies his mother<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">After the impurity of the first 7 days, the next 33 days for a male or 66 days for a female no genital discharge of the mother induces biblical impurity.</i>, also the stillbirth should purify his mother? Since I cannot prove it by an argument <i>de minore ad majus,</i> [therefore} the Torah said <i>a male,</i> to include the stillbirth<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Since the mention of a female in v. 5 implies that the preceding verses refer to a male, the explicit mention of “male” is unnecessary. It is concluded that the verses refer to <i>any</i> fetus recognizably male.</i>.
Similarly said Rebbi Jehudah, “you should dwell in huts<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The reference obviously is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i>23:42</a> where the text reads either בַּסֻּכֹּ֥ת תֵּֽשְׁב֖וּ or יֵֽשְׁב֖וּ בַּסֻּכֹּֽת. The quote is correct in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 17 10" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_17.10"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 17(10</a>) but incorrect in <i>Midrash Haggadol</i> (Note 71) p. 763. The reference is to the holiday of Tabernacles.</i>,” a hut made of anything. For Rebbi Jehudah was saying, it is an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> that the hut should come only from the Four Kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This can only mean that the thatched roof of the hut, which must be of vegetal material, should be composed of willow, myrrh, or palm branches. The fourth kind, the fruit of the <i>hadar</i> tree (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a>), probably cannot be used for this purpose. [In medieval Germany the roof of the festival hut usually was covered with willow branches, <i>Sefer Maharil</i> (ed. S. J. Spitzer, Jerusalem 1989, p. 363)].</i>. Since the <i>lulav</i> which is not used in nights as in days may come only from the Four Kinds, it is only logical that a hut which is used by nights as well as by days should come only from the Four Kinds. They told Rebbi Jehudah, any argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> which you argue in the beginning as a restriction but it turns out in the end to be a leniency is no argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> As a consequence, if he did not find of the Four Kinds, should he sit without a hut? But the Torah said, “you should dwell in huts,” a hut made of anything. [And so Ezra said<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 8:15" href="/Nehemiah.8.15"><i>Neh.</i> 8:15</a>. The proof is from the continuation of the verse, not quoted in the text: <i>Bring olive leaves, and oil-wood leaves, and myrrh leaves, and palm leaves, and ˋavot-tree leaves, to make huts as it is written.</i> Corrector’s addition from the parallel sources.</i>, <i>they informed and did proclaim in all cities and in Jerusalem, go to the mountain, etc.</i>]
Rebbi Jehudah came back and presented another argument: Leavened matter is forbidden as food and leftover<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrificial meat left over after the time allotted for its consumption, depending on the kind of sacrifice either day and night or two daytimes with the night in between.</i> is forbidden as food. Since the latter is to be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:8" href="/Leviticus.19.8"><i>Lev.</i> 19:8</a>.</i>, the former is to be burned. They told him, carcass meat disproves since it is forbidden as food and is not to be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut.</i> 14:21</a>.</i>. He said to them, leavened matter is forbidden as food and for usufruct and leftover is forbidden as food and for usufruct; carcass meat does not disprove since it is not forbidden for usufruct. They told him, the stoned ox<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 24b" href="/Pesachim.24b">Babli 24b</a> explains that one might use fat from the stoned ox to cover a wound.<br>The ox was stoned by order of the court because it killed humans (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28-29" href="/Exodus.21.28-29"><i>Ex</i>. 21:28–29</a>). Its meat is forbidden for usufruct as explained in the sequel. Cf. <i>Mekhilta dR.Ismael Mišpaṭim</i>10 (p. 282).</i> disproves which is forbidden as food and for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since it says, <i>its meat may not be eaten</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:18" href="/Exodus.21.18"><i>Ex.</i> 21:18</a>) in the passive voice, according to everybody this implies prohibition of usufruct.</i> and is not to be burned. He said to them, leavened matter is forbidden as food, and for usufruct, and makes liable for extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:19" href="/Exodus.12.19"><i>Ex</i>. 12:19</a>.</i>, [and leftover is forbidden as food, and for usufruct, and makes liable for extirpation]<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:8" href="/Leviticus.19.8"><i>Lev</i>. 19:8</a>.</i>; the stoned ox does not disprove since it does not make liable for extirpation. They told him, the fat<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:25" href="/Leviticus.7.25"><i>Lev</i>. 7:25</a>.</i> of the stoned ox disproves which is forbidden as food, and for usufruct, and makes liable for extirpation, and is not to be burned. He said to them, leavened matter is forbidden as food, and for usufruct, and makes liable for extirpation, and is dependent on time, and leftover is forbidden as food, and for usufruct, and makes liable for extirpation, and is dependent on time; the fat of the stoned ox does not disprove since it does not depend on time. They told him, a suspended sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice by a person who suspects that he inadvertently committed a deadly sin. He may not bring a purification sacrifice since that is possible only if there is proof of inadvertent sin; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17-19" href="/Leviticus.5.17-19">Lev. 5:17–19</a>.</i> following your opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:6" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.6">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 7:6</a> it is stated that Sages hold that the body of an animal dedicated as a hung sacrifice which was wrongly slaughtered has to be burned, but R. Jehudah requires that it be buried.</i> does disprove since it is forbidden as food, and for usufruct, and makes liable for extirpation, and is dependent on time, [but is not to be burned]. Rebbi Jehudah remained silent<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">And practice does not follow him.</i>.
It was stated: Before the time of its elimination you eliminate it in any way you wish; at the time of its elimination you eliminate it by burning. This follows Rebbi Jehudah. There are Tannaim who state, before the time of its elimination you eliminate it by burning; at the time of its elimination you eliminate it in any way you wish<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 12b" href="/Pesachim.12b">Babli disagrees, 12b</a>.</i>. This follows the rabbis. Rebbi says, <i>remove sour dough from your houses,</i> anything which may not be seen nor found<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex.</i> 12:15</a>. Rebbi disagrees with both the preceding <i>baraitot.</i> Since it is spelled out in Chapter 13 that on Passover sour matter may neither be seen nor found, the only acceptable form of removal both before noontime of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> or at noontime is by burning which transforms everything into ashes. If one would bury the leavened matter or crumble it and scatter it in the wind, it still would exist and could be found. 90a The firstling of a donkey which was not redeemed by a lamb given to a Cohen must be killed by breaking its neck (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:13" href="/Exodus.13.13"><i>Ex.</i>13:13</a>). An animal which killed a human must be stoned (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>). If both conditions apply there are no rules to decide which precept to apply.</i>. How is this? By burning. Rebbi Jeremiah asked, if the firstling of a donkey killed, what is the form of his execution? By breaking the neck or by stoning<sup class="footnote-marker">90a</sup>? Rebbi Benjamin bar Levi asked about leftover flat-bread from a thanksgiving sacrifice, should we say that if they were left before the time ofelimination you may eliminate it in any way you wish; at the time of its elimination you have to eliminate it by burning<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">A thanksgiving offering must be accompanied by leavened bread (Chapter 1, Notes 34, 143). The argument is that if the offering was brought on the 14<sup>th</sup> of Nisan (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.5.2">Chapter 1, Note 148</a>) and there was no time to eat the bread, it is not biblical leftover; before noontime any elimination is purely rabbinical and can be done in any way. At noontime it no longer can be eaten by biblical standards, it becomes biblical leftover before its time and has to be burned. That latter statement is independent of the disagreements between R. Jehudah, Rebbi, and the Sages.</i>?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Non-Jew’s leavened matter which existed during Passover is permitted for usufruct but a Jew’s is forbidden for usufruct since it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:7" href="/Exodus.13.7"><i>Ex.</i> 13:7</a>.</i>, <i>it should not be seen in your possession.</i> If a Non-Jew gave a loan to a Jew secured by leavened matter, after Passover it is permitted for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If the leavened matter is mortgaged, the Jew may not sell or destroy it; therefore it is considered the creditor’s property. The Babli disagrees and restricts the Mishnah to the case that the Non-Jew has custody of the material.</i> but if a Jew gave a loan to a Non-Jew secured by leavened matter, after Passover it is forbidden for usufruct. Leavened matter on which debris fell<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">When a wall or a house collapsed.</i> is as if eliminated; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, only one which cannot be dug out by a dog.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Therefore it is forbidden for eating<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah is formulated “is permitted for usufruct” and not simply “is permitted”, it appears that the addition “for usufruct” is a restriction, excluding the use as kosher food.</i>? The Mishnah refers to a place where they are used not to eat Gentile bread; [but at a place where they used to eat Gentile bread] it is permitted even for eating.
What is it during Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">May the Jew use the Gentile’s leavened matter for industrial purposes on Passover?</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, it is permitted. Rebbi Yose said, it is forbidden. Rebbi Yose objected: but was it not stated: An Israel may not lease his animal to a Gentile to use it to transport leavened matter? He<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jeremiah.</i> explains it, if he comes with him. But was it not stated: An Israel may not lease his ship to a Gentile to use it to transport leavened matter? He explains it, if he comes with him. But was it not stated: An Israel may not lease his house to a Gentile to store leavened matter in it. Can you say, if he dwells with him<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore practice has to follow R. Yose. However, since the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:14" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.14">Tosephta 2:14</a> states: “An Israel may lease his animal to a Gentile to transport leavened matter from place to place”, Raviah (§449) reads the quotes as declarative sentences supporting R. Jeremiah, that leasing animal or ship to a Gentile is forbidden only if the Jew drives the animal or steers the ship. Then the last question is not rhetorical and is left without answer.</i>?
If the roof of a Gentile was adjacent to a Jew’s roof and leavened matter rolled from the Gentile’s roof to the Jew’s roof, he pushes it away with a stick<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">But he may not touch leavened matter on Passover.</i>. If it was Sabbath or holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since leavened matter is forbidden, it is <i>muqṣeh</i> on Sabbath or holiday and may not be moved. A similar statement appears in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6a" href="/Pesachim.6a">Babli 6a</a>.</i>, Rav said, he covers it with a vessel.
Rav said, one has to say “any leavened matter which I have in my house unknown to me shall be nullified.<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6b" href="/Pesachim.6b">Babli 6b</a>. While the Babli does not mention “unknown to me”, the discussion there implies that this is understood.</i>”
Rav said, one has to say “Who sanctified us by His Commandments and commanded us about the precept of eliminating leavened matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 7a" href="/Pesachim.7a">Babli 7a</a> (bottom) in the name of later Amoraim. The benediction has to be introduced by the formula: “Praise to You, Eternal, our God, King of the universe”. Cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah,</i> Northvale 1995, p.194.</i>”
Rav said, he who affixes leavened matter to his house has to eliminate<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Ravan (ed. S. Albeck, Warsaw 1904) §7: “For what is customary to rub off walls which were touched by leavened matter before Passover and also to wash the chairs, I found support in the Yerushalmi: Rav said, he who affixes leavened matter to his house has to eliminate.” Copied by Raviah (§451) and later authors.</i>. It was stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, if one turns dough into a clump, it is nullified<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 45b" href="/Pesachim.45b">Babli 45b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 129a" href="/Chullin.129a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 129a</a>. In the Babli: “A clump used to sit on it.” The leavened matter no longer is food.</i> He<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Rav, who requires that leavened matter on walls be eliminated.</i> explains it, either that one disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar or that this clump is repugnant.
<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:13-14" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.13-14">Tosephta 2:13–14</a>.</i>“If a Jew and a Gentile were travelling by ship<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">On the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan.</i> and leavened matter was in the Jew’s hand, he sells it to the Gentile or gives it to him as a gift; then he reverts and buys it from him after Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Since the leavened matter was the Gentile’s property on Passover, it is permitted for the Jew after the holiday, as stated in the Mishnah.</i>, on condition that it was an unconditional gift<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">It may not be given on condition that the Gentile return it after the holiday. It is necessary that the entire transaction result in some monetary reward for the Gentile.</i>. The Jew may say to the Gentile, instead of buying it for a mina, come and buy it for 200 [denar]<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">While this cannot refer to dough (except in times of hyperinflation), it is common practice at the least to double the price in the sale for Passover of valuable enterprises engaged in processing leavened matter (e. g., a beer brewery), in order to guard against the Gentile selling the enterprise to an outsider.</i>. Instead that you buy from a Gentile, come and buy it from a Jew, maybe I shall have need, then I shall buy from you after Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">While the Jew cannot sign a contract requiring him to buy back the items after Passover, he can use language which the Gentile will interpret to that effect.</i>.”
If somebody rents out his house to another, as long as this one does not enter into it<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">As the following remark of R. Simeon shows, this cannot mean that the lessee actually started using the house, but it must mean that the contract entered into effect on the 13<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 4a" href="/Pesachim.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i>, the lessor has to eliminate; after this one entered into it, the lessee has to eliminate. Rebbi Simeon said, when? If he handed him the key, but as long as he did not hand him the key, he does not have to eliminate<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the lessor controls the access to the house, even though the lessee has the right of use, the lessor is required to eliminate.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Notes 435–438.</i> Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, I asked: What if he handed him the key<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann points out that this cannot mean that the key was handed over after a contract for lease or sale was signed, since for a lease the matter was settled in the preceding paragraph and for a sale it is stated that transfer of the key is transfer of the property (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.3"><i>Bava batra</i> 3:1, Note 12</a>). Nevertheless <i>Sefer Haˋittur</i> (vol. 2, p. 121a, Note 17) reads the question as complement of the preceding statement.</i>? Rebbi Zachariah the son-in-law of Rebbi Levi said, this is the disagreement between Rebbi Simeon and the Sages, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.7.1">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i> 7:1</a>. The vulgar person is one who does not observe the rules of purity. He is considered a source of original impurity; cf. <i>Introduction to Tractate Demay.</i></i>: “If somebody hands over his key to a vulgar person, the house remains pure since he only entrusted him with safekeeping the key.” It was stated: Rebbi Simeon declares it impure<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 8:1" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.8.1">Tosephta <i>Tahorot</i> 8:1</a>. He holds that handing over the key implies authorization for unlimited entry into the premises.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6a" href="/Pesachim.6a">Babli 6a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:11" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.11">Tosephta 2:11</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Yohay Ba</i> (p. 23 1.16).</i>“If a Gentile comes to a Jew with leavened matter in his hand, one does not need to eliminate. If he deposited with him, he has to eliminate<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If the Jew is responsible for loss.</i>. If he gave him a separate room<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Which is not used for the Jew’s property during Passover.</i> he does not need to eliminate.” If he did not eliminate<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile’s leavened matter for which the Jew is responsible.</i>, what is its status after Passover? Rebbi Jonah said, it is permitted; Rebbi Yose said, it is forbidden. Rebbi Yose said, it is the Gentile’s leavened matter [but] the Jew acted wrongly and did not eliminate<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">While it is not the Jew’s property, it is his responsibility. Rabbinically one forces the Jew to destroy the leavened matter and to indemnify the Gentile. There is no need to emend the text.</i>.
Who is the Tanna of <i>it should not be seen in your possession</i><sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The first part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, which requires elimination of the Gentile’s leavened matter for which the Jew is responsible.</i>? Rebbi Jehudah, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Somewhat differently quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 28" href="/Pesachim.28">Babli, 28a/b</a>.</i>: He who eats leavened matter after the sixth hour<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">On the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan.</i>, as well as leavened matter which was in existence during Passover violates a biblical prohibition but there is no extirpation, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Simeon says, anything for which there is no extirpation there is no biblical prohibition. Rebbi Simeon agrees about the prohibition that it is prohibited. What is its prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">This is asked for R. Simeon. It is clear that R. Jehudah holds that the prohibition is biblical since he forbids leavened matter one hour before it becomes biblically forbidden.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, its prohibition is a word of the Torah. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose both say, its prohibition is from their words. What is Rebbi Jehudah’s reason? <i>No leavened matter may be eaten, today</i><sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:4-5" href="/Exodus.13.4-5"><i>Ex.</i> 13:4–5</a>. “Today” belongs to the next sentence.</i><i>.</i> Where do we hold? If during the holiday, it already is written, <i>you may not eat leavened matter with it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:3" href="/Deuteronomy.16.3"><i>Deut</i> 16:3</a>.</i><i>.</i> But if it does not refer to the time during the holiday, transfer it to the time after the holiday. How does Rebbi Simeon explain Rebbi Jehudah’s reason, <i>no leavened matter may be eaten, today</i>? Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, he will explain if following Rebbi Yose the Galilean, as it was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 8:21" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).8.21">Tosephta 8:21</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:5:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.5.1-4">Mishnah 9:4</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 28b" href="/Pesachim.28b">Babli 28b</a>; <i>Tanḥuma Bo</i> 11; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i><i>Bo</i> 16 (p. 62), <i>dR. Simeon Bar Yohay, Bo</i> (p. 38 1. 15).</i> Rebbi Yose the Galilean says, I am saying that in Egypt the Passover was only one day as it was said, <i>no leavened matter may be eaten, today.</i>
<i>It should not be seen in your possession</i><sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:7" href="/Exodus.13.7"><i>Ex.</i> 13:7</a>.</i><i>.</i> There are Tannaim who state, <i>it should not be seen in your possession,</i> yours you may not see, [but]<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition. As the sentence after the next shows, the addition is unnecessary.</i> you may see Heaven’s. There are Tannaim who state, not even Heaven’s. He who said, yours you may not see, you may see Heaven’s, if its dedication precedes its elimination<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that Temple property is exempt from elimination. A person may dedicate his leavened matter to the Temple, to be sold and the proceeds used for the service. If he did this before noon-time on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, the dedication is valid. But if it was done on or after noontime, the matter already is (rabbinically) forbidden for usufruct, it is worthless, and nothing worthless can be dedicated to the Temple. The dedication is invalid and he is guilty of disregarding the obligation to eliminate leavened matter in a timely fashion.</i>. He who said, not even Heaven’s, if its dedication is after its elimination. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said before Rebbi Zeˋira: Explain it about <i>sancta</i> for which he is responsible in case of alienation following Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that here also one refers to matters dedicated to the Temple, which are <i>sancta</i> as long as they are not redeemed for the benefit of the Temple. It is assumed that the dedication was not “this is given to the Temple” but “I will give a certain value to the Temple”. In the latter case only actual delivery fulfills the vow and according to R. Simeon [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bava Kamma 7:5" href="/Mishnah_Bava_Kamma.7.5">Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i> 7:5 (Note 56</a>)] even after dedication it remains the votary’s personal property until delivered.</i>.
<i>It should not be seen in your possession.</i> There are Tannaim who state, <i>it should not be seen in your possession,</i> yours you may not see; you may see it in the street<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πλατεῖα (sc., ὁδός) “a wide (road)”, equivalent of Hebrew רחוֹב.</i>; there are Tannaim who state, not even if in the street;. He who says, yours you may not see, you may see it in the street, if his renunciation of ownership precedes its elimination<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">If the leavened matter was abandoned in the street and declared ownerless before noontime of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>. However, this statement has to be qualified, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:2:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.2.15">Note 139</a>.</i>; he who said, not even in the street, if his renunciation of ownership follows its elimination. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, explain it for <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is a mindless copy from the preceding paragraph and is to be deleted.</i><i>,</i> etc.
If he declared his leavened matter ownerless on the thirteenth; what is its status after Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that it is forbidden during Passover (unless picked up by a Gentile before the holiday). May the original owner go back and pick it up again after the holiday?</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is forbidden; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is permitted. Rebbi Joḥanan objected to Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. Do you not agree with me that it is forbidden after noontime<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">How can the prohibition be removed automatically?</i>? He answered, there its prohibition caused it; here what can you say<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Even on the afternoon of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> it only is forbidden if no Gentile picked it up; it never was intrinsically forbidden; if the outside cause disappeared, the prohibition disappeared.</i>? Rebbi Yose said to Rebbi Phineas, do you remember that we said, Rebbi Joḥanan follows Rebbi Yose, and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement of RR. Yose (ben Halaphta) and Meïr is in <i>Peah</i> 6:1 (Notes 18,19) where R. Meïr holds that an object becomes ownerless as soon as it is abandoned in a public place and declared to be ownerless, whereas for R. Yose it remains the original owner’s property until picked up by another person.</i>? It is not so. Only Rebbi Joḥanan is concerned about cunning<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The owner might circumvent the obligation of elimination by declaring it ownerless but from the start intend to take it back after the holiday.</i>, but Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is not concerned about cunning. What is the difference? If debris fell on it. He who says cunning, there is no cunning and it is permitted. He who said acquisition<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Yose the leavened matter remains the owner’s property. He does not have to dig it out to burn since it is neither visible nor can it be used, but it remains permanently prohibited if it would be dug out afterwards.</i>, there is no acquisition and it is prohibited. Everybody agrees about a proselyte who died<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">If he failed to contract a Jewish marriage and start a Jewish family, his estate has no heirs and has the status of abandoned property. Cf. <i>Bava qamma</i> 9:15 Notes 111,121.</i> and the Jews plundered his property. For him who says cunning, it is permitted, and for him who says acquisition, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">142*</sup><i class="footnote">If the proselyte died before Passover and Jews took possession of his estate with leavened matter after Passover, the estate became ownerless by itself. The suspicion of cunning does not apply. Since a dead person cannot own anything, even R. Yose must agree that it was not property of a Jew during Passover; it is permitted.</i>.
There, we have stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.2-7"><i>Giṭtin</i> 4:4</a> where the few differences in spelling are noted and which is explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:13-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.13-14">Notes 75–78</a>, 120–130.</i>“A slave whom his master gave as mortgage<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὑποθήκη.</i> to others and then freed him [in strict law does not owe anything, but for the public good one forces the master to formally manumit him and he writes a bond for his own value. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, only the one who manumits writes.] Who frees? Rav says, either his first or his second master. Rebbi Joḥanan says, only his first master alone is able to free. Rebbi Haggai objected before Rebbi Yose: Does not a <i>baraita</i> disagree with Rav? If a Jew gave a loan to a Gentile on the latter’s leavened matter, it is permitted after Passover. If you say that the Jew has property rights in it, it would be forbidden. What does Rav do with this? Rebbi Yudan said, manumission is made easy, as it was stated: If somebody gives his slave as mortgage<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὑποθήκη.</i>, if he sold him, he is not sold; if he freed him, he is freed. The strength of Rebbi Joḥanan is from the following: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, only the manumittor writes. If somebody mortgaged his field to another, then went and sold it, can the creditor not come and foreclose? Rebbi Abbahu said, in this matter Rebbi Joḥanan opened for us a door to illuminate. We do not find that a slave can again be enslaved after having been freed. If that is so, he should not have to write a bond for his value! Rebbi Ila said, it is better that a person say to him, give me the 200 <i>zuz</i> which you owe me than say to him, you are my slave! The rabbis of Caesarea say in Rebbi Nasaʹs name: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel follows Rebbi Meïr. Just as Rebbi Meïr imposes a fine for words, so Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel imposes a fine for words. As it was stated: With a bond documenting both principal and interest one can collect neither principal nor interest, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, one collects the principal but not the interest.
“Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, only one which cannot be dug out by a dog..” How far<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">How far must one presume that a dog may dig?</i>? Rebbi Abun, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Josadaq: Up to three hand-breadths.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody who eats leavened heave on Passover, if in error pays its value and a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">A Non-Cohen who eats sanctified heave (which either was dedicated leavened before Passover or was dedicated unleavened but became leavened by itself on Passover) has to pay restitution to the priests together with a biblically ordained surcharge; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Terumot 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Terumot.6.1">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 6:1 Note 1</a>.</i>, if intentionally is not liable for restitution even as value of wood<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Intentional desecration of heave (e. g., used as food by a Non-Cohen) is too great a sin to be forgiven for just a monetary fine (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Terumot 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Terumot.7.1">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 7:1 Note 2</a>). Also, while the restitution money and the fine for inadvertent consumption are <i>sancta,</i> the restitution for intentionally consumed heave is profane (<i>loc. cit.</i>) and therefore has to follow the rules of civil debts. Since leavened matter on Passover is forbidden for usufruct, it has no monetary value, not even as fuel to be burned.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">This name tradition raises serious questions. While it is likely that the young R. Simeon ben Josadaq (of the generation of transition from Tannaim to Amoraim) knew the very old R. Simeon ben Eleazar (a 5<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> generation Tanna), it is most unlikely that the latter would formulate a tradition in the former’s name. Also this would be the only statement of R. Simeon ben Josadaq in the Talmudim not transmitted by his student R. Joḥanan.</i> says in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Josadaq: Our Mishnah if he separated it as unleavened and it became leavened<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">On Passover (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 32a" href="/Pesachim.32a">Babli 32a</a>). It seems that the Yerushalmi agrees with the Babli that leavened heave dedicated before Passover is included though it should have been burned if not consumed by the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan.</i>. But if he separated it leavened, it does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Giving leavened heave on Passover is impossible. Since leavened matter is forbidden for usufruct, giving leavened heave is giving nothing; nothing cannot be heave.</i>. If he separated unleavened for leavened? Rebbi Zeˋira said, any heave which does not permit the remainder as food is no heave<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Since leavened matter may not be eaten on Passover, and is worthless; giving heave for it does not change its status. But heave is given in order to make the remainder of the food available for profane use.</i>. [Even]<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Unnecessary addition by the corrector.</i> if he separated leavened for unleavened? Rebbi Zeˋira said, since if he gave from its kind it would not be heave, and even if he gives from this kind for another place it is not heave<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Since leavened matter cannot be used for leavened matter (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.3.2">Note 150</a>) it cannot be used in this capacity for any other matter.</i>.
(If he beat)<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The word was unnecessarily deleted by the corrector, who apparently did not understand it although it appears in both Talmudim.</i> four quarters for themselves and made them leavened, and another four quarters for themselves, and mixed them<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">A dough made of at least <sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup>/<sub>4</sub> <i>qab</i> of flour (of the kinds enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>) is subject to <i>ḥallah</i> (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Ḥallah</i>)<i>.</i> Before <i>ḥallah</i> is removed, bread baked from the dough is <i>ṭevel</i> and forbidden as food. If the dough becomes leavened on Passover it becomes forbidden for usufruct and its consumption is a deadly sin. Therefore even if later it is mixed with another <i>qab</i> of new flour, there is no <i>ṭevel</i> since the first <i>qab</i> forbidden for usufruct cannot become <i>ṭevel,</i> and the second <i>qab</i> is less than the minimum quantity triggering the obligation of <i>ḥallah.</i> Clearly the mixture if forbidden as food and for usufruct because of the laws of Passover.</i>. If one warned him<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">No criminal prosecution is possible unless the perpetrator was warned by two witnesses that his intended act would be criminal, and only if the nature of the crime was spelled out.</i> because of eating <i>ṭevel,</i> he is not flogged, because of one who eats leavened matter on Passover, he is flogged. A severe prohibition falls upon a minor prohibition but a minor prohibition does not fall on a severe prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 35b" href="/Pesachim.35b">Babli 35b</a> (last line), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 101a" href="/Chullin.101a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 101a</a>.</i>. But if he mixed them<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Then the mixture is forbidden as <i>ṭevel;</i> if later it also becomes forbidden both as food and for usufruct as leavened on Passover, the prohibition of <i>ṭevel</i> is not removed.</i> and afterwards made them leavened, if one warned him because of eating <i>ṭevel.</i> he is flogged, because of one who eats leavened matter on Passover, he is flogged. A severe prohibition falls upon a minor prohibition but a minor prohibition does not fall on a severe prohibition.
If he made a grain heap and worshipped it<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">It becomes forbidden for usufruct as object of pagan worship (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:26" href="/Deuteronomy.7.26"><i>Deut.</i> 7:26</a>).</i>, then smoothed it<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">If it were not forbidden as object of pagan worship, the completion of threshing by storage of the grain in a smooth heap causes the obligation of tithes; the heave of the tithe included in the tithe would make the entire heap <i>ṭevel</i> (Mishnah <i>Maˋserot</i>1:6). But since worshipped grain is not food, it cannot become subject to tithe.</i>, and then ate from it: if one warned him because of eating <i>ṭevel,</i> he is not flogged, because of one who eats idolatrical food, he is flogged. A severe prohibition falls upon a minor prohibition but a minor prohibition does not fall on a severe prohibition. [But if he smoothed it<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">And now it becomes <i>ṭevel</i> as food. (Corrector’s addition.)</i> and then worshipped it, if one warned him because of eating <i>ṭevel</i>, he is flogged, because of idolatrical food, he is flogged. A severe prohibition falls upon a minor prohibition but a minor prohibition does not fall on a severe prohibition.]
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: He who eats leavened heave on Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Where the Mishnah requires restitution even though it is prohibited for usufruct.</i>, to whom does he pay? It is a disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, since the disagreed: One who robbed heave from his mother’s father who was a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">When the grandfather dies and the grandson from an Israel father is the only heir.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan said, he pays to the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he is the legal heir of the Cohen, and if he had not stolen the heave he would have inherited it and could sell to a Cohen, he is forced to give it away but he has the right to choose the recipient.</i>; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he pays to himself<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">He must separate heave, which becomes a <i>sanctum</i> and is forbidden to any layman, including himself. He may then sell the heave to a Cohen who will offer little money since there are very few competitors for heave food.</i>. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose: It is reasonable that Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agree with Rebbi Joḥanan about the fifth that he has to pay it to the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Since the additional fifth is a fine, it is unreasonable to assume that he may have usufruct from it other than the goodwill which he gets from the recipient of his heave.</i>. He told him, I also do agree with this, since impure heave is forbidden to the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Since impure heave may only be used as fuel it is of little value; nevertheless illegitimate use triggers the obligation of paying the fine.</i>. There came Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: Even in this case they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">167*</sup><i class="footnote">Since for R. Simeon ben Laqish the Mishnah is purely rabbinic.</i>.
Rebbi Yose asked, if somebody benefited from Temple property less than the value of a <i>perutah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Which therefore cannot be claimed in court and there is no coin with which to pay.</i><i>,</i> is he liable for reimbursement? (He said to him.)<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Addition by the corrector; to be deleted.</i> Let us hear from the following: <i>What he appropriated from sancta he has to repay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:16" href="/Leviticus.5.16"><i>Lev.</i> 5:16</a>. This seems to exclude amounts smaller than the smallest coin in circulation since such an amount cannot be paid.</i>, except what is less than the value of a <i>perutah.</i> There are Tannaim who state, to add. (He who said except, for) [he who said “except what is less than the value of a <i>perutah</i>” for reimbursement<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:1-5:26" href="/Leviticus.1.1-5.26"><i>Sifra Ḥovah (Wayyiqra II) Parashah</i>11(7</a>). The argument there is based on the expression <i>from sancta,</i> even a minute part.</i> and] sacrifice. He who said “to add”, for reimbursement<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">While is cannot be paid for in coin, it can be restituted in kind. Everybody agrees that no sacrifice as prescribed in 5:15 is due in this case. (The scribe’s text, in parentheses, is preferable.)</i>. As you are saying there<sup class="footnote-marker">172*</sup><i class="footnote">Appropriating less than the value of a <i>perutah</i> from <i>sancta</i> does not trigger the obligation to pay an additional fifth since no amount less that a <i>perutah</i> can be invoiced.</i>, intentionally less than the value of a <i>perutah,</i> even though he does not pay the fifth and a reparation sacrifice he has to pay to the tribe, one says the same here. The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose, is that not a Mishnah? “Somebody who eats leavened heave on Passover, if in error pays its value and a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">A Non-Cohen who eats sanctified heave (which either was dedicated leavened before Passover or was dedicated unleavened but became leavened by itself on Passover) has to pay restitution to the priests together with a biblically ordained surcharge; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Terumot 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Terumot.6.1">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 6:1 Note 1</a>.</i>, if intentionally he is not liable for restitution even as value of wood<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Intentional desecration of heave (e. g., used as food by a Non-Cohen) is too great a sin to be forgiven for just a monetary fine (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Terumot 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Terumot.7.1">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 7:1 Note 2</a>). Also, while the restitution money and the fine for inadvertent consumption are <i>sancta,</i> the restitution for intentionally consumed heave is profane (<i>loc. cit.</i>) and therefore has to follow the rules of civil debts. Since leavened matter on Passover is forbidden for usufruct, it has no monetary value, not even as fuel to be burned.</i>”? He told them, there it is impossible to complete<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Since leavened matter on Passover is worthless, rules about it cannot be invoked in discussions about money’s worth.</i>, but here it is possible to complete.
In error for heave and intentional for leavened matter, in error for heave and intentional for nazir, in error for heave and intentional for the Day of Atonement. If one explains it with two things, it is fine. If one explains it for one, this is the disagreement of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">This text is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.2-19"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1</a> (after <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.6">Note 33</a>); there is a somewhat defective copy in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Notes 47–51</a>.<br>The disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish referred to here is not the one quoted earlier, but the one discussed in <i>Ketubot</i> and <i>Terumot,</i> whether a criminal conviction precludes monetary claims arising from the same case or not. If one holds that with one action two different laws have been broken, each infraction is punished according to its separate rules and everybody agrees that for heave he has to pay. But if one holds that for one action there can be only one punishment, he has to pay only following R. Joḥanan.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> With the following one fulfills his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">While on the holiday of Passover there only is a prohibition of leavened matter, on the first night there is a positive commandment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:18" href="/Exodus.12.18"><i>Ex.</i> 12:18</a>) to consume azyme bread (cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah,</i> Northvale NJ 1995, p. 329). Only bread made from cereal which can become leavened is admitted; by tradition only the grains enumerated in the sequel qualify in this respect.</i> on Passover: With wheat, and with barley, and with spelt, and with rye, and with fox grain<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi, oats. Maimonides in his Commentary to <i>Kilaim</i> 1:1: prairie barley.</i>, and with <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Grain of which it is not known whether tithe was removed. Since one is permitted to feed <i>demay</i> to the poor and passing travellers, it is profane food by biblical standards.</i><i>,</i> and with First Tithe whose heave was removed<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">This is totally profane in the hand of the Levite.</i>, and with Second Tithe or <i>sancta</i> which were redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Once redeemed all sanctity is transferred to the redemption money; the produce reverts to profane status.</i>, and the Cohanim with <i>ḥallah</i> and heave<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">These are <i>sancta,</i> but since the Cohanim are obliged to consume them, they may consume them to fulfill their obligation.</i>. But not with <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Grain after threshing and storage, before heave and tithe was taken. It is forbidden as food; it would be sinful to use it for the biblical obligation.</i>, nor with First Tithe whose heave was not removed<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is not really necessary since it is <i>ṭevel.</i></i>, nor with Second tithe or <i>sancta</i> which were not redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">They have to be consumed in purity in Jerusalem; one action cannot be used to satisfy two distinct biblical obligations.</i>. Flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering and wafers of a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">These are obligatory additions to the sacrifices; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:12" href="/Leviticus.7.12"><i>Lev.</i> 7:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:15" href="/Numbers.6.15"><i>Num.</i> 6:15</a>.</i><i>,</i> if he made them for himself one cannot fulfill his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Since then they are <i>sancta.</i></i>; if he made them to sell on the market one fulfills his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">They are profane; only dedication by the buyer might make them <i>sancta.</i></i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">This and the the following paragraphs up to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.4.5">Note 207</a> are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.1.2-5"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:1, Notes 6–21</a>, <b>ח</b>.</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:19" href="/Numbers.15.19"><i>Num.</i> 15:19</a>.</i>: <i>It shall be when you eat of the bread of the Land you shall lift a heave</i><sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">This is <i>ḥallah</i> which follows the rules of heave.</i><i>for the Eternal.</i> I could think that everything<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Since לֶחֶם also means food in general.</i> is subject to <i>ḥallah;</i> the verse says <i>of the bread</i> but not all bread<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Which are the main bread grains.</i>. If <i>of the bread</i> and not all bread, that might be only wheat and barley<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:20" href="/Numbers.15.20"><i>Num.</i> 15:20</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:21" href="/Numbers.15.21">21</a>.</i>? From where the other kinds? The verse says <i>the first of your dough</i><sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:20" href="/Numbers.15.20"><i>Num.</i> 15:20</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:21" href="/Numbers.15.21">21</a>.</i><i>,</i> this includes<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Not only rye, spelt, and fox grain but also rice and millet would be included.</i>. Does it include everything? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Simeon:<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">This must be R. Simeon ben Laqish. R. Yose asserts that R. Ismael accepted the inference as valid; “dough” includes every bread-dough made from grains similar to wheat and barley. R. Aqiba’s interpretation of these verses is reported in <i>Sifry Num.</i> 110.</i> Rebbi Ismael stated this. Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, in the name of Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Confirmed this tradition.</i>.
Rebbi Mana said, I went to Caesarea and heard Rebbi Aḥava ben Rebbi Zeˋira<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">The son of R. Zeˋ ira who had been a <i>baraita</i> teacher in his father’s academy.</i> (who said, I) [my father] said in the name of Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text <i>Sifry Num.</i> 110. As regards Passover only, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 35a" href="/Pesachim.35a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 35a</a>, <i>Mekhilta deR. Ismael Bo</i> Chap. 8, 17; <i>Sifry Num.</i> 146.</i>: “Bread” is mentioned for Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:2" href="/Deuteronomy.16.2"><i>Deut.</i> 16:2</a>.</i> and “bread” is mentioned for <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:19" href="/Numbers.15.19"><i>Num.</i> 15:19</a>.</i><i>.</i> Since bread mentioned in a discussion of Passover is something that can be either <i>mazzah</i> or leavened, bread mentioned for <i>ḥallah</i> must be something that can be either <i>mazzah</i> or leavened. They checked and found that only the five kinds can be either <i>mazzah</i> or leavened; all others cannot be <i>mazzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Rice cakes, while unleavened, cannot be called <i>mazzah</i> since rice bread (not containing gluten) does not quality as leavened bread. If left standing with leavening it will not rise but spoil.</i> or leavened but would spoil.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:17" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.17">Tosephta 2:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:1:2-27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.1.2-27"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 35a" href="/Pesachim.35a">Babli 35a</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, <i>qeramit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Geonim (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 33" href="/Pesachim.33"><i>Ozar Hageonim Pesaḥim</i> p. 33</a>) a grain growing wild among reeds in swamps, used as human food in times of famine. In the opinion of I. Löw, (<i>Flora der Juden</i> l, p. 703) <i>Glyceria fluitans.</i> a grain preferring swampy ground, frequently used for animal feed, also for soups and flour. {Also cf. Latin <i>gramen, -inis, n.</i> “grass, dog’s grass” (<i>Plin. Hist. Nat.</i>24,19,118, §178) (E.G.)}.</i> is obligated for <i>ḥallah.”</i> Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, <sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts a Genizah text edited by L Ginzberg (<i>Yerushalmi Fragments from the Genizah.</i> New York 1909, pp. 102–115.)</i> it can be either <i>mazzah</i> or leavened., but the rabbis say, it cannot be either <i>mazzah</i> or leavened. Let them check! They disagree about the outcome of the checking. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, they checked and found that it can be either <i>mazzah</i> or leavened., but the rabbis say, they checked and did not find that it can be either <i>mazzah</i> or leavened.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 10:2. The paragraph also appears there, Notes 15–19, as well as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 3:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.3.3.2"><i>Šabbat</i> 3, Notes 67–70</a> with the mention of “cooking” and “souring” switched correctly.</i>, we have stated: “If a mashed apple is added to dough which soured, [the dough] is forbidden. But if barley grains fell into a cistern of water, even though they made it stink, [the water] is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Sentence added unnecessarily by the corrector from the Mishnah in <i>Terumot.</i> The discussion only refers to the first sentence.</i>. It was stated: Rebbi Yose says, it is permitted. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: They disagree when it becomes sour from the juice [of the apple]. But if it becomes sour from its solid substance it is permitted according to everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">Even the anonymous Sages admit that dry mixing of a mashed apple with flour will not induce souring.</i>. Rebbi Yose stays with his opinion; just as he says there, its cooking is not clearly cooking<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose permits to use the sun’s rays to make poached eggs on the Sabbath. Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 3:3.</i>, so he says here, its souring is not clearly souring.
<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph does not belong here; it is copied from Sabbat 3 immediately following the text of the preceding paragraph (Note 71). Kilaim 1:9 (Note 167); Babli Sabbat 123a, Eruvin 77a.</i> It was stated: “An unripe fig which he hid in straw or a flat pita which he hid in coals may be taken on the Sabbath if they were partially uncovered, otherwise they may not be taken.”
From where that the Cohanim may fulfill their obligation with ḥallah or heave, and Israel with Second Tithe on Passover? The verse says, you shall eat mazzot<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:18" href="/Exodus.12.18"><i>Ex.</i> 12:18</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:20" href="/Exodus.12.20">20</a>. <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Yohay</i> 12:20 (p. 24).</i>, this adds.
Could I think that they may fulfill their obligation with First Fruits? The verse says, <i>in</i><b>all</b><i>your dwelling places you shall eat mazzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:20" href="/Exodus.12.20"><i>Ex</i>. 12:20</a>.</i><i>, mazzah</i> which may be eaten at any dwelling place; this excludes First Fruits which are not eaten at any dwelling place<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">First Fruits are <i>sancta</i> presented to the priest in the Temple and consumed in Jerusalem only (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.2.1">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 2:2</a>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 39a" href="/Pesachim.39a">Babli 39a</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Ba</i> 10, <i>dR. Simeon ben Yohay</i> 12:20 (p. 24).</i>. They objected, but Second Tithe may not be eaten at any dwelling place<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">It may be eaten only in Jerusalem, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.2.1">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 2:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:23" href="/Deuteronomy.14.23"><i>Deut.</i> 14:23</a>.</i>! It may be redeemed and be eaten at any dwelling place<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:24" href="/Deuteronomy.14.24"><i>Deut.</i> 14:24</a>. Redemption of pure Second Tithe is possible only outside of Jerusalem.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: What was bought with tithe money and became impure, following Rebbi Jehudah, <sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets is an addition from the corrector who was misled by the expression “asked” because the statement of R. Abun bar Ḥiyya is a straight declarative sentence: “What was bought with tithe money which became impure, following Rebbi Jehudah since it is not subject to being redeemed and eaten at any dwelling place, one may not fulfill one’s obligation with it.” The question here and in the next sentence is whether there is any objection to the inference drawn. Since the scribe’s text is confirmed by G, the addition should be deleted.</i>[what is its status? As it was stated: “If what was bought with tithe money became impure, it should be redeemed. Rebbi Jehudah says, it should be buried<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann points out that this cannot mean that the key was handed over after a contract for lease or sale was signed, since for a lease the matter was settled in the preceding paragraph and for a sale it is stated that transfer of the key is transfer of the property (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.3"><i>Bava batra</i> 3:1, Note 12</a>). Nevertheless <i>Sefer Haˋittur</i> (vol. 2, p. 121a, Note 17) reads the question as complement of the preceding statement.</i>. They said to Rebbi Jehudah, if original Second Tithe which became impure is redeemed, what was bought with tithe money and became impure certainly should be redeemed. He said to them, no! If you referred to original Second Tithe, which can be redeemed when it is pure and far from the Place, can you say the same about what was bought with tithe money which cannot be redeemed when it is pure and far from the Place?<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Maˋaser šeni</i> 3:11 (p. 111).</i>”] since it is not subject to being redeemed and eaten at any dwelling place, one may not fulfill one’s obligation with it. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked: <i>Ḥallah</i> from dough made from Second Tithe in Jerusalem, since it is not subject to being redeemed and eaten at any dwelling place, one may not fulfill one’s obligation with it<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>ḥallah</i> is heave and if impure must be burned, even for the majority which disagrees with R. Jehudah it cannot be used for the Passover obligation. Since no objection is raised, this is accepted doctrine in the Yerushalmi, rejected in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 38a" href="/Pesachim.38a">Babli 38a</a>.</i>.
I could think that they may fulfill their obligation with flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering and wafers of a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">The question is raised about <i>mazzot</i> which were baked to be used with a thanksgiving offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:12" href="/Leviticus.7.12"><i>Lev.</i> 7:12</a>) or with the offerings required at the end of a vow of <i>nazir</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:15" href="/Numbers.6.15"><i>Num.</i> 6:15</a>) but were not used for that purpose, as implied by the text of the Mishnah. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 38a" href="/Pesachim.38a">Babli 38a</a> as Amoraic statement.</i>.The verse says, <i>seven days you shall eat mazzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex</i>. 12:15</a>.</i><i>; mazzah</i> which may be eaten all seven days. But flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering and wafers of a <i>nazir</i> may not be eaten all seven days<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">Both kinds of bread may be eaten only on the day of the sacrifice and the following night; if it was intended not to be eaten for a longer period it may not be used even if it was not used for the original purpose.</i>. Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: since flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering and wafers of a <i>nazir</i> may be eaten in the entire domain of the Land of Israel, the presenter could not exclude because of “dwelling place”<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">The argument really goes the other way. Since the statement at the start of the paragraph (which also in the Yerushalmi seems to be Amoraic as shown by the expression שׁוֹנֶה instead of תַּנֵּי) uses a verse different from the one quoted in the preceding Tannaitic source, it follows that the verse quoted earlier is not applicable. The bread accompanying the animal sacrifices must be consumed at the place prescribed for consumption of the meat.</i>. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: this implies that wafers of a <i>nazir</i> may be eaten in the entire domain of the Land of Israel, the presenter could not exclude because of “dwelling place”. One understands about flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering. But it is not so for wafers of a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">While during the existence of the Sanctuary at Shilo or after the building of the Temple all sacrifices had to be offered at the place of the Sanctuary, before the building of the Sanctuary at Shiloh and after its destruction voluntary offerings could be given at private altars (“minor elevated places”) whereas obligatory offerings were restricted to altars erected at the place of the Tabernacle or the Ark of Covenant (“principal elevated places”, Gilgal, Nob, and Gibeon); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.11.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 1:13</a>. Therefore there were times when thanksgiving offerings could be presented anywhere in the Land, but while making a vow of <i>nazir was</i> voluntary, once it was made the sacrifices of <i>nezirut</i> became mandatory.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no <i>nazir</i> here; <i>nezirut</i> is an obligation. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said, explain it if his purification offering was presented in Shiloh but his elevation and well-being offerings in Nob or Gibeon<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">Not that they were sacrificed at Nob or Gibeon, but they were sacrificed at the time when Nob or Gibeon were principal elevated places, i. e., the sanctuary at Shiloh was presumed destroyed immediately after the purification sacrifice was offered. Since elevation and well-being offerings are by their nature voluntary (even though in this case they are obligatory), they can be offered at a local sanctuary and the accompanying bread eaten anywhere in the Land.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah, Rebbi [Azariah]<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this was his name; the spelling עֶזְרָה in the Leiden text is irregular since <i>Ezra</i> should be spelled with א.</i> asked before Rebbi Mana: Did not the teacher say in the name of Rebbi Yose: Holiday well-being offerings brought at an elevated place are qualified but they are not counted against an obligation of the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">Well-being sacrifices mostly are voluntary; they can be offered at a local shrine according to all the rules spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3" href="/Leviticus.3"><i>Lev.</i> 3</a>. But the holiday offering is obligatory; at a local shrine they cannot satisfy an obligation. Therefore the accompanying bread cannot be eaten at all places.</i>? But it must be for Rebbi Jehudah, as Rebbi Jehudah said, purification and Passover offerings of individuals at a principal elevated place, but no purification and Passover offerings of individuals at a minor elevated place<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">But all other obligatory sacrifices can be brought locally, including two of the three sacrifices of the <i>nazir.</i></i>. It only follows Rebbi Simeon, since “Rebbi Simeon said, when one of the bloods was sprinkled, the <i>nazir</i> is permitted to drink wine and to defile himself for the dead.<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Nazir 6:11" href="/Mishnah_Nazir.6.11">Mishnah <i>Nazir</i> 6:11 (Note 224</a>). Therefore only the first sacrifice of the <i>nazir</i> is obligatory, the others together with the bread may be offered locally.</i>”
Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The olive-sized piece of <i>mazzah</i> with which a person fulfills his obligation on Passover must be without fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">The dough must be made with water to the exclusion of any other fluid. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 36a" href="/Pesachim.36a">Babli 36a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, this was said for a meritorious deed<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">In this opinion the <i>mazzah</i> which either was made from dough containing other fluids or where fluid was rubbed into it after baking is undesirable but not forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, this was said for a meritorious deed. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, this was said as a necessary condition. The word of Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya implies that this was said as a necessary condition. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya objected, did we not state: “flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering”? Are there flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering without fluids<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:12" href="/Leviticus.7.12"><i>Lev.</i> 7:12</a> prescribes “flat cakes mixed with oil”. If the Mishnah permits such cakes when commercially made, it must permit all <i>mazzah</i> kneaded with a mixture of oil and water.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, there it is a <i>quartarius;</i> one <i>quartarius</i> splits into many kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 9:3" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.9.3">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i>9:3</a> specifies that half a <i>log</i> (2 <i>quartarii,</i> slightly more than 1/4 liter) was used to bake the bread required for a thanksgiving offering, 10 flat cakes, 10 wafers, and flour mixed with oil. The amount used for a single flat cake was negligible. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 38b" href="/Pesachim.38b">Babli 38b</a>.</i>. But what implies this? “One might think and a person could satisfy his obligation with pancake, the verse says, <i>guard the mazzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:17" href="/Exodus.12.17"><i>Ex.</i> 12:17</a>; a similar <i>baraita</i> is quoted in <i>Midrash Haggadol Ba</i> 12:17; where it is spelled out that a pancake made from flour fried in oil is unacceptable since it never can become leavened.</i><i>,</i> a <i>mazzah</i> which needs guarding, excluding this one which does not need guarding.” Because it does not need guarding; therefore if it would need guarding one would satisfy his obligation with it. And so we stated: “One fulfills his obligation with spiced <i>mazzah,</i> even if it does not taste of grain, on condition that it be mostly grain”<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:21" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.21">Tosephta 2:21</a>.</i>. They thought to say, spiced by fluids. We may say, spiced by sesame, spiced by nuts. But the following says it: One might think that a person only could satisfy his obligation on Passover with (roasted) [whole grain]<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote “roasted bread”, i. e., baked on the open fire (parallel to the roasting prescribed for the Passover sacrifice.). The corrector changed this to “bread from the second milling”, i. e., from non-white flour. This correction is induced from the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 36b" href="/Pesachim.36b">Babli 36b</a> and should be disregarded.</i> bread? From where even with Solomon’s <i>mazzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">Which certainly was made from pure white flour. <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 130.</i>? The verse says, <i>you shall eat mazzot,</i> it included. If it is so, why does it say <i>bread of affliction</i><sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:3" href="/Deuteronomy.16.3"><i>Deut.</i> 16:3</a>.</i>? To exclude <i>sursīn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">This word is unexplained. סוּרְסִי is the name of the Syriac language. We do not know what “Syriac cakes” are. <i>Sifry</i> only mentions חָלוּט “pancake fried in oil” and cake. The rule is mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:20" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.20">Tosephta 2:20</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 119b" href="/Pesachim.119b">Babli 119b</a>; the Babli version also in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Ba</i> 10 (end).</i><i>,</i> and pancake, and cake.
One may fulfill his obligation with <i>mazzah</i> thick up to one hand-width like the shew bread<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">Rejected in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 37a" href="/Pesachim.37a">Babli, 37a</a>.</i>. One may fulfill his obligation with raw <i>mazzah</i> if only it does not draw fibers when broken<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 37a" href="/Pesachim.37a">Babli 37a</a>, bottom. It does not really mean raw but lightly baked so that the <i>mazzah</i> is not dark like a bread crust. The Babli’s expression is הינא (هنا “easily digested”). A <i>mazzah</i> is fully baked if it breaks cleanly.</i>. An old <i>mazzah</i> is subject of a disagreement between the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel. Rebbi Yose said, it is everybody’s opinion; since he did not make it for Passover it is certain that he did not care to make it accurately<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">An old <i>mazzah</i> is one baked before Passover. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:21" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.21">Tosephta 2:21</a> permits the use of old <i>mazzah</i> if it was baked for Passover use.</i>. One may fulfill his obligation with <i>seriqin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:19" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.19">Tosephta 2:19</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 37a" href="/Pesachim.37a">Babli 37a</a>. It seems that <i>seriqin</i> are today’s common <i>mazzot:</i> flat bread grated with a comb (מַסְרֵק) exhibiting an orderly pattern of tiny holes (<i>Orhot Hayyim</i> I, <i>Hilkhot Hamez Umazzah</i> 103, p. 75d ed. Firenze 1734.)</i> both with figures or without figures, even though they said, one does not make painted <i>seriqin</i> on Passover.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:19" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.19">Tosephta 2:19</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 37a" href="/Pesachim.37a">Babli 37a</a> (without the last statement.)</i> “Boethos ben Zenon asked Rabban Gamliel and the Sages at Jabneh: May one make <i>seriqin</i> with figures on Passover? They told him, no, because a woman would spend time with it and it would become leavened. He said to them, then one should make it in a form<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τύπος, ὁ, “figure, form”.</i>. They told him, one would say, all <i>seriqin</i> are forbidden but the <i>seriqin</i> of Boethos ben Zenon are permitted. Rebbi Yose said, one may make <i>seriqin</i> like wafers; one does not make (wafers) <i>[seriqin]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of G [in brackets] is confirmed by the Tosephta.</i> as loaves<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κόλλιξ; S. Fraenkel conjectures a diminutive κολλίσκιον, τό.</i>.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> And with the following vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">For the bitter herbs accompanying the <i>mazzah</i> or the Passover sacrifice. The names of the plants are described in the Halakhah; for a comparison with the somewhat different explanations of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 39a" href="/Pesachim.39a">Babli (39a</a>) and their Medieval interpretations cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah,</i> Northvale 1995, p.333.</i> a person can fulfill his obligation on Passover: With Romaine lettuce, with endives, with <i>gingidium. harhabina</i> and bitter herb. One fulfills his obligation with both fresh and wilted, but not marinated or cooked in water or cooked in broth; and they may be combined for the volume of an olive. One fulfills his obligation with their stalk, and with <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Grain of which it is not known whether tithe was removed. Since one is permitted to feed <i>demay</i> to the poor and passing travellers, it is profane food by biblical standards.</i><i>,</i> and with First Tithe whose heave was removed<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">This is totally profane in the hand of the Levite.</i>, and with Second Tithe or <i>sancta</i> which were redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Once redeemed all sanctity is transferred to the redemption money; the produce reverts to profane status.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “With <i>hazeret</i>”, lettuce<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic خسّ.</i>. “With <i>ˋulšin</i>”, τρώξιμον<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τρώξιμος, -ον, “edible”; τά τρώξιμα “vegetables eaten raw”, in rabbinic sources traditionally used for endives.</i>. “And with <i>tamka</i>”, γιγγίδιον<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">A plant of the family of carrots.</i> “With <i>harhabina</i>”. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, <sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Rome ms. Of the Yerushalmi <i>Zeraˋim</i> for the explanation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.2.2-7"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:2</a> חזרת גלין which Maimonides in his Mishnah Commentary explains as “wild growing lettuce” (<i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 1, Notes 37,51,52.)</i>יסי חלי. “And with <i>maror</i>”. A bitter vegetable turning grey and containing sap. They objected, is not lettuce sweet? Is it not called “lettuce” only if it be sweet?
