Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Hoshaya: (Itself it depends only on change) [Itself it is only called “change”]<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">A play on words from the Hebrew root חזר “to return” which in Rabbinic Hebrew is used in the combination חזר בו “he changed his mind”. The text of G [in brackets] is preferable.<br>The same homily but without the play on words is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 39a" href="/Pesachim.39a">Babli 39a</a>.</i>. (As) <i>hazeret</i> is sweet at the beginning and bitter at the end, so did the Egyptians behave towards our forefathers in Egypt. At the start, <i>in the best part of the land settle your father and your brothers</i><sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 47:6" href="/Genesis.47.6"><i>Gen.</i> 47:6</a>.</i><i>,</i> and after that <i>they embittered their lives with hard labor, with mortar and bricks</i><sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 1:6" href="/Exodus.1.6"><i>Ex.</i> 1:6</a>.</i><i>.</i>
“And wilted”. There are Tannaim who state, “but not wilted”. Rav Ḥisda said, he who said “and wilted”, about a stalk. He who said, “but not wilted”, about leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 39b" href="/Pesachim.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.8"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1 (Notes 34–41</a> ,<b>ב</b>).</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Yohanan: One says “Creator of the fruit of the tree” on marinated olives<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">The required benediction before the consumption of raw fruits growing on a tree. He seems to hold that marinating does not change the nature of the fruit; then it is difficult to understand why marinated bitter herbs cannot be used on Passover.</i>. Rebbi Benjamin bar Jephet in the name of Rebbi Yohanan: One says “By Whose word everything was created” on water-cooked vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">The benediction for anything that cannot be classified as agricultural produce. He is supported by the Mishnah here which seems to indicate that once processed, vegetables are no longer counted as agricultural produce.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said: a Mishnah supports Rebbi Benjamin bar Yephet: “but not marinated or cooked in water or cooked in broth”; if they were recognizable in their state one could fulfill his duty with them on Passover. Rebbi Zeˋira said, who understands well what Rebbi Yohanan said, Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba or Rebbi Benjamin? Not Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba? And in addition from the fact that we see great rabbis going to a mourner’s meal, eat lupines<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">Greek θέρμος, ὁ. Raw lupines are cattle feed, too bitter for human consumption. They become edible only after long cooking. This statement seems to supprt R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i> and recite on them “Creator of the fruit of the earth;” Are not lupines certainly cooked? If you wish, you may say there is a difference, since the Torah said <i>bitter herbs,</i> and cooked lupines have lost their bitterness. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, they have no disagreement. Since olives usually are eaten fresh, they are recognizable in their state even if they are marinated. Vegetables are changed once they are cooked<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">One must assume that roots of lupine keep their shape and general appearance in cooking. The rule that vegetables which are inedible unless cooked require the blessing “Creator of the fruit of the earth” is the opinion of Rav Ḥisda in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 38b" href="/Berakhot.38b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>38b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may not soak bran as chicken feed but one may parboil<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">Bran, the outer shell of wheat kernels, also may become leavened. Since leavened matter is forbidden for usufruct, it also is forbidden as animal feed. It is assumed that the bran becomes inert if parboiled in boiling water.</i>. A woman may not soak bran to take it in her hand to the bath house but she may rub it dry on her skin<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">To be used as cleansing powder, even if applied to her wet body, since it will be washed off before it can become leavened.</i>. A person may not chew wheat kernels to put on his wound since they become leavened<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">By the spittle.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Challah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Challah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta <i>Ḥallah</i> 1:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.4.5">Yerushalmi <i>Ḥallah</i> 1:5 (Note 189</a>). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 37b" href="/Pesachim.37b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i>37b</a>, the definitions are switched.</i>: “Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose said in his father’s name: What is parboiled? If one adds hot water to flour. Dumpling, flour into hot water.” But here either water into bran or bran into water<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">all cases it is forbidden.</i>.
If there is any fat in them<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">One is forbidden to chew wheat kernels for medical use if there is any moisture left in them, the moisture being called “fat” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:14" href="/Deuteronomy.32.14"><i>Deut.</i> 32:14</a>. If the kernels are completely devoid of moisture, they are inert and may be chewed.</i>. Unguent<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μάλαγμα The base of the unguent contains flour.</i> which became putrid, there are Tannaim who state, he is required to eliminate; and there are Tannaim who state, he is not required to eliminate. He who said, he is required to eliminate, if it became leavened before becoming putrid; he who said, he is not required to eliminate, if it became putrid before becoming leavened<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">If the unguent becomes unu sable and worthless before Passover, or even on Passover but it was spoiled before showing signs of leavening, it is neither food nor valuable and not subject to the Passover prohibition.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One adds flour neither to <i>haroset</i><sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">The sauce in which the bitter herbs are dipped, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.3.1">Mishnah 10:3</a>; <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i> pp. 333–334.</i> nor to mustard<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">Prepared to be used with the meat. Since both this and <i>haroset</i> are moist, leavening would be caused by the addition of flour.</i>. If one did add, he has to eat it immediately, but Rebbi Meïr forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, he does not believe that it will be eaten quickly enough.</i>. One may not cook the Passover sacrifice either in fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Neither water (which is explicitly for bidden by the verse) nor fruit juice.</i> or in fruit juice, but one may rub it or immerse it in them<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">Before the Passover lamb is roasted over the open fire, it may be basted with fluids as taste enhancers.</i>. Water used by a baker<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">.Water in which he dips his hands while kneading the dough. This water will contain particles.</i> has to be poured out because it causes leavening.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: “One adds flour neither to <i>haroset</i><sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">The sauce in which the bitter herbs are dipped, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.3.1">Mishnah 10:3</a>; <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i> pp. 333–334.</i> nor to mustard<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">Prepared to be used with the meat. Since both this and <i>haroset</i> are moist, leavening would be caused by the addition of flour.</i>; it one did add, he has to eat it immediately, on condition that he not tarry. Rebbi Meïr forbids since he tarries.<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 40b" href="/Pesachim.40b">Babli 40b</a> restricts this argument to mustard, requires <i>haroset</i> to be burned.</i>”
It is written: <i>or cooking cooked in water</i><sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:9" href="/Exodus.12.9"><i>Ex.</i> 12:9</a>.</i><i>.</i> I not only have water, from where other fluids? The verse says, <i>cooking cooked,</i> in any way. So far following Rebbi Aqiba; following Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">Who considers the combination of infinitive and perfect a form of common speech, not a duplication.</i>? An argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> Since for water which does not mask its taste you are saying it is forbidden, other fluids which mask its taste not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 41a" href="/Pesachim.41a">Babli 41a</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Ba, Parašah</i> 6, pp. 20=-21/</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta 3:5</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 36a" href="/Pesachim.36a">Babli 36a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 52a" href="/Menachot.52a"><i>Menaḥot</i>52a</a>.</i>“One does not knead <i>mazzah</i> with fluids<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Neither water (which is explicitly for bidden by the verse) nor fruit juice.</i> but one may rub it with them. Rebbi Aqiba said, I was with Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua on a ship and kneaded their <i>mazzah</i> with fluids. One may not knead <i>mazzah</i> with boiling water since it parboils<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">Bran, the outer shell of wheat kernels, also may become leavened. Since leavened matter is forbidden for usufruct, it also is forbidden as animal feed. It is assumed that the bran becomes inert if parboiled in boiling water.</i>, nor with lukewarm water because this makes leavened, but one kneads with cold water.” But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 5:2" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.5.2">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i>5:2</a>, speaking of the flour offerings in the Temple where leavening is forbidden (<i>Lev</i>.2:11).</i>: “All flour offerings are kneaded with lukewarm water and one guards them lest they become leavened”? Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: There the matter is in the hands of the Cohanim and Cohanim are quick; here it is in the hands of women and women are slow. Then it is a problem about Cohanim themselves, may they knead their <i>mazzah</i> with lukewarm water? <sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.1.9">Chapter 1:1 Notes 37–38</a>. Here the corrector’s additions are justified by G.</i> It should be parallel to what Rebbi Zachariah the son-in-law of Rebbi Levi stated: A menstruating woman washes her hair and combs. A priestly woman does not wash her hair and combs. A menstruating priestly woman washes her hair and combs, not to differentiate between one menstruating woman and another. So also here, not to differentiate between <i>mazzah</i> and <i>mazzah.</i>
“Water used by a baker<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">.Water in which he dips his hands while kneading the dough. This water will contain particles.</i> has to be poured out because it makes leavened.” There are Tannaim who state, he pours it into a depression<sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote">It is presumed that the water will be absorbed into the ground.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, he pours it into a declivity<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">Greek adjective καταφερές, “inclined”.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, he who said, he pours it into a depression, if the depression was large. He who said, he pours it into a declivity, if the declivity was consierable. Rebbi Phineas said to him in the name of Rebbi Ila: So was he<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">In G: “so was my teacher …”.</i> discussing it: If he soaked wheat or barley grains in water, if they became leavened, they are forbidden; if they did not become leavened, they are permitted. Rebbi Yose said, if he soaked barley in water, if they split they are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 40a" href="/Pesachim.40a">Babli 40a</a>.</i>. If he soaked them in vinegar they are permitted since the vinegar contracts them.
Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac had wine which was getting sour. He added barley to it that it should become vinegar<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote">. In G (confirmed by quotes in Raviah and Meïri): That it certainly should become vinegar. Wine which starts to get sour can be sold neither as wine nor as vinegar; it is better to turn it into vinegar as quickly as possible.</i>. He asked Rebbi Immi, who told him, you have to eliminate<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote">Since the barley grains became leavened matter in the wine, in contrast to barley preserved in fully sour vinegar.<br>It seems that the Yerushalmi implies from the linguistic relation between חָמֵץ “leavened matter” and חוֹמֶץ “vinegar” that the processes which turn dough into leavened bread and wine into vinegar are essentially the same. Rabbenu Tam, in a ms. Of his Pentateuch Commentary edited by S. E. Stern (קובץ המיעדים פסח vol. 1, p. 16, Moriah, Jerusalem 2005), notes that this and the following example imply that all fluids except vinegar may cause grain to become leavened, in contrast to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 35b" href="/Pesachim.35b">Babli (35b</a>) who states categorically that fruit juices (with no water added) cannot cause leavening.</i>. [Rebbi Ḥanina the son of Rebbi Kihai {Kirai}<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of G. The text added by the corrector is confirmed by G.</i> had honey adulterated with flour. He asked Rebbi Mana who told him, you have to eliminate. One of the people of [the house of]<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G.</i> Rebbi Kirai had leather sacks of oil in the storage room of wheat. He asked the rabbis,<i>who told him, go and clean out from under them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">The wheat kernels on the flour have to be removed before Passover since they may have become wet during the rainy season.</i><i>. Rebbi Immi instructed about those leather sacks of kutah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote">Yogurt for which the source of the bacteria is mouldy bread, which is certainly leavened matter. But since the contents of the leather sacks always were cold, three days of leaching by water is enough.</i><i>, one fills them with water for three days [and empties them]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G.</i><i>every</i>24<i>hours.</i>

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> And the following are removed on Passover: Babylonian <i>kutah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.7.6">Chapter 2, Note 290</a>.</i><i>,</i> and Median beer<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Made from barley.</i>, and Edomite vinegar<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Made with barley: cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.7.6">Chapter 2, Note 286</a>.</i>, and Egyptian <i>zythum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ζῦθος, ὁ, a kind of beer.</i><i>,</i> and dyers’ gravy<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ζύμη, ἡ.</i>, and cooks’ starch, and scribes’ glue<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κόλλα, -ης, ἡ, “flour paste”.</i>; Rebbi Eliezer says, also women’s make-up<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If the base contains flour.</i> This is the principle: Anything made from grain one removes on Passover; these are included in the warning<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">They are biblically forbidden.</i> but they are not cause for extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If edible, the grain is no longer visible. If not edible, consumption is not punishable.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “And the following are removed on Passover,” etc. Rebbi Mana said, all by the action of water<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees with the Babli and disagrees with the opinion expressed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.7.6">Chapter 2, Note 287</a>, that fluids other than water can induce fermentation leading to leavening of grains.</i>.
“Babylonian <i>kutah”,</i> for he puts in crumbs of dough.
“Median beer<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Made from barley.</i>,” for he puts in barley flour.
“Edomite vinegar<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Made with barley: cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.7.6">Chapter 2, Note 286</a>.</i>,” Southern aromatic. In earlier times, when one made wine in purity for libations, wine did not turn sour<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">But in later Talmudic times, at the beginning of the global cooling characterizing the time of migration of the nations, in many years sour wine naturally turned into vinegar. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 42b" href="/Pesachim.42b">Babli 42b</a>.</i>. One had to spike it with barley to make it sour; one did call it “Southern aromatic.”
“Egyptian <i>zythum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ζῦθος, ὁ, a kind of beer.</i><i>,”</i> ζύθος.
“Dyers’ gravy<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ζύμη, ἡ.</i>,” for he puts in <i>qussam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">As S. Liebermann points out (<i>Tosephta kiFshutah</i>3, <i>Šabbat</i> 8, pp. 111–112) if one adopts the reading of the text, the word is Syriac “barley flower” (as pointed out by E. S. Rosenthal), but the reading of G has to be explained as plural of קצי “morsel of bread”. There remains the difficulty that in the preceding lines both “barley flour” and “bread crumbs” appear in their Hebrew forms. {Perhaps cf. Latin <i>quasso</i> “to shake, toss” (E. G.).}</i> so the dye should be absorbed.
“Cooks’ starch.” Rebbi Hitta bar Abba said, one brings ears of grain which are less than one-third ripe, pounds them, forms them like loaves of saffron, and puts them in the pot, so they should absorb the impurities<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χλῆδος, ὁ, “dirt, slime”.</i>.
“Scribes’ glue<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κόλλα, -ης, ἡ, “flour paste”.</i>.” In Alexandria they make tubs of dough<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The glue used in the manufacture of papyrus was grain-based.</i>.
“Rebbi Eliezer says, also women’s make-up.” There are Tannaim who state “make-up”; and there are Tannaim who state “treatments”. He who says “treatments”, so much more “make-up”; he who says “make-up”, but not “treatments”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 42b-43a" href="/Pesachim.42b-43a">Babli 42b–43a</a> explains that ‘treatments” are depilatories used on a woman’s body whereas make-up is used on her face. According to the second opinion, depilatories are so far removed from edibles that they need not be removed for Passover even if grain-based.<br>In G, the statement is quoted as a non-authoritative opinion: “they wanted to say that he who said …”.</i>.
“It is written: <i>Any leavened matter you shall not eat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:20" href="/Exodus.12.20"><i>Ex.</i> 12:20</a>.</i><i>,</i> to include Babylonian <i>kutah,</i> and Median beer, and Edomite vinegar, in the admonition. I might think that these are subject to extirpation, the verse says, <i>for anybody who eats leavened bread will be extirpated</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex.</i> 12:15</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 33a" href="/Pesachim.33a">Babli 33a</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Ba</i> 10 (p. 35), <i>dR. Simeon ben Yohay</i>12:20 (p.24).</i>.“The colleagues asked before Rebbi Jonah: Here it is written <i>any,</i> and there it is written <i>any.</i> Here you are adding but there you are excluding<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Both in v. 15 and in v. 20 is written <i>any.</i> Why in matters of the prohibition one includes admixture of leavening but in matters of extirpation one excludes it?</i>. He told them, there He added eaters but there He added edibles<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In both cases, “any” implies addition and extension. In v. 15, <i>any who eats,</i> includes women who are obligated to eat <i>mazzah</i> even though this is a positive commandment activated at a fixed date from which in general women are exempted (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:7</a>). V. 20 <i>any leavened matter</i> includes admixture of leavening to edibles.</i>. They objected, was it not stated: “One fulfills his obligation with spiced <i>mazzah</i>, even if it does not taste of grain, on condition that it be mostly grain”<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:4:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.4.10">Chapter 2, Note 233</a>.</i>. And for these<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Median beer. One agrees that Babylonian <i>kutah</i> might not trigger extirpation since the amount of leavened matter is small, but why should beer, which essentially is water and malt, be treated differently from bread which is water and flour?</i>, because they are mostly grain, he should be liable. He told them, there is a difference, for it is written <i>bread</i>, and these are not bread. Rebbi Yose objected, was it not stated that only <i>mazzah</i> is called bread, <i>seven days you shall eat mazzot, the bread of deprivation</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:3" href="/Deuteronomy.16.3"><i>Deut</i>. 16:3</a>. Since <i>mazzah</i> is called bread, it is clear that the positive commandment to eat <i>mazzah</i> can only be fulfilled by eating azyme bread. But leavened matter is always called חָמֵץ, and never is explicitly called “bread”; there seems to be no reason why extirpation should be restricted to those who eat bread.</i>? But here you infer <i>mazzah</i> from leavened bread? In addition, from the following which was stated: “A person may acquit himself of his obligation with a soaked wafer, or a cooked wafer, as long as it did not lose its shape.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 41a" href="/Pesachim.41a">Babli 41a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 38b" href="/Berakhot.38b"><i>Berakhot</i> 38b</a>.</i>” It only says, “as long as it did not lose its shape,” therefore not if it lost its shape. But in the matter of leavened bread you are saying, if he mashed leavened bread and slurped it, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">It is true that the positive commandment can be fulfilled only with bread but the prohibition extends to anything produced from leavened flour.</i>. How is this? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Idi: Their<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The items enumerated in the Mishnah.</i> leavening is not clear leavening. Should he be flogged? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Concerning flogging, he cannot be flogged, as it was stated: On certain leavened bread he is subject to extirpation, for its admixture he receives forty [lashes]<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:20" href="/Exodus.12.20"><i>Ex</i>. 12:20</a> states a general prohibition for food with an admixture of leavened matter, transgression has to be punished by the generic punishment prescribed for all prohibitions for which no particular punishment is specified.<br>G has an additional sentence: “The word of Rebbi (probably meaning <i>Rabbenu</i>, i. e., Rav) implies that he is not flogged.” On the other hand, a sentence of the ms. text is missing in G because of homoioteleuton.</i>. Rav said, that is sour dough. He could have said, that is Babylonian <i>kutah</i>, and Median beer<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav explains that one is flogged for consuming something containing an admixture of sour dough but not the items enumerated in the Mishnah, one may conclude that only active souring agent exposes one to flogging.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said before Rebbi La: Explain it if leavened bread and <i>mazzah</i> were mixed<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding argument may be irrelevant since the <i>baraita</i> can be explained as referring directly to bread, eaten alone or with other edibles.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, I pointed out a difficulty for Rebbi Abun bar Cahana: Where do we hold? If most of it was leavened bread, he is subject to extirpation. If most of it is <i>mazzah</i>, he could use it to fulfill his obligation on Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since by biblical standards, anything greater than 50% is counted as whole. Since the mixture still is forbidden, the argument is possible only for R. Yose (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 13:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.13.3.4"><i>Šabbat</i> 13, Note 56</a>), but nobody else.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, Rebbi Joshua from Ono stated: Explain it if the amount of leavened bread was less than the volume of an olive, following Rebbi Simeon, since Rebbi Simeon said, the most minute amount for flogging<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 17a" href="/Makkot.17a">Babli <i>Makkot</i> 17a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 21a" href="/Shevuot.21a"><i>Ševuot</i> 21a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 24b" href="/Shevuot.24b">24b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 4a" href="/Menachot.4a"><i>Menaḥot</i>4a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Meilah 18a" href="/Meilah.18a"><i>Meˋilah</i>18a</a>. R. Simeon restricts the possibility of a purification sacrifice to the case that a person ingested at least the volume of an olive of food forbidden under punishment of extirpation (such as forbidden fat or leavened matter on Passover) but admits the possibility of criminal prosecution for the most minute amount.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac in the name of Rav: In a pot in which one cooked with water<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">מודה, a reading confirmed by G, cannot have the usual meaning “to agree”. The unique meaning here is derived from Arabic مذى “mix with much water”. If leavening was cooked with little water in a clay pot, everybody agrees that it cannot be completely cleaned with much water, Rav prohibits the use Passover.</i> one may cook the same kind only after Passover. Therefore another kind is permitted although only after three dishes<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">If the pot was used before Passover three times with water but no leavened matter, one assumes that the leavened matter absorbed in the walls was leached out and the pots may be used on Passover.</i>. If it becomes insignificant by other kinds, should it not become insignificant also by its own kind<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Where one assumes that no leavened matter was used.</i>? A kind awakes its own kind to prohibit<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">A general principle recognized in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 9a" href="/Eruvin.9a">Babli (<i>Eruvin</i> 9a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 73a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.73a"><i>Avodah zarah</i> 73a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 22a" href="/Bekhorot.22a"><i>Bekhorot</i> 22a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 33a" href="/Bekhorot.33a">33a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi said, Rebbi Abbahu ordered the millers<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, this is a general instruction to the millers preparing Passover flour. In G the singular is used, “the miller”, referring to the one who delivered to his household.</i> not to put boxes one on top of the other lest they heated up and became leavened. Rebbi Ḥinena bar Pappos went to the millers<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">He personally supervised the milling for Passover.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun milled himself as guest<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Guest of the miller’s. Reading צרף as ضرف, “to somebody’s guest”.</i>. Rebbi Abun instructed the millers to fill a <i>quartarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Following the reading of G; τέταρτον is the Greek equivalent of the Roman <i>quartarius</i>, “a quarter”..</i> of water in the <i>modii</i> and moisten it four times. Rebbi Ḥananiah and Rebbi Mana, one said, grasses are permitted, moistened it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Grain silos used to be padded with grasses, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 3:5" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.3.5">Mishnah <i>Makhshirin</i> 3:5</a>. It is difficult to understand how anybody could permit storing grain on moist grasses. S.<br>Liebermann conjectures that instead of מטננה one has to read בטיט הנגוב “in dried clay”. But since the text is confirmed by G, emendation is problematic.</i>, but the other one switches. Rebbi Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Hillel said, even the one who permits grasses, if he collected them in the afternoon. Why? For dew faded away from it<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Here ends this fragment from G.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Dough in crannies of the baking trough: if there is the volume of an olive at one place one is obligated to eliminate; less than this becomes insignificant. Similarly in matters of impurity, if he is bothered by it it separates; if he desires its existence it is like the baking trough<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since for cleansing from impurity the verse requires that one immerse “all his flesh” in water, any dirt on the skin prevents the water to touch the skin and invalidates the immersion. The Mishnah restricts that to unwanted dirt; wanted adhesions separate only in the volume of an olive. Dry dough which acts as glue becomes part of the trough.</i>. Deaf dough<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, dough refrigerated immediately after kneading.</i> is forbidden if a similar kind became sour.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: If it could be scraped off together<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">“At one place” mentioned in the Mishnah does not mean that the leavened matter must be in one lump, only that the dough somehow must be connected (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 45b" href="/Pesachim.45b">Babli 45b</a>). If it is moist, it can be scraped off and remain connected; it dry, it will break when being scraped off. Nevertheless, since it was connected before being scraped off, the rule of the Mishnah applies.</i>. So far moist; dry? Since if it were moist it could be scraped together, even if dry the same applies.
Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: Two volumes of half an olive in one house, the house does not combine them; in one vessel, the vessel does combine them. But did we not state, “less than this becomes insignificant”? One if plucked, one if connected<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah states that leavened matter less than the volume of an olive is insignificant; R. Zeˋira states that several insignificant pieces in one vessel combine to be significant. Since the Amora R. Zeˋira cannot disagree with the Mishnah, how can his statement be interpreted? The Mishnah deals with specks of חָמֵץ material embedded in the walls of the vessel; they are permanently separate. R. Zeˋira speaks of movable specks; they combine since they can be assembled at one place (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 45b" href="/Pesachim.45b">Babli 45b</a> in the name of Ulla.)</i>. If he reduced it, did it become insignificant, or since it was to be eliminated it has to be completely eliminated? How much? They wanted to say, up to most of the trough. How is this? If he reduced most of the trough, the remainder becomes insignificant, or since it was to be eliminated it has to be completely eliminated<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If the walls of the trough were covered with a thin film of חָמֵץ, is it enough to clean the walls so that the remainder be less than the volume of an olive or must it be thoroughly cleaned? No answer is given; since the obligation of elimination is biblical, the automatic answer is that it must be thoroughly cleaned.</i>? (There, Rebbi Jeremiah said in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: Two volumes of half an olive in one house, the house does not combine them; in one vessel, the vessel does combine them; and here he says so?<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses does not belong here, it is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.9">Halakhah 3, Note 87</a>.</i>).
“If he is bothered by it it separates” for immersion; so if he is not bothered it does not separate for immersion<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since for cleansing from impurity the verse requires that one immerse “all his flesh” in water, any dirt on the skin prevents the water to touch the skin and invalidates the immersion. The Mishnah restricts that to unwanted dirt; wanted adhesions separate only in the volume of an olive. Dry dough which acts as glue becomes part of the trough.</i>. The same is true for immersion and for sprinkling<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">For the ritual of cleansing from the impurity of the dead, sprinkling with water containing ashes of the Red Cow (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>). If the water only is sprinkled on dirt which would invalidate immersion, the implement is purified.</i>. It<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The dough in the volume of an olive sticking to the trough, as described in the Mishnah.</i> is not a connection for impurity, from the following: “if he desires its existence it is like the baking trough,” otherwise it is not really<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann points to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 6:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.6.4.2"><i>Yoma</i> 6:4 (Note 83</a>), where גּוּפוֹ “its body” is used as <i>opposite</i> of מַמָּשׁ “really it”.</i> like the trough. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, so did Rebbi formulate: Dirt under the chair<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Used as glue.</i> is connection for impurity, but no connection for sprinkling<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The two statements are not quite parallel. Immersion requires that the object to be purified be completely immersed; “connection” then means that if the glue is wetted, the wood below also is purified. In sprinkling, only drops are used; if a drop falls on the glue, the object is not purified.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Rebbi Simeon bar Cahana: An atrophied limb, an atrophied nail<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Inoperative but still connected to the body.</i>, the ballast of a pumpkin and its hollow<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A hollowed-out pumpkin used as a bottle. Since it is lighter than water, it cannot be immersed and filled with water without a ballast, usually a stone. While it is not part of the pumpkin, it is an intrinsic part of the bottle, as much as its hollow. All these are included in “connection for impurity, but no connection for sprinkling.”</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, also down which he plucked<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Downy facial hair. If a woman insists on removing it it acquires the status of unwanted dirt described in the Mishnah, being an obstacle to immersion and no receptor of sprinkling drops.</i> is connection for impurity but no connection for sprinkling. What is the difference between impurity and sprinkling? Rebbi La said, about sprinkling it is written, <i>he shall sprinkle on the tent and on all vessels</i><sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:18" href="/Numbers.19.18"><i>Num</i>. 19:18</a>.</i>. But here, <i>for you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Many occurrences in the rules of impurity in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 118a:11" href="/Chullin.118a.11"><i>Lev</i>. 11: Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 118a</a>, a discussion of people having different needs at different times in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 48b" href="/Shabbat.48b"><i>Šabbat</i> 48b</a>.</i>, all you have use for.
There, they are saying, <i>tracta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>tractum, tracta</i>, Greek τρακτόν, τό “long piece of dough drawn out in making pastry”. Mentioned in Apicius (<i>De re coquinaria</i>, Ed. M. E. Milham, Leipzig 1969 Bk. IV iii) as used in serving fish, Bk. VII using “three small <i>tracta</i> balls” for <i>pultes tractogalatae</i>.</i>, Rebbi Isaac instructed that one may make one; two or three are forbidden unless one cleans his hands with water<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since preparation of <i>tracta</i> takes time, one may bake these for Passover use only if one thoroughly cleanses his hands between any two of these, to avoid fermentation of the dough on the pastry cook’s hands.</i>. It was stated: Bireh boxes<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κιβωτός “box, chest” (in LXX used to translate תֵּיבָה). Obviously not the boxes have to be eliminated but their contents have to be cleaned out. It seems that the boxes made in Bireh were used to store pastry. (S. Liebermann).</i> have to be eliminated. Rebbi Yasa instructed that the surroundings of baking troughs<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Liebermann reads προλήνια, places surrounding the ληνός, “kneading trough.” Not only the implement where one makes dough has to be cleaned, but also its surroundings.</i> have to be eliminated.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Cooled dough<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, dough refrigerated immediately after kneading.</i>. If there was not other dough like it, how long<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">How long is it permitted to keep cooled dough in preparation for Passover, without risk of fermentation.</i>? Rebbi Jacon bar Aḥa, Rebbi Ulla of Caesarea: Up to four <i>mil</i>’s walk<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Slightly more than one standard hour.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does one separate <i>ḥallah</i> in impurity on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is mainly for the holiday of Passover. Since <i>ḥallah</i> is a kind of heave, it must be consumed by a Cohen in purity. Dough of a minimum volume (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Ḥallah</i>) is forbidden as food as long as its heave was not taken. It is permitted to bake bread on a holiday which is not a Sabbath. If the dough is impure (e. g., if the person kneading the dough is impure, as is everybody today), the <i>ḥallah</i> is impure and has to be burned as impure heave. But since <i>sancta</i> other than Temple sacrifices may not be burned on any holiday, the impure <i>ḥallah</i> may not be burned immediately and, therefore, can be expected to become fermented. (In the talmudic languages, <i>ḥallah</i> never means “Sabbath bread” as in Eastern European Yiddish.)</i>? Rebbi Eliezer says, it should not be given a name until after it is baked<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">He permits to bake the bread without <i>ḥallah</i> being taken from the dough (even though in this case the bread in the oven is forbidden as food) and to remove <i>ḥallah</i> (preferably a small loaf intended as <i>ḥallah</i> from the start) and declare it as such, which then releases the remainder of the bread for human consumption and retroactively justifies baking it on the holiday.</i>. Ben Bathyra<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah ben Bathyra of Nisibis on the upper Tigris. In the interpretation of the Halakhah, he requires that the dough be kneaded continuously with cold water until the end of the holiday.</i> says, one should keep it in cold water. Rebbi Joshua said, this is not leavening where one is warned that it should not be seen nor found, but one separates it and leaves it until the evening, and if it fermented, it fermented<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>ḥallah</i> is for Cohanim, it is not property of the maker of the dough and since the prohibition is that <i>yours shall not be seen, yours shall not be found</i>, it does not apply in this case and it is preferable to bake the dough into bread when clearly permitted as food.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah if it became impure after rolling. But if it became impure before rolling, he should make them into pieces of single <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">After kneading the dough was shaped ready to be baked. This is the end of preparation of dough and, as for all heave, the completion of processing induces the obligation of heave. While the obligation of <i>ḥallah</i> starts only with completion of the dough, the possibility of giving <i>ḥallah</i> legally exists from the moment the preparation of the dough has begun. Later in this paragraph R. Yose ben R. Abun notes that it became customary to give <i>ḥallah</i> from pure dough at the earliest possible moment, to protect it from possible impurity during processing. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 3:1</a> states that the obligation of <i>ḥallah</i> exists only for a dough of <sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup>/<sub>4</sub> <i>qab</i> of flour. Using impure flour on a holiday one is required to make loaves not larger than 1 <i>qab</i> (of 4 <i>log</i> of 4 <i>quartarii</i>); then the dough is never obligated for <i>ḥallah</i> and the problem of the Mishnah does not even start.</i>. If it was kneaded on the holiday. But if it was kneaded before the holiday, it is as it was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:14" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.14">Tosephta <i>Yom Ṭov</i> 1:14</a>. The text from here to the end of the paragraph is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.8.2"><i>Beṣah</i> 1:7</a> (<b>צ</b>).</i> “if one kneads dough on a holiday, he separates its <i>ḥallah</i> on the holiday. If he kneaded it before the holiday but forgot to take its <i>ḥallah</i>, it is forbidden to move it; it is unnecessary to say, to take <i>ḥallah</i> from it.” If he mixed water and flour? He only mentioned “kneaded”, so not when he mixed<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Even though <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 3:1</a> permits separating <i>ḥallah</i> immediately after mixing the flour with water.</i>? Rebbi Samuel, brother of Rebbi Berekhiah, said: explain it if the dough was impure where he takes <i>ḥallah</i> only at the end. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it should have been the rule that for pure dough one should take <i>ḥallah</i> only at the end. They instituted that one should take it at the start, lest the dough become impure<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since then only the <i>ḥallah</i> has to be guarded from impurity but not the dough itself.</i>. The Mishnah is about the holiday of Passover; therefore on Pentecost and Tabernacles it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The remark applies both to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 3:3</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:14" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.14">Tosephta <i>Yom Ṭov</i> 1:14</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Aḥa, even on Pentecost and Tabernacles it is prohibited, because of <i>no work shall be done on them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex</i>. 12:16</a>. Since impure <i>ḥallah</i> may not be eaten, it may not be baked on a holiday.</i>.
What is the difference between this and cleansing heave vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is permitted to trim heave vegetables on a holiday; the parts eliminated are no longer human food but as coming from <i>sancta</i> may not be used as animal feed. Cleansing vegetables as a regular component of preparing food is permitted on a holiday.</i>? The latter is possible food, the former is not possible food. The rabbis of Caesarea asked, what is the difference between this and picking out legumes on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This question is similar to the preceding one, therefore the answer given to the first also applies here. The assumption here is that the chaff eliminated when picking out the edible parts is not even animal feed.</i>?
What should he do in Rebbi Eliezer’s opinion? He is cunning and says, this I want to eat[ and this I want to eat], and bakes all of them. When he removes the loaves, he is cunning and says, this I want to leave for later, and this I want to leave for later, and leaves one as remainder<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">While he forms a small loaf that in the end will be impure <i>ḥallah</i>, he treats it as if he wanted to eat it while it is being baked, and only at the end, before he takes the first bite of the bread, declares it to be <i>ḥallah</i>. Then he leaves it in the oven where eventually it will be burned and turned into coal.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The following is a much shortened version, mostly by the corrector, of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 10:14:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.10.14.2-4"><i>Eruvin</i> 10, Notes 165–175</a>.</i> Rebbi Joshua said to him, are you not like one who burns <i>sancta</i> on a holiday? Rebbi Eliezer told him, it is burned automatically. Rebbi Joshua said to him, are you not transgressing <i>it should not be seen, and it should not</i><i>be found</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:7" href="/Exodus.13.7"><i>Ex</i>. 13:7</a>.</i>? He told him, it is better to violate a prohibition that was not caused by you than a prohibition which will come before you.
[Rebbi Phineas said, this disagreement is parallel to other disagreements.
As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 8:10" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.8.10">Mishnah<i>Zevaḥim</i> 8:10</a>.</i>: “[Blood] to be given in one batch etc. Rebbi Eliezer said to him, would you not transgress <i>do not diminish</i>? Rebbi Joshua answered him, would you not transgress <i>do not add</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:2" href="/Deuteronomy.4.2"><i>Deut</i>. 4:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:1" href="/Deuteronomy.13.1">13:1</a>.</i>” He said to him, it is better to violate a prohibition that was not caused by me than a prohibition which will come before me.]
In Rebbi Eliezer’s opinion, should he not give it its name and then take it down<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Why does R. Eliezer require that the <i>ḥallah</i> remain in the oven (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.2">Note 67</a>)?</i>? It will be forbidden to move it<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is neither food nor implement, it is <i>muqṣeh.</i></i>. Then should he not take it down and then give it its name? Maybe he would forget and eat it. Should he not take it down, then give it its name, and with another piece put it in an isolated corner, following what we did state there, “one may move impure heave together with pure one or with profane food<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 21:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.21.1.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 21:1</a>.</i>”? It cannot be compared; there impure for the needs of pure<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">It is permitted to move impure heave on the Sabbath only if this is needed to allow access to permitted items (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 142a" href="/Shabbat.142a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 142a</a>.)</i>, but here profane for the needs of impure. The colleagues asked, could he not throw it onto the garbage heap and then dedicate it<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Could one not remove some raw dough, throw in on the garbage heap (where it is abandoned and may be taken by scavengers). Since it no longer is his possession, he should not be concerned about it fermenting.</i>? May a person dedicate what is not his<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If it is abandoned and no longer his property, it is not his dough anymore and cannot be declared as <i>ḥallah.</i></i>? Then could he not dedicate it and throw it on the garbage heap? Can a person declare ownerless something that is not his<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>ḥallah</i> is the Cohen’s property, he cannot declare it ownerless; even on the garbage heap it remains his property and he has sinned if it ferments there.</i>?
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Could he not separate it, throw it in the air in the house<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Take the piece of raw dough reserved for <i>ḥallah</i>, separate it into minuscule parts, throw them into the air in the house, and only then declare them to be <i>ḥallah.</i> By <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a> he would not be liable if some of the flakes fermented, and in any case these would soon be swept away if the house is cleaned.</i> and then give it its name<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Declare it to be <i>ḥallah.</i></i>? There<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah states that leavened matter less than the volume of an olive is insignificant; R. Zeˋira states that several insignificant pieces in one vessel combine to be significant. Since the Amora R. Zeˋira cannot disagree with the Mishnah, how can his statement be interpreted? The Mishnah deals with specks of חָמֵץ material embedded in the walls of the vessel; they are permanently separate. R. Zeˋira speaks of movable specks; they combine since they can be assembled at one place (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 45b" href="/Pesachim.45b">Babli 45b</a> in the name of Ulla.)</i>, Rebbi Jeremiah said in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: Two volumes of half an olive in one house, the house does not combine them; in one vessel, the vessel does combine them; and here he says so? Rebbi Yose said, when it still is in the air of the house he gives it its name. Does the air of the house combine for <i>ḥallah</i> but the ground of the house does not combine for leavening<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The question of R. Jeremiah rests on an unproven and unreasonable hypothesis. For R. Eliezer there is no way to give <i>ḥallah</i> for impure dough easier than the complicated procedure explained earlier.</i>?
<i>It and its young</i><sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:28" href="/Leviticus.22.28"><i>Lev.</i> 22:28</a>. It is prohibited to slaughter an animal and its young on the same day. Therefore, if both an animal and its young fell into a cistern on a holiday, only one of them can be potential food. The other one is <i>muqṣeh</i> and cannot be moved by humans. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 117b" href="/Pesachim.117b">Babli 117b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 37a" href="/Beitzah.37a"><i>Beṣah</i> 37a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.2">Tosephta <i>Yom Ṭov</i> 3:2</a>.. The paragraph also appears as <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.4.3">Halakhah <i>Beṣah</i> 3:5</a> with the references to “here” and “there” switched correctly.</i> fell into a cistern. Rebbi Eliezer said, he should lift the first one for the purpose of slaughtering it and slaughter it. The second one has to be provided for so it should not die. Rebbi Joshua says, he should lift the first one for the purpose of slaughtering it and not slaughter it, and be cunning and lift the second one<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">As long as it is not determined which animal is to be turned into food, both are potential food and can be moved.</i>. Even though he had no intention of slaughtering either of them, he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">He is permitted to declare both animals as potential food even though he had no intention of slaughtering either one.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: Is the argument of Rebbi Eliezer not inverted? There he says, one is prohibited from cunning but here he says, one is permitted to be cunning<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In the matter of an animal and its young he requires strict adherence to the rules; in the matter of impure <i>ḥallah</i> he permits bending them.</i>. Here it is because “not to be seen nor found”; there, what do you have<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In matters of dough on Passover there is no other way out (short of not making food on the holiday); in the matters of the animals it is possible to follow all the rules.</i>? Is the argument of Rebbi Joshua not inverted? There he says, one is permitted to be cunning, but here, he says, one is prohibited from cunning<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">For the animals in the cistern he allows a fake declaration which permits their rescue; for impure <i>ḥallah</i> he removes the prohibition by declaring it inapplicable.</i>. Rebbi Idi said, there it is a rabbinic Sabbath prohibition, but here liability for a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Muqṣeh</i> is rabbinic; eating bread without taking <i>ḥallah</i> is a deadly sin.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, there it is to protect Jews’ money; here what do you have<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The animals in the cistern are valuable; impure <i>ḥallah</i> is worthless.</i>?
Rebbi Isaac and Rebbi Joshia, one like one of them, the other like the other<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Isaac and R. Josia are last generation Tannaim, their disagreement must refer to the difference between the first generation RR. Eliezer and Joshua.<br>The sentence is copied in <i>Beṣah.</i></i>. Rebbi Berekhiah preached following Ben Bathyra<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">He decided that practice has to follow Ben Bathyra.</i>, the Mishnah says so: “she shall beat it and wet it with cold water.<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.4.1">Mishnah 3:4</a>. Vocalization and translation follow Maimonides in his Mishnah Commentary (cf. Note 17 in the edition by R. Y. Qafeh). Dough is not fermenting only as long as it is worked on with cold water.</i>” The words of the rabbis disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">RR. Isaac and Josia disagree but by the rule formulated by the Amoraim one has to accept R. Berekhia’s determination. The rule is affirmed <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 2:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.2.1.7"><i>Orlah</i> 2:1 (Note 30</a>), <i>Ta’aniot</i> 2:14, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 42b" href="/Yevamot.42b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 42b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 7a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.7a"><i>Avodah zarah</i> 7a</a>.</i>, but Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: In every instance where Rebbi formulated a disagreement and later formulated it anonymously, practice follows the anonymous statement<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">RR. Isaac and Josia disagree but by the rule formulated by the Amoraim one has to accept R. Berekhia’s determination. The rule is affirmed <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 2:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.2.1.7"><i>Orlah</i> 2:1 (Note 30</a>), <i>Ta’aniot</i> 2:14, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 42b" href="/Yevamot.42b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 42b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 7a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.7a"><i>Avodah zarah</i> 7a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rabban Gamliel says, three women knead dough simultaneously and bake in one oven one after the other, but the Sages say, three women are occupied with dough; one is kneading, one is forming, and one is baking<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This determines the time from the start of making dough to baking when no fermenting is occurring. The details are described in the Halakhah.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, neither all women nor all wood nor all ovens are equal. This is the principle, she shall beat it and wet it with cold water<sup class="footnote-marker">100,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.4.1">Mishnah 3:4</a>. Vocalization and translation follow Maimonides in his Mishnah Commentary (cf. Note 17 in the edition by R. Y. Qafeh). Dough is not fermenting only as long as it is worked on with cold water.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding paragraph makes it clear that this sentence is accepted by everybody; it is not part of R. Aqiba's dissent.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: The first one waits for heating, the second for two heatings and one baking, and the third for three heatings and two bakings. If she made one of the first loaves last, for three heatings and three bakings<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This follows Rabban Gamliel. If three women finish kneading the dough and forming the loaves all at the same time and the first to use the oven bakes only part of it the first time and for the remainder she waits until after the other two are finished with theirs, she will use the oven again after three baking runs. The ovens are small and have to be refuelled for each run. This is purely as explanation of the Mishnah since the last sentence of the Mishnah explains, in the words of Maimonides, “know that all the time the hand is working on the dough it does not start to ferment even if this takes an entire day”.</i>.
It was stated: If one finished kneading, one finished forming, and one finished baking. The first one waits for heating, the second for her colleague’s heating and baking, and the third for two heatings and one baking. If she made one of the first loaves last, for two heatings and two bakings<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the Sages, for whom one starts baking while the second one is still forming loaves and the third kneading dough. Since the first baking run is made while the second and third batches are not ready, it is not counted.</i>.
“Rabban Gamliel says, three women knead dough simultaneously and bake in one oven one after the other, but the Sages say, three women are occupied with dough; one is kneading, one is forming, and one is baking.” Rebbi Hoshaia stated: And another one comes and kneads in their stead<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">To work on the dough during the waiting period as required.</i>. There are Tannaim who state: Three <i>qab</i> wheat or four <i>qab</i> barley<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">These are maximal quantities permitted to be worked on in one batch without fear of fermentation. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 48a" href="/Pesachim.48a">Babli 48a</a>.</i>. There are Tannaim who state: Four <i>qab</i> wheat or three <i>qab</i> barley. He who said, three <i>qab</i> wheat, if they have fat, or four <i>qab</i> barley, if they have no fat. He who said, four <i>qab</i> wheat, if they are soft, or three <i>qab</i> barley, if they are hard<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">“Fat” grain produces flour which easily ferments; soft grain needs little water and therefore is not prone to quick fermentation, hard grain has to be soaked.<br>The translation of רטש as “hard” presumes a metathesis and reads the word as טרש.</i><sub>.</sub>

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Sour dough<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Dough in the process of fermentation which is not edible for most people. Extirpation is decreed only for people eating leavened food on Passover.</i> is to be burned but a person who eats it is not liable; cracked dough<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Not fully fermented but edible dough.</i> is to be burned and a person who eats it is liable for extirpation. What is sour dough? Like locusts’ antlers<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The CO<sub>2</sub> created in the fermentation process causes small fissures the size of locusts’ antlers.</i>; cracked if its fissures joined one another, the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but the Sages say, in both cases one who eats it is liable. What is sour dough? Any which is pale like a man whose hair is standing up<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">. In fear.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rav Huna in the name of Rav: One is permitted to feed it [to his dog]<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is inedible, the prohibition of eating cannot imply prohibition of usufruct; cf. Chapter 2, Notes 9,14.</i>. Bar Qappara stated: There is no fissure on top which does not have several fissures below<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">He gives the reason why the Sages reject the definition of R. Jehudah. Even if only one fissure the size of a locust antler is visible, it is a confluence of a number of interior bubbles. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 48b" href="/Pesachim.48b">Babli 48b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Fourteenth falls on a Sabbath one eliminates everything before the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">By burning on Friday.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr, but the Sages say, on their time<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since the remaining leavened matter cannot be burned on the Sabbath, if not eaten by that time it either has to be given to Gentiles or it has to be crumbled into minute pieces which are strewn to the wind, the preferred method of disposal of the Sages (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.1">Mishnah 2:1</a>)..</i>. Rebbi Eleazar bar Sadoq says, heave before the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave must be consumed by Cohanim, who are few, the ways of disposal on the Sabbath are not available; it must be disposed of on Friday. But profane matter may be disposed of following the Sages.</i> and profane on their time.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It turns out that Rebbi Meïr parallels Rebbi Eliezer but is more stringent than Rebbi Eliezer. For Rebbi Eliezer says “that he not transgress <i>not to be seen nor be found</i>.” Rebbi Meïr says, that he not possibly transgress <i>not to be seen nor be found</i><sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:2-11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.2-11">Halakhah 3</a>, R. Eliezer requires that the rules of <i>ḥallah</i> be bent to avoid the <i>ḥallah</i> dough becoming fermented. Here R. Meïr requires that heave be eliminated early even through it is a biblical requirement that it be treated as <i>sanctum</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:8" href="/Numbers.18.8"><i>Num</i>. 18:8</a>) and be consumed by the priest and their families (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:13" href="/Numbers.18.13">v. 13</a>).</i>. It turns out that the rabbis parallel Rebbi Joshua but are more stringent than Rebbi Joshua. For Rebbi Joshua says that one does not burn <i>sancta</i> on a holiday, and the rabbis say, even on weekdays one does not burn <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since profane food may be disposed of at any time (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.2-16">Halakhah 2:1</a>), it is clear that the Sages only insist that heave may not destroyed before it time. This parallels the opinion of R. Joshua who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:2-11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.2-11">Halakhah 3</a> insist that <i>ḥallah</i> dough may not be touched.</i>.
It was stated: “Rebbi Jehudah said, the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel did not disagree <sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts a new Genizah fragment (G).</i> about pure heave that it is forbidden to burn it<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">On a Friday the 13th of Nisan.</i>, and about impure heave that one is permitted to burn it<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">On a Friday the 13th of Nisan.</i>; about what did they disagree? About the suspended<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Heave whose purity is in question. It may not be eaten since it may be impure; it may not be disposed of since it may be pure.</i> one, about which the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The spelling is the Babylonian one of the corrector; it should be disregarded.</i> say, one may not burn it, but the House of Hillel say, one burns it. The House of Shammai said to the House of Hillel: Do you not say that the pure one may not be burned because I am saying that maybe a Cohen was keeping the Sabbath within the domain and he might come and eat in on the Sabbath, so also the suspended one may not be burned because I am saying that maybe Elijah<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">He may appear anywhere and as a member of the Divine Court he knows the true facts about everything.</i> was keeping the Sabbath on Mount Carmel and he might come and testify on the Sabbath that it is pure. The House of Hillel told them, we are sure that Elijah will come neither on a Sabbath nor on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 13a" href="/Pesachim.13a">Babli 13a</a> in a related <i>baraita</i>: “neither on a Friday nor on the day preceding a holiday because of the trouble [this would cause.]”</i>.”
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It turns out that Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Sadoq parallels Rabban Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:6</a> allows more time to consume leavened heave than leavened profane food.</i>. Just as Rabban Gamliel (said)<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Omit with G.</i> distinguishes between profane and heave, so Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Sadoq (said)<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Omit with G.</i> distinguishes between profane and heave. Since you say that practice follows Rabban Gamliel, similarly practice has to follow Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Sadoq<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Chpater 1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6">Halakhah 6, Note 160</a>; Maimonides <i>Hames</i> <i>umazzah</i> 3:3, Note by Ravad.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person who goes to slaughter his Passover sacrifice, or to circumcise his son, or to eat the betrothal meal at his father-in-law’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">On the 14th of Nisan.</i>, and remembers that he has leavened matter in his house, if he can return, eliminate it, and return to his meritorious deed, he shall return and eliminate; otherwise, he has to nullify it in his thought<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">While the condition given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.2.5">Chapter 2, Note 101</a>, is not strictly satisfied, in an emergency situation mental annulment is sufficient as legal abandonment to satisfy the biblical requirement not to have one’s own leavened matter in his possession.</i>. To save from the army<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The Roman army on the march in the Roman empire. Since the soldiers will steal anything not hidden, this is an emergency.</i>, or from a river<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">A flood.</i>, or from a conflagration, or from a house collapse, he has to nullify it in his thought<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">These are real emergency situations.</i>. To celebrate the holiday according to his wishes<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">He goes to celebrate the holiday at another place of his choosing, not to fulfill a religious precept.</i>, he has to return immediately.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, come and see how great is peace that it is bundled with two things about whom one is liable to extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, a meal with his future in-laws is put on the same level as the Passover sacrifice and circumcision; two commandments whose omission is punished by Divine extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 17:14" href="/Genesis.17.14"><i>Gen.</i> 17:14</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:13" href="/Numbers.9.13"><i>Num.</i>9:13</a>).</i>.
His son’s circumcision and the slaughter of his Passover sacrifice. what has precedence? Rebbi Phineas said, since it is written, <i>all his males have to be circumcided, then he will be admitted to nake it</i>, this implies that his sons’ circumcisions has priority over the slaughter of his Passover sacrifice.
Even if he could return and eliminate, or go and save<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since the second series of cases in the Mishnah is formulated as apodictic rule, not subject to a condition as in the first series, it follows that a person starting to save from soldiers, flood, fire, and collapse, is not permitted to return and eliminate but forced to annul mentally.</i>. Even if he could go and acquire rest status, return and (nullify) [eliminate]<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">. In the last case, if he leaves his place but not to go to Jerusalem, he is required to return immediately even if he could go to the place of his destination, deposit some food there to symbolically move his dwelling to that place, then return to his own house, eliminate the leavened matter, and return to his place of holiday rest</i>.
[What is]<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">This was written by the scribe and then erased by him; it should be read following G..</i> (no)<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of the corrector; to be deleted with G.</i> to celebrate the holiday according to his wishes? At his teacher’s or at somebody greater than he in wisdom. Here did Rebbi state that action precedes study<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Since the action of eliminating leavened matter, even though it could have been replaced by a declaration of nullification, has precedence over a possibility of further study.</i>. They voted in the upper floor of the house of Arius in Lydda that study precedes action<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 40b, this was an early Tannaitic decision following R. Aqiba against R. Tarphon. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:7:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.7.2-6"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:7</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu sent his son Rebbi Ḥanina to acquire merit in Tiberias. They came and said to him, he does works of charity<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Burying the dead.</i>. He sent to tell him, are there no graves in Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">A reference to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 14:11" href="/Exodus.14.11"><i>Ex.</i> 14:11</a>.</i> that I sent you to Tiberias? For they already voted and decided<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Delete with G.</i> in the upper floor of the house of Arius in Lydda that study precedes action<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore he has to spend his time studying in the Academy there.</i>. The rabbis of Caesaria say, that is, if there is somebody there who will do it. But if nobody is there who will do it, the action has precedence<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">In particular, religious obligations of a personal nature, which cannot be delegated, have precedence over study.</i>. Example. Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Yasa, Rebbi Immi, were late in coming to Rebbi Eleazar. He asked them, where have you been? They told him, performing an act of charity. He asked them, where there no others? They answered him, it was a neighbor<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">It was an obligation irrespective of the number of attendees at the funeral.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Similarly, somebody who left Jerusalem and remembers that he has with him sacrificial meat<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Family sacrifices which can be eaten anywhere within the walls of Jerusalem but become invalid and impure the moment they are taken outside.</i>, if he passed Mount Scopus<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">When he no longer can see the walls of the city. For “citadel” see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:8:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.8.2-4">Halakhah 7:8</a>.</i> he burns it on the spot, otherwise he returns and burns it before the citadel on altar wood. Up to what<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">What is the minimal volume for which one has to return, either to eliminate leavened matter or to burn <i>sancta.</i></i> do they return? Rebbi Meïr says, in both cases the size of an olive; Rebbi Jehudah says, in both cases the size of an egg<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Slightly more than three olives.</i>. But the Sages say, sacrificial meat in the volume of an olive, but leavened matter in that of an egg.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: It is written, <i>on that day will all shadow of horses be holy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 14:20" href="/Zechariah.14.20"><i>Zach</i>. 14:20</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 50a" href="/Pesachim.50a">Babli 50a</a>.</i> etc.; to the place where the horse runs without having a shadow<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The future size of the suburbs of Jerusalem will be as far as a horse can run outside the walls of Jerusalem during noontime at the summer solstice when it casts no shadow (Rashi in the Babli, explanation by R. Makhir.)</i>.
Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac asked, if it is a <i>sanctum</i> should he burn it on the spot?
