There is a difference, for a trellis is usually checked<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">On a trellis the branches are stretched out widely; one can go over the branches as many times as he wants as long as he does not move away.</i>. And it does not even contradict Rebbi Hoshaia, for Rebbi Hoshaia said, when I was mashing olives with the great Rebbi Ḥiyya, he told me that any olive you can reach when stretching out your hand is not forgotten<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">But Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 7:8 makes it clear that one may go around a free-standing vine as many times as he wants, as long as he does not move away.</i>. There is a difference, since every single freestanding vine is a separate planting.
Rebbi Abba bar Cahana asked before Rebbi Immi: If one leased his house to another and needs the money? He said to him, it is unthinkable that he should have to starve to death<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts the rule of Roman law that sale breaks lease.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi [Il]la both say, μίστωσις like ὠνή is acquired by buying<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann’s remark is convincing that they choose to formulate the statement in Greek terms, “lease is acquired like a buy by acquisition” to make the point of their rejection of the Roman law clear to all traders. They consider the right to use leased property as a valuable asset which once acquired in due form cannot be taken away.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 21a" href="/Arakhin.21a">Babli Arakhin 21a</a>. Since property donated to the Temple is given only for its money’s worth, not the house was given but the future income.</i>: If one leased his house to another and went and dedicated it, [the renter] lives in it and pays the rent to the Sanctuary. When? If he did not pay the rent in advance, but if he paid the rent in advance he lives there for free. In the days of Rebbi Mana there was a company<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">Latin numerus; a military unit of native auxiliaries in the late Roman Empire. For nonpayment, the government threatened to enslave the children.</i> in Sepphoris, and their children were taken as pledges. When they were about to leave, Rebbi Mana published (so) [a proclamation]<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The text of <b>ג</b> [in brackets] seems preferable.</i> following Rebbi Immi. He said, not that I am of his opinion, but because of the Sepphoreans that they should not permanently lose their children<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">This was an emergency measure (probably to permit sale of houses to Gentiles). He could not have agreed with the underlying principle since he was a student of his father R. Jonah, student of R. Jeremiah, student of R. Zeˋira.</i>.
“They ate wind-fall.” Where do we hold? If it fell off before the holiday, everybody agrees that it is permitted; if it fell off on the holiday, everybody agrees that it is forbidden. But we must hold if it is unknown<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Since fruit on the tree at nightfall of a Sabbath or holiday is <i>muqṣeh,</i> it cannot become permitted on the Sabbath or holiday. The only problem is whether <i>muqṣeh</i> is to be considered as rabbinic prohibition, which would require a case of doubt to be treated leniently as permitted, or as biblical, which requires a case of doubt to be treated restrictively as prohibited. Since the principle of <i>muqṣeh</i> is recognized by Sadducees (cf. Introduction to Tractates <i>Šabbat</i> and <i>Eruvin</i>)<i>,</i> the Mishnah treats it as biblical prohibition. Quoted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Pesachim 56b:3:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Pesachim.56b.3.1">Tosaphot 56b <i>s. v</i>. מחלוקת</a>.</i>.
“They gave <i>peah</i> from vegetables.” <sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 3:20" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).3.20">Tosephta 3:20</a>.</i>“They only gave for beets and leeks<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κεφαλωτόν “headed”.</i> since these are harvested once. Rebbi Yose said, also cabbage.” <sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Esther rabba</i> 2, on v. 1:4.</i> It happened that the son of Ben Meviyayin<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">“Importer of wine”.</i> gave <i>peah</i> of vegetables to the poor. His father came and found them standing at the door of the garden plot. He told them, put down what is in your hands, they put down what was in their hands. He gave them double from what had been tithed. Not that he was a miser, but he was concerned about the words of the Sages. Once the rabbis needed contributions; they sent to him Rebbi Aqiba and another rabbi with him. When they came to enter at his place they heard the voice of a lad saying to him, what shall we buy for you today? He said, endives<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τρώξιμον vegetable “eaten raw”.</i>, not from today but from yesterday which are wilted and cheap. They left him and went away. After all people had (acquired merit) [contributed]<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">The text from the Leiden ms. is in parentheses, from <b>ג</b> in brackets.</i>, they came to him. He said to them, why did you not come to me first, as you were used to? They told him, we already came but heard the voice of a lad saying to you, what shall we buy for you today? You said, endives, not from today but from yesterday which are wilted and cheap. He said, you know what was between me and the lad; do you know what is between me and my Creator? Nevertheless, go and tell her<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">His wife.</i> to give you a <i>modius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">A measure of volume; Roman 8.5 1, Syrian 17 1.</i> of denars. They went and told her. She said to them, what did he say to you, heaped or flat? They answered her, he said it vaguely. She told them, I am giving you heaped. If he said so, I filled it. If not, I shall account for the excess from my dowry<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">Greek φερνή, ἡ.</i>. When her husband heard this, he doubled her <i>ketubaḥ.</i>

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The daily sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The service of the daily afternoon sacrifice which concludes the Temple service for the day is concluded at 3:30 pm local time every day of the year except Passover Eve. In Roman style, an hour is defined as 1/12 of the time of daylight. The night is divided into watches, not hours.</i> is slaughtered at [hour] eight and a half and brought at nine and a half. On Passover Eve it is slaughtered at [hour] seven and a half and brought at eight and a half whether it be weekday or Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is slaughtered after the service of the daily sacrifice is completed, there must be time for several groups of people to prepare their <i>Pesaḥ.</i> Since it must be done on the 14th of Nisan, the rules of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> have precedence over those of the Sabbath.</i>. If Passover Eve falls on a Friday, it is slaughtered at [hour] six and a half and brought at seven and a half<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, roasting the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is an obligation of the 14th of Nisan, and it does not push aside the Sabbath prohibitions on the 15th if that day be a Sabbath. The slaughter has to be early enough to allow for roasting before nightfall.</i>. The <i>Pesaḥ</i> is following<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases, the <i>Pesaḥ</i> follows the completion of the ordinary Temple service.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The daily sacrifice is slaughtered,” etc. It is written: <i>The following you shall do on the altar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:38" href="/Exodus.29.38"><i>Ex.</i> 29:38</a>. As usual the reference is to the second part of the verse, not quoted in the text: <i>sheep, yearlings, two per day, in perpetuity.</i></i>, etc. I would have said, both of them should be sacrificed in the morning, or both of them in the afternoon. The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:39" href="/Exodus.29.39"><i>Ex.</i> 29:39</a>.</i>, <i>one sheep you shall do in the morning.</i> I would have said, the morning one should be sacrificed at dawn and the evening one at dusk; the verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:39" href="/Exodus.29.39"><i>Ex.</i> 29:39</a>.</i>, <i>between the evenings.</i> It is said here <i>between the evenings</i> and it is said there<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:6" href="/Exodus.12.6"><i>Ex.</i> 12:6</a>, about the <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifice.</i> <i>between the evenings.</i> Since <i>between the evenings</i> which is said there means after six hours<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As noted immediately afterwards, this assertion is unproven.</i>, also <i>between the evenings</i> which is said here must mean after six hours. What did you see that makes you say, <i>between the evenings</i> means after six hours? [Even though it is no proof there is a hint:]<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence was not written by the scribe, and also is missing in <b>ג</b>. It was added by the corrector from the parallels, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 11" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_11"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 11</a>, <i>Mekhilta deR. Ismael Bo Parasha</i> 5 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 17).</i> <i>woe on us that the day has turned, that evening shadows are turned</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 6:4" href="/Jeremiah.6.4"><i>Jer.</i> 6:4</a>.</i><i>.</i> Since <i>evening</i> which is said there means after six hours<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The verse shows that “evening” is the time when shadows point East. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 58a" href="/Pesachim.58a">Babli holds (58a</a>) that this starts not at noontime but about half an hour later. But it seems that for Yerushalmi, Sifra and Mekhilta “six hours” means 12 noon.<br>The argument is only a hint, not a proof, since the thesis that a word can have only one meaning is valid only for the Pentateuch, not for later biblical books.</i>, also <i>evening</i> which is said here must mean after six hours. Then should it not be qualified after six hours<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann thinks that this refers to the <i>Pesaḥ</i> since the Mishnah states that on a 14th of Nisan which is a Friday the daily sacrifice is brought at 12:30 pm. This time must be qualified on all days. But this is a Babli argument, not valid for the Yerushalmi. The question is about the daily sacrifice; why on a 14th of Nisan which is a Friday is the service of the daily evening sacrifice not started at noon?</i>? Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, what is <i>between the evenings</i>? Split the evening into two, give it two and a half hours before, two and a half hours afterwards, and one hour for its work<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since from noon to sunset there are 6 variable hours, the sacrifice should be brought at 3 pm. Since one does not consider smaller units of time, “3 pm” really means “from 2:30 pm to 3:30 pm”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 11b" href="/Zevachim.11b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 11b</a>.</i>. It turns out that the daily sacrifice is brought at nine hours and a half.
Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi left out the beginning<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Joshua ben Levi actually is an older <i>baraita</i> which makes it explicit that the times of the evening daily sacrifice and the <i>Pesaḥ</i> are identical.</i> and said the end; is that not what was stated: “Ḥanania ben Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:6" href="/Exodus.12.6"><i>Mekhilta deR. Simeon ben Yoḥai</i> (p. 12, <i>ad Ex.</i> 12:6</a>), which according to J. N. Epstein is the <i>Tanna debe Ḥizqiah.</i> Ḥanania ben Ḥakinai).</i> says, I hear between evenings, between [two]<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <b>ג</b> and <i>Mekhilta deR. Simeon ben Yoḥai.</i></i> evenings, between the evening of the 14th and the evening of the fifteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Reading עַרְבַּיִם as עֲרָבִים. For the rest of the Halakhah <b>ג</b>  is not readable.</i>. I could think that day and night are included; the verse says, day<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:6" href="/Exodus.12.6"><i>Ex.</i> 12:6</a>: <i>It should be guarded by you until the fourteenth day of this month.</i></i>. Since He said day, this excludes the night. If it is day, I could think at two hours into the day; the verse says, in the evening. If in the evening, I could think after it became dark. The verse says, between the evenings. How is this? Split the evening into two, give it two and a half hours before, two and a half hours afterwards, and one hour for its work. It turns out that the daily sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">And the <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i> is brought at nine hours and a half.
Rebbi Yose said, that is, for merit<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If one follows all the rules, both <i>Pesaḥ</i> and the daily evening sacrifice have to be brought at 3:30 pm. But as the Mishnah shows, a deviation does not invalidate the sacrifice.</i>. But for invalidation it is what we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:3</a>. The Mishnah supports the Yerushalmi position that “6 hours” means exactly this and not “half past twelve”. Any <i>Pesaḥ</i> or daily evening sacrifice slaughtered before noon is invalid.</i>: “If one slaughtered it before noon it is disqualified; after noon it is immediately qualified. Before the daily evening sacrifice it is qualified.” Could one say that it also is the same for the morning<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning that the daily morning sacrifice should preferably be slaughtered at 2½ hours (8:30 am), rather than at dawn?</i>? Here it is written <i>between the evenings</i>; do you have a verse <i>between the mornings</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since it says '"morning" but never "between the mornings", there is no reason why it should not be meritorious to start the morning service at soon as possible.</i>?
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, they learned prayers from the patriarchs. One repeats this<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Here one should insert a copy of the text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 4:1:8-33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.4.1.8-33"><i>Berakhot</i> 4:1 (Notes 11–124</a>). A number of Medieval authorities (e. g., Rav Nissim Gaon in his <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 27b" href="/Berakhot.27b">Commentary to <i>Berakhot</i>27b</a>, penultimate line) note that the text of <i>Berakhot</i> also appears in <i>Pesaḥim</i>(and partially in <i>Taˋaniot</i> 4:1). In the Venice <i>editio princeps</i> and the later printed editions the sentence has been shortened to become unintelligible.</i> up to “but made him head of the court.”
Who is the Tanna of “whether it be weekday or Sabbath”? Rebbi Ismael, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 58a" href="/Pesachim.58a">Babli 58a</a>. In <i>Sifiy zuṭa Beha ˋalotekha</i>9(3) (Yalqut 720) the name tradition is the opposite of that of the Talmudim.</i>: “As its order is on a weekday so its order is on a Sabbath, the words of Rebbi Ismael. Rebbi Aqiba says, as its order is on a Friday so is its order on a Sabbath.” This means that Rebbi Ismael makes it an hour later for its arguments<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Maˋaser šeni</i> 1:1 (Note 19) פִּלְפּוּלוֹ refers to the discussion of the rules of tithe money. So it seems that here the interpretation of <i>Pene Moshe</i> is correct, that there is time to solve all questions that arise before the sacrifice is slaughtered on a tight schedule.</i>, and Rebbi Aqiba makes it an hour earlier to occupy oneself with it, so they would not fulfill the commandment while inebriated<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is a Sabbath where usually three meals with bread and wine are prescribed, he makes sure that the entire afternoon no alcohol is consumed and the people are awake for the celebration in the night.</i>. Could not the <i>Pesaḥ</i> be brought first and the daily sacrifice afterwards<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since if the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> is a Friday neither the <i>Pesaḥ</i> nor the daily sacrifice are brought at the time which was determined to be the correct <i>between the evenings,</i> it should not make any difference which sacrifice was brought first.</i>? If you are saying so, you are eliminating <i>between the evenings</i> of the <i>Pesaḥ.</i> If you are keeping <i>between the evenings</i> of the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> are you not eliminating <i>between the evenings</i> of the daily sacrifice? If you are neglecting <i>between the evenings</i> of the daily sacrifice now, you can observe it at other times. If you are neglecting it now for the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> when can you ever observe it? Could not <i>Pesaḥ</i> be brought first, followed by the daily sacrifice? The one about which is written <i>in the evening</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:6" href="/Deuteronomy.16.6"><i>Deut.</i> 16:6</a>: <i>There you shall slaughter the Pesaḥ in the evening, at sundown, at the time of your exodus from Egypt.</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 59a" href="/Pesachim.59a">Babli 59a</a>.</i> and <i>between the evenings</i> shall be delayed more than one about which is written [only] <i>between the evenings.</i>
Rebbi Jeremiah asked, what did you see to say that <i>evening</i> is later<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since starting at 12:01 pm one greets with “good evening”.</i>; could we not say that <i>between the evenings is later</i>? But it must be: The one about which is written <i>between the evenings at sundown</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since starting at 12:01 pm one greets with “good evening”.</i> shall be delayed more than one about which is written only <i>between the evenings.</i> The formulation of a <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 9a" href="/Berakhot.9a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 9a</a>, <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 113 in the name of R. Eliezer; different wording and authors <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bo</i> 5, last sentence.</i> supports Rebbi Jeremiah: <i>“There you shall slaughter the Pesaḥ in the evening. In the evening</i> you are slaughtering, <i>at sundown</i> you are eating, <i>at the time of your exodus from Egypt</i> you are roasting<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since obviously roasting must precede eating and the day of the Exodus was the 15th of Nisan, the day after the night dedicated to the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> with the parallel sources one has to understand “roasting” as “burning” the remainders (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:10" href="/Exodus.12.10"><i>Ex.</i> 12:10</a>).</i>.” There are Tannaim who state: <i>“In the evening</i> you are eating, <i>at sundown</i> you are slaughtering, <i>at the time of your exodus from Egypt</i> you are roasting.”
Could not both be brought simultaneously? Rebbi Yose said, the sheep is second, the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is not second. It was stated: Rebbi Nathan says, every day the daily sacrifice was slaughtered at eight and a half, and brought at nine and a half. What means “every day”? Every Passover Eve. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the reason of this Tanna is to allow half an hour between one group and the next.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Num.</i> 143, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 58b" href="/Pesachim.58b">Babli 58b</a>. The argument seems to be that the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> while a personal sacrifice from the offerers, has the status of a public sacrifice, since only public sacrifices can override Sabbath prohibitions and be offered if the people are impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.4.1">Mishnah 9:3</a>). Then the verse states that the daily evening offering has to be the second public offering of the day, not the third. (This implies that the additional offerings of Sabbath and holidays are counted as appendices to the daily morning sacrifice.)</i>: “The blood of the daily sacrifice and its limbs precede the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> and the <i>Pesaḥ</i> the incense, and the incense the trimming of the lights.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 27:2" href="/Exodus.27.2"><i>Ex.</i> 27:2</a>.</i>” There are Tannaim who state: “The blood of the daily sacrifice and its limbs precede the incense, and the incense the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> and the <i>Pesaḥ</i> the trimming of the lights, so that the libations of the daily sacrifice should be accompanying it<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">“It” here is the burning of the incense on the golden altar in the Temple. Since the daily sacrifices and in the morning may precede, in the evening follow, the daily offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 59a" href="/Pesachim.59a">Babli 59a</a>)</i>.” There is nothing preceding the daily morning sacrifice and nothing later than the daily evening sacrifice except <i>Pesaḥ</i> and incense on Passover Eve. “<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 2 10" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_2.10"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 2(10</a>).</i> From where that nothing should precede the daily morning sacrifice? The verse says, <i>on it ˋolah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann conjectures that here one reads עולה as Aramaic אַוְלַא “first”.</i><i>.</i> And from where that nothing should invalidate the daily evening sacrifice? The verse says, <i>on it šelamim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Reading שִׁלּוּמִים “finishing”.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish says, it is not written here <i>he arranges on it ˋolah</i> but <i>he arranges on it the ˋolah,</i> that no elevation offering precede the daily morning sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 58b" href="/Pesachim.58b">Babli 58b</a>. The definite article describes an offering that certainly is brought on the altar immediately after it is readied for service; this must be the daily morning sacrifice.</i>. It was stated: Rebbi Ismael the son of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa says, people lacking expiation<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">People forbidden access to <i>sancta</i> unless a reparation sacrifice has been brought in the Temple on their behalf: The woman after childbirth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:6-8" href="/Leviticus.12.6-8"><i>Lev.</i> 12:6–8</a>), the male sufferer from gonorrhea (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:14-15" href="/Leviticus.15.14-15"><i>Lev.</i>15:14–15</a>), the female sufferer from flux (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:29-30" href="/Leviticus.15.29-30"><i>Lev.</i> 15:29–30</a>), and the person healed from skin disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:19" href="/Leviticus.14.19"><i>Lev.</i> 14:19</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:31" href="/Leviticus.14.31">31</a>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 59a" href="/Pesachim.59a">Babli 59a</a>.</i> bring their expiatory sacrifices after the daily evening sacrifice to enable them to immerse themselves and eat their <i>Pesaḥ</i> in the evening. Rebbi Yudan said, that is, for a rich sufferer from skin disease. But a poor sufferer from skin disease, does he not bring a bird<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:19" href="/Leviticus.14.19"><i>Lev.</i> 14:19</a> that of the two sacrifices offered, an elevation (עולה) and a reparation (חטאת) one, only the latter is expiatory. But in case this is a bird (in all cases except the wealthy sufferer from skin disease), nothing is burned on the altar, only its blood is squeezed out on wall and bottom of the altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:9" href="/Leviticus.5.9"><i>Lev.</i> 5:9</a>). Therefore these offerings by their nature are not subject to the limitations imposed on offerings of which parts are burned on the altar.</i>? Said Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon, does he not bring a reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">And <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:21" href="/Leviticus.14.21"><i>Lev.</i> 14:21</a> declares also the reparation offering as expiatory.</i>?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Pesaḥ</i> which he<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice may be slaughtered by its owner, or by a Levitic slaughterer attached to the Temple. The blood must be received in a vessel, transported to the altar, and sprinkled on the walls of the altar by a Cohen. Everybody involved in the service must have the intent of performing his duty explicitly for the <i>Pesaḥ.</i> If he started with the correct idea but in the act changed his mind and thought, e. g., that he is performing for the festival offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:6</a>), we do not say that his prior thought is a dedication which excludes additional dedications, but the association of <i>Pesaḥ</i> and any other kind of sacrifice in any order disqualifies the sacrifice; it must be disposed of by burning outside the Temple district.</i> slaughtered not for its purpose, received [the blood], transported it, or poured it, not for its purpose, or for its purpose and not for its purpose, or not for its purpose and for its purpose, is disqualified. What is “for its purpose and not for its purpose”? As <i>Pesaḥ</i> and as well-being offering. “Not for its purpose and for its purpose”? As well-being offering and as <i>Pesaḥ.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 7b" href="/Zevachim.7b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 7b</a>. The topic here really is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:1</a>: “All sacrifices which were not slaughtered specifically for the benefit of their owners are qualified except for <i>Pesaḥ</i> and purification offerings.” In all matters of required intent, the rules for <i>Pesaḥ</i> and purification offerings are equal.</i> From where that he must slaughter it for its purpose? Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: <i>And you shall say, a Pesaḥ slaughter it is</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 20:27. Quoted in this sense in <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Yoḥai</i> (p. 26, line 20).</i>; to imply that if he slaughtered it for the purpose of <i>Pesaḥ</i> it is <i>Pesaḥ</i>, otherwise it is not <i>Pesaḥ.</i> From where the remainder of its actions? <i>You shall make [a Pesaḥ]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:1" href="/Deuteronomy.16.1"><i>Deut</i>. 16:1</a>. The unspecific עשה always is interpreted to include all necessary actions.</i>, that all its actions shall be for the purpose of <i>Pesaḥ.</i> But then also the burning of its parts<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Greek αἱ μοῖραι [τοῦ θεοῦ], the fat which is forbidden for human consumption.</i>? The verse says, <i>slaughter</i> But then also the burning of its parts<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Greek αἱ μοῖραι [τοῦ θεοῦ], the fat which is forbidden for human consumption.</i>? The verse says, <i>slaughter</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">זבח indicates slaughter in preparation of a meal; often “sacrifice of which at least a part is consumed as sanctum”; whereas שחט (سحط) specifically means “to cut the throat”.</i><i>.</i> Since slaughter is particular in that it invalidates expiation<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is spelled out that <i>the blood is it which atones for the soul</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:11" href="/Leviticus.17.11"><i>Lev.</i> 17:11</a>), if anything goes wrong in any action necessary up to the pouring of the blood on the altar’s wall the sacrifice is invalid, but nothing that happens afterwards can invalidate the offering. If the parts to be burned become impure, they have to be burned outside the Temple district but the sacrifice remains valid.</i>, this excludes the burning of its parts which does not invalidate expiation.
From where for purification offerings? <i>He shall slaughter it as purification offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:33" href="/Leviticus.4.33"><i>Lev</i>. 4:33</a>.</i><i>.</i> From where the remainder of its actions? [<i>He</i>]<i>shall make</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:1" href="/Deuteronomy.16.1"><i>Deut</i>. 16:1</a>. The unspecific עשה always is interpreted to include all necessary actions.</i> <i>one as purification offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">There is no verse exactly as quoted in the text. The reference seems to be to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:30" href="/Leviticus.16.30"><i>Lev.</i>16:30</a>, <i>the Cohen shall bring one as purification offering.</i></i><i>.</i> But then also the burning of its parts<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Greek αἱ μοῖραι [τοῦ θεοῦ], the fat which is forbidden for human consumption.</i>? The verse says, <i>he shall slaughter.</i> Since slaughter is particular in that it invalidates expiation, this excludes the burning of its parts which does not invalidate expiation.
From where that a purification sacrifice must be for the name of its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">All Temple personnel dealing with the sacrifice have to know the name of the owner, and the purpose of the sacrifice, and have to intend to help his expiation.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, <i>the Cohen shall make the purification offering and expiate for the one who purifies himself from his impurity</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:19" href="/Leviticus.14.19"><i>Lev.</i> 14:19</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 8a" href="/Zevachim.8a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 8a</a> in the name of Rava, a contemporary of R. Jeremiah.<br>From here through Chapter 7 there exists a series of Genizah fragments from the Kaufmann collection in Budapest, published by S. Loewinger in the Hebrew part of the Alexander Marx Jubilee Volumes (New York 1950), indicated here by K.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Yose said to him, but then also the burning of its parts<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Greek αἱ μοῖραι [τοῦ θεοῦ], the fat which is forbidden for human consumption.</i>? The verse says, <i>and expiate.</i> Since pouring is particular in that it invalidates expiation<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is spelled out that <i>the blood is it which atones for the soul</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:11" href="/Leviticus.17.11"><i>Lev.</i> 17:11</a>), if anything goes wrong in any action necessary up to the pouring of the blood on the altar’s wall the sacrifice is invalid, but nothing that happens afterwards can invalidate the offering. If the parts to be burned become impure, they have to be burned outside the Temple district but the sacrifice remains valid.</i>, this excludes the burning of its parts which does not invalidate expiation.
From where that a <i>Pesaḥ</i> must be in the name of its owner? Is it not a logical argument<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">דִּין usually introduces an informal argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i></i>? Since a purification sacrifice, where intent for the uncircumcised or impure<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices of the uncircumcised (e. g., a hemophiliac who may not be circumcised) or an impure person (e. g., a resident outside the Land) sent through third persons are accepted in the Temple. But any uncircumcised is excluded from the <i>Pesaḥ</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:48" href="/Exodus.12.48"><i>Ex.</i>12:48</a>) and the person who will not be pure by nightfall is excluded by the requirement that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> be slaughtered for the group of subscribers (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:3-4" href="/Exodus.12.3-4"><i>Ex.</i> 12:3–4</a>); adding the name of a person prohibited from eating sacred food will invalidate the slaughter. This argument is somewhat circular; since the argument is rejected for other reasons, this does not have to be pointed out.</i> does not invalidate it, needs to be in the name of the owner, <i>Pesaḥ,</i> where intent for the uncircumcised or impure does invalidate it, is it not logical that it needs to be in the name of its owner? No. If you are saying about purification sacrifice which is most holy<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">It may be eaten only by male Cohanim in the Temple precinct.</i>, would you say that about <i>Pesaḥ</i> which is a simple <i>sanctum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">It may be eaten by every pure person within the walls of the city of the Temple.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, did you not argue about intent? The Torah insisted about intent for <i>Pesaḥ</i> more than for purification sacrifice. Rebbi Ḥananiah said before Rebbi Mana: Do we infer this from the purification sacrifice of the sufferer from skin disease? But is not the purification sacrifice of the sufferer from skin disease separate for something new<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 9:6" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.9.6">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 9:6</a>, no purification offering other than that of the sufferer from skin disease needs accompanying offerings of flour and wine. The offering of flour is explicit in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:10" href="/Leviticus.14.10"><i>Lev.</i> 14:10</a>; that of wine is inferred in <i>Sifra Mesoraˋ Pereq</i> 2(10).</i>? And one cannot infer from anything which is separate for something new<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Ismael’s 12th hermeneutical principle: Anything which was in a group, but is taken from the group to be under a separate rule, cannot be returned to its original group unless the verse returns it explicitly. An example is the reparation sacrifice of the sufferer from skin disease, whose blood is not for the altar but for the right thumb and right great toe of the owner, but which <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:13" href="/Leviticus.14.13"><i>Lev.</i> 14:13</a> declares to follow the rules of reparation sacrifices in all respects. Such a note is missing for the purification sacrifice. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 8a" href="/Zevachim.8a">Babli, <i>Zevaḥim</i> 8a</a>, accepts the argument as valid.</i>. He told him, from where do you infer that it be invalid if not for its purpose? Not from the following verse, <i>he shall slaughter it as purification sacrifice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:33" href="/Leviticus.4.33"><i>Lev.</i> 4:33</a>. <i>Sifra Wayyiqra II (Ḥovah)</i><br><i>Pereq</i> 11(3).</i>, and it is written: <i>this is the doctrine of the purification sacrifice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:18" href="/Leviticus.6.18"><i>Lev.</i> 6:18</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 9a" href="/Zevachim.9a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 9a</a>. Interpreted differently in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Section 3 1" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Section_3.1"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Parašah</i> 3(1</a>).</i><i>.</i> There is one doctrine for all purification sacrifices. But from the place where it is being inferred, there it permits inferences<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since the flour offering does not accompany the purification offering of the sufferer from skin disease but his elevation offering (14:20), the attribution of the wine offering to the purification offering is an inference of the oral tradition which cannot override <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:18" href="/Leviticus.6.18"><i>Lev.</i> 6:18</a>.</i>.
If he slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to sprinkle its blood not for its purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The slaughterer, who most probably is not priestly, is different from the person who will pour the blood. He has the correct intention for his own action, but intends a future action to be disqualifying. Does this have an influence on the status of <i>Pesaḥ</i> or purification offering? <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 9b-10a" href="/Zevachim.9b-10a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i>9b/10a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 39a" href="/Chullin.39a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 39a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan  said, there is transfer of “not for its purpose” from service to service and it is disqualified. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, is there transfer of “not for its purpose” from service to service<sup class="footnote-marker">66a</sup><i class="footnote">In K: “Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, there is no transfer of ‘not for its purpose’ from service to service.”</i>? It should be qualified. Rebbi Ila said, Rebbi Joḥanan  learned this from thought of <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The source is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:7" href="/Leviticus.19.7"><i>Lev.</i> 19:7</a>. A well-being offering must be eaten on the day of its offering and the next. <i>But if it is intended to be eaten on the third day, it is piggul</i>(“mushy”) <i>and will not be wanted.</i> Eating from sacrificial meat on the third day is sinful, but this has no influence on the validity of the sacrifice. But from the start intending to eat on the third day disqualifies the sacrifice and any consumption of its meat, even on the first day, is a deadly sin. This clearly is disqualification by intent for a future action by a different person. The argument is accepted in both Babli sources.</i>. If he poured not for its purpose would that not cause <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">It would cause disqualification but not <i>piggul.</i></i>? If he slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to pour its blood not for its purpose, it is disqualified. Rebbi Yose said, in two aspects is the intent of <i>piggul</i> not equivalent to the intent of disqualification<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">He objects of comparing required actions in the presentation of sacrifices to <i>piggul.</i></i>. If he slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to receive its blood not for its purpose, would that not be qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Cohen who receives the blood must be present at the moment of slaughter, the Cohen’s intent is what counts; a contrary intent by the slaughterer is irrelevant since it cannot precede the Cohen’s.</i>? If he slaughtered for its purpose and received its blood not for its purpose, it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">In this sentence and the next, only the scribe’s text is reproduced and translated; the corrector’s additions and deletions are disregarded. The scribe’s text is fully confirmed by K.<br>The argument goes as follows: For any action up to the pouring of the blood, the wrong intent before or during the action disqualifies. But for actions required after the pouring of the blood, such as burning of the parts or eating the meat, a wrong intent before the pouring of the blood disqualifies, but a wrong intent at the moment of the action is irrelevant. Therefore, the rules of <i>piggul</i> cannot be used to infer rules for actions preceding the pouring of the blood.</i>. If he slaughtered for its purpose in order to burn its parts not for its purpose, would that not be <i>piggul</i>? If he slaughtered for its purpose and in order to burn its parts not for its purpose, it is qualified.
Rebbi Yose said, I saw Rebbi Jeremiah grabbing Rebbi Abba and saying to him, tell me the reason of Rebbi Joḥanan , why it is disqualified if one slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to pour its blood not for its purpose? It is made from the start as if he slaughtered for its purpose and not for its purpose, and it is disqualified. He told him, if this is so, even if he slaughtered not<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">For the text to make sense, this word has to be deleted with K.</i> for its eaters and to sprinkle its blood not for its eaters, it should be as if from the start he slaughtered for its eaters and not for its eaters and be qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>. This you cannot do, as Rebbi Ila said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan , if he slaughtered for its eaters and to pour its blood not for its eaters, it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, here one has to read “disqualified,” even though the text is confirmed by K.</i>. There came Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan , if he slaughtered for its eaters and to pour its blood not for its eaters, it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Yose is correct; the tradition of R. Ila is incorrect, and R. Joḥanan  is consistent in his opinions.</i>. Rebbi Yose got his strength<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">To reject a comparison with <i>piggul.</i></i> from the two reasons of Rebbi Joḥanan . Why is it disqualified if one slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to pour its blood not for its purpose? It is made from start as if he slaughtered for its purpose and not for its purpose, and is disqualified. Why is it qualified if he slaughtered for its eaters and to pour its blood not for its eaters? It is made from the start as if he slaughtered for its eaters and not for its eaters, and is qualified.
Rebbi Ḥananiah said before Rebbi Mana in the name of Rebbi Yudan. The reason of Rebbi Joḥanan that if one would come to perform a certain action without thinking<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Since sacrifices may become disqualified if a necessary action is made with the wrong intent, it is preferable that the Cohen not have any thought other than doing his duty for whom and for what it is needed.</i>, one continues thinking from another action. If one slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to pour its blood not for its purpose, would that not be <i>piggufl</i>68? If one slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to pour its blood not for its purpose, it is disqualified. If one slaughtered for its purpose and to receive its blood not for its purpose, would that not be qualified, since on the act of receiving he is thinking<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Cohen who receives the blood must be present at the moment of slaughter, the Cohen’s intent is what counts; a contrary intent by the slaughterer is irrelevant since it cannot precede the Cohen’s.</i>. Therefore, if one would come to perform a certain action without thinking, one continues the thinking from another action. If one slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to burn its parts not for its purpose, would that not be <i>piggul</i>? If at the moment of burning he does not think, then if one would come to perform a certain action without thinking, one continues the thinking from another action<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.2.6">Note 71</a>, the intent at the moment of the action after the pouring of the blood cannot have retroactive influence on the validity of the sacrifice.</i>. He told him, do not object to me about disqualification because of <i>piggul,</i> since Rebbi Yose said, in two aspects is the intent of <i>piggul</i> not equivalent to the intent of disqualification.
What results from the difference between them? Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon said, switching on the other days of the year. If one slaughtered for its purpose with the intent to sprinkle its blood not for its purpose, on the other days of the year. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan  it is qualified; in the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Joḥanan , <i>Pesaḥ</i> at any other day is a well-being sacrifice which is not disqualified by an action not done for its purpose (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:1</a>). For R. Simeon ben Laqish, <i>Pesaḥ</i> on any other day is out of order.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also appears in <i>Šeqalim</i>2:4 (<b>ש</b>). The readings of the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Babli with Yerushalmi <i>Šeqalim</i> are noted (<b>שׁ</b>); those of interest of the very shortened Munich ms. of the Babli as (M). The version of <i>Šeqalim</i> in the Babli is characterized by much babylonized spelling; there is an addition in Babylonian Aramaic directly taken from the parallel in the Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 8b-9a.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan  said, about this Abba bar Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">He is the father of Samuel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 8b" href="/Zevachim.8b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 8b</a>), head of the school of Nahardea in the generation of transition from Tannaim to Amoraim. He reports a Babylonian tradition.</i> enlightened me, for they are saying, from where that <i>Pesaḥ</i> is changed<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">An offering in the Temple declared as <i>Pesaḥ</i> on any day other than the 14th of Nisan automatically is for well-being. Therefore animals dedicated as <i>Pesaḥ</i> but not needed on the 14th, at nightfall of the 15th automatically become dedicated well-being offerings.</i> into the denomination of well-being sacrifices? The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3:6" href="/Leviticus.3.6"><i>Lev.</i> 3:6</a>.</i>, <i>and if his sacrifice be from small cattle as meal well-being offering</i>; anything from small cattle comes as well-being offering. They objected, is there not an elevation offering [from small cattle]<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Scribe’s text, incorrectly deleted by corrector and missing in printed editions but confiirmed by <b>ש</b>. K is lacunary at this point.</i>? Anything which only comes from small cattle; this eliminates the elevation offering which even may come from large cattle. They objected, is there not reparation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Which never comes from large cattle.</i>? Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said, “<i>from small cattle”.</i> this eliminates the reparation offering, which only comes from rams. [Rebbi Abun objected,]<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Scribe’s text, incorrectly deleted by corrector and missing in printed editions but confiirmed by <b>ש</b>. K is lacunary at this point.</i> everywhere you are saying that מִן is to include, but here you are saying that מִן is to exclude<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The text is difficult since it is standard rabbinic interpretation to consider prefix <i>mem</i> or מִן as privative, excluding certain categories (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 7:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.7.1.11"><i>Šabbat</i> 7 Note 26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.2.4"><i>Ševuot</i> 1:2 Note 75</a>, <i>Bava Mesiaˋ</i> 4:8 Note 122, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.3"><i>Nazir</i>5:4 Note 105</a>). Also in the next sentence, R. Mana gives the interpretation that here מִן is privative. On the other hand, the testimony of K, M, and the scribe’s text of <b>ש</b> do not permit emendation. It seems that here “every where” is derogatory, meaning Babylonian. The sequence of arguments leads to a contradiction. Abba bar Abba treats מִן as inclusive, R. Abun bar Cahana as exclusive. R. Mana explains that מִן always is partitive; automatic switch to well-being offerings is possible only for sacrifices that totally correspond to the declaration צאן, i. e., both sheep and goats, male and female.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, it excludes it, since it only comes from rams. They objected, is there not written,<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:10" href="/Leviticus.1.10"><i>Lev.</i> 1:10</a>.</i> <i>and if his sacrifice be from small cattle, from sheep or goats, as elevation offering;</i> then excess <i>Pesaḥ</i> should become elevation offering? Rebbi Abun said, one changes something to be eaten into something to be eaten, [but one does not change something to be eaten into something not to be eaten.]<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Addition by the corrector from <b>ש</b>, confirmed by K.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, one changes simple <i>sancta</i> into simple <i>sancta,</i> but one does not change simple <i>sancta</i> into most holy sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The latter category includes both elevation and reparation sacrifices.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan  said, about what Rebbi Ḥanina enlightened, that you are saying, <i>Pesaḥ</i> is changed<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">An offering in the Temple declared as <i>Pesaḥ</i> on any day other than the 14th of Nisan automatically is for well-being. Therefore animals dedicated as <i>Pesaḥ</i> but not needed on the 14th, at nightfall of the 15th automatically become dedicated well-being offerings.</i> into a well-being offering only if he slaughtered it for the purpose of well-being offering; but I am saying, even for the purpose of an elevation offering. Rebbi [Il]la said, the reason of Rebbi Joḥanan : <i>And if his sacrifice be from small cattle as meal well-being offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3:6" href="/Leviticus.3.6"><i>Lev.</i> 3:6</a>.</i>; anything to be consumed as <i>sanctum</i> is a well-being offering. Does it change with respect to disqualifying thoughts<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">If the animal dedicated as <i>Pesaḥ</i> is used against the rules for something other than a well-being offering, do the rules of the other kind apply or is it disqualified and no rules of intent apply.</i>? How is this? If he slaughtered it for the purpose of an elevation offering in order to pour its blood the next day<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This being forbidden certainly disqualifies.</i>. In any case, it is disqualified. If you are saying that it changes with respect to disqualifying thoughts, it is <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">If the animal still is a sacrifice, now under the rules of elevation sacrifices, the intention to perform any required action out of its prescribed time-frame generates <i>piggul,</i> which is a deadly sin causing extirpation.</i><i>.</i> If you are saying that it does not change with respect to disqualifying thoughts, it is disqualified.
<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also has a parallel in <i>Šeqalim.</i></i> For its purpose and not for its purpose on the other days of the year<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The slaughterer has intent both for <i>Pesaḥ</i> and for well-being offering. Since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> then is a well-being offering, it should not make any difference.</i>? Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya in the name of Samuel bar Abba: Since it is left without a name it is as if from the start he slaughtered for its purpose and not for its purpose in silence and is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since now <i>Pesaḥ</i> and well-being offering mean the same, there is no contradiction. But since the names are different, it may be treated as if it was slaughtered for “what may apply”. It is presumed that slaughtering a simple sacrifice without spelling out the category is qualified.</i>. They said to him. if it is so, even if he slaughtered for its purpose to pour the blood not for its purpose it should be treated as if from the start he slaughtered for its purpose and not for its purpose in silence and be qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Since two different designations were used, the presumption should be that this is disqualified, as given in K.</i>. Rebbi Abba Mari said, who says that in silence it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The premise of Samuel bar Abba is unproven. The question remains unanswered.</i>? Or may we say, in silence it is disqualified?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he slaughtered it for those who cannot not eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">For example a sick person who will not be able to eat a full olive-sized piece of meat.</i>, for those who did not subscribe to it<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Pesaḥ</i> may be eaten only by people who beforehand formed a group to this purpose, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:4" href="/Exodus.12.4"><i>Ex.</i> 12:4</a>.</i>, for the uncircumcised,<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">He is forbidden to participate even if he is a hemophiliac who may not be circumcised, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:48" href="/Exodus.12.48"><i>Ex.</i> 12:48</a>.</i> or for the impure<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">If he will be impure in the evening, such as a person impure in the impurity of the dead who is impure for seven days. For an impure person eating <i>sancta</i> is a deadly sin, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:21" href="/Leviticus.7.21"><i>Lev.</i> 7:21</a>.</i>, it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The meat of a simple sacrifice must be eaten; it may not be slaughtered in the absence of people who would consume it.</i>. For those who eat it and those who will not eat it, for those who subscribed to it and those who did not subscribe to it, for the circumcised and the uncircumcised, or for the pure and the impure, it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since people may decide to join the group until the blood is poured on the walls of the altar, which is after slaughtering, the slaughter is qualified as long as there is at least one person for whom it is validly slaughtered.</i>. If one slaughtered it before noon it is disqualified since it was said about it: <i>between the evenings</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">See Halakhah 1.</i><i>.</i> If one slaughtered it before the daily evening sacrifice it is qualified, on condition that somebody stir the blood until after the blood of the daily evening sacrifice was poured;<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Since any sacrifice may be slaughtered by a lay person and the sacral activities only start with the reception of the blood in a sacral vessel by a Cohen, the status of a <i>Pesaḥ</i> slaughtered before the daily sacrifice, whose blood was stirred to prevent jellying and which then was poured on the walls of the altar after the daily sacrifice, is identical with one which was slaughtered after the daily sacrifice.</i> but if the blood was poured it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the pouring was done before the daily sacrifice. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.1.4">Note 20</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> From where that he needs to slaughter for those who will eat it? Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Ismael: <i>For each man according to his eating you shall cut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:4" href="/Exodus.12.4"><i>Ex.</i> 12:4</a>. This translation reads the <i>hapax</i> תָּכֹ֖סּוּ with Ibn Ezra as derived from a root כסס “to cut into pieces”, as in Accadic. R. Josia reads it as imperative of Syriac נכס “to slaughter”. <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bo Parašah</i> 3, end. Differetly <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 61a" href="/Pesachim.61a">Babli, 61a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 78b" href="/Pesachim.78b">78b</a>.<br>K reads: <i>For each man according to his eating,</i> one has to slaughter according to its eaters.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Josia said, it is a Syriac expresssion, as if a person say to his neighbor, slaughter for me this lamb.
“How is ‘those who cannot eat it’? If he slaughtered it in the name of a sick person, in the name of an old person, who cannot eat the volume of an olive. How about ‘those who did not subscribe to it’? If he slaughtered it in the name of a different group.<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 61a" href="/Pesachim.61a">Babli 61a</a>.</i>” This implies that a baby can be counted to disqualify<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since the baby cannot eat solid food. In <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Ba, Parašah</i> 3, the baby is mentioned instead of the old person.</i>. This implies that if one was on a distant trip<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Who cannot possibly be near the central sanctuary to participate in the <i>Pesaḥ; Num.</i> 9:9.</i> and he slaughtered it in this person’s name, it is disqualified. This implies that it supports the Southerners, since the Southerners are saying, one thinks about parts which were lost as if they were in existence<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Meilah 7a" href="/Meilah.7a"><i>Meˋilah</i> 7a</a>.<br>It has been stated before that the validity of a sacrifice depends on the intent of the slaughterer. There is a general rule, that intent regarding less than the volume of an olive is disregarded (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.3.7">Note 125</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 29b" href="/Zevachim.29b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 29b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 31a" href="/Zevachim.31a">31a</a>). But if the intent was for at least the volume of an olive, but in fact it was impossible that it be the volume of an olive, as in the case of the baby or the sick person, it is intent that counts and not the fact. Similarly, if the intent was that parts should be burned out of time, it makes the sacrifice <i>piggul</i> even if the parts are lost and it is physically impossible to burn them out of time.</i>. This implies that if one slaughtered so that half of the group should eat, it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Part of the group were sick and could not eat the volume of an olive. If the <i>Pesaḥ</i> was slaughtered for those who can eat and those who cannot, it is qualified as stated in the Mishnah. But if it was slaughtered for all of them as being able to eat the volume of an olive, the slaughter was not “for those who eat it and those who will not eat it” and therefore those who cannot eat it will make it disqualified.</i>. Rebbi Haggai said before Rebbi Yose, explain it that all of them were sick<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Certainly as a group they are able to consume meat in the volume of an olive.</i>. He told him, then one should have stated, “in the name of disqualified members of the group.” He said to him, if there were qualified people, but he slaughtered in the name of the disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">hen certainly the sacrifice is invalid.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah if he slaughtered it for those who eat it, but it was covered by those who eat it and those who cannot eat it. But it is disqualified if from the start he slaughtered it so that it should be covered by those who eat it and those who cannot eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that R. Eleazar interprets the Mishnah that the sacrifice is qualified if the slaughterer intended it for the entire group, without inquiring whether all members of the group were entitled to eat or able to consume a minimum. If then it turned out that some members were barred or unable to participate, there was no false intent and the sacrifice is qualified. But if from the start there was explicit intent to include incapable or disqualified persons, the intent was disqualifying (S. Liebermann).</i>.
Rebbi Simlai came to Rebbi Jonathan<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 62b" href="/Pesachim.62b">Babli 62b</a>. R. Simlai became famous as a preacher. K and the Medieval sources add that he was too young to be taught these matters.</i>. He said to him, teach me homiletics. He answered him, I have a tradition from my forefathers not to teach homiletics either to a Babylonian or to a Southerner, since they are gross in spirit and have little learning. And you are from Nahardea and live in the South. He said to him, tell me this one thing, what is the difference between “for its purpose and not for its purpose” and “for those who eat it and those who cannot eat it”<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Why is the former disqualified and the latter qualified.</i>? He answered him, “for its purpose and not for its purpose”, the disqualification is intrinsic. “For those who eat it and those who cannot eat it”, the disqualification is of others. “For its purpose and not for its purpose”, you cannot pick out the disqualified from the qualified. [“For those who eat it and those who cannot eat it”, you can pick out the disqualified from the qualified.]<sup class="footnote-marker">118a</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s addition is justified by K, which reads “you can’t” instead of “you can.</i> “For its purpose and not for its purpose”, applies to all sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:1</a>: “All sacrifices, except <i>Pesaḥ</i> and purification offerings, which were slaughtered not for their purpose are qualified but do not relieve their owners from their obligations.”</i>; “for those who eat it and those who cannot eat it” applies only to the <i>Pesaḥ</i>.
Rebbi Yose said, from that of Rebbi Eleazar one may deduce two [conclusions]. Since Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah if he slaughtered it for those who eat it, but it was covered by those who eat it and those who cannot eat it. But it is disqualified if from the start he slaughtered for those who eat it and it was covered by those who eat it and those who cannot eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that R. Eleazar interprets the Mishnah that the sacrifice is qualified if the slaughterer intended it for the entire group, without inquiring whether all members of the group were entitled to eat or able to consume a minimum. If then it turned out that some members were barred or unable to participate, there was no false intent and the sacrifice is qualified. But if from the start there was explicit intent to include incapable or disqualified persons, the intent was disqualifying (S. Liebermann).</i>. Then its disqualification is of others and you can pick out the disqualified from the qualified, and you are saying so? But it must be the following, “for its purpose and not for its purpose”, applies to all sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:1</a>: “All sacrifices, except <i>Pesaḥ</i> and purification offerings, which were slaughtered not for their purpose are qualified but do not relieve their owners from their obligations.”</i>; “for those who eat it and those who cannot eat it” applies only to the <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Of all the arguments of R. Jonathan, only the last is valid.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, there are others. “For its purpose and not for its purpose”, applies to all services<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:4" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.4">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:4</a>: The sacrifice becomes disqualified [by wrong intent] in four cases: For slaughter, for reception of the blood, for carrying the blood to the altar, for pouring the blood.</i>; “for those who eat it and those who cannot eat it” applies only to slaughtering.
For those who eat it in volumes of an olive and those who cannot eat it in volumes of an olive, it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">This is the normal case mentioned in the Mishnah.</i>. For those who eat it in volumes of an olive and those who cannot eat it in volumes of half an olive, it is qualified <i>a</i> <i>fortiori</i>. For those who eat it in volumes of half an olive and those who cannot eat it in volumes of half an olive? Where do we hold? If intent was qualifying, intent is disqualifying<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">If intent regarding consumption of less than the volume of an olive is counted as qualifying, then it also must be counted as disqualifying. In this case, the rule formulated by R. Eleazar applies as if the intent was for volumes of an olive.</i>. If intent is not disqualifying then the intent should be qualifying.<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">If intent regarding consumption of less than the volume of an olive is disregarded, then the slaughter was made without special condition, “for whom it may apply”, and is qualified.</i> Rebbi Yose said, a Mishnah implies that the intent in qualifying, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 2:5" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.2.5">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 2:5</a>.</i>: “To eat half the volume of an olive and to burn half the volume of an olive is qualified since slaughter and burning do not combine<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice was slaughtered with the<br>intent that half the volume of an olive should be eaten out of its allotted time and half the volume of an olive be burned on the altar out of its allotted time. Since it is stated that the sacrifice is qualified, it follows that any intent about half the volume of an olive is disregarded. Therefore in the preceding case the intent is disregarded and the sacrifice is qualified.</i>.” If he slaughtered it that half of its group should be covered, Rebbi Jonah disqualifies. It is as grabbing the cover for these and those. Since these are not covered, neither are those covered<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Since an entire group paid for the <i>Pesaḥ</i>, they are its owners. If the intent was to eliminate part of the owners, the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is slaughtered not for its owners and therefore disqualified.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, it is qualified. During the second cycle<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">When the entire material of the Yerushalmi was reviewed a second time in the Academy of RR. Yose in Tiberias, he</i>, Rebbi Yose retracted. Rebbi Phineas said to him, did the rabbi not teach us, it is qualified. He told him, is this fixed for you by nails?
“If one slaughtered it before noon it is disqualified,” [after noontime] immediately it is qualified. “If one slaughtered it before the daily sacrifice it is qualified.” It was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry zuta Pinhas</i> 28(3). The question was whether slaughter of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> prohibits later slaughter of the daily sacrifice. The answer is that the wording of the verse <i>Num..</i> 28:4, וְאֵת֙ הַכֶּ֣בֶשׂ הַשֵּׁנִ֔י תַּֽעֲשֶׂה֭ בֵּ֥ין הָֽעַרְבָּֽיִם when it would have been possible to say simply וְאֵת הַכֶּבֶשׂ הַשֵּׁנִי בֵּין הָֽעַרְבָּיִם implies that it must be broughtr in any case.</i>: One could think that if <i>Pesaḥ</i> preceded the daily sacrifice, the daily sacrifice could not be delayed. The verse says, <i>do</i>, adding. Rebbi Abun said, the Mishnah implies that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> itself is qualified. If you would say, it is disqualified, it would be as if it did not precede.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Num.</i> 143, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 58b" href="/Pesachim.58b">Babli 58b</a>. The argument seems to be that the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> while a personal sacrifice from the offerers, has the status of a public sacrifice, since only public sacrifices can override Sabbath prohibitions and be offered if the people are impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.4.1">Mishnah 9:3</a>). Then the verse states that the daily evening offering has to be the second public offering of the day, not the third. (This implies that the additional offerings of Sabbath and holidays are counted as appendices to the daily morning sacrifice.)</i>, you are saying: “The blood of the daily sacrifice and its limbs precede the <i>Pesaḥ</i>, and the <i>Pesaḥ</i> the incense, and the incense the trimming of the lights.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 27:2" href="/Exodus.27.2"><i>Ex.</i> 27:2</a>.</i>” And here you are saying so? Rebbi La said, one if it is living, the other if it is slaughtered<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> requires that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> be brought after the limbs of the daily sacrifice are on the altar, but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> mentions only pouring of the blood, without mention of the limbs. If the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is still alive, one has to wait until the limbs have been disposed of; if it is slaughtered, one waits only for the pouring of the blood.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who slaughters the <i>Pesaḥ</i> on leavened matter transgresses a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:18" href="/Exodus.23.18"><i>Ex.</i> 23:18</a>; 34:25: <i>Do not sacrifice on leavened matter the blood of My sacrifice; the sacrifice of the Pesaḥ pilgrimage.</i> “On leavened matter” means that he has leavened matter in his possession.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says, also the daily sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is an elevation offering which is completely burned, it is <i>My sacrifice.</i></i>. Rebbi Simeon says, the <i>Pesaḥ</i> on the Fourteenth, if for its purpose, he is liable, not for its purpose he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">A disqualified sacrifice is no sacrifice at all and therefore not a subject of the prohibition.</i>. All other sacrifices, whether for their purposes or not for their purposes, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse refers only to the <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>. On the holiday, for its purpose he is not liable, not for its purpose he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon reads the verse as referring not only to the 14th of Nisan but also to the entire Holiday of Unleavened Bread, Nisan 15–21. Since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> slaughtered for its purpose on any day other than the 14th is disqualified, it does not count. But not for its purpose it is a qualified well-being sacrifice.</i>; for all other sacrifices, whether for their purposes or not for their purposes, he is liable except for the purification offering which he slaughtered not for its purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Which is disqualified (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishna <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:1</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> From where that one who slaughters the <i>Pesaḥ</i> on leavened matter transgresses a prohibition? The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:18" href="/Exodus.23.18"><i>Ex.</i> 23:18</a>; 34:25: <i>Do not sacrifice on leavened matter the blood of My sacrifice; the sacrifice of the Pesaḥ pilgrimage.</i> “On leavened matter” means that he has leavened matter in his possession.</i>, <i>do not slaughter on leavened matter the blood of My sacrifice</i>. I have not only the slaughterer, from where the one who pours the blood? The verse says, <i>not on leavened matter the blood</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, since he becomes guilty for pouring, this implies that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> be qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">132,</sup><i class="footnote">A disqualified sacrifice is no sacrifice at all and therefore not a subject of the prohibition.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 4:3.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, explain it that leavened matter came to him between slaughter and pouring or that one person was slaughtering and another pouring<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">In both cases the slaughter was correct and only the pouring incorrect. One still has to assume that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> slaughtered on leavened matter even in the afternoon of the 14<sup>th</sup> is disqualified.</i>.
Ḥizqiah stated: <i>Do not slaughter on leavened matter the blood of My sacrifice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:18" href="/Exodus.23.18"><i>Ex.</i> 23:18</a>; 34:25: <i>Do not sacrifice on leavened matter the blood of My sacrifice; the sacrifice of the Pesaḥ pilgrimage.</i> “On leavened matter” means that he has leavened matter in his possession.</i>, the Torah called it “My sacrifice”<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that it is a valid sacrifice. Cf. <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Yoḥai</i> p. 219, line 1.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, if Ḥizqiah had not stated this, do we find anything disqualified because of which one is liable for a purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Without Ḥizqiah”s statement we would have declared the sacrifice disqualified since in fact we find that a person who sacrifices outside the official sanctuary is liable (i. e., for extirpation or a purification sacrifice) both for the slaughter and for burning on an altar even though the slaughter disqualifies (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 13:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.13.1">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i>13:1</a>).</i>? If somebody leavens the disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to flour offerings the entire year. They are required to be unleavened, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:11" href="/Leviticus.2.11"><i>Lev.</i>2:11</a>.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, he is liable; there are Tannaim who state, he is not liable. Rav Ḥisda said, he who says liable, if it became disqualified because of its leavening<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">Being inside the sacred district, they could not sit down.</i>; he who says not liable, if it did not become disqualified because of its leavening.
The burning of the parts on leavening<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">On the afternoon of the 14th of Nisan. Differently Babli 63b-64a.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, he is liable; there are Tannaim who state, he is not liable. He who says liable, <i>blood</i> anywhere. He who says not liable, <i>sacrifice</i>. Since slaughtering for sacrifice is particular in that it is indispensable for atonement, this excludes burning of the parts, which is not indispensable for atonement.
If one broke a bird’s neck on leavening<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">This must refer to the middle days of the holiday following R. Simeon, since it was stated earlier that on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> one is liable only for the <i>Pesaḥ</i> and possibly the daily sacrifice.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, he is liable; there are Tannaim who state, he is not liable. He who says liable, <i>blood</i> anywhere. He who says not liable, <i>sacrifice</i>. This excludes the bird which is not slaughtered as sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">זֶבַח is a <i>sanctum,</i> parts of which are consumed by lay people. A bird sacrifice, not killed by <i>šehitah,</i> if eaten at all is reserved for priests.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi asked before Rebbi Yose.
