Eliezer call this optional?</i>, “One who desecrates <i>sancta,</i> or abases the holidays, or breaks the Covenant of our father Abraham, or is insolent against the Torah, even if in his hands are learning and good deeds, has no part in the Future World.” Rebbi Jeremiah said, what is optional<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">There could have been meals prepared beforehand. There is no religious obligation to slaughter on a holiday, it truly is optional.</i>? If he wants he cooks, if he does not want he does not cook.
Rebbi Yose said, so should Rebbi Eliezer have embarrassed Rebbi Joshua. Does not the festival offering disprove according to your system, where they permitted the category of work but prohibited the category of Sabbath rest<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Eliezer is a follower of the House of Shammai, who permit the festival sacrifice to be brought on the holiday but prohibit the (biblically required but purely rabbinically prohibited) laying of hands on the sacrificial animal, he would have to agree that not all rabbinic restrictions are waived on Sabbath and holiday.</i>? He should have answered, no. If you are saying this about the festival offering about which one is not liable for extirpation, what can you say about <i>Pesaḥ</i> where one is liable for extirpation? He also could have objected as in the Mishnah: No, if you are saying this about the holiday about which one is not liable for extirpation, what can you say about <i>Pesaḥ where</i> one is liable for extirpation? It is as Rebbi Immi said, they were rich in answers<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The same statements <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.3.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 2:5 Note 89</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.4"><i>Nazir</i> 5:4 Note 109</a>.</i>. Or it may be as Rebbi Nassa said, like a man who has two arguments but he moves one of them.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Aqiba answered, sprinkling<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Purifying a person impure by the impurity of the dead by sprinkling with water containing of the ashes of the Red Cow. In Second Temple times this was a public act (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 11:4" href="/Mishnah_Parah.11.4">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 11:4</a>) not performed on the Sabbath.</i> is a counter example, for it is a commandment and because of Sabbath rest does not push aside the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">If the 14th of Nisan is a Sabbath and a person’s seventh day of impurity falls on that day, he may not be purified by sprinkling, but this is not biblically forbidden, and he has to celebrate his <i>Pesaḥ</i> on the 14th of Iyar. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.3.1">Mishnaiot 3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:1:1-9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.1.1-9.1">4</a>.</i>. So you should not wonder about these<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah is a continuation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:1:1-18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.1.1-18">Mishnah 1</a>, where R. Eliezer holds that all actions needed for the <i>Pesaḥ</i> push aside the Sabbath. The discussion in particular is about the list of rabbinically prohibited actions at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:1:1-18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.1.1-18">Mishnah 1</a>, which are permitted by R. Eliezer but prohibited by the Sages (represented by R. Joshua.)</i>, even though they are commandments and because of Sabbath rest but shall not push aside the Sabbath.<br>Rebbi Eliezer said to him, about this I am arguing. Since slaughter, which is work, pushes the Sabbath aside, sprinkling, which is because of Sabbath rest, shall push aside the Sabbath. Rebbi Aqiba said to him, or the other way. Since sprinkling, which is because of Sabbath rest, does not push aside the Sabbath, slaughter, which is work, shall not push the Sabbath aside<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is agreed that the obligation to slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> on the Sabbath if it is the 14th of Nisan is a biblical decree (Halakhah 6:1), what R. Aqiba proves is that biblical and rabbinic decrees cannot be compared in formal or informal arguments <i>de minore ad majus.</i></i>.<br>Rebbi Eliezer said to him, Aqiba! You uprooted what is written in the Torah, <i>between the evenings, at its fixed time</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:2" href="/Numbers.9.2"><i>Num.</i> 9:2</a>.</i>, whether on weekdays or on the Sabbath. He answered him, Rabbi, bring me a fixed time for these similar to the fixed time for slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">There is no biblical authorization for preparations of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> to be executed in violation of Sabbath rules.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba formulated a principle: Anything which can be done on Friday does not push the Sabbath aside. Slaughter which cannot be done on Friday pushes the Sabbath aside.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Thirteen years did Rebbi Aqiba enter at Rebbi Eliezer’s and he did not take notice of him. This is his first retort before Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Joshua said to him, <i>is this not the people which you despised; go now and fight against it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 9:38" href="/Judges.9.38"><i>Jud.</i> 9:38</a>.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.2.1">Mishnah 9:2</a>.</i>, we have stated: “What is ‘far away’<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">That a person at this place on the 14th of Nisan is not obligated to bring his <i>Pesaḥ</i> on the 14th of Nisan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:13" href="/Numbers.9.13"><i>Num.</i> 9:13</a>).</i>? From Modiin and farther away, and in the same distance in every direction, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Eliezer says, from the doorstep<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Accadic <i>askuppu.</i></i> of the Temple courtyard and farther away.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Eliezer said this only not to make him liable for extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">But he will agree that anybody within commuting distance to the Temple is obligated by a positive commandment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:2" href="/Numbers.9.2"><i>Num.</i>9:2</a>) to offer the <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah implies this: “Rebbi Aqiba answered him, sprinkling is a counter example, for it is a commandment.” Is it a commandment to sprinkle<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The purification from the impurity of the dead is an obligation for anybody coming to the Sanctuary. If he is not going there, there is no obligation to be sprinkled on.</i>? Explain it that his seventh day happened to be the Fourteenth which fell on a Sabbath. If it had been a weekday, one would have sprinkled on him, then he would have come, slaughtered his <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This is an inexact statement. The person who was sprinkled with the water containing the ashes of the Red Cow on the 3<sup class="footnote-marker">rd</sup> and 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> days of his impurity and then immerses himself in a <i>miqweh</i> is able to enter the sanctuary and eat <i>sancta</i> after sundown (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:19" href="/Numbers.19.19"><i>Num.</i> 19:19</a>). This means that another person has to slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> for him; but he can then come and eat from the sacrifice in the night. Rav Hoshaia holds that one may not slaughter for a person who could not slaughter by himself; therefore he has to transfer the situation to the 13th.</i>, and entered and eaten it in the evening. Because it is a Sabbath and one does not sprinkle on him, it results that he his prevented from fulfilling the commandment. Rav Hoshaia said, explain it that his seventh day happened to be the Thirteenth which fell on a Sabbath. If it had been a weekday, one would have sprinkled on him, then the next day he would have come, slaughtered his <i>Pesaḥ</i>, and entered and eaten it in the evening. Because it is a Sabbath and one does not sprinkle on him, it results that he is prevented from fulfilling the commandment. It was found stated<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>baraita</i> (not quoted) supports R. Eleazar against Rav Hoshaia.</i> following the first [explanation].
Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, explain it following him who said, from the doorstep of Jerusalem and outside. Would one object from outside Jerusalem to something inside Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann has noted that this statement refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 8:2" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).8.2">Tosephta 8:2</a> where R. Eliezer defines “far away” as “outside the place where [the <i>Pesaḥ</i>] is consumed” and argues by comparing <i>Pesaḥ</i> to Second Tithe. Both definitions point to Jerusalem, and not the Temple courtyard, as R. Eliezer’s “not far away”; else one would compare “outside of Jerusalem” with “inside Jerusalem (but outside the Temple enclosure).” The version of the Tosephta is supported against the version of the Mishnah.</i>?
Is there anybody who says to his teacher, it is the other way around? Since Rebbi Eliezer was teaching him practice currently that the festival offering does not push aside the Sabbath and he objected to him at the time of argument, therefore he told him, it is the other way around<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to their discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>. S. Liebermann points out that the use of כָּפַר in the meaning of “contradict” is characteristic of <i>Sifry zuṭa,</i> and the remark refers to the formulation of the Mishnaic discussion in <i>Sifry zuṭa Behaˋalotekha</i> 9(2).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">These paragraphs also are <i>Šabbat</i> 19 (17a line 16), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:1:19-3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.1.19-3.2">Notes 66–76</a>. The origin of the first paragraph is here, the second (of which part of the introduction and a sentence are missing here) is from there.</i> It was stated: “Rebbi Eliezer says, just as slaughter pushes the Sabbath aside so the preparations of slaughter push the Sabbath aside. Rebbi Aqiba said to him, no. If you speak about slaughter which is impossible to be done on Friday and pushes the Sabbath aside, what can you infer about preparations of slaughter than can be done on Friday, should they push the Sabbath aside? Rebbi Eliezer said to him, the public parts shall prove which he could do after the Sabbath and which nevertheless push aside the Sabbath. What is the difference between preparations of slaughter before the slaughter and preparations of slaughter after slaughter? Rebbi Aqiba said to him, about preparations of slaughter after slaughter which push aside the Sabbath when the slaughter already had pushed away the Sabbath, what does this imply for preparations of slaughter before slaughter when the slaughter had not yet pushed away the Sabbath? Another explanation: Maybe the sacrifice would be found disqualified, then it turned out that the Sabbath was pushed aside without a slaughter.”
What can you say [about a baby]? Maybe the baby would become sick; then the Sabbath would be pushed aside without circumcision. They objected: If the altar collapsed on the Sabbath, it will not be possible to build on the Sabbath!<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Added from K: Some kind of altar can be built the day before.</i> Think of it, if it developed a wart? It cannot be cut on the Sabbath! Warts can be cut on the day before. Think of it, if his Seventh day falls on a Sabbath, there cannot be sprinkling on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The purification from the impurity of the dead is an obligation for anybody coming to the Sanctuary. If he is not going there, there is no obligation to be sprinkled on.</i>! Sprinkling may be done on the day before.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> When does one bring a festival offering<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since every group had only one <i>Pesaḥ</i>, if the number of participants was large so that only a small portion of the meat was available for each participant, the main dish as the meal was another sacrifice which was given under the rules of festival sacrifices even though it could not be counted as satisfying the biblical requirement of such an offering during the holiday itself.</i> with it? When it comes on a weekday, in purity, and in small portions<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If only small portions of <i>Pesaḥ</i> meat were available for each participant, not enough to satisfy. Then the festival offering had to be consumed first.</i>. When it comes on the Sabbath, in large portions, or in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">If most of the public is impure, only public sacrifices may be offered in the Temple. The <i>Pesaḥ</i> has the status of a public sacrifice in this respect, but the accompanying festival offering does not.</i>, one does not bring a festival offering with it.<br>The festival offering came from small cattle or bovines, from sheep or goats, from males and females<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Whereas the <i>Pesaḥ</i> must be a male goat or sheep.</i>, and may be eaten during two days and one night<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">In this case really 2 times 24 hrs.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: The festival offering of the Fourteenth could come from tithe. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Samuel bar Abba: This means that it is voluntary. If you would say that it is obligatory, can anything which is obligatory come from tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Animal tithe (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:32" href="/Leviticus.27.32"><i>Lev.</i> 27:32</a>) automatically is a <i>sanctum</i> which must be sacrificed and eaten by the family; therefore it is no longer at the disposal of the owner to use it to fulfill any obligation. If it is acceptable for use with the <i>Pesaḥ</i>, this cannot represent fulfilling an obligation. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 7b-8a" href="/Chagigah.7b-8a">Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i> 7b–8a</a>.</i>?
It was stated: The festival offering which comes with the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is eaten first so that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> be eaten when one is satiated<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 5:3" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).5.3">Tosephta 5:3</a>.</i>. Why should the <i>Pesaḥ</i> not be eaten so one be satiated? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Dosay: Lest one come to break a bone<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 70a" href="/Pesachim.70a">Babli 70a</a>. <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bo</i> 6 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 20).</i>.
It was stated: The festival offering which comes with the <i>Pesaḥ</i> has to be eliminated with it. Come and see, a festival offering may be eaten for two days but the <i>Pesaḥ</i> only until midnight, and you are saying so? If it comes to the table with it<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since remainders of the Pesah have to be burned (Ex. 12:10), any dish which came into contact with the Pesah has to be burned with it since it may be contaminated with particles of the Pesah. The Babli 70a disagrees and holds that it must be eliminated (as a biblical requirement) only in the opinion of Ben Tema.</i>. Dishes who come to the table with it have to be eliminated with it<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 5:3" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).5.3">Tosephta 5:3</a>, opinion of R. Simeon ben Elazar.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that he who ate soft cheese and wants to eat meat must eliminate the bread slices<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Accepted by <i>Šulḥan Arukh Yoreh Deˋa</i>89(4).</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who slaughters the <i>Pesaḥ</i> not for its purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">As well-being sacrifice.</i> on the Sabbath is for this liable for a purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">If he slaughtered in error, i. e., either if he did not know that slaughtering not for its purpose is forbidden or if he did not realize that it was a Sabbath.</i>. For any other sacrifice which he slaughtered for the purpose of <i>Pesaḥ</i>, if they are not suitable<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If they are not male sheep or goat yearlings.</i> he is liable, if suitable, Rebbi Eliezer declares him liable for a purification sacrifice but Rebbi Joshua declares not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">For him a valid sacrifice even if wrongly presented cannot trigger liability.</i>.<br>Rebbi Eliezer said to him, since for <i>Pesaḥ</i>, which is permitted for its purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">If the 14th is a Sabbath.</i>, he is liable if he changed its purpose, sacrifices which are forbidden for their purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Festival offerings or general well-being offerings on a Sabbath.</i>, it should be logical that he be liable if he changed their purpose. Rebbi Joshua answered him, no. If you argue about <i>Pesaḥ,</i> which he changed to something forbidden, what can you infer about sacrifices which he changed to something permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since anything called <i>Pesaḥ</i> may be slaughtered on a 14th of Nisan which is a Sabbath.</i>? Rebbi Eliezer said to him, public parts<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The altar’s parts of those public sacrifices of which the rest is consumed by the priests, such as holiday purification sacrifices and the public well-being sacrifice of Pentecost.</i> should prove it, which are permitted for their purpose and he who slaughters for their purpose is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">If he erroneously slaughters any other sacrifice for the purpose of public sacrifice.</i>. Rebbi Joshua answered him, no. If you argue about public parts which are fixed in number, what can you infer about <i>Pesaḥ</i> which is not fixed in number? Rebbi Meïr says, also he who slaughters for the purpose of public parts is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since he slaughtered with the intent of fulfilling a commandment, there can be no liability, and R. Eliezer’s premise is incorrect.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah if he realized that it was <i>Pesaḥ</i> but he slaughtered it for the purpose of well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">If he knew that it was <i>Pesaḥ</i> and intentionally changed the purpose, there is liability if he erred in thinking that this was permitted, since his intent was executed. But if it was <i>Pesaḥ</i>, only the slaughterer thought that it was a well-being offering and he slaughtered it as well-being offering, it is valid <i>Pesaḥ</i> since an intent based on a false premise has no legal consequence. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 72a" href="/Pesachim.72a">Babli 72a</a>.</i>. If he knew that it was a well-being offering but he slaughtered it for the purpose of an elevation offering. Rebbi Mana said, there is fulfilling of a commandment in his action. Rebbi Yose said, there is no fulfilling of a commandment in his action<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This is a continuation of the preceding argument. The animal was dedicated as <i>Pesaḥ,</i> the slaughterer thought that it was a well-being offering and he slaughtered it as elevation offering. R. Mana holds that the slaughterer’s intent is ineffective since it is based on a wrong premise; the sacrifice is qualified as <i>Pesaḥ.</i> R. Yose holds that in all cases the slaughterer’s intent determines qualification.</i>. Rams of the public which he thought were sheep and he slaughtered them for the purpose of rams, are they perhaps not counted as rams for the public<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Rams are supplementary elevation sacrifices on holidays, as are the sheep mentioned here. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 49a" href="/Menachot.49a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i>49a</a>.</i>? It was stated thus: Rams of the public which he thought were sheep and he slaughtered them for the purpose of rams, already are counted for the public as fulfillment of their obligation. The Mishnah applies if he thought it was <i>Pesaḥ</i> and he slaughtered it for the purpose of <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The second case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.5.1">Mishnah 8</a>, “for any other sacrifice which he slaughtered for the purpose of <i>Pesaḥ</i>, …”, if an animal was dedicated as well-being offering but is of the kind qualified for <i>Pesaḥ,</i> and was slaughtered as <i>Pesaḥ,</i> R. Joshua declares him not liable since the sacrifice is qualified and he erred in the fulfillment of a commandment.</i>. If he knew that it was a well-being sacrifice but thought to say that it is permitted to change well-being sacrifice into <i>Pesaḥ,</i> Rebbi Mana said, there is no fulfilling of a commandment in his action; Rebbi Yose said, there is fulfilling of a commandment in his action<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The difference here is the interpretation of R. Joshua’s stance. R. Mana holds that since he knew that the animal was not a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> it cannot be called error in the fulfillment of a commandment and even R. Joshua will agree that he is liable. R. Yose holds that since the sacrifice is qualified it must be called error in the fulfillment of a commandment and R. Joshua will hold that he is not liable.</i>. It is reasonable following Rebbi Mana in the first case, following Rebbi Yose in the last.
<b><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:5:3-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.5.3-6">Halakhah 7</a></b><sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.5.1">Mishnah 9</a>.</i>: They said, that argument of Rebbi Eliezer is no answer to Rebbi Joshua, since he could say to him, how can you answer me with an argument about something that is apt to change for something that is not apt to being changed<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Pesaḥ</i> after the 14th is automatically a well-being offering; but animals dedicated as well-being offerings cannot validly be changed into anything else.</i>? Neither is the argument of Rebbi Joshua an answer to Rebbi Eliezer, since he could say to him, the <i>Pesaḥ</i> of Reuben which he slaughtered in the name of Simeon is changed into something qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:3</a>.</i>; and you are saying, he is liable?
“Rebbi Meïr says, also he who slaughters for the purpose of public parts is not liable.” Rebbi Eleazar said, the words of Rebbi Meïr: even a calf<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">No public sacrifice is a calf. According to him, R. Meïr in interpreting R. Joshua’s position that no action resulting in a qualified sacrifice can trigger liability.</i>. You understand from here two things. You understand something which has no fixed number, or that is apt to being changed, only if the act is meritorious<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Even if there are two detriments as enumerated in the Mishnah, as long as there is an angle of merit there is no liability.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if the act is meritorious, for example his sister-in-law which is menstruating and he slept with her<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">If the sister-in-law is a childless widow the brother-in-law is biblically obligated to sleep with her (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:5" href="/Deuteronomy.25.5"><i>Deut.</i> 25:5</a>). Incidentally by this act she becomes his wife, but this is not the point here. Since his act is in obeying a biblical commandment it cannot be sanctioned; even though he was negligent in not asking her whether sex with her was permissible; in the interpretation of R. Joshua’s position given here, the purification offering due for erroneously sleeping with a menstruating woman is neither due nor possible in this case.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if the act is [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">An addition by the corrector which seems to be erroneous.</i> meritorious, for example two spits, one with slaughtered meat, the other with carcass meat, and he intended to eat from one but ate from the other<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence seems to be corrupt. Eating non-kosher meat is a simple violation, not a deadly sin whose commission in error would require a purification sacrifice. One might try to amend the text; which has been done in very many different ways, none of which is convincing. If one assumes that the uncorrected text is genuine, one has to assume, in the general spirit of the discussion, that the kosher meat is <i>sanctum,</i> whose consumption is meritorious. R. Joḥanan asserts that if the intended act was meritorious, the actual action, while a sin, cannot be sinful.</i>. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Šabbat</i> 19:4, about two babies to circumcise, one on the Sabbath and one on Sunday.</i>: “If he forgot and circumcised the one for Sunday on the Sabbath, Rebbi Eliezer makes him liable for a purification sacrifice but Rebbi Joshua declares him not liable.” In this case the act is not meritorious and Rebbi Joshua declares not liable. Samuel from Cappadocia said, the next day it is meritorious<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Since on Sunday the baby is lawfully circumcised, the Mishnah cannot be used to show that R. Joshua declares not liable even if no commandment was obeyed in the action.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The text from here to the end of the Halakhah is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 19:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.19.4.2-3"><i>Šabbat</i> 19, Notes 110–141</a>. The connection with the topics here seems to be the mention of <i>mazzah</i> in the last two sentences.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr said, for something where there is a commandment performed one is not liable, but if no commandment is performed whether he is liable is disputed. Rebbi Simeon says, where there is a commandment performed one is liable, but if no commandment is performed whether he is not liable is disputed. Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Joḥanan: The words of Rebbi Meïr are, on condition that it be a sheep without blemish, a yearling, and a well-being sacrifice fit to be changed into a Passover sacrifice. One understands from this three consequences. One understands something which is not a fixed number. And which is not usually exchanged. And doing something which fulfills a commandment. What means “something which is not a fixed number”? Rebbi Jeremiah wanted to say that the Torah did not specify how many <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifices should push the Sabbath aside in any given year. Rebbi Yose wanted to say, where you cannot determine the amount. When Rebbi Yose came to these “children in doubt” he used to say, Rebbi Jeremiah taught us correctly. Could you say that you cannot determine the amount? But the Torah did not specify how many <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifices should push the Sabbath aside in any given year. Rav Ḥisda said, one may explain the words of Rebbi Simeon if a group was there which did not slaughter. Rebbi Zeˋira said, the word of Rebbi Yannai implies that he forgot and circumcised the one for the Sabbath on Friday. It he circumcised him in the morning, Rebbi Zeˋira said that Rebbi Yannai was of the opinion that he is not liable. Rebbi Abba said, he is liable. In relation to what did Rebbi Yannai say it? He comes to tell you in which cases can there be a switching of children. In Rebbi Zeˋira’s opinion following Rebbi Yannai Rav followed Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose, what Rav Ḥisda said follows Rebbi Simeon, and what Rebbi Yannai said follows Rebbi Meïr. Can one object anything between Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Simeon? Do we find a difference between Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Simeon about the remainder? How do you treat the babies in doubt? Like something which is a fixed amount or something which is not a fixed amount? If you are treating it like something which is a fixed amount, even if there is no (one) [other] baby to circumcise. If you are treating it like something which is not a fixed amount, only if there remains (one) [another] baby to circumcise. Come and see: If he anticipated his time he is not liable, if he delayed his time he is liable? Rav Ḥuna said, the Mishnah is the other way around, as it was stated: Rebbi Simeon said, Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua did not disagree about one which was to be circumcised after the Sabbath if he circumcised him on the Sabbath that he is liable, but one who was to be circumcised before the Sabbath if he circumcised him on the Sabbath where Rebbi Eliezer makes him liable for a purification sacrifice and Rebbi Joshua declares him not liable. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, because of this objection it was difficult for Rebbi Yannai, only if he forgot and circumcised the one of the Sabbath on Friday. Rebbi Ada bar Ahawah said, these are the words of Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Simeon, but the words of Rebbi Yose [are]: Even if no commandment is performed he is not liable since he erred on behalf of a commandment.
If he finished he can return only for fibers which would invalidate the circumcision. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the words of Rebbi Yose, even if he finished he may even return for fibers which do not invalidate the circumcision. Which [statement of] Rebbi Yose? That which we stated, “Rebbi Yose says, if the first day of Tabernacles falls on a Sabbath, if he forgot and took the <i>lulav</i> out into the public domain he is not liable because he took it out with permission.” Does the same hold for a knife for circumcision, the same for unleavened bread? Since Rebbi Joḥanan said, the words of Rebbi Yose, even if he finished he may even return for fibers which do not invalidate the circumcision; this implies the same even for a knife for circumcision, the same for unleavened bread.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He is liable if he slaughtered it<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">For a <i>Pesaḥ</i> on the Sabbath he is liable for a purification sacrifice if acting in error.</i> not for its eaters, or not for its subscribers, for the uncircumcised, or for the impure<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>Pesaḥ</i> is disqualified if nobody is able to eat it.</i>. He is not liable if for its eaters and not for its eaters, for its subscribers and not for its subscribers, for the circumcised and the uncircumcised, for the pure and the impure<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:3</a>.</i>. If he slaughtered and it was found defective, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> may not be defective (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:5" href="/Exodus.12.5"><i>Ex.</i> 12:5</a>), it is necessary to inspect the animal before slaughter.</i>. If he slaughtered and it was found internally tom, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since this cannot be determined beforehand, no guilt can be incurred. The last two statements are equally valid for the Sabbath sacrifices during the year.</i>. He is not liable if he slaughtered and it became known that the owners became disinterested in it, or they died, or they became impure, because he slaughtered with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua’s opinion in the preceding Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said, for whom is this<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">This discusses the statement in the Mishnah that he who slaughters a defective <i>Pesaḥ</i> on a Sabbath is liable for a purification sacrifice.</i> needed? For Rebbi Meïr. Does Rebbi Eleazar parallel Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? Just as Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish requires that his action be meritorious, does Rebbi Eleazar require that his action be meritorious<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since a defective animal never is acceptable as a sacrifice, it cannot be dedicated as a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> nor can it ever be used for any purpose in the Temple.</i>? If you are saying that there is a difference, since this one has a defect, and a defective animal is not apt to be changed<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The previous argument, while probably true, is not proven, since R. Meïr also requires that the sacrifice be apt to be changed into one of the correct type, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.5.4">Note 122</a>.</i>, also a calf is not apt to be changed, and Rebbi Eleazar said, the words of Rebbi Meïr: even a calf<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">No public sacrifice is a calf. According to him, R. Meïr in interpreting R. Joshua’s position that no action resulting in a qualified sacrifice can trigger liability.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, if Rebbi Eleazar were of Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion, it would be understandable, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, a defective animals is not apt to be changed, but a calf is apt to be changed, because (it was a problem for Hillel) [it is needed for the slain]<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The correct text is the sribe’s one in parentheses, confirmed by K. Since Hillel raised the question why a <i>Pesaḥ</i> cannot be a calf when there is a verse seemingly endorsing its use (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.1.9">Note 25</a>), is using a calf as <i>Pesaḥ</i> not an excusable error? The text in brackets is the corrector’s, it makes little sense. (Maybe the corrector did not understand the scribe’s text, reading הַלֵּל instead of הִלֵּל). A calf is used in the rite of atonement of an unsolved murder, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.1-9"><i>Deut.</i> 22:1–9</a>, but this is not a Temple ceremony.</i>. Therefore {if it was torn} in the open, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the next clause in the Mishnah, if there was a defect in an internal organ, the sacrifice is disqualified but the slaughterer is not liable. (Not only is he not liable, but he did not commit any sin.) But if the defect was visible, it is a case of the previous clause; there is liability.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, it<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The statement in the Mishnah, when the <i>Pesaḥ</i> was slaughtered even though the owners decided to belong to a different group. In the preceding text from <i>Šabbat</i>19, R. Simeon is more restrictive than R. Meïr; therefore if the Mishnah is compatible with R. Simeon’s position, it certainly is with R. Meïr’s.</i> is Rebbi Simeon’s. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, it is Rebbi Simeon’s. Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina: If somebody became disinterested in his <i>Pesaḥ,</i> its body is brought as well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">. Since a <i>Pesaḥ</i> whose time has passed automatically is dedicated as well-being offering, the case here must be that it was slaughtered on the 14th of Nisan. If it is not <i>Pesaḥ,</i> it must be a well-being offering. The question is whether it is qualified and must be eaten, or is disqualified and may not be eaten. If it is disqualified the next question is whether it is disqualified <i>Pesaḥ</i> and must be burned immediately outside the Temple district or is qualified but unused and as well-being sacrifice has to wait until the third day, when it biblically becomes forbidden and must be burned there (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.4.1">Mishnaiot 7:6–7</a>).</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, qualified well-being offering. Rebbi Yose said, disqualified well-being offering. But did we not state, “not liable”<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">“Not liable” implies that a sinful act was committed, only it is not criminally prosecutable.</i>? They wanted to say, not liable and qualified. They explain it, not liable and disqualified. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, the only <i>Pesaḥ</i> which itself is brought as well-being offering is what was lost and found after the owner had fulfilled his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 97a" href="/Pesachim.97a">Babli 97a</a>.</i>. But did we not state, “not liable”? They wanted to say, not liable and qualified. They explain it, not liable and disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the Mishnah, the <i>Pesaḥ</i> which is left without eaters.</i>. Samuel said, in any case where they said that a purification sacrifice is allowed to die<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 4:1–2: The offspring of an animal dedicated as purification offering, the (illegal) replacement of a purification offering, a purification offering whose owner had died, a purification offering which had been lost and was found after the owner had used a replacement, and one who became too old.</i>, a <i>Pesaḥ</i> will be brought as well-being sacrifice. But did we not state, “not liable”? They wanted to say, not liable and disqualified. They explain it, not liable and qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 97a" href="/Pesachim.97a">Babli 97a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, it<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">The last sentence in the Mishnah, that if the owner(s) lost interest, died, or became impure, the slaughterer on the Sabbath is not liable. A difficulty is created by idiomatic rabbinic Hebrew, where “owner” is בְּעָלִים with the verb in the plural whether there is one owner or there are many owners.</i> is Rebbi Jehudah’s, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.7.1">Mishnah 8:7</a>. R. Jehudah may read the Mishnah here that if a <i>Pesaḥ</i> was slaughtered for a number of subscribers, and all of them except one either lost interest, or died, or became impure, the sacrifice still is valid even though it would not have been permitted to be slaughtered if the facts had been known beforehand.</i>: “One does not slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> for a single individual, the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but Rebbi Yose permits.” Rebbi Zeˋira asked before Rebbi Mana: Where do we find “not liable and qualified”? He told him, here we stated “not liable”, and there we stated “qualified”<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.7.1">Mishnah 8:7</a> R. Jehudah only says “one does not slaughter;” he does not state that if one slaughtered for a single eater the <i>Pesaḥ</i> was disqualified. Therefore it is qualified even for R. Jehudah.</i>. But about all of them did Rebbi Ismael the son of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa say, it should lose its shape and be brought to be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 73b" href="/Pesachim.73b">Babli 73b</a>. Since the disqualification is extrinsic, the sacrifice cannot be burned immediately; one has to wait until it must be burned even if it is treated as well-being offering.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose asked before Rebbi Yose, one understands if they died or became impure. If the owners became disinterested<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">In the first two cases, it is an accident and one understands that the sacrifice cannot be burned immediately. But if the owner became disinterested, the animal may not be slaughtered. If it was slaughtered anyhow, the action is illegitimate and the carcass should be burned immediately.</i>? If they are alive, does disqualification qualify? It is disqualified. If after it was slaughtered, can one become disinterested after slaughter? Since he has the right to become disinterested, the disqualification qualifies<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">K: “It must be R. Simeon’s, since R. Simeon said, one may become disinterested after slaughtering” (up to the time of pouring the blood, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.3.1">Mishnah 8:3</a>) is suspect as <i>lectio facilior.</i> In keeping with the earlier statements, one may read the scribe’s text as implying that, since before slaughter one may subscribe or cancel the subscription at will, cancelling after slaughter is an excusable error, not intrinsic, and has to follow the general rules of sacrifices becoming disqualified by extrinsic factors.</i>, and it needs {losing its} shape.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does one roast the <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:9" href="/Exodus.12.9"><i>Ex.</i> 12:9</a> requires: <i>Do not eat from it raw, or cooked in water, but roasted in fire, its head with its hooves and its innards.</i></i>? A spit of pomegranate wood<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Hardwood which will not ooze sap into the meat. The sap would cook the meat near the spit; and the meat may not be eaten as <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i> he sticks from its mouth to its buttocks and puts the hooves and its innards into it, the words of Rebbi Yose the Galilean. Rebbi Aqiba says, this is a kind of cooking<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since the innards will be heated not by the fire but by hot moist meat.</i>; but one hangs them outside.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How does one roast the <i>Pesaḥ”</i> etc. Why of pomegranates? Rebbi Ḥiyyas bar Abba said, all wood oozes fluid, but of pomegranates does not ooze fluid. Where do we hold? If fresh, even pomegranate {[wood} oozes. If dry, all other wood does not ooze. But it is so: All other wood if it is dry outside still is moist inside. Pomegranate {wood} if dry outside is dry inside.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 5:8" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).5.8">Tosephta 5:8</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 74a" href="/Pesachim.74a">Babli 74a</a>.</i> in the name of Rebbi Jehudah: He roasts it on a metal spit. They said to him, is it not hot and heats<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Then the inner parts of the lamb are cooked by the heat of the spit, not roasted by the fire outside; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:2:2-11:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.2.2-11.4">Halakhah 2</a>.</i>? He said to them, just as the wooden one is not burned, so the metal one does not heat. They said to him, wood is not comparable to metal, for wood, if it is hot somewhere, is not hot everywhere, but metal if hot somewhere is hot everywhere.
There are Tannaim who stated, he sticks from its buttocks to its mouth. In the opinion of this <i>baraita</i> teacher he turns it around<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The argument implies that the lamb was roasted on a vertical spit, like shawarma. According to the Tanna of the Mishnah, the pointed end of the stick stands out at the top; the lamb, whose meat has not been koshered, hangs down and the blood can flow out through the cut in the neck; koshering is not needed. But in the scenario of the <i>baraita,</i> the spit has to be turned around, with the pointed end at the bottom.</i>.
What? Does Rebbi Yose the Galilean consider it as thick hips<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Is his argument a practical one? Since it is assumed that the inner parts also will be roasted by the fire burning under the lamb, there is no reason that the innards, if put inside, will not be roasted.</i>, or does he explain the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:9" href="/Deuteronomy.12.9"><i>Deut.</i> 12:9</a>. As explained in the next paragraph, he reads “on” to mean in direct bodily contact; and this is guaranteed at all times only if the hooves and innards are tucked inside the body.</i>, <i>its head, on its hooves, and on its innards.</i> What is the difference between them? If he roasted it like a profane lamb<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Innards and hooves separate from the body.</i>. If you are saying that Rebbi Yose the Galilean considers it as thick hips, it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read “qualified”. This is the reading of the quote in <i>Or Zaruaˋ</i> II §229. This author is usually trustworthy in his readings.</i>; if you are saying that he explains the verse, <i>its head, on its hooves, and on its innards,</i> it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Read: disqualified, confirmed by <i>Or zaruaˋ.</i> Clearly, if the innards hang in the fire separate from the body, they will be roasted but the verse is violated.</i>. If he cut some meat from it and roasted it inside, if you are saying that Rebbi Yose the Galilean considers it as thick hips, it is qualified; if you are saying that he explains the verse, <i>its head, on its hooves, and on its innards,</i> it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case the meat will be roasted, therefore being qualified as <i>Pesaḥ;</i> but the verse does not permit to roast anything inside the body except innards and hooves.</i>. If he roasted it in the steam of a pot, if you are saying that Rebbi Yose the Galilean considers it as thick hips, it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Read: qualified (<i>Or zaruaˋ</i>)<i>.</i></i>; if you are saying that he explains the verse, <i>its head, on its hooves, and on its innards,</i> it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Read: disqualified (<i>Or zaruaˋ</i>)<i>.</i></i>. But did one not state, <i>roasted in fire,</i> not roasted in a pot. [Explain it,] if it touched the body of the pot. This<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Not the preceding inconclusive discussion but the following discussion of the verse.</i> implies that he explained the verse.
Everybody agrees that “on it” means “in contact”. Where do they disagree? About “on”. <sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 11:5" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.11.5">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 11:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 97a" href="/Menachot.97a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 97a</a>, about the placement of incense on the table with the shew-bread. The verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:7" href="/Leviticus.24.7"><i>Lev.</i> 24:7</a>): <i>Put pure incense on the array</i>(מַעֲרֶכֶת not מַעֲרָכָה). Abba Shaul says nearby, the rabbis say on top.</i> Abba Shaul says, <i>on the array,</i> near the array. But the rabbis say, <i>on the array,</i> on top of the array. It turns out that Abba Shaul parallels Rebbi Aqiba, and the rabbis Rebbi Yose the Galilean<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Aqiba requires the innards to bu hung outside, i. e. nearby.</i>. Rebbi says, even if he made them two arrangements of fourteen, one considers the upper one as nonexistent and the lower ones are qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">There are twelve shew-breads. If one made two too many, 7 to a row instead of 6, the additional two can be disregarded and the incense put on top, since we do not read “on” to imply touching.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi and Abba Shaul use in the method of Rebbi Meïr for the table<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 11:5" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.11.5">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 11:5</a> The table in the Tabernacle was 2 cubits long. According to R. Jehudah, the cubit was 5 hand-breadths, according to R. Meïr 6 hand-breadths. Six shew-breads in one row took up 10 handbreadths. This leaves 2 hand-breadths space where according to Abba Shaul the incense was placed, and according to Rebbi another bread could be placed.<br>The argument shows that the text of the Leiden ms. is correct, attributing the statement about the shew-bread to Rebbi, against the reading of K which attributes it to R. Yose the Galilean.</i>.
Inside-out, the words of Rebbi Tarphon. Rebbi Ismael says, helmeted<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi’s explanation, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 74a" href="/Pesachim.74a">Babli 74a</a>.</i>. It turns out that Rebbi Tarphon parallels Rebbi Aqiba, and Rebbi Ismael Rebbi Yose the Galilean; as we have stated: Rebbi Yose the Galilean said, <sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta <i>Yom Ṭov</i> 2:15</a>. The same text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.7.2-3"><i>Beṣah</i> 2:7</a> (61c line 62); cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 74a" href="/Pesachim.74a">Babli 74a</a>.</i>“what is a helmeted kid-goat? Entirely roasted, <i>its head, on its hooves, and on its innards.</i> If he parboiled part of it, or cooked part of it, this is not a helmeted kid-goat. One serves helmeted kid-goat in the evening of the first day of Tabernacles, and on the last day of Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Tosephta’s and the corrector’s text in <i>Beṣah</i>; one Tosephta ms. and K have “on the first day of Passover”. Both texts are acceptable; the roasted kid-goat is forbidden only during the first night. It is remarkable that Pentecost is not mentioned in this context; this may indicate an early form of the usage of German Jews to eat dairy on the first day of Pentecost.</i>. One serves helmeted calf on the first night of Passover but not helmeted kid-goat<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Roast meat is acceptable as long as it is not sheep or goat.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the <i>Beṣah</i> text (<b>צ</b>), this paragraph also is in <i>Moˋed qaṭan</i> 3:1 (81d line 27,<b>ק</b>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 53a" href="/Pesachim.53a">Babli 53a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 19a" href="/Berakhot.19a"><i>Berakhot</i> 19a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 23a" href="/Beitzah.23a"><i>Beṣah</i>23a</a>.</i> It was stated: Rebbi Yose said, Theudas of Rome led the people of Rome to eat helmeted kid-goat in the Passover nights, the Sages sent and said to him, if you were not Theudas, would we not put you in the ban? Who was Theudas? Rebbi Ḥananiah said, because he was providing for the rabbis. Are you not causing the public to eat <i>sancta</i> outside the Temple? And anybody causing the public to eat <i>sancta</i> outside the Temple has to be put in the ban.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: This<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The story of Theudas.</i> implies that a person is forbidden to say to another, here you have money and use it to buy meat for <i>Pesaḥ</i> for yourself<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 53a" href="/Pesachim.53a">Babli 53a</a>.</i>. But he may say to him, here you have money and buy me meat to roast.
Rebbi Immi asked, if one cleaned out the <i>tannur</i> and roasted it in it<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>tannur</i> is a truncated clay cone with an open top and some vents on the bottom, used to generate more heat by creating a draft and keeping the heat together in its walls. The question is whether by hanging the lamb into a <i>tannur</i> one roasts it in fire, as required by the verse, or one roasts it in the <i>tannur,</i> which would be forbidden just as using a metal spit is forbidden (see also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:2:2-11:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.2.2-11.4">Halakhah 2</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 95a" href="/Pesachim.95a">Babli 95a</a>.)</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, his problem is following Rebbi Yose the Galilean, but it is obvious for him following Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since for R. Yose the Galilean the innards are roasted indirectly, inside the lamb, it is possible that he accept the use of a <i>tannur</i> while for R. Aqiba it should be clear that the roast must be in the open air.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, even following Rebbi Aqiba it is problematic for him. From the coals into the air of the <i>tannur</i> it is roasted in half an hour. From the coals into free air it is roasted in one hour. What is the difference between being roasted half because of the <i>tannur</i> or being roasted entirely because of the <i>tannur</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the <i>tannur</i> does nothing for the coals, it only keeps the heat together<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">One has to say that since in open air one may increase the heat by using bellows to increase the flames, the bottom vents of the <i>tannur</i> do not make the roasting a funxtion of the <i>tannur. Tosaphot</i> 95a s.v. וברפו.</i>. If the coals are inside the <i>tannur</i> it is roasted in half an hour; if the coals are in free air it is roasted in one hour.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One roasts the <i>Pesaḥ</i> neither on a spit<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">A metal spit.</i> nor on a grill<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐσχάρα, ἡ.</i>. Rebbi Ṣadoq said, it happened that Rabban Gamliel said to his slave Tabi, go and roast us the <i>Pesaḥ</i> on a sacrificial hearth<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that anything heated by fire is as good as fire itself. Since the metal gets all its heat from the fire, it is permitted. This Rabban Gamliel must be Gamliel I, preceding R. Jehudah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.1.3">Note 4</a>) by three generations, showing that Rebbi Jehudah is a reliable historical source.</i>. If it touched the pottery of the <i>tannur,</i> he has to shave off its place<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since that place on the surface of the meat was cooked by the wall of the <i>tannur,</i> not roasted by the air heated by the fire.</i>. If some of its fluid fell on clay and returned, one has to remove this place<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The the fluid was heated by the clay walls; falling back onto the meat it penetrated into it and therefore surface shaving is insufficient.</i>. If some of its fluid fell on flour, one has to remove its place<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Since the entire lamb has to be consumed <i>roasted in fire,</i> the fluid in the flour may not be consumed; it has to be removed and burned separately.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <i>Fire roasted,</i> not spit roasted, nor pot roasted, nor grill roasted, nor metal roasted, nor roasted in any other way<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bo</i> 6 (p. 19); cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 74a" href="/Pesachim.74a">Babli 74a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 76a" href="/Pesachim.76a">76a</a>. The corrector’s addition is only found in the Babli.</i>.
“<i>A fire burn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:24" href="/Leviticus.13.24"><i>Lev.</i> 13:24</a>, in the rules of skin disease.</i><i>,</i> I could think if it stays moist<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This is Maimonides’s interpretation (<i>Negaˋim</i> 7:8), based on the reading in <i>Sifra</i> מורדת. The reading here, מורדת could be interpreted, parallel to Arabic استمرّ “to stay unchanged”, that the wound does not heal.</i>, the verse says, <i>if the burn was healed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:27" href="/Leviticus.13.27"><i>Lev.</i> 13:27</a>.</i><i>.</i> If the burn was healed, I could think until it becomes scar tissue, the verse says, <i>a fire burn.</i> How is that? It was partially healed; and so it says below, <i>it is a burn scar</i>;<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:28" href="/Leviticus.13.28"><i>Lev.</i> 13:28</a>.</i> until it forms a membrane in the thickness of a garlic peel.”<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Tazria Pereq</i> 7(3).</i> And here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Why for skin disease does one include anything which minimally corresponds to the description in the verses, but for <i>Pesaḥ</i> one excludes everything but strict adherence to the prescribed manner.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar says, there <i>fire roasted, only fire roasted</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:8" href="/Exodus.12.8"><i>Ex.</i> 12:8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:9" href="/Exodus.12.9">9</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 95a" href="/Pesachim.95a">Babli 95a</a>.</i><i>,</i> the verse repeated it to make it indispensable. But here, <i>if the burn was healed,</i> in any way. Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon said, there “law, teaching” makes it indispensable<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For the <i>Pesaḥ</i> “law” is written in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:43" href="/Exodus.12.43"><i>Ex.</i>12:43</a>, “teaching” in 12:49. Any commandment labelled “law” or “teaching” must be kept to the letter; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 19a" href="/Menachot.19a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 19a</a>. For skin disease, “teaching” is mentioned the first time for the purification rites (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:43" href="/Leviticus.14.43"><i>Lev.</i>14:43</a>).</i>. But here what do you have?
Does Rabban Gamliel not have <i>fire roasted</i>? He does. The <i>Pesaḥ</i> in Egypt was fire roasted; the <i>Pesaḥ</i> for generations is not fire roasted<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In the rules for the <i>Pesaḥ</i> to be observed in the future, at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12" href="/Exodus.12"><i>Ex.</i> 12</a> and the beginning of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9" href="/Numbers.9"><i>Num.</i> 9</a>, roasting in fire is not mentioned.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rabban Gamliel did not consider consequences of fire as fire; the rabbis consider consequences of fire as fire<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that the places of “did not consider” and “consider” have to be switched. Since the problematic text also is in K, it is not a scribal error.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 95a" href="/Pesachim.95a">Babli, 95a</a> top, explains the problem away.</i>. Rabban Gamliel disagrees with the rabbis and in practice acts on his own opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.30"><i>Berakhot</i> 1:2, Note 111</a>; also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.10.2"><i>Avodah zarah</i> 3:12, Note 252</a>.</i>.
If it had said “do not eat from it except fire roasted” and had not said “raw”, I would have said that if he singed it and roasted it would be permitted; therefore he needed to say, “raw”. Or if it had said “do not [eat from it] except fire roasted” and had not said “cooked”, I would have said that if he parboiled it and roasted it, it would be permitted; therefore he needed to say, “cooked”. Or if it had said “do not eat from it except fire roasted” and had not said “thoroughly cooked”, I would have said that if he cooked it and roasted it would be permitted. Therefore it was needed to say “raw”, and it was needed to say [“cooked”, and it was needed to say] “thoroughly cooked.”<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Mekhilta dR. IsmaelBo</i> 6, ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 21; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 41a" href="/Pesachim.41a">Babli 41a</a>. “Raw” is interpreted as “rare”. C”s addition is confirmed by K.</i>
If somebody ate the volume of an olive raw when it still was daylight<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">On the 14th of Nisan.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, he is liable, and there are Tannaim who state, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 41b" href="/Pesachim.41b">Babli 41b</a>.</i>. He who says that he is liable, <i>do not eat from it raw</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">It may not be eaten but because it is indirectly disqualified one has to wait until it would have to be burned even if qualified; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.6.4">Chapter 6, after Note 151</a>.</i><i>,</i> in any case. But he who says that he is not liable, at a time when he is obligated to eat fire-roasted he is under obligation not to eat raw; at a time when he is not obligated to eat fire-roasted he is not obligated not to eat raw.
If he broke a bone of it when it still was daylight. There are Tannaim who state, he is liable, and there are Tannaim who state, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 84b" href="/Pesachim.84b">Babli 84b</a>.</i>. He who says that he is liable, <i>do not break a bone in it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:46" href="/Exodus.12.46"><i>Ex.</i> 12:46</a>.</i> etc., in any case. But he who says that he is not liable, at a time when he is not obligated to eat fire-roasted there is no <i>do not break a bone in it</i>; at a time when he is obligated to eat fire-roasted there is <i>do not break a bone in it.</i>
“If it touched the pottery of the <i>tannur,”</i> it is a disability of its body and it has to be burned immediately. “If of its fluid fell on clay,” it is a disability on the instrument and needs [losing its] shape<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">It may not be eaten but because it is indirectly disqualified one has to wait until it would have to be burned even if qualified; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.6.4">Chapter 6, after Note 151</a>.</i>. “If of its fluid fell on flour, one has to remove its place.” Ḥiyya bar Ada in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: that is, if it is hot<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 75b-76a" href="/Pesachim.75b-76a">Babli 75b/76a</a>.</i>. But if it is cool, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to “liable”, “forbidden” means rabbinically forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar, the Torah did not say, it should not cook others, but it should not be cooked because of others<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees and notes that since there is no biblical prohibition if the hot fluid falls into cold edibles; there is no reason for a rabbinic prohibition either.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he basted it with heave oil, if there is a group of priests, they shall eat it. If of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For whom consumption of heave is a deadly sin.</i>, if it is raw he shall soak it, if roasted he has to peel off the outside. If he basted it with oil of Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Which is property of the farmer, to be eaten in purity at the place of the Temple. Second Tithe may be redeemed outside of Jerusalem, with the sanctity of the produce transferred to the money which has to be spent on pure food in Jerusalem. There is neither redemption nor sale of Second Tithe in Jerusalem.</i>, he may not charge the members of the group for it since one may not sell Second Tithe in Jerusalem.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> He only said “basted”. Therefore, if he immersed it, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Either this means that if the lamb absorbed too much of the oil, no Israel will be allowed to eat from it in any way. Or it may be that טִיבְּלוֹ stands for תִּיבְּלוֹ “spiced it”, when it will be forbidden to everybody since the spices cover the taste of the lamb’s meat.</i>. A person cooked a chicken using oil to be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">“Oil to be burned” is impure heave oil. While it may not be consumed, it may be used by a Cohen as fuel. Use as fuel by an Israel is stealing from the Cohanim. R. Bisna’s order has to be classified as imposing a fíne.</i>. He came and asked Rebbi Bisna, who told him, go and cook it in water for an extended period.
So is the Mishnah: “One does not redeem Second Tithe in Jerusalem.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Mishnah in the Babli.</i>”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Five kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The only impurity permitted in the Temple is the impurity of the dead, which can be removed only by a lengthy procedure and depends on the availability of the ashes of a red cow. All other impurities prevent a person from entering the sacred precinct. If most or all of the people are impure, the public service is conducted by the Cohanim without interruption. Most of the public sacrifices are elevation sacrifices which are totally burned on the altar; for them the question of consumption does not arise. The only time impure non-Cohanim are admitted to the sacred precinct is the 14th of Nisan, for the <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i> are brought in impurity but are not eaten in impurity: The ˋOmer<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The offering of barley flour from the new harvest, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:9-14" href="/Leviticus.23.9-14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:9–14</a>, of which a fistful is burned on the altar and the remainder eaten be the Cohanim. If offered in impurity everything has to be burned.</i>, and the Two Breads<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The two leavened breads of new wheat harvest presented on Pentecost, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:17" href="/Leviticus.23.17"><i>Lev.</i> 23:17</a>, intended to be eaten entirely by the Cohanim.</i>, and the shew-bread<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:9" href="/Leviticus.24.9"><i>Lev.</i> 24:9</a>.</i>, and public well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The two sheep accompanying the two breads, to be eaten by the Cohanim with the breads; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:19-20" href="/Leviticus.23.19-20"><i>Lev.</i> 23:19–20</a>.</i>, and the goats of New Moons<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The obligatory purification offerings, not only for New Moons but also all holidays, of which only a small part is burned on the altar and most of it eaten by the Cohanim in the Temple precinct; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:15" href="/Numbers.28.15"><i>Num.</i>28:15</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:22" href="/Numbers.28.22">22</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:30" href="/Numbers.28.30">30</a>; 29:5.11,16, 19,22,25,28,31,34, 38.</i>. <i>Pesaḥ</i> which is brought in impurity is eaten since from the start this is what it is for<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:4" href="/Exodus.12.4"><i>Ex.</i> 12:4</a>), <i>Everybody according to his eating you should slaughter the lamb.</i></i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Are not all public sacrifices brought in impurity? He only comes to tell you, even though they are brought in impurity they are not eaten in impurity. Both for him who says that the bread is the main item, as also for him who says that the sheep are the main item, there should be only four<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">A dispute between R. Aqiba and R. Simeon ben Nanas in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 4:3" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.4.3">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i>4:3</a>. The verse states that the lambs of the well-being sacrifice of Pentecost have to be brought <i>on the bread</i>, which R. Aqiba reads as meaning that the Two Breads are indispensable for bringing the lambs, but not the other way around, and R. Simeon ben Nanas holds the opposite opinion.</i>. The Tanna takes note of them and stated them as five.
There are Tannaim who state that all of them are inferred from the <i>Pesaḥ.</i> There are Tannaim who state that each of them is inferred from its place. He who said that all of them are inferred from the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> since <i>at its fixed time</i> which is said (here) [about <i>Pesaḥ</i> pushes impurity aside, also <i>at its fixed time</i> which is written about all of them] pushes impurity aside<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since it says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:4" href="/Leviticus.23.4"><i>Lev.</i>23:4</a>): <i>These are the</i> <i>times of the Eternal, holy convocations, which you have to proclaim at their fixed times.</i> Cf. Chapter 6, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.10.2-4">Halakhah 1</a>.</i>. He who said that each of them is inferred from its place, from where does he have it? It comes as it is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 77a" href="/Pesachim.77a">Babli 77a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 73a" href="/Menachot.73a"><i>Menaḥot</i>73a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 17 13" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_17.13"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 17(13</a>).</i>: “Rebbi says, why does the verse say, <i>Moses told the holidays of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:44" href="/Leviticus.23.44"><i>Lev.</i> 23:44</a>.</i><i>.</i> Since we learned only about <i>Pesaḥ</i> and the daily sacrifices that they push the Sabbath aside, since it is said about them <i>at its fixed time</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:2" href="/Numbers.9.2"><i>Num.</i> 9:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:2" href="/Numbers.28.2">28:2</a>.</i><i>,</i> from where the rest of public offerings? The verse says, <i>these you shall offer to the Eternal at your fixed times</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:39" href="/Numbers.29.39"><i>Num.</i> 29:39</a>.</i><i>.</i> For the <i>Omer</i> and what is brought with it, and for the Two Breads and what is brought with them, we have no information. But since it is said, <i>Moses told the holidays of the Eternal to the Children of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 77a" href="/Pesachim.77a">Babli 77a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 73a" href="/Menachot.73a"><i>Menaḥot</i>73a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 17 13" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_17.13"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 17(13</a>).</i><i>,</i> this fixed it as obligation that all of them have to be offered in impurity.” Just as they are brought in impurity, should they not be eaten in impurity? It is a decision of the verse: <i>Any meat that touched anything impure may not be eaten</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:19" href="/Leviticus.7.19"><i>Lev.</i> 7:19</a>.</i><i>.</i> One would say that the same is valid for the <i>Pesaḥ.</i> This is different since from the start this is what it is for<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:4" href="/Exodus.12.4"><i>Ex.</i> 12:4</a>), <i>Everybody according to his eating you should slaughter the lamb.</i></i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the meat became impure while the fat is unchanged, he does not pour the blood<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is brought only to be eaten, if there is no meat to be eaten, there is no <i>Pesaḥ.</i> If the <i>Pesaḥ</i> was brought but the blood may not be poured, the owner has to bring a second <i>Pesaḥ</i> on the 14th of Iyar.</i>. If the fat became impure while the meat is unchanged, he pours the blood. With sacrifices it is not so, but even if the meat became impure while the fat is unchanged, he pours the blood<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Since for <i>sancta</i> burned on the altar only pouring the blood permits the parts to be brought to the altar.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> This does not follow Rebbi Nathan<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 78b" href="/Pesachim.78b">Babli 78b</a>.</i>, for Rebbi Nathan says, one fulfills one’s obligation by pouring without eating. What is the reason? <i>All of Israel shall slaughter it between the evenings</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:6" href="/Exodus.12.6"><i>Ex.</i> 12:6</a>.</i><i>.</i> “It”, even if there is only one <i>Pesaḥ,</i> all fulfill their obligation with one pouring. Ii is impossible that there be an olive-sized bit for everyone<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If the entire people bring only one lamb, there is not one olive-size bite for every one. But eating less than an olive-size bite is not counted, it is equivalent to nobody eating anything.</i>.
The following is obvious: If the meat became impure but the parts are unchanged, one pours the blood for [the parts. If the parts became impure and the meat is unchanged, one pours the blood for] the meat<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since for all Temple sacrifices which are partially eaten, neither Heaven’s parts can be brought to the altar nor the meat eaten before the blood was poured on the walls of the altar. If one of the two kinds still exists in pure shape, pouring the blood is a positive act and therefore permitted.</i>. If the meat became impure and the parts were lost, Rebbi Shammai said, is the (existing) [fistful]<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of K.</i> not as if the meat became impure and the parts were lost<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A flour offering is eaten by the priests after a fistful has been burned on the altar. If after the fistful was in the hand of the Cohen but before it was brought to the altar the flour became impure, or was burned, or lost, R. Eliezer permits to burn the fistful on the altar but R. Joshua forbids (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 3:4" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.3.4">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i>3:4</a>). The situation can be compared to that of animal sacrifices when only the blood is left.<br>The text of K has to be followed here; the text of S is a scribal error.</i>? You are saying that he takes the fistful; also here he pours. Rebbi [Mana]<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of K.</i> said, explain it following Rebbi Eliezer, since Rebbi Eliezer says, even if there is no remainder there is a fistful<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In practice we follow R. Joshua. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 77b" href="/Pesachim.77b">Babli 77b</a>.</i>.
If it comes in impurity of the officiants<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The case mentioned in the Mishnah, that the people were pure but all officiating priests impure.</i>? How is that? If defective persons<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">It could have stated, “a pure non-priest”. The statement seems to imply that a priest with a bodily defect, who is barred from officiating <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:16-24" href="/Leviticus.21.16-24"><i>(Lev.</i> 21:16–24</a>), still is permitted to act as slaughterer in the Temple.
