The rabbis explain, since nothing of the blood was brought to the interior, <i>you shall certainly eat it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 10:18" href="/Leviticus.10.18"><i>Lev.</i> 10:18</a>.</i><i>.</i> Therefore if some of the blood had been brought to the interior, you<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">Aaron’s sons, addressed by Moses.</i> [would have done well] in burning it. Rebbi Yose the Galilean explains, since not all of the blood was brought to the interior, <i>[you shall certainly eat it.</i> Therefore if all of the blood had been brought inside,] you would have done well in burning it. What is the rabbis’ reason? <i>Any purification offering of whose blood was brought;</i> even part of the blood<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 6:33. As usual, a prefixed <i>mem</i> is interpreted to mean “some, not all”.</i>. What is Rebbi Yose the Galilean’s reason? <i>Behold, its blood was not brought inside the Sanctuary</i><sup class="footnote-marker">250,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 10:18" href="/Leviticus.10.18"><i>Lev.</i> 10:18</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">If Lev. 10:18 is read to refer to rules of the purification sacrifices applicable at all times then it seems to contradict Lev. 6:33 since the prefixed mem is missing.</i>. [This fits with] what was stated: Rebbi Yose the Galilean says, the entire matter only speaks of bulls to be burned and goats to be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">The purification offering of the High Priest <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:1-12" href="/Leviticus.4.1-12"><i>(Lev.</i> 4:1–12</a>), of the people <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13-21" href="/Leviticus.4.13-21"><i>(Lev.</i>4:13–21</a>), and of the day of Atonement <i>(Lev.</i>1627). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 83a" href="/Pesachim.83a">Babli 83a</a> top, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 82a" href="/Zevachim.82a"><i>Zevaḥim</i> 82a</a>.</i>, to prohibit eating them and to teach that if they are disqualified they are burned inside the citadel<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">Whereas all the other disqualified sacrifices have to be burned outside like the impure <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>. They asked him, from where that a purification sacrifice becomes disqualified if some of its blood is brought inside? Not from this verse, <i>behold, its blood was not brought inside the Sanctuary?</i> There it does not say <i>of whose blood</i> but all of its blood<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is the formulation in the actual case decided by Moses, it is the operative version.</i>. An answer to Rebbi Aqiba who was saying, <i>of whose blood,</i> not all of its blood<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">Whose opinion is that of the “Sages” opposing R. Yose the Galilean.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Pesaḥ</i> which left<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">The walls of Jerusalem.</i> or which became impure has to be burned immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">On the 14th of Nisan.</i>. If the owners became impure or died it shall lose its shape<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">Be spoiled and become unusable as sacral meat.</i> and be brought to the place of burning<sup class="footnote-marker">229a</sup><i class="footnote">The place where disqualified sacrifices and those to be burned outside the sacred domain have to be disposed of.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa says, this one also has to be burned immediately since it has no eaters.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> A bone with no meat on it, Rebbi Joḥanan said, one is forbidden to break it; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one is permitted to break it<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">While it is a biblical commandment not to break a bone of the <i>Pesaḥ (Ex.</i> 12:46), it is generally accepted that bone material is inert like wood; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.1.4.2"><i>Orlah</i> 1:4, Note 163</a>. Therefore the prohibition can only refer to a bone with some meat on it or to the marrow inside the bone. The question may be asked only for a bone whose marrow does not add up to the volume of an olive, since otherwise the marrow, being edible, becomes leftover and has to be burned with the leftover meat.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan objected to Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Did we not state, “the bones, the sinews, and leftovers are to be burned on the Sixteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">258a</sup><i class="footnote">Text of the Mishnah in the independent Mishnah mss. and the Babli.</i>”? Could he not chop? Because of the marrow in a marrow-bone<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">One treats all bones as marrow bones to avoid problems in borderline cases. A different approach in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 84b" href="/Pesachim.84b">Babli 84b</a> referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.11.1">Mishnah 8</a>.</i>. Could one not strip off all meat from the bone and profit from the bone? They wanted to say that one may not strip off from the disqualified. Rebbi Eleazar said, and even if you say that one may strip, he explains it following Rebbi Jacob, since Rebbi Jacob said, the prohibition of breaking a bone applies if at the start it was qualified and then became impure<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 84b" href="/Pesachim.84b">Babli 84b</a>. The prohibition of breaking a bone applies even to the leftovers with or without marrow; cf. <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bo</i>15 (pp. 53–54).</i>. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: why did they say, “the bones, the sinews, and leftovers are to be burned on the Sixteenth”? Because he who burns causes breaking<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden when roasting the lamb on the 14th to cut the meat from the bones and expose the bones to direct fire to cause them to break and give access to the bone marrow.</i>. Samuel said, one may subscribe to the brain in the head but one may not subscribe to the marrow in a marrow bone<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 84b" href="/Pesachim.84b">Babli 84b</a></i>. One may subscribe to the brain in the head because one can take it out through the ear. But one may not subscribe to the marrow in a marrow bone since one could only extricate it by breaking. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one may subscribe to the marrow in a marrow bone. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, one may not subscribe to the marrow in a marrow bone, but if he subscribed, he subscribed<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with the previous statement that burning is like breaking. Since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> may be eaten only by the subscribers to it, if somebody subscribed to the marrow he may open the bone by holding it into the fire; even though this is frowned upon it is not forbidden.</i>. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion, could he not burn and subscribe? Because of the loss of <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">Since some of the edible marrow also will be burned and become inedible.</i><i>.</i> In Samuel’s opinion, could he not burn and subscribe? Samuel thinks like Rebbi Jacob, as Rebbi Jacob said, the prohibition of breaking a bone applies if at the start it was qualified and then became impure.
<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 2:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.2.1.11"><i>Šabbat</i> 2, Notes 50–51</a>; it starts with a question the reason of which is found only there. Here it is used to explain why the Mishnah requires the leftovers to be burned on the Sixteenth when from the verse one would have expected that it would be the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i> What did you see that you said so? <i>You shall not leave any leftovers until the morning; what is left over from it until morning you shall burn in fire</i><sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:9" href="/Exodus.12.9"><i>Ex.</i> 12:9</a>.</i><i>.</i> After two mornings, one the morning of the (14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>) and the other the morning of the (15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>)<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">The correct version is in K and <i>Šabbat,</i>15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> and 16<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>. And it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:17" href="/Leviticus.7.17"><i>Lev.</i>7:17</a>.</i>, <i>what is left of the well-being sacrifice should be burned on the third day.</i>
May one start fire on a pyre of leavened matter<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">May one dispose of one’s leavened matter by making a pyre on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> and have it burn into the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> which is a holiday.</i>? For him who infers from leftovers it is prohibited; for him who does not infer from leftovers it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">This is the disagreement between R. Jehudah and the rabbis explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.12">Halakhah 2:1, Notes 66</a> ff. As stated in the Mishnah, leftover <i>sacra</i> may not be burned on a holiday.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, they came to it <sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 2:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.2.1.12"><i>Šabbat</i> 2, Notes 58–59</a>.</i> following the argument of Rebbi Ismael. (etc.) [Just as Rebbi Ismael said, a baby whose time has passed can be circumcised either during the day or during the night, so when its time has passed the <i>sanctum</i> may be burned either during the day or during the night.]

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Anything eaten of a large bull shall be eaten of the soft lamb<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is forbidden to break a bone of the <i>Pesaḥ (Ex.</i> 12:46), any part of the animal which cannot be eaten of an adult bull even after extended cooking is considered bone also of a lamb whose sinews are still soft. Since these may not be broken they cannot be eaten. The borderline cases are cartilage and shoulder tendons which become edible in adult cattle by extended cooking. These are considered meat and the number of subscribers to one <i>Pesaḥ</i> may be increased accordingly.</i>, including the ends of the shoulder bone and cartilage. He who breaks a bone of a pure <i>Pesaḥ</i> is whipped forty [lashes]; but he who leaves over of a pure<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is prohibited to have edible leftovers of the <i>Pesaḥ</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:10" href="/Exodus.12.10"><i>Ex.</i> 12:10</a>), leaving leftovers is a transgression by inaction which cannot be prosecuted.</i> or who breaks of an impure is not whipped forty [lashes].
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.2.3"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 8:2, Note 23–29</a>.</i> What is the rule about soft sinews? Rebbi Joḥanan said, one subscribes to them; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one does not subscribe to them. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: The argument of Rebbi Joḥanan is inverted; the argument of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is inverted. As they disagreed about what is stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 9:2" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.9.2">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 9:2</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:1:1-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.1.1-8">Mishnah 9:1</a> states that in general the hide of an animal is subject to the rules of impurity of food, but not to those of impurity of carcasses. Then <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> lists some animals whose hides follow the rules of flesh in all respects; general consensus exists only for humans and domesticated pigs. R. Joḥanan holds that for eating pigskin one never can be prosecuted, while R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that eating pigskin, not yet transformed into leather, is as punishable as eating pork.</i>: “The following have their hides treated like their flesh: Human skin, and the hides of domesticated pigs, Rebbi Yose says also of wild pigs. <sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets, the remainder of the Mishnah, was added by the corrector; it is neither in K nor in <i>Sanhedrin</i> and is not relevant for the discussion here.</i>[The soft skin of camel’s hump, the soft skin of a calf’s head, the skin near the hooves, the skin of genitals, the skin of an embryo, the skin under the fat tail, and the skin of <i>anaqa, koah, leta’ah,</i> and <i>homet</i> lizards.<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">The lizards in the list of “crawling animals”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29-30" href="/Leviticus.11.29-30"><i>Lev.</i> 11:29–30</a>, whose carcasses are severely impure.</i> Rebbi Jehudah says, a lizard is like a mole. In all cases, if one tanned them, or started to use them as working material, they are pure, except for human skin. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri says, the “eight crawling animals” have hides<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote">None of the animals mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29-30" href="/Leviticus.11.29-30"><i>Lev.</i>11:29–30</a> fall under the exceptions of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.2.1">Mishnah 9:2</a>.</i>.”] Rebbi Joḥanan said, this was only said as prohibition and regarding impurity, but for flogging it is hide. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, Rebbi stated a complete Mishnah, for prohibition, for flogging, for impurity. The reasoning of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seems inverted. There, he treats it as flesh, but here, he does not treat it as meat. Rebbi Judah bar Pazi said, there is a difference, since there one refers to skin which in the end will become hard. This emphasizes that the reasoning of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seems inverted! Since there, where in the end it will harden, he treats it as flesh, here where in the end it will not harden<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote">Animal hide will become inedible; soft sinews and cartilage will remain edible after cooking.</i>, not so much more? Rebbi Abun said, the reason of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is: <i>they shall eat the meat in that night</i><sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:8" href="/Exodus.12.8"><i>Ex.</i> 12:8</a>.</i><i>,</i> not sinews.
The rabbis of Caesarea are saying, Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Issy<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote">Since Issy also is a nickname for Josef, probably one should read “Yasa”. K reads “Yasi” R. Ḥiyya here is R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i>, one switches and one follows as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement of R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>. The one who switches does not have these problems<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">If the names are switched in the statement, the arguments of each one of the parties are consistent.</i>.And how much does he have to break<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">How much of the bone has to be broken to constitute a prosecutable offense. In the following, “liable” means liable to possible criminal prosecution and punishment by whipping.</i>? Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: until the hand be grated<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">If passing with the back of the hand over the bone, the skin is grated.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Zeˋira and Rebbi Abba both in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan. One said, until the hand be grated; but the other said, even a finger nail<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote">One notices a dent in the surface of the bone if moving a finger nail along its surface.</i>. He who said the hand, so much more the finger nail. He who said the finger nail, but not the hand.Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both are saying, one never is liable unless one break a bone on which there is meat in the size of an olive, at one place of meat<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 84b" href="/Pesachim.84b">Babli 84b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Samuel bar Abba asked, then he never should be liable unless he take a stone and smash it into little pieces<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote">Since if one starts to break a bone with meat on it, the meat will be loosened in the process and then there no longer will be an olive sized piece firmly attached to the bone unless the breaking is done with one stroke or by smashing with a stone.</i>. <i>A bone,</i> to make him liable for each single bone<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">The singular used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:46" href="/Exodus.12.46"><i>Ex.</i> 12:46</a> implies that breaking multiple bones is counted as multiple offenses.</i>. This is, with one warning<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">Since there can be no criminal prosecution unless the perpetrator was duly warned that his intended behavior was criminal, no prosecution can exceed the scope of the warning (cf. <i>Introduction to Tractate Sanhedrin,</i> on Chapter Five). Unless the warning was extended to cover both breaking multiple bones and multiple breaking of one bone, only one prosecution is possible.</i>. But with two warnings, then he will be twice liable even for a single bone which he broke repeatedly. Rebbi Jeremiah asked, does this not disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? Since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one is permitted to break a bone without meat. He said this only that he cannot be whipped, but as a prohibition, it is prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote">Breaking any bone of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> always is sinful, even if not prosecutable.</i>.
“But he who leaves over of a pure <i>{Pesaḥ}</i> or who breaks of an impure is not whipped forty {ashes}.” Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: The Mishnah if it came in impurity from the start. But if it came in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">292</sup><i class="footnote">If the blood was poured in purity.</i> and became impure it is as if coming in purity and one is whipped for its breaking<sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote">This is in line with his earlier statement, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.10.2">Note 260</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 84" href="/Pesachim.84">Babli 84a–b</a> this is a matter of disagreement of Tannaim.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan objected to Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Did we not state, “the bones, the sinews, and leftovers are to be burned on the Sixteenth”? Could he not chop? Because of the marrow<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">One treats all bones as marrow bones to avoid problems in borderline cases. A different approach in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 84b" href="/Pesachim.84b">Babli 84b</a> referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.11.1">Mishnah 8</a>.</i>. Could one not strip off all meat from the bone and cut? Not to break under the flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote">Except for the last sentence this is copied from earlier, Halakhah 9. Here it is R. Joḥanan ’s objection to R. Simeon ben Laqish who declares breaking a bone without flesh not prosecutable. Then why does the Mishnah require that one take out the entire bone after stripping off the meat; could one not strip off the meat and then break the bone, without cutting the sinews at the next joint? The answer is that in theory one could do this but in practice one could never be sure whether all meat was safely stripped off; for R. Simeon ben Laqish Mishnah 9 is rabbinical prescription. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 85a" href="/Pesachim.85a">Babli 85a</a>, opinion of Abbai.)</i>.

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A limb which partially was taken out<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">If part of a limb was taken out of Jerusalem and therefore became impure, or in the night of the 15th of Nisan was taken out of the house where it was eaten and therefore became forbidden as food <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:46" href="/Exodus.12.46"><i>(Ex.</i>12:46</a>).</i> one cuts until he reaches the bone, and pares until he reaches the joint, where he cuts<sup class="footnote-marker">295</sup><i class="footnote">At the joint he cuts the sinews, not the bone, to remove the bone. The meat pared from the bone remains edible.</i>; but for sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">All other sacrifices.</i> he cuts with a dagger since there breaking bones is not forbidden..<br>From the wing and inside it is like inside; from the wing and outside it is like outside<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">The wing is the place of the hinges and the door bay.</i>. Windows and the thickness of the wall are like inside<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote">Windows are slits in the wall without glass. Since there was nothing to close off parts of the wall from the interior, the entire thickness of the wall is counted as interior.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied in the last Halakhah of Chapter 10 (10).</i> Rebbi Simon, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the name of Bar Pedaya: <i>Piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.2.6">Chapter 5, Note 67</a>.</i> and leftovers add together to make hands impure<sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote">The impurity of hands as separate from impurity of the body is not pentateuchal. By tradition, it is an institution of First Temple times.</i> in the amount that exposes to punishment, the volume of an olive. Do they disqualify heave? It is a argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> If they make hands impure to disqualify heave, they themselves not so much more? This which left<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">If part of a limb was taken out of Jerusalem and therefore became impure, or in the night of the 15th of Nisan was taken out of the house where it was eaten and therefore became forbidden as food <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:46" href="/Exodus.12.46"><i>(Ex.</i>12:46</a>).</i> what are you doing with it? Does it make hands impure or does it not make hands impure? If you are saying that this which left makes hands impure, would <i>piggul</i> and leftovers not disqualify heave<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote">Since they make hands impure and the touch of impure hands disqualifies heave, it is obvious that the touch of <i>piggul</i> and leftovers must disqualify heave. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Meilah 17b" href="/Meilah.17b"><i>Meˋilah</i>17b</a>.</i>? If you are saying that <i>piggul</i> and leftovers do not disqualify heave, about that which left they did not decide anything<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to <i>piggul</i> and leftovers, use of <i>sancta</i> which were taken out of their required places are not infractions punishable by extirpation.</i>. For if it were not so, the outer part would make the inner part impure<sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote">The outer part of the bone, which became impure, touching the inner part would make the inner part and with it the entire <i>Pesaḥ</i> impure and disqualified.</i>. Rebbi Abbin said, who holds that something can become impure by [touching]<sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote">Added from 10, needed for clarity.</i> itself? Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">307</sup><i class="footnote">The previous argument has to be qualified. Everybody agrees that touching at interior parts (מַגַּע בֵּית הַסְּתָרִים “touch of secret places”) does not transmit impurity <i>(Sifra Mesoraˋ Pereq</i> 6(10), quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 44b" href="/Niddah.44b">Babli <i>Niddah</i> 44b</a>). Rebbi Meïr excludes from this rule the case that an object be partially impure and partially pure; then the inner contact of both parts makes everything impure. Therefore the preceding argument is valid only for R. Meïr, who is not followed in practice.</i>. Did not Rebbi Joḥanan say, everything becomes pure by its greater part? When he cut off the greater part, is it not separated<sup class="footnote-marker">308</sup><i class="footnote">If the pure part is larger than the impure, the object is pure. There is no problem for the <i>Pesaḥ</i> since the bone cannot be cut, the impurity is not transmitted since the touch is interior, and the entire bone is removed. But for other <i>sancta,</i> the moment the bone is cut, the touch is external and transmits impurity, which disqualifies the entire sacrifice. How can the Mishnah permit cutting the bone in this case?</i>? Then it would be touching and be disqualified. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, if he cuts off minimal pieces and throws away<sup class="footnote-marker">309</sup><i class="footnote">The bone cannot be cut but must be shaved off in pieces smaller than the size of an olive which then are discarded immediately. Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Pesachim 85a:14:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Pesachim.85a.14.1"><i>Tosaphot</i> 85a, <i>s.v.</i>ולרבינא</a><i>.</i></i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: They did not sanctify under the wing in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">310</sup><i class="footnote">The gates of the walls of Jerusalem were sanctified, to be used as place for the consumption of <i>sancia,</i> only within the closed doors.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rav Samuel bar Rav Isaac: That the sufferers from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote">While the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:2" href="/Numbers.5.2"><i>Num.</i> 5:2</a> says, <i>they shall exile from the camp any sufferer from skin disease, any sufferer from gonorrhea, and any one defiled by a corpse,</i> the rabbinic interpretation is that the sufferers from skin disease have to be exiled from any walled city, the sufferer from gonorrhea only from the Temple Mount, and the person impure by the impurity of corpses only from the Temple district proper. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 67a" href="/Pesachim.67a">Babli 67a</a>, <i>Sifry Num.</i> 1.</i> might find shelter in summer from the sun and in the rainy season from rain. Did they similarly not sanctify under the wing of the Temple Mount, that sufferers from gonorrhea and flux<sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote">While the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:2" href="/Numbers.5.2"><i>Num.</i> 5:2</a> says, <i>they shall exile from the camp any sufferer from skin disease, any sufferer from gonorrhea, and any one defiled by a corpse,</i> the rabbinic interpretation is that the sufferers from skin disease have to be exiled from any walled city, the sufferer from gonorrhea only from the Temple Mount, and the person impure by the impurity of corpses only from the Temple district proper. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 67a" href="/Pesachim.67a">Babli 67a</a>, <i>Sifry Num.</i> 1.</i> might find shelter in summer from the sun and in the rainy season from rain? The sufferer from skin disease has no place to find shelter; the sufferer from gonorrhea finds shelter in all of Jerusalem. Rebbi Joḥanan bar Marius in the name of Rebbi Phineas: Since we see that the rabbis take off their sandals under the wing of the Temple Mount<sup class="footnote-marker">312</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is forbidden to walk with shoes on the Temple Mount proper; Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 9:8.</i>, this implies that they did not sanctify under the wing of the Temple Mount. Rav asked the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya: What is the status of roofs in Jerusalem? He told him, since one uses as proverb: the <i>Pesaḥ</i> in an olive-sized bit and <i>Hallel</i> breaks the roof,<sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote">So many people have subscribed to one <i>Pesaḥ</i> that each only got one olive-sized piece, and the noise of the <i>Hallel</i> which they were singing while eating on the roof damaged the roof. This proves that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> was eaten on the roof, and therefore the roofs of the houses of Jerusalem were sanctified.</i> this implies that the roofs of Jerusalem are sanctified. Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Miasha, Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac in the name of Rav: The roofs of Jerusalem are profane. But did we not state, “From the wing and inside it is like inside; from the wing and outside it is like outside<sup class="footnote-marker">314</sup><i class="footnote">Since the roofs are of houses inside the walls, the blanket statement that all inside is sanctified also includes the roofs.</i>”? Explain it that the Mishnah speaks about a roof surrounded by buildings forming a courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">315</sup><i class="footnote">Roofs on street level of houses dug out from the earth, not built on it.</i>. But did we not state, “windows and the thickness of the wall are like inside<sup class="footnote-marker">316</sup><i class="footnote">The “thickness of the wall” cannot refer to the material since this is inaccessible; it cannot refer to the windows which are mentioned separately. Therefore it must refer to the roofs.</i>”? Explain it again that the Mishnah speaks about a roof surrounded by buildings forming a courtyard. This parallels what Rebbi Aḥa said in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena: <i>glacis and wall mourned</i><sup class="footnote-marker">317</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Lamentations 2:8" href="/Lamentations.2.8"><i>Thr.</i> 2:8</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 86a" href="/Pesachim.86a">Babli 86a</a>.</i>, the wall and the ante-wall. If they sanctified the thickness of the wall, not so much more the window? Rebbi Aḥa said, it is necessary for the window on top of the wing. Even though you are saying that they did not sanctify under the wing of Jerusalem, they sanctified the window over the wing.
It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah: the tunnels under the Temple were profane, the Temple roofs were sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">318</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 86a" href="/Pesachim.86a">Babli 86a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: A Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">319</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tamid 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Tamid.1.1">Mishnah <i>Tamid</i> 1:1</a>.</i> says so, “there was a fireplace there, and an honorable bathroom. [What was its honor? If one found it locked, one knew that it was occupied, unlocked one knew that it was unoccupied.”] Rebbi Yose said, is excrement impure? Is it not simply cleanliness? But the following is it, “he<sup class="footnote-marker">320</sup><i class="footnote">A priest watching in the Temple who becomes impure has to leave immediately by the shortest way.</i> leaves and goes in the walkway under the Temple, and lights were burning on both sides, until he comes to the place of immersion.” If it were sanctified he would have to go by the shortest way<sup class="footnote-marker">321</sup><i class="footnote">Either (Babli) two different groups have subscribed to different parts of one and the same <i>Pesaḥ</i> or <i>(Mekhilta dR. Ismael)</i> eating different <i>Pesaḥim.</i> Both cases are possible for the Yerushalmi. They may use the same room only if they are sitting back to back.</i>.

Halakhah 13

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Two groups which were eating in the same room, these are turning their faces in one direction while eating, and those are turning their faces in one direction while eating<sup class="footnote-marker">321</sup><i class="footnote">Either (Babli) two different groups have subscribed to different parts of one and the same <i>Pesaḥ</i> or <i>(Mekhilta dR. Ismael)</i> eating different <i>Pesaḥim.</i> Both cases are possible for the Yerushalmi. They may use the same room only if they are sitting back to back.</i>, and the samovar<sup class="footnote-marker">322</sup><i class="footnote">Producing hot water to mix the wine for the required Four Cups in the standard ratio of one part wine to two parts water.</i> is in the middle. When the waiter stands to mix, he closes his mouth and turns his head<sup class="footnote-marker">323</sup><i class="footnote">So people will not suspect him to eat with a group to whose part of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> he did not subscribe.</i> unless he is near his own group and eats. But the newly-wed<sup class="footnote-marker">324</sup><i class="footnote">Who is not yet used to be in company; she may turn her face away from the company and will not be suspected of not eating with her own group.</i> may turn aside her face and eat.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “It is written, <i>do not remove any meat from the house to the outside</i><sup class="footnote-marker">325</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:46" href="/Exodus.12.46"><i>Ex.</i> 12:46</a>.</i><i>.</i> I have not only outside the house, from where outside the group? The verse says, <i>do not remove to the outside.”</i><sup class="footnote-marker">326</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 86a" href="/Pesachim.86a">Babli 86a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 6:11" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).6.11">Tosephta 6:11</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bol</i> 15 (p. 55), <i>dR. Simeon bar Yoḥai</i> p. 36, <i>Tanḥuma Bo</i> 10, also cf. the <i>Targumim.</i></i> Rebbi Yudan said, from here that he makes himself liable if he (said I am) [removed] away from the group<sup class="footnote-marker">327</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the scribe’s, the one in brackets the corrector’s. Since in the corrector’s text R. Yudan adds nothing to the <i>baraita,</i> the scribe’s text must be the correct one. If a person (after the pouring of the blood) declares himself not to be part of his group, he commits a sin.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, the verse should have said, <i>do not remove to the outside,</i> and we would have said, if he makes himself liable outside the group, not so much more outside the house<sup class="footnote-marker">328</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of the house seems to be superfluous. <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i> disagrees; the meat becomes disqualified and impure only by being removed to the outside.</i>?
Rebbi Immi asked, if he removed from group to group (the volume of two olives)<sup class="footnote-marker">329</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that instead of כִּשְׁנִי זֵיתִים one should read בִּשְׁנֵי בָתִּים “in two houses”. Since “house” usually means a one-room dwelling, “house” also may mean “room” in a big house.</i> he is twice liable, because of <i>do not remove from the house</i> and <i>do not remove to the outside.</i> If they subscribed to a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> and an individual removed the volume of an olive, he is liable. Two or three persons are not liable since the members of the group may be drawn after them; but they infringe on a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">330</sup><i class="footnote">Beginning of v. 12:46: <i>In one house it shall be eaten.</i></i>; and following Rebbi Simeon they do not infringe. As it was stated, <i>over the houses in which they are eating it there</i><sup class="footnote-marker">331</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:7" href="/Exodus.12.7"><i>Ex.</i> 12:7</a>.</i><i>,</i> this teaches that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> may be eaten in two places<sup class="footnote-marker">332</sup><i class="footnote">In the same room, as described in the Mishnah. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 86" href="/Pesachim.86">Babli 86a/b</a>.</i>. I could think that its eaters also could eat it in two places, the verse says, <i>in one house it shall be eaten.</i> How is that? A <i>Pesaḥ</i> may be eaten in two places, but its eaters may not eat it in two places. [Rebbi Simeon says, also its eaters could eat it in two places.] How does Rebbi Simeon uphold <i>in one house it shall be eaten?</i> Lest part of one group eat inside and another part outside. An individual who removed the volume of an olive, since the members of the group may be drawn after him, becomes absolved from the positive commandment. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba asked, why following Rebbi Simeon? Even following the rabbis, since the members of the group may be drawn after him, they are absolved from the prohibition; neither should they be under the positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">333</sup><i class="footnote">Since the question is not answered it seems to be accepted. The positive commandment referred to is not in Maimonides’s list of commandments.</i>.
The waiter who ate the volume of an olive when he was near the oven,<sup class="footnote-marker">334</sup><i class="footnote">In which the one <i>Pesaḥ</i> is roasted.</i> if he is intelligent he fills his belly from it. If they want to honor him, they come and eat with him near the oven; otherwise they give him his part and he eats at his place. There<sup class="footnote-marker">335</sup><i class="footnote">In the same house.</i>, they are saying, this is Rebbi Simeon’s, since they did not hear what Rebbi Hila, Rebbi Issy<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote">Since Issy also is a nickname for Josef, probably one should read “Yasa”. K reads “Yasi” R. Ḥiyya here is R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i>, Rebbi Eleazar said in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Everybody agrees that at the start they may split, at the end they may not split<sup class="footnote-marker">336</sup><i class="footnote">Once they start eating.</i>. Where do they disagree? When they were sitting and a beam cracked over them. Rebbi Simeon says, part of them remove themselves and eat at another place, but the rabbis say, no part of them may remove themselves and eat at another place<sup class="footnote-marker">338</sup><i class="footnote">Since the rabbis agree that a group may split into two before they start eating, there is no fixed place of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> before they start eating and there can be no removal.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina asked: Then<sup class="footnote-marker">338</sup><i class="footnote">Since the rabbis agree that a group may split into two before they start eating, there is no fixed place of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> before they start eating and there can be no removal.</i> one who removes should not be liable up to the moment when he eats. Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan : One who removes is not liable up to the moment when he eats. Rebbi Zeˋira said, but it was stated there, an individual who removed the volume of an olive from a group is liable but he did not disqualify himself as a member339 of the group. This implies, even if he did not eat. If you would say, he ate, why did he not disqualify himself as a member of the group? Rebbi Yose said, when he removed it he disqualified it. Even if he ate, he ate something disqualified. Where was this said? There is breaking after breaking but not removing after removing<sup class="footnote-marker">340</sup><i class="footnote">(Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:11:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.11.3">Note 290</a>). It is sinful to break a broken bone of a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> but it is not sinful to further remove a removed part.</i>. Either this means that when he removes it, he disqualifies it, or it means that he is not liable up to the moment when he eats<sup class="footnote-marker">341</sup><i class="footnote">Either there can be no removal after removal because the first removal disqualifies the meat, and moving disqualified <i>Pesaḥ</i> is not sanctioned, or “removing” means “removing and eating” and the first removal did not count.</i>.
The following is obvious: If one group started eating when the other became impure, the pure group acquired the part of the impure<sup class="footnote-marker">342</sup><i class="footnote">Since both groups subscribe to the same animal and one group becomes unable to use it.</i>. Not only this but even if one group started eating and they became impure, the pure group acquired the part of the impure. But if both had started eating when one of them became impure, the pure group did not acquire the part of the impure<sup class="footnote-marker">343</sup><i class="footnote">Since each group already took possession of its part.</i>.
An individual can move his part in the entire room, but a group cannot move their part in the entire room<sup class="footnote-marker">344</sup><i class="footnote">A single person eating a <i>Pesaḥ</i> may change his place while eating while members of a group have to remain sitting where they started eating.</i>. But did we not state “two groups”<sup class="footnote-marker">345</sup><i class="footnote">They are eating from the same <i>Pesaḥ</i> at two distinct tables. But one single group cannot be separated.</i>? He only said two; therefore not one. But did we not state, “the samovar in the middle”<sup class="footnote-marker">346</sup><i class="footnote">It serves both groups at the same time; the hot water has to be transported through the entire room. But the waiter shows by his behavior as described in the Mishnah to which group he belongs.</i>? The place of the waiter proves where his group is. But did we not state, “the newly-wed may turn aside her face and eat”<sup class="footnote-marker">347</sup><i class="footnote">Does she not show by turning away from the group that she does not want to belong?</i>? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, because of shyness

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A woman living in her husband’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If she only is preliminarily married, she still lives in her father’s house and eats <i>Pesaḥ</i> as her father’s child</i>, and both her husband and her father slaughtered for her<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Both are in Jerusalem and both counted her on the list of subscribers to their <i>Pesaḥ</i>.</i>, should eat of her husband’s. If she went to her father’s house for the first holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There was a general custom of the bride returning to her father’s house for the first holiday after the definitive marriage.</i> and both her father and her husband slaughtered for her, she may eat where she desires. An orphan for whom guardians<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">. If there are multiple guardians (Greek ἐπίτροπος, ὁ).</i> slaughtered may eat where he desires. A slave of two owners may not eat from either of them<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since neither of the joint owners has the right to take from the other, the part of the slave belonging to one cannot profit from the other’s food. He has to participate in a separate <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>. A person who is half slave and half freedman may not eat of his master’s<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">He has to behave like a free person.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A woman living in her husband’s house,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.8"><i>Sotah</i> 2:1, Notes 15–23</a> which is the original since the last sentence, which makes the argument intelligible, is missing here.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, for the four who are missing atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Anyone whose own body was the source of impurity, when he is pure again cannot enter the Temple precinct unless he first brought a sacrifice of cleansing: The woman after childbirth <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:6-8" href="/Leviticus.12.6-8"><i>(Lev.</i> 12:6–8</a>), the person healed from skin disease <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-32" href="/Leviticus.14.1-32"><i>(Lev.</i> 14: 1–32</a>) and the persons healed from genital discharges <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:14-15" href="/Leviticus.15.14-15"><i>(Lev.</i> 15:14–15</a>,29–30).</i> others may dedicate without their knowledge; these are the following: Man or woman [healed from] genital discharges, the woman after childbirth and one [healed from] skin disease, since a father may dedicate for his small son who is lying in a crib<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Who could have been afflicted with skin disease and, if female, with a discharge at her birth mimicking menstruation</i>. One understands man or woman [healed from] genital discharges or [healed from] skin disease, but a woman after childbirth? May a minor give birth? <sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This text also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.2.9"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:2, Note 153</a>. It implies that a woman giving birth is an adult and no longer in her father’s power.</i> Did not Rebbi Redifa, Rebbi Jonah, say in the name of Rav Ḥuna: If a woman became pregnant and gave birth before she grew two hairs, she and her son will die. After she grew two hairs, she and her son will live. If she became pregnant before she grew two hairs and gave birth after she grew two hairs, she will live but her son will die. How is the situation? Since a man may dedicate for his underage daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, he should be able to dedicate for his wife who also is dependent upon him.</i>. Since he married her off<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Even without growing pubic hair, if he married her off she is emancipated from him.</i>, she already left his power. But it must be since a man may dedicate for his deaf-mute wife. Here, in the case of the suspected wife, the case of the minor does not apply since <sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This statement also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.5"><i>Sotah</i> 1:2, Note 91</a>. In the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 33b" href="/Yevamot.33b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i>33b</a>, “the seduction of an under-age girl is rape.”</i> Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Yosa<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">With the text in <i>Sotah</i> read: Yasa.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan : An underage girl who whored has no will to be forbidden to her husband. The case of the deaf-mute does not apply since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>Num.</i> 5:22</a>. The answer of the wife is a requirement that cannot be waved. Therefore, a mute woman cannot undergo the <i>sotah</i> ordeal. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 27b" href="/Sotah.27b">Babli concurs, <i>Sotah</i>27b</a>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:18" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.18"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(18</a>).</i>: <i>The woman shall say: Amen, amen.</i> Rebbi Abun said, since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:26" href="/Deuteronomy.14.26"><i>Deut.</i> 14:26</a>. <i>A man’s house</i> usually means his wife. The missing final sentence explains that since he cannot enjoy himself if his wife is forbidden to him, she cannot hinder him in the preparations for her rehabilitation. This answers the original question for the husband. At the same time, R. Abun disagrees with R. Joḥanan and holds that only the husband may dedicate the purgation offering of the woman after childbirth without her knowledge since he has a direct interest in it. While the woman after childbirth is permitted to her husband once she is recovered and pure, she cannot enjoy the holiday sacrifices with him as long as her sacrifice has not been handed over to the Temple personnel.</i>: <i>You shall enjoy together with your house.</i>
Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan : A person chooses another’s <i>nezirut</i> sacrifice without the other’s knowledge. But he cannot dedicate it without the other’s knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.5.2"><i>Nazir</i> 2:5 Note 84</a>. Since an animal cannot be brought to the Temple without having been dedicated, and the dedication has to be for a specific obligation, the <i>nazir</i> whose sacrifices are paid for must be informed of the dedication for it to be valid. Denied by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 36a" href="/Nedarim.36a">Babli, <i>Nedarim</i> 36a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan : A person chooses another’s purification sacrifice for fat<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A general name for inadvertent sins punishable by extirpation, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:25" href="/Leviticus.7.25"><i>Lev.</i> 7:25</a>. Denied by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 36a" href="/Nedarim.36a">Babli, <i>Nedarim</i> 36a</a>.</i> without the other’s knowledge. But he cannot dedicate it without the other’s knowledge.
Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: A person may slaughter another’s <i>Pesaḥ</i> without his knowledge, but he cannot dedicate it without his knowledge. Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah says so: “A woman living in her husband’s house, and both her father and her husband slaughtered for her, should eat of her husband’s.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The first sentence of the Mishnah, which prescribes the wife’s place, seems to contradict the second, which gives her freedom of choice. It is to be assumed that neither her father nor her husband are permanent dwellers in Jerusalem so that for the <i>Pesaḥ</i> she actually is neither in her husband’s nor in her father’s permanent home.</i>” Where do we hold? If giving instructions<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Reading מַמְחָה as מַנְחָה; not derived from any of the roots מחה “protest; erase, rub out”.</i>, normally a woman gives instructions saying, I want to be with my children<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">So one may assume that the Mishnah does not refer tot a wife who already has children.</i>. So not giving instructions<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Neither father nor husband had her agreement to eat with him. This would follow R. Eleazar who allows slaughtering the <i>Pesaḥ</i> without the knowledge of the other person.</i>. The Mishnah may be about giving instructions<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah cannot be used to prove that practice agrees with R. Eleazar.</i>. There came Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan , the Mishnah is about giving instructions. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Eleazar, since Rebbi Eleazar said, a person may slaughter another’s <i>Pesaḥ</i> without his knowledge, but he may not choose it without his knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Then she at least has to be informed beforehand. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 36a" href="/Nedarim.36a">Babli, <i>Nedarim</i> 36a</a>.</i>? There is a difference between <i>Pesaḥ</i> and a purification sacrifice. If a <i>Pesaḥ</i> grows it may be used to satisfy an obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">An animal dedicated for <i>Pesaḥ</i> but not used automatically is dedicated as well-being sacrifice to be used later for satisfying a vow by the owner.</i>; a purification sacrifice if it grows may not be used to satisfy an obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">An animal dedicated as purification sacrifice but not used may not be used for anything else.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, they taught this about the follow-holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Using the root רדף not in the biblical sense “to pursue” but the related meaning “to follow closely”, Arabic ردف. The definition of follow-holiday is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i>7:5 (Notes 43–45</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 71b" href="/Ketubot.71b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 71b</a>.</i>. What is the follow-holiday? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, that is the first holiday when her father follows her to her husband’s house. If she did not go for the first holiday, does the second holiday become the follow-holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">So if that should be Passover she has a choice whether to join her father or her husband for the <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>? She always has a follow-holiday. Does a widow have a follow-holiday? Let us hear from the following: “If she went to her father’s house for the first holiday.” Either this means that she always has a follow-holiday or it means that a widow has a follow-holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">To make sense of the duplication in the Mishnah since following R. Joḥanan also the first sentence of the Mishnah refers to a newly-wed who still has no child.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it that she has children but no husband. Normally a woman gives instructions saying, I want to be with my children<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Once she has children, the question of where she celebrates the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is resolved; she no longer has to be asked where she wants to be.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.17"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:7, Notes 612–623</a>. The origin probably is here.</i> Rebbi Eleazar said: The <i>Pesaḥ</i> of women is voluntary<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann has emphasized repeatedly that in the Yerushalmi רְשׁוּתmeans “meritorious act which is not obligatory.” The Babli disagrees in the name of R. Eliezer, 91a.<br>In the Babli and related texts, it is taken for granted that women present in Jerusalem on Passover eve are obligated to take part in the Passover meal, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:4" href="/Exodus.12.4"><i>Ex.</i> 12:4</a> which designates the participants as “persons”, not “men” (<i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Pisḥa</i> 3, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Yoḥai</i> p. 10; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:1:1-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.1.1-10">Mishnah 8:1</a>,7 Tosephta 8:10; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 91" href="/Pesachim.91">Babli 91a/b</a>.) But it should be noted that missing the sacrificial meal is a deadly sin only for men (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:13" href="/Numbers.9.13"><i>Num.</i> 9:13</a>); only men are required (and permitted) to make up the missed sacrifice on the 14th of Iyar <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:13" href="/Numbers.9.13"><i>(Num.</i> 9:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 8:10" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).8.10">Tosephta 8:10</a>).<br>The statement means that an unaffiliated female, living neither in her father’s nor a husband’s house, is not obligated to seek a group with which to celebrate Passover. It would be considered most inappropriate if a single woman would join a group of men unrelated to her. But since she is included in the circle of celebrants of Passover by biblical law, if she alone or a group of similarly situated women want to organize their own <i>Pesaḥ</i> and meal, they are authorized to do so.<br>Circumcised slaves are included in the group of Passover celebrants in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:44" href="/Exodus.12.44"><i>Ex.</i> 12:44</a>. The owner of uncircumcised slaves is prohibited from bringing a <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>, but one pushes the Sabbath away for it<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since women are biblically empowered to celebrate Passover, the rule also applies to groups of women.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: The Passover sacrifice from women and slaves is voluntary; certainly one does push the Sabbath away for it. What is their <i>mazzah</i>? Rebbi Eleazar said, it is obligatory. Rebbi Zeˋira said, it is voluntary<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qiddušin:</i> This is in dispute.</i>. Rebbi Hila said, it is everybody’s {opinion}. A <i>baraita</i> supports the one and a <i>baraita</i> supports the other. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Zeˋira: Lettuce, <i>mazzah</i> and <i>Pesaḥ</i> are obligatory the first night; on all other days they are voluntary. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Hila: It was said: <i>Do not eat leavened matter with it, for seven days you shall eat with it mazzot, the bread of deprivation</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:3" href="/Deuteronomy.17.3"><i>Deut</i> 17:3</a>. Rules stated in the same verse must be of one and the same category.</i><i>.</i> Anybody under the prohibition of leavened matter is under the obligation to eat <i>mazzah,</i> and women, being under the prohibition of leavened matter, are under the obligation to eat <i>mazzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 91b" href="/Pesachim.91b">Babli, 91b</a>, this is a statement by R. Eleazar, probably the Tanna. In <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 130, its contrapositive is a statement of R. Simeon.</i><i>.</i> But did we not state: “For any positive commandment triggered by time, men are obligated but women are free”<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:7</a>.</i>? Rebby Mana said, it is a powerful positive commandment since it is implied by a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore follow the rules of prohibitions, obligatory for women.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <i>Ḥallah</i> 4:12 and originates there. It refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.4.5.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i>4:12</a> which reports that a certain Joseph Hakohen brought his entire family, women and children, to celebrate Second <i>Pesaḥ</i> but the Temple authorities admitted only the adult males.</i> Does it follow him who says the <i>Pesaḥ</i> of women is voluntary? It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 8:10" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).8.10">Tosephta 8:10</a>.</i>: “A woman may make the First <i>Pesaḥ</i> by herself and the Second joining \others, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose says, a woman may make the Second <i>Pesaḥ</i> by herself, and certainly the First. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This should read with the other sources: R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon.</i> says, a woman may make the First <i>Pesaḥ</i> joining others but does not make the Second.” What is the reason of Rebbi Meïr? <i>Every man a sheep for the family,</i> if they want <i>for the house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:3" href="/Exodus.12.3"><i>Ex</i>. 12:3</a>. Everywhere “house” is read as synonym of “wife”.</i><i>.</i> What is the reason of Rebbi Yose, <i>every man a sheep for the family,</i> a fortiori <i>for the house.</i> What is the reason of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon? “Every man”, not a woman. How do the rabbis uphold “man”? A man, not a minor. Rebbi Jonah said, even according to him who says it is an obligation, it is different here since the occasion was news, lest it become an obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If a renowned authority does something, everybody will rush to emulate him and in the next generation it will already be a common standard and acquire the status of “practice of the forefathers from time immemorial”. Even R. Yose will agree that in such a situation one should not allow a public display of special devotion.</i>.
“An orphan for whom guardians<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">. If there are multiple guardians (Greek ἐπίτροπος, ὁ).</i> slaughtered may eat where he desires.” The Mishnah refers to an underage orphan. But an adult orphan is like one who subscribed to two <i>Pesaḥim;</i> as Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 7:3" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).7.3">Tosephta 7:3</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 89b" href="/Pesachim.89b">Babli 89b</a>.</i>, one who subscribed to two <i>Pesaḥim</i> eats from the one which was slaughtered first<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore the adult orphan has no choice but the order of slaughter determines where he is obligated to eat.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, the Mishnah says so, “if he slaughtered a kid goat and a lamb, one eats from the first.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.2.1">Mishnah 8:2</a>, referring to a case where the person who slaughtered was fully empowered to do so.</i>”
“A slave of two owners may not eat from either of them.” Rebbi Yose said, his master has no right to say, it is impossible that you should be counted for a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> but he may say to him, you may not be counted for this one, only for that one<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">To resolve the problem spelled out in the Mishnah, one of the masters may bar him from his own <i>Pesaḥ</i> and thereby force him to eat from the other owner’s <i>Pesaḥ.</i> But he cannot forbid him a <i>Pesaḥ</i> since it is a biblical obligation.</i>. Sometimes he is counted for three <i>Pesaḥim</i> simultaneously. How is this? A slave of two owners must subscribe to a <i>Pesaḥ.</i> If one of them freed his part, he has to subscribe to another <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The part of his which is free cannot fulfill his absolute obligation with the dedication for the limited obligation of a slave.</i><i>.</i> If both of them freed, he has to subscribe to yet another <i>Pesaḥ.</i> Samuel bar Abba asked, may the slave eat from all three of them? If you are saying that the slave may not eat from all three of them, then he should not be able to subscribe to a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> since perhaps his master might want to free him and it turns out that an invalid dedication is mixed up in the offering<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">It is a biblical obligation that circumcised slaves eat from their master’s <i>Pesaḥ</i> (and the master is barred from <i>Pesaḥ</i> if he owns uncircumcised slaves). If manumission would invalidate the dedication, neither slaveholder nor his slaves would be able to eat any <i>Pesaḥ</i> since there always would be the possibility that the master manumit a slave which would retroactively invalidate the dedication and an undedicated animal may not be sacrificed. Therefore it is clear that manumission does not invalidate dedication.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said that Rebbi Zeˋira asked, may the owner fulfill his obligation by the slave’s dedication? Is that not a Mishnah: “One says to his slave, go and slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> for me.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>. It is clear from the remainder of the text of this Mishnah that the slave is authorized to buy the animal for <i>Pesaḥ</i> and to dedicate it on behalf of his master.</i>” The Mishnah on the instruction of his master, what is problematic for him not on the instruction of his master.
“A person who is half slave and half freedman may not eat of his master’s.” Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If a man who is half slave and half freedman preliminarily married a woman, one disregards his preliminary marriage. It is the same for divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously if there can be no marriage there can be no divorce. Therefore the text, which also is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.5.2"><i>Giṭtin</i> 4:5 (Notes 135–141</a>), has to be interpreted in the light of the explanation given there, following the House of Hillel who say that he works for his owner one day and for himself the other day. On his owner’s day he is a slave unable either to marry or divorce; on his own day he can marry preliminarily (but is unable to consummate the marriage, as explained there.) In <i>Giṭtin</i> and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:32" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.32"><i>Qiddušin</i>1:1 (Note 139</a>), the statement about divorce is a straight declarative sentence which is unchallenged. Here it is possible to read it as a question: is it the same for divorce? Samuel holds that since on his own day he may contract a preliminary marriage, just as a half-freed woman can contract a preliminary marriage to be consummated after her complete emancipation, so he can dissolve the preliminary marriage on his own day</i>. Samuel says, one takes notice of his divorce. Samuel’s statement parallels Rebbi Jehudah’s, as we have stated, “One who kidnapped a person half slave and half free Rebbi Jehudah declares punishable but the Sages make him not prosecutable.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">He delegates him to dedicate and slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> without detailed instructions about what to do.</i>”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One says to his slave, go and slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> for me<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">He delegates him to dedicate and slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> without detailed instructions about what to do.</i>. If he slaughtered a kid goat, it should be eaten; if he slaughtered a lamb, it should be eaten. If he slaughtered kid goat and lamb, one eats from the first. If he forgot what his master had told him<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">But he remembers that he was instructed to buy a particular kind of animal. Then he is not empowered to dedicate an animal not conforming to his master’s instructions; also a <i>Pesaḥ</i> may not be dedicated if it is known beforehand that it will not be eaten. Since only two kinds of animal are allowed fur <i>Pesaḥ,</i> the slave can resolve the problem by buying two animals and making sure that there are people able to eat either one of them.</i>, what can he do? He shall slaughter a kid goat and a lamb and say, if my master said to me a kid goat, the kid goat is his and the lamb mine, but if my master said to me a lamb, the lamb is his and the kid goat mine. If his master had forgotten what he told him, both shall be brought to the place of burning<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since it cannot be determined which animal has to be slaughtered for him and since “<i>Pesaḥ</i> comes only for its eaters,” the slaughter cannot permit the meat to be eaten. But since an attempt was made to bring the First <i>Pesaḥ,</i> the conditions for a Second are not satisfied.</i> but they are free from bringing the Second <i>Pesaḥ.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 7:3" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).7.3">Tosephta 7:3</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 89b" href="/Pesachim.89b">Babli 89b</a>.</i>: One who simultaneously subscribed to two <i>Pesaḥim</i> eats from the one which was slaughtered first. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah implies this; “if he slaughtered kid goat and lamb, one eats from the first.” Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Joḥanan both are saying, this is Rebbi Nathan’s since Rebbi Nathan said, one fulfills his obligation by pouring without eating. Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal: Explain it that the master forgot between slaughter and pouring<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 88b" href="/Pesachim.88b">Babli 88b</a>. Then the blood was poured when it was known that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> would be eaten and it is qualified according to everybody.</i>. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, one of the rabbis objected; some say that it was himself: Did we not state, “If he forgot what his master had told him, he shall slaughter a kid goat and a lamb<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Since this presupposes that the master knows what he told him, the next sentence must deal with the case that the master did nor remember at the time of slaughter and the Mishnah necessarily follows R. Nathan.</i>”?
Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he dedicated a <i>Pesaḥ</i> before he converted and then converted<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since a Gentile may bring a well-being offering, and an unused <i>Pesaḥ</i> automatically reverts to a well-being offering, since the Gentile is barred from offering a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> he dedicated a well-being offering, and after conversion is without a dedicated <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>, before he was manumitted and then was manumitted, before he grew two pubic hairs<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">An adult is a person growing two pubic hairs. As a minor he has no obligation to bring a <i>Pesaḥ;</i> therefore he cannot dedicate one.</i> and then grew two pubic hairs, has to subscribe to another <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">However Maimonides <i>(Qorban Pesaḥ</i>4:9) and Meïri <i>(Pesaḥim,</i> ed. Y. Klein, Jerusalem 1964, p. 366b) read: “he brings [the animal when he is ineligible] as <i>Pesaḥ.”</i></i><i>.</i> Rebbi Yudan said, all doubts<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Whether a sacrifice required to be eaten (either by the Cohanim or by lay people) is acceptable in case that its blood was poured but it could not be eaten.</i> follow Rebbi Nathan, except for him who pours the blood of his purification offering or the blood of his reparation offering when there is a doubt whether he poured during nighttime or poured during daytime<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">No sacrificial act is possible (except burning excess sacrifices) during nighttime.</i>, where there is a doubt of atonement, and every doubt of atonement atones<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Even following the majority no other sacrifice is required.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one says to his children, I am going to slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> in the name of whoever of you will come first to Jerusalem. When the first of them entered with his head and most of his body, he acquired his part and acquires for his siblings with him. Forever one subscribes to it as long as there is the volume of an olive for each participant. One subscribes to it or cancels until it has been slaughtered; Rebbi Simeon says until the blood is poured.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no “I am going to slaughter” here, only “I am going to dedicate.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Since after slaughter the animal is Heaven’s property, nobody can acquire a part of the animal. If the Mishnah mentions that the first to come acquires his own part and those of his siblings, by necessity this must be for the dedication as sacrifice.</i>” And why was it stated “I am going to slaughter”? To urge him on.
Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: It is a stipulation of the Court that one dedicate his <i>Pesaḥ</i> and the other his money<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is, how can one have people subscribe to a <i>Pesaḥ</i> that already is dedicated as a sacrifice? It is Heaven’s property and no longer can be considered the owner’s; how can he take money for participation? Subscription is only possible because of a stipulation, probably part of Ezra’s reorganization of Jewish worship, that a <i>Pesaḥ</i> be open to subscription and that money dedicated for this purpose become profane in the hand of the person who dedicated the animal.</i>. He lets him subscribe to his [part] and the money automatically becomes profane. How? It becomes profane and then dedicated again? Or for this purpose it was dedicated from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since no Mishnah in <i>Pesaḥim</i> mentions the monetary aspect, it is up to Amoraim to determine how to harmonize this exception with the generally known rules of dedication of <i>sancta.</i> Either the <i>Pesaḥ</i> after dedication is not considered a <i>sanctum</i> but becomes one piecewise when olive-sized parts of it are sold to a subscriber and the sanctity of the dedicated money is transferred to the piece of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> while the money becomes profane in the hand of the seller, as is the general rule for money dedicated for sacrifices which become profane in the hand of the seller of the animal which is dedicated in being bought as sacrifice, or the money dedicated for <i>Pesaḥ</i> has a status different from money dedicated for any other sacrifice in that it is intrinsically profane with the restriction to be used only for the <i>Pesaḥ.</i> Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 89b" href="/Pesachim.89b">Babli 89b</a>.</i>? What is the difference between them? If he dedicated 100 <i>mina</i> for his <i>Pesaḥ</i> and used 50 to subscribe<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This is a typically 3rd Century example from the time of the inflation of the Military Anarchy in the Roman Empire. 100 <i>mina</i> are 10’000 <i>drachma (denar),</i> while in the old (Augustean) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:2</a> the value of a sacrifice is between an <i>obolus</i>(⅙ <i>denar)</i> and 2 <i>denar.</i></i>. If you are saying that it becomes profane and is dedicated again, the remainder of the monies are profane. If you are saying that for this purpose he dedicated it from the start, the rest of the monies are (profane) [dedicated]<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the original text of the scribe, the one in brackets his own correction. It seems that the scribe felt compelled to correct the text since in the preceding sentence he wrote “profane”. But it seems more logical to assume that the first “profane” is in error and should be replaced by “dedicated” while in the second sentence “profane” is the correct expression.<br>In the preceding sentence “it” refers to the <i>Pesaḥ</i> animal, to which the sanctity of the subscription money is transferred while the unused part in the hand of the buyer is not touched by the transaction; it remains dedicated. But if the dedication of the money was conditional for the stated purpose of acquiring part of a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> the remaining money never was dedicated and is profane.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Samuel bar Abba asked: Think of it if he lets him subscribe gratis. Where do you have “becomes profane and is dedicated again”<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since no money changes hands, there is dedicated money whose sanctity can be transferred to parts of the <i>Pesaḥ.</i> In order to have a fully dedicated sacrifice it should be forbidden to invite non-paying guests for the <i>Pesaḥ.</i> This is a restriction which nowhere is mentioned and has to be rejected.</i>? The words of the rabbis imply that it does not become profane and then dedicated again. We have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 6:4" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.6.4">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 6:4</a>, dealing with wages of prostitution which may not be used for Temple dedications (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:19" href="/Deuteronomy.23.19"><i>Deut.</i> 23:19</a>). The reference is to the sentence following the quote, “if he (the customer) gave her (the prostitute) <i>sancta,</i> they are permitted (as sacrifice)” since as Heaven’s property they never became the prostitute’s.</i>: “If he gave her monies, they are permitted; wines, oils, and fine flour, or anything of whose kind one offers on the altar, are forbidden.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if he lets him<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This must be “her”. R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that a sacrifice in the hands of a prostitute is barred from the Temple, but if the prostitute is paid by a subscription to his <i>Pesaḥ</i> or his festival offering it is an acceptable arrangement. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 30b" href="/Temurah.30b"><i>Temurah</i> 30b</a>.</i> subscribe to his <i>Pesaḥ</i> and to his festival offering. This implies that it does not become profane and then dedicated again. If you would say that it becomes profane and then dedicated again, it would be forbidden as harlot’s wages<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.3.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 4:3</a>, detailing what A can legally do for B if A made a vow that B may not have any usufruct from him.
