The strength of Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac is from the following: “after the time of slaughtering the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> it shall be brought as well-being offering.” If you are saying, if he substituted for it on the Thirteenth and the substitute is brought as a well-being offering, is it not better to infer the {rules of the} substitute of a <i>Pesaḥ</i> from the {rules of the} <i>Pesaḥ</i> than to infer the [rules of the] substitute of a well-being sacrifice from the {rules of the} <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, which treats the substitute together with the lost and found animal is more logically consistent than R. Zeˋira’s correction; the Mishnah should be followed to the letter.</i><i>?</i>
Rebbi Hila in the name of Samuel: If the owner died<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">And no family member is subscribing. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 98a" href="/Pesachim.98a">Babli 98a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 9:16" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).9.16">Tosephta 9:16</a>.</i> on the Thirteenth, its body is offered as a well-being sacrifice. Why did he not say, on the Fourteenth? Any {animal} which is due to be sacrificed as <i>Pesaḥ,</i> its body is not offered as a well-being sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The rule that an animal which had been designated for a certain sacrifice, but at the moment of actual sacrifice was not used, never could be used for another sacrifice, has precedence over the rule which states that a designation of <i>Pesaḥ</i> automatically reverts to that of well-being sacrifice after the 14<sup>th</sup> of Nisan.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 4:1" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.4.1">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i>4<i>:</i>1</a>.</i>, we have stated: “The offspring of a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The purification offering from a private person is a female sheep or goat. If it produces a lamb, the lamb is a <i>sanctum</i> but since only its mother can be sacrificed, it may neither be redeemed nor used for any other sacrifice.</i>, and the substitute of a purification offering, and a purification offering whose owner died<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is dedicated, it may not be redeemed. As a purification offering, the dedication may not be transferred to the benefit of any other person (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishna <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:1</a>).</i>, …<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The portion missing here states that all the previously enumerated animals must be allowed to die since they cannot be used; even if they develop a defect they cannot be sold.</i>, and whose year has passed<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Sheep are acceptable as sacrifice only if one year old.</i>, and which was lost and found, defective<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The question whether one has to read “which was lost and found defective” or “which was lost and found, and the defective” is resolved in the discussion in favor of the first alternative. For this second group the Mishnah prescribes that they have to die if found after the owners had offered a substitute but if found before that time they are to be put out to graze, to be sold once no longer qualified as sacrifices.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah is discussed item by item, the hypothesis being that any animal which if dedicated as purification offering is left to die, as <i>Pesaḥ</i> is brought as well-being offering, but those put out to graze follow the same rules in both cases.</i>“The offspring of a purification offering,” as they disagreed: If the community dedicated a female, and so the prince who dedicated a she-goat, Rebbi Jeremiah said, it is not dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Since the purification offering of the community has to be a bull <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:14" href="/Leviticus.4.14"><i>(Lev.</i> 4:14</a>) and that of a prince <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:23" href="/Leviticus.4.23"><i>(Lev.</i> 4:23</a>) a ram. He holds that a dedication against the rules is void.</i> and its offspring may be offered; Rebbi Yose said, it is dedicated and its offspring must die<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The dedication is valid but obviously the animal cannot be used. Not only the calf but also its mother must die.</i>. The strength of Rebbi Yose is from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>baraita</i> also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 16a" href="/Temurah.16a">Babli, <i>Temurah</i> 16a</a>.</i>: “You cannot speak about the offspring of a purification offering of the community, since the community does not bring a female.” He only said, “you cannot speak about”; therefore if he dedicated it<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> does not state that there is no dedication.</i> it is dedicated. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Jeremiah said it correctly. Is it not Rebbi Simeon’s? As Rebbi Simeon said, a female as elevation offering is dedicated only for its money’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">An elevation offering has to be male <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:3" href="/Leviticus.1.3"><i>(Lev.</i> 1:3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:10" href="/Leviticus.1.10">10</a>). The dedication of a female is valid as gift to the Temple but invalid as sacrifice; therefore the animal has to be given to the Temple to be sold for its benefit. (A matter of dispute in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 20" href="/Temurah.20">Babli, <i>Temurah</i> 20a/b</a>.) R. Jeremiah may also hold that the value of the animals is dedicated to the Temple, but this does not imply any sacrificial status.</i>.
“And the substitute of a purification offering,” as they disagreed: if he substituted for it on the Thirteenth, Rebbi Zeˋira said, the substitute is brought as a well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since the dedication of a <i>Pesaḥ</i> animal which is not used automatically is that of well-being sacrifice.</i>; Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, the substitute is not brought as a well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">But it must be sent to graze, as spelled out in the Mishnah.</i>.
“And a purification offering whose owner died,” [has to die. Parallel to what] Rebbi Hila said in the name of Samuel, If the owner died<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">And no family member is subscribing. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 98a" href="/Pesachim.98a">Babli 98a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 9:16" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).9.16">Tosephta 9:16</a>.</i> on the Thirteenth, its body is offered as a well-being sacrifice. Why did he {not} say, on the Fourteenth? Everybody agrees that the body of any {animal} due to be sacrificed as <i>Pesaḥ,</i> is not offered as a well-being sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The rule that an animal which had been designated for a certain sacrifice, but at the moment of actual sacrifice was not used, never could be used for another sacrifice, has precedence over the rule which states that a designation of <i>Pesaḥ</i> automatically reverts to that of well-being sacrifice after the 14<sup>th</sup> of Nisan.</i>.
“And whose year has passed.” Parallel to what Rav Ḥisda said<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:4:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.4.12">Chapter 5, Notes 178–179</a>.</i>, If its year was completed between First and Second. Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: When the time for its propitiation was passed.
“And which was lost and found.” (Defective.) [Is it not certainly permitted?] Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: No body of a <i>Pesaḥ</i> is brought as well-being sacrifice except the one which was lost but found only after the owners propitiated<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In all other cases, while he agrees that the value of the animal is dedicated for well-being offerings, he demands that the animal be put out to grazing, to be sold once disqualified as sacrifice. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 97b" href="/Pesachim.97b">Babli 97b</a>.<br>The scribe’s text “defective” is certainly a scribal error from the following paragraph.</i>.
“And the defective.” Rebbi Zeˋira asked before Rebbi Yasa: Is it not reasonable that the defective quoted here is the one which was lost<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Since a defective animal cannot be dedicated as sacrifice; its dedication automatically would only be for its money’s worth. It is to be sold without delay for the benefit of the Temple.</i>? He said to him, I also am of this opinion.
If he dedicated his <i>Pesaḥ,</i> but it became lost, and he dedicated another in its stead. Before he could sacrifice the second, the first was found, and now both are at his disposal. There are Tannaim who state, it is meritorious to sacrifice the first; there are Tannaim who state, it is meritorious to sacrifice the second. Samuel stated as follows: He selects the better of the two, and the other one shall graze until it becomes unusable, then be sold, and he shall bring for its value well-being offerings on the Sixteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 9" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).9">Tosephta 9</a>:12 the text is: “If he dedicated his <i>Pesaḥ,</i> but it became lost, and he dedicated another in its stead. Before he could sacrifice the second, the first was found, and now both are at his disposal. He should bring the one he chooses following the words of the Sages. Rebbi Yose says, it is meritorious to sacrifice the first one, but if the second is better he should sacrifice that one and the first one shall graze until it becomes unusable, then be sold, and he shall bring for its value well-being offerings on the Sixteenth.”<br>The excess sacrifice cannot be brought before the Sixteenth since the Fifteenth is a holiday and only obligatory sacrifices are permitted on that day. No commentary known to me addresses the problem that first it is required that the animal graze for an indeterminate period but then be sacrificed on short notice on the second day after the first sacrifice. Probably one should read the text as “not before the Sixteenth”.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar says, itself shall be brought as well-being offering on the Sixteenth. Rebbi comes with Rebbi Eleazar, and Samuel stated according to his system<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The rule that an animal which had been designated for a certain sacrifice, but at the moment of actual sacrifice was not used, never could be used for another sacrifice, has precedence over the rule which states that a designation of <i>Pesaḥ</i> automatically reverts to that of well-being sacrifice after the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan.</i>, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 97a" href="/Pesachim.97a">Babli 97a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 22b" href="/Temurah.22b"><i>Temurah</i> 22b</a>, has a completely different tradition. One may not emend the Yerushalmi according to the Babli, in particular in this case where R. Salomon Adani in his commentary מלאכת שלמה to the Mishnah quotes the Yerushalmi from a Yemenite ms. in the form given here.</i>: “Rebbi says, no purification offering {dies}<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The word is missing in the text here but is quoted by R. Salomon Adani and is needed for understanding. The quote is based on the rule explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.6.6">Note 96</a>, that any animal left to die as purification sacrifice is brought as well-being sacrifice in the case of <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i> but one which was found after that the owner had been atoned<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The question whether one has to read “which was lost and found defective” or “which was lost and found, and the defective” is resolved in the discussion in favor of the first alternative. For this second group the Mishnah prescribes that they have to die if found after the owners had offered a substitute but if found before that time they are to be put out to graze, to be sold once no longer qualified as sacrifices.</i>, no money is thrown into the Dead Sea except which was found after the owner had been atoned<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">No animal had been bought yet but money was dedicated to be used for a purification sacrifice. Since gifts to Heaven are irrevocable (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a>), if the money was lost and only found after a substitute animal had been offered, it has to be destroyed in a way in which nobody could have future usufruct of any kind, such as throwing the coins into the Dead Sea where the metal will quickly be dissolved.</i>.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he dedicates a female as his <i>Pesaḥ</i> or a two year old male<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:5" href="/Exodus.12.5"><i>Ex.</i> 12:5</a> requires <i>a male yearling,</i> the dedications as <i>Pesaḥ</i> are invalid. Since the animal never was a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> the rule that a dedication of <i>Pesaḥ</i> implies a dedication as well-being offering cannot be applied.</i>, it shall graze until it becomes unusable, then be sold, and the monies given for voluntary sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple account from which elevation offerings are paid to cover periods where otherwise the altar would be empty.</i>. If he dedicates his <i>Pesaḥ</i> and dies, his son may not bring it after him as <i>Pesaḥ</i> but as well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Only if the <i>Pesaḥ</i> was left without subscribers; i. e., the son was not a subscriber to his father’s <i>Pesaḥ</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 9:17" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).9.17">Tosephta 9:17</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah after propitiation<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The question is why in the first sentence of the Mishnah one does not distinguish as always between the situations before and after the 14th of Nisan. The sentence cannot deal with the situation before Passover when everything stilli can be put in order.</i>, following {neither Rebbi nor<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">These words are missing in the ms., they are quoted by R. Salomon Adani from a Yemenite ms. of the Yerushalmi and are necessary for the understanding of the text. The agreement of Rebbi and R. Simeon is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 9:19" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).9.19">Tosephta 9:19</a>.</i> Rebbi Simeon, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 9:20" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).9.20">Tosephta 9:20</a>, about a female sheep dedicated as <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>: “Before Passover it shall graze until it becomes unusable, then be sold, and he shall bring for its value a <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Since the money always was intended for <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i><i>.</i> After Passover it shall be brought as well-being sacrifice. Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “R. Simeon ben Jehudah”.</i> says, before Passover it shall be sold without a defect, after Passover it shall be brought as well-being sacrifice.” Did we not understand that “the body of any {animal} due to be sacrificed as <i>Pesaḥ</i> is not offered as a well-being sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The rule that an animal which had been designated for a certain sacrifice, but at the moment of actual sacrifice was not used, never could be used for another sacrifice, has precedence over the rule which states that a designation of <i>Pesaḥ</i> automatically reverts to that of well-being sacrifice after the 14<sup>th</sup> of Nisan.</i>”? Explain it if he dedicated coins.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a <i>Pesaḥ</i> was mingled with sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Other than well-being sacrifices, since a <i>Pesaḥ</i> mixed with well-being sacrifices is brought as well-being sacrifice on the 16th of Nisan.</i>, all of them shall graze until they become unusable, then be sold, and for the value of the best among them he shall bring one of each kind and lose the difference on his account<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">He redeems all animals and but spends money in a multiple of the proceeds obtained for the best in the flock.</i>. If it was mingled with firstlings, Rebbi Simeon says, if it is a company of priests, they shall eat<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Temple service for <i>Pesaḥ</i> and firstlings is identical. The majority disagrees since firstlings may be eaten during two days and the night in between but a <i>Pesaḥ</i> only half a night.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah also is a paragraph in <i>Maˋaser Šeni</i> 3:2 (<b>ם</b>) Notes 28–36. Sabbatical produce must be consumed by humans, Second Tithe money must be used to buy produce to be eaten in purity at the place of the Temple. The situation is similar to that of a <i>Pesaḥ</i> mingled with a firstling; the question is whether the disagreement of R. Simeon and the rabbis carries over to tithes and Sabbatical produce.</i> It was stated: “One does not buy Sabbatical [produce] with tithe money.” Rebbi Yose said, that is a disagreement. Rebbi Jonah said, that is the opinion of everybody. The eaters of heave are careful. Rebbi Ḥananiah objected before Rebbi Mana: Did we not state: “If they were mingled with firstlings, Rebbi Simeon says, if it is a company of Cohanim they should eat.” And we have stated on that, they should be eaten following the more stringent rules. He said to him, eaters of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> in its time are as careful as the eaters of heave. You should know this since we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:14. “They should be roasted before nightfall”.</i>: “One roasts meat, onion, or egg only that they should be roasted“, but we have stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:15, contradicting the preceding Mishnah unless one accepts R. Mana’s argument (which is that of his father R. Jonah).</i> “One lowers the <i>Pesaḥ</i> into the oven at nightfall.”

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A group whose <i>Pesaḥ</i> was lost and they said to one of them go, and search for it, and slaughter for us. He went, found<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The original sheep.</i>, and slaughtered, and they bought and slaughtered. If his was slaughtered first, he eats of his, and they eat with him<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Since all of them are subscribers to the one.</i>; but if theirs was slaughtered first, they eat of theirs, and he eats of his<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">By their action they cancelled their subscription to the first sheep, but he never was involved with the second.</i>. If it is not known which one was slaughtered first, or they slaughtered simultaneously, they eat of theirs, but he does not eat with them<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is not known whether they still are subscribers to their sheep or whether the subscription was cancelled by their action.</i>. His is brought to be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">This disposal of any disabled <i>sanctum</i> is by burning outside the sacred domain.</i>, and he is not liable for a Second <i>Pesaḥ.</i><br>If he told them, if I would tarry, slaughter for me; he went, found, and slaughtered, and they bought and slaughtered. If theirs was slaughtered first, they eat of theirs, and he eats with them; but if his was slaughtered first, he eats of his and they eat of theirs. If it is not known which one was slaughtered first, or they slaughtered simultaneously, he eats of his, but they he may not eat with him; and theirs is brought to be burned, but they are not liable for a Second <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Mishnah in the Yerushalmi is that of the <i>editio princeps,</i> it is not part of the manuscript text. It is obvious that this Mishnah was copied twice; the text of the second version is that of the Babli and Maimonides. Since the Mishnah is not discussed, it is impossible to decide between the versions. Maimonides declares the Mishnah to be obvious and not in need of explanation.</i><i>.</i><br>If he told them, if I would tarry, go and slaughter for me; he went, found, and slaughtered, and they bought and slaughtered. If theirs was slaughtered first, they eat of theirs, and he eats with them<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Since he does not renounce his membership in the group and their <i>Pesaḥ</i> was slaughtered first, which makes his redundant.</i>; but if his was slaughtered first, he eats of his and they eat of theirs. If it is not known which one was slaughtered first, or they slaughtered simultaneously, they eat of theirs, and he may not eat with them; and his is brought to be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Since he had made them his agents to slaughter a <i>Pesaḥ,</i> the slaughter of the first makes his redundant.</i>, but he is not liable for a Second <i>Pesaḥ.</i><br>If he said to them and they said to him, all of them eat of the first. If he did not talk to them nor did they talk to him, they are not responsible for one another.<br>Two groups whose <i>Pesaḥim</i> were commingled<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">When the animals are alive and it is possible to change affiliations.</i>, each of them draws one for them. One of each group joins the other and they<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Each group has to make a separate declaration.</i> declare: If this <i>Pesaḥ</i> is ours, your hand is removed from yours and you are subscribing to ours, and if this <i>Pesaḥ</i> is yours, our hand is removed from ours and we are subscribing to yours.<br>The same holds for five groups of five members each, or ten of ten, one exchanges members of each group and so they declare<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">If <i>n Pesaḥim</i> were commingled, each group must have at last <i>n</i> members so that one member of each group joins another. They all have to make the declaration formulated in the previous Mishnah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The statement in this and the following Mishnah that one is not liable for a Second <i>Pesaḥ</i> since the blood of their sheep was correctly poured on the altar, even though it turned out that they could not eat from it.</i> is Rebbi Nathan’s, since Rebbi Nathan said, one fulfills one’s obligation by pouring without eating.
Bar Qappara stated: Silence behoves the Sages, and so much more the fools. And also Solomon says<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 17:28" href="/Proverbs.17.28"><i>Prov</i>. 17:28</a>.</i>, <i>also a silent dolt is considered to be wise,</i> and it is not necessary to say, a silent sage<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since the only uncomplicated case in the Mishnaiot is when nobody says anything. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 99a" href="/Pesachim.99a">Babli 99a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the <i>Pesaḥim</i> of two people were intermingled<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">And no other people subscribed to these <i>Pesaḥim.</i> They cannot simply re-subscribe each to one of the animals since then the dedicated <i>Pesaḥim</i> would for a moment be left without subscribers and therefore disqualified.</i>, each of them takes one, and each one invites another from the street to subscribe with him<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Then there are two groups of two people whose <i>Pesaḥim</i> were intermingled and one may apply the rule spelled out in Mishnah 11.</i>. One of each group joins the other, and each one declares<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">To the one who comes to join the other group.</i> as follows: If this <i>Pesaḥ</i> be mine, your hand is removed from yours and you are subscribing to mine, and if this <i>Pesaḥ</i> be yours, my hand is removed from mine and I am subscribing to yours.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann writes: “I cannot explain this paragraph without major emendations.” In fact, as R. Yose the Amora notes in the second part of the paragraph, Mishnah 13 must follow R. Yose the Tanna, since according to R. Jehudah every <i>Pesaḥ</i> belongs to a group of at least 2 subscribers and therefore should be treated following Mishnah 11. A minimally invasive emendation would be to read in R. Joḥanan ’s statement: “This is not <i>R.</i> Jehudah’s.”</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, this is Rebbi Jehudah’s as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.7.1">Mishnah 8:7</a>.</i>, “One does not slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> for a single person, the words of Rebbi Jehudah; but Rebbi Yose permits.” Therefore not of the Fourteenth. Did not Rebbi Joḥanan say, this is Rebbi Jehudah’s? Rebbi Yose said, does it proclaim that it is Rebbi Jehudah’s? As we have stated there, “One does not slaughter the <i>Pesaḥ</i> for a single person, the words of Rebbi Jehudah; but Rebbi Yose permits.”

Chapter 10



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In the afternoon of Passover Eve close to the time of afternoon prayers one should not eat until it gets dark<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The time of the usual afternoon prayers starts at about 3:30 pm. Close to the time would be about 3 pm. One should not eat in the afternoon so as to eat with appetite the formal meal in the night.</i>. And even the poorest person in Israel should not eat until he lies on a couch<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since in those times every formal dinner was eaten on couches. סבב really means “to form a circle”, since the couches were arranged in almost circular fashion in the <i>triclinium.</i></i>. One shall not give him less than four cups of wine, even from the communal tray<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The תַּמְחוּי is the communal chest where contributions to charity are collected in kind. Four cups of wine have to be drunk by every participant of the <i>Seder</i> ceremony in the evening of the first day of Passover. For the entire Chapter, see the author’s commentary in <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah,</i> Northvale NJ 1995, pp. 183–377.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “In the afternoon of Passover Eve close to the time of afternoon prayers,” etc. The Mishnah is Rebbi Jehudah’s, as it was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 99b" href="/Pesachim.99b">Babli 99b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i> 5:1</a>. The Babli denies that in the special case of Passover Eve R. Yose disagrees.</i> “On Sabbath Eve starting at the time of afternoon prayers one should not taste anything until nightfall in order to enter the Sabbath with an appetite, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Yose says, he continues eating until he finishes.” <sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 100a" href="/Pesachim.100a">Babli 100a</a>.</i>“One interrupts<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah requires that one formally end the meal by reciting Grace and, probably after Evening prayers, restarts the meals by reciting <i>qidduš.</i></i> for the Sabbath, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Yose says, one does not interrupt.” <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 100a" href="/Pesachim.100a">Babli 100a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i> 5:2</a>.</i>“It happened that Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel and Rebbi Yose ben Halaphta were lying on couches in Acco on Sabbath Eve, and in the meantime the Sabbath became holy. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said to Rebbi Yose ben Halaphta, do you want to interrupt for the Sabbath? He answered him, the entire day you preferred my words over those of Rebbi Jehudah, and now you are preferring the words of Rebbi Jehudah over mine, <i>also to rape the queen in my palace</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 7:8" href="/Esther.7.8"><i>Esth.</i> 7:8</a>.</i><i>?</i> He said to him, therefore we shall not interrupt, that practice in Israel should not be fixed following R. Jehudah. They did not leave there before they fixed practice following Rebbi Yose.” Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: These are the words of Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Yose. But the words of the Sages, one spreads a cloth and makes <i>qiddush</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This disagrees with R. Yose since one acknowledges the Sabbath by a short interruption of the meal, it also disagrees with R. Jehudah since one is not required to formally close the meal or to recite Grace.</i><i>.</i> May one eat kinds of cookies<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are eaten without bread, they cannot be considered a meal.</i>? May one eat kinds of dried fruit<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τράγημα, τό.</i>? Rebbi Yudan the Prince bathed and became thirsty. He asked Rebbi Mana, since I am thirsty, may I drink? He told him, Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: A person may not taste anything before nightfall<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore the previous questions have to be answered in the negative. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 107b" href="/Pesachim.107b">Babli disagrees, 107b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Levi said, he who eats <i>mazzah</i> on Passover Eve is like one who sleeps with his betrothed in his father-in-law’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">After the preliminary but before the definitive marriage, when she is forbidden to him.</i>, and he who sleeps with his betrothed in his father-in-law’s house is flogged. It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra says, both bread and <i>mazzah</i> are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">On the entire 14th of Nisan, even in the morning when leavened matter still is permitted.</i>. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Rebbi ate neither bread nor <i>mazzah.</i> No <i>mazzah</i> because of that of Rebbi Levi, and no bread because of that of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra. Was Rebbi a student of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra? No, he was a student of Rebbi Jacob ben Qorshai. But it was because he was a firstborn<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Even though this reason is refuted in the following and it is not mentioned in the Babli, under the influence of Good Friday the fast of the firstborn has become a holy rite <i>(Tur Šulḥan Arukh Orah Hayyim</i> §470).</i>. Rebbi Mana said, my father Rebbi Jonah was a firstborn and he ate. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, it is neither for this nor for that reason; Rebbi was asthenic<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀσθενής. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 108a" href="/Pesachim.108a">Babli 108a</a>, about Rav Sheshet.</i> and when he ate during the day, he could not eat in the night. Why did he not eat during this day? To enter the Sabbath with appetite<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore it seems that Rebbi fasted every Friday.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the detailed discussion of the Mishnah.</i> Rebbi Levi said, because usually slaves eat while standing, and here one is forced to eat lying on couches, to proclaim that they went from slavery to freedom. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: This olive-sized bit with which a person fulfills his obligation on Passover he must eat lying down. Rebbi Yose asked before Rebbi Simon, even a slave before his master, even a woman before her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">For the entire paragraph, see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 108a" href="/Pesachim.108a">Babli 108a</a>. According to the Babli, only aristocratic women have to lie down.</i>? He said to him, great personality, that is all I heard.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, because it is not agreeable for a person to eat from public assistance, and here even from the tray<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In general, people are encouraged not to use public assistance even if they are entitled to it. But for the Passover <i>Seder</i> it is an obligation of the poor to apply even to the distribution of food in kind, which is more demeaning than distribution of money, if otherwise he could not fulfill the requirements of the meal.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 109a" href="/Pesachim.109a">Babli 109a</a>.</i>: “A man has to make his family happy on a feast of pilgrimage. How does he make them happy? With wine. Rebbi Jehudah used to say, women with what is appropriate for them, and minors with what is appropriate for them. Women with what is appropriate for them, for example shoes<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Reading מסאנין. N. J. Epstein reads ביסנין, Greek βύσσινα, “(garments) made of byssos, fine linen”. It probably is better to read “shoes”, which are accessories like belts, rather than “luxury linen garments” which are not quite what the Babli recommends for women in the Land of Israel.</i> and belts, and minors with what is appropriate for them, for example nuts and pistachios.” They said, Rebbi Tarphon acted in this way.
<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For an explanation of this paragraph, see the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah,</i> pp. 186–187.</i> From where the Four Cups? Rebbi Joḥanan  in the name of Rebbi Benaiah: Corresponding to the four deliveries<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 6:7-8" href="/Exodus.6.7-8"><i>Ex.</i> 6:7–8</a>.</i>: <i>Therefore, say to the Children of Israel, I am the Eternal, and I shall take you out,</i> etc. <i>And I shall take you as My people,</i> etc. <i>I shall take you, I shall save you, I shall free you, I shall take you.</i> Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, corresponding to the four cups of Pharao: <i>The cup of Pharao was in my hand; I took the grapes and squeezed them into Pharao ’s cup, and gave the cup in Pharao ’s hand. You will give the cup in the hand of Pharao</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 40:11" href="/Genesis.40.11"><i>Gen.</i> 40:11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 40:13" href="/Genesis.40.13">13</a>. The relation to Passover is explained in Targum Pseudo-Jonathan <i>ad loc.</i></i><i>.</i> Rebbi Levi said, corresponding to the four kingdoms. But our teachers say, corresponding to the four cups of doom that the Holy One, praise to Him, will make the Gentiles drink at the end of days. <i>Truly, so said the Eternal, the God of Israel, to me: take this cup of the wine of wrath</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 23:15" href="/Jeremiah.23.15"><i>Jer.</i> 23:15</a>.</i><i>. The golden cup of Babylon is in the hand of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 51:7" href="/Jeremiah.51.7"><i>Jer</i>. 51:7</a>.</i><i>. Truly a cup is in the hand of the Eternal, intoxicating wine, fully to be mixed; He shall sprinkle from it but its dregs shall be drunk, squeezed to the last, by all the wicked of the earth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 75:9" href="/Psalms.75.9"><i>Ps.</i> 75:9</a>.</i><i>. He shall let coals rain on the wicked; fire, sulphur, and burning wind is the portion of their cup</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 11:6" href="/Psalms.11.6"><i>Ps.</i> 11:6</a>.</i><i>.</i> What does <i>the portion of their cup</i> mean? R. Abun said: a double cup<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διπλόν ποτήριον, τό.</i> like the double cup taken after a thermal bath. And in accordance with this correspondingly the Holy One, praised be He, will let Israel drink four cups of consolation at the End of Days: <i>The Eternal is the portion of my part and my cup</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 16:5" href="/Psalms.16.5"><i>Ps.</i> 16:5</a>.</i><i>. You anointed my head with oil; my cup is overflowing</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 23:5" href="/Psalms.23.5"><i>Ps.</i>23:5</a>.</i><i>. I shall lift up the cup of salvations</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116:13" href="/Psalms.116.13"><i>Ps.</i>116:13</a>.</i> counts for two.
<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 8:1:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.8.1.2-8"><i>Šabbat</i> 8, Notes 23–50</a>.</i>“One who brings out wine to mix a cup,” etc. Rebbi Zeˋira asked Rebbi Joshiah, what is the measure of cups? He told him, let us infer the hidden from the explicit since Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, the Four Cups which they said add up to an Italic <i>quartarius</i> of wine.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 18:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.18.1.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 18:1</a>.</i>: “One removes four or five baskets.” Rebbi Zeˋira asked Rebbi Joshiah, what is the measure of baskets? He told him, let us infer the hidden from the explicit since we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.3.2.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 3:2</a>.</i>: “The contributions for the Temple were removed in three baskets of three <i>seah</i> each.”
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it is Rebbi Jehudah’s. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 8:10" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).8.10">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 8:10</a>.</i> “Water, a gulp; Rebbi Jehudah says, for the mixer’s mixing. Mixed by how much? Let us hear from the following: “Water. a gulp; Rebbi Jehudah says, for mixing a cup. Wine. a gulp; Rebbi Jehudah says, for mixing a cup.” What is the measure of cups? Rebbi Abun said, τέταρτον<sup class="footnote-marker">37a</sup><i class="footnote">Τεταρτεύς, -έως, ὁ, or τεταρτηρόν, τὸ, a measure of capacity.</i> is a quarter.
May one drink them together? Since Rebbi Joḥanan  said about <i>Hallel,</i> if he heard it in the synagogue he has fulfilled his obligation, this implies that if he drank them together he fulfilled his obligation. May one drink them with interruptions? They said that he should drink, not that he should get drunk. If he drinks them with interruptions, would he not become drunk? May one fulfill his obligation with Sabbatical wine? Rebbi Hoshaia stated, one may fulfill his obligation with Sabbatical wine. May one fulfill his obligation with spiced wine? Since Bar Qappara stated, spiced wine is like wine, which implies that one may fulfill his obligation with spiced wine. May one fulfill his obligation with mixed wine? Since Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: the Four Cups which they prescribed, one may fulfill his obligation either with unmixed or mixed, on condition that it have the taste and looks of wine. Rebbi Jeremiah said, it is meritorious to fulfill one’s obligation with red wine. What is the reason? <i>Do not see wine when it shows its red color</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 23:31" href="/Proverbs.23.31"><i>Prov.</i> 23:31</a>.</i><i>.</i> It was stated, cooked for spice. May one fulfill his obligation with cooked wine? Rebbi Jonah said, one may fulfill his obligation with cooked wine. Rebbi Jonah follows his own opinion, since Rebbi Jonah drank his four cups in the Passover night and had a headache until Pentecost.
Rebbi Jehudah bar Ilai drank his four cups in the Passover night and had a headache until Tabernacles. A lady saw that his face was shiny. She said to him, old man, old man, one of three things applies to you. Either you are drunk from wine, or you are lending on interest, or you are raising pigs. He answered her, this woman’s spirit shall be blown away, not one of these three things applies to me, but my learning is ever present with me, as it is written, <i>a man’s wisdom illuminates his face.</i>
Rebbi Abbahu descended to Tiberias. The students of Rebbi Joḥanan  saw that his face was shining. They said before Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Abbahu found a treasure. He asked them, why? They told him, his face is shining. He said to them, maybe he understood a new teaching. He came to visit him. He asked him, what new teaching did you hear? He said, an old Tosephta. He recited about him, <i>a man’s wisdom illuminates his face</i>
Rebbi Ḥanina said, the <i>log</i> of the Torah is the old Sepphorean eighth of fish sauce. Rebbi Jonah said, I know it. In the House of Rebbi Yannai they were measuring honey with it. It was stated, half of the old Tiberian eighth. Rebbi Joḥanan  said, this one we used. Why did he not say, the old one? Because it was in his days. Some are saying, it was small, then was enlarged, and diminished, but it was not made small as before. What is the measure of a cup? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi, Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Samuel: Two fingers by two fingers high a finger and a half and a third of a finger<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This value of 2×2×1<sup>5</sup>/<sub>6</sub> = <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.10.2-4">7<sup>1</sup>/</a><sub>3</sub> cubic digits compares to a Babylonian value of 10.8 cubic digits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 109a" href="/Pesachim.109a">Babli 109a</a>). A cubit is 24 digits. Various cubits were in use; therefore it is difficult to define this volume in modern terms. The variability of the cubit is mirrored in the Greek world by the many values given for the <i>stadion,</i> which varies from 148 m for the (Egyptian) <i>itinerant stadion</i> to the Greek sacramental 184 m. Based on the itinerant <i>stadion</i> and 1 mile = 7.5 stadia = 2000 cubits, one would obtain 91 cm<sup>3</sup> for the cup.<br>In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.5.3"><i>Terumot</i> 10:7 (Note 80</a>), a <i>log</i> is defined as (volume corresponding to a weight of) 200 denar. If volume of water is intended, this would make a cup of 155 cm<sup>3</sup>. In <i>Terumot</i> 5:3, the <i>log</i> is defined as the volume of 4 eggs, the <i>modius</i> (16 <i>log)</i> as 96 eggs (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 83a" href="/Eruvin.83a">Babli, <i>Eruvin</i> 83a</a>, 217 eggs, which, however, refers not to the Roman but the Syrian <i>modius,</i> twice the volume of the Roman). For the standard <i>modius</i> of 8.536 1, this gives the volume of a cup as the standard <i>quartarius</i> of 133 cm<sup>3</sup> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.1.1">Note 1</a>).</i>. It was stated, dried like the volume of an olive, the words of Rebbi Nathan. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Nathan follows Rebbi Simeon. Just as Rebbi Simeon says, a <i>quartarius,</i> so Rebbi Nathan says, a <i>quartarius,</i> when it jells it will have the volume of an olive.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One mixes him<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The celebrant of the <i>Seder.</i> Everybody else’s cup also is filled but only the celebrant recites the benediction of <i>Qiddush.</i> The Mishnah is paralleled by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.1.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 8:1</a> (referring to the Sabbath). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:2:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.2.2-5">Halakhah 2</a> in its entirety is paralleled in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.1.2-10"><i>Berakhot</i> 8:1</a> (<b>ב</b>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:1:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.1.1-4">Notes 2–18</a>.</i> the first cup. The House of Shammai say, he pronounces the benediction of the day and after that he pronounces the benediction over the wine. But the House of Hillel say, he pronounces the benediction over the wine and after that he pronounces the benediction of the day.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is the reason of the House of Shammai? The Sanctification of the Day causes the wine to be served; he was obligated for the Sanctification of the Day before the wine was served. What is the reason of the House of Hillel? The wine causes the Sanctification of the Day to be recited. Another explanation: The wine is frequent, Sanctification is less frequent.
Rebbi Yose said: From the words of both of them it follows that for wine and <i>Havdalah,</i> the wine comes first. Is not the reason of the House of Shammai that the Sanctification of the Day causes the wine to be served? But here, <i>Havdalah</i> does {not}<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <i>Berakhot,</i> missing here, but logically necessary.</i> cause the wine to be served; wine therefore has precedence. Is not the reason of the House of Hillel that the wine is frequent, Sanctification of the Day is less frequent? Also here, wine is frequent and <i>Havdalah</i> is less frequent: the wine has precedence. Rebbi Mana said: From the words of both of them it follows that for wine and <i>Havdalah, Havdalah</i> comes first. Is not the reason of the House of Shammai that he was obligated for the Sanctification of the Day before the wine came? Also here, since he is obligated for <i>Havdalah</i> before the wine came, <i>Havdalah</i> has precedence. Is not the reason of the House of Hillel that the wine causes the Sanctification of the Day to be recited? But here, the wine does {not}<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <i>Berakhot,</i> missing here, but logically necessary.</i> cause <i>Havdalah</i> to be recited: <i>Havdalah</i> has precedence.
Rebbi Zeˋira said: One infers from the words of both of them that one may make <i>Havdalah</i> without wine but one may not make <i>Qiddush</i> without wine. That is the opinion of Rebbi Zeˋira since Rebbi Zeˋira said: One makes <i>Havdalah</i> on alcoholic beverages but one goes from one place to another to hear <i>Oiddush.</i>
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said: There, they have the custom that at a place without wine, the reader descends before the Ark, recites one benediction that contains seven, and closes by “He Who sanctifies Israel and the Sabbath Day”.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They bring before him vegetables and Romaine lettuce<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">“Romaine lettuce” is used as stand-in for all bitter herbs admissible for the <i>Seder</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.5.1">Mishnah 2:5</a>. General vegetables are mentioned only in the Mishnah of the Yerushalmi; from the Halakhah which mentions that one is permitted non-bitter vegetables for the first dip it seems that they were not mentioned in the Mishnah underlying the Halakhah.</i>. One dips Romaine lettuce<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As an appetizer, since on Passover the cookies usually served as appetizers may not be used; cf. <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah,</i> pp. 228–233.</i>, until he comes to filling the bread<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">After the discussion of the Exodus, described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.4.1">Mishnaiot 4</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:1:1-10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.1.1-10.1">5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:1:1-4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.1.1-4.1">6</a>. “Filling the bread” refers to the meal where in the absence of forks and knives one ate everything as fillers of pitta-bread; in this case, freshly baked soft <i>mazzah.</i></i>, when one brings before him<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Unless the entire plate was brought on a table at the start.</i> <i>mazzah,</i> and Romaine lettuce, and <i>haroset</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">A moist mixture of fruits and spices in which the bitter herbs have to be dipped.</i><i>,</i> and two dishes<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, a remembrance of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> and the festival offerings of Temple times.</i>, even though <i>haroset</i> is not an obligation; Rebbi Eliezer ben Rebbi Sadoq says, it is an obligation. In the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In Jerusalem in Temple times.</i> one brings before him the body of the <i>Pesaḥ.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One has to dip Romaine lettuce twice. Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One need not dip Romaine lettuce twice<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that after the <i>Qiddush</i> one has to eat vegetable, with the appropriate benediction of “Him Who created the produce of the earth,” and at the start of the meal one has to eat bitter herbs, in fulfillment of the biblical obligation, without benediction on the bitter herbs as food. The only problem is whether the benediction for the food must be for bitter herbs, or whether any vegetable is fine, and the benediction can be intended to include the bitter herbs consumed later. There are two conflicting traditions in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if he did not dip the first time, he has to dip the second time<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the first dipping is optional, an opinion rejected by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 114b" href="/Pesachim.114b">Babli 114b</a>.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish; “On all other nights we dip once, this night twice.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is of one opinion with Bar Qappara; the Mishnah disagrees with Bar Qappara: On all other nights we dip with bread<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">One fills the pitta with food and dips the bread in sauce; without knives and forks this is the usual way to eat dinner. To dip vegetable without bread is uncommon.</i>, but here we dip it by itself. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan, one fulfills his obligation with <i>mazzah,</i> whether he intended it or did not intend<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Since in his opinion a biblical obligation is discharged when the required action is completed, with or without particular intention, then it should be impossible to eat bitter herbs the second time in fulfillment of the biblical obligation since the obligation already was discharged unintentionally with the first dipping.</i>. But here since he was lying on a couch, the presumption is that he intended<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">But for vegetables this cannot be presupposed.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah objected before Rebbi Zeˋira, does not the Mishnah disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? “one brings before him <i>mazzah,</i> and Romaine lettuce, and <i>haroset,</i> … even though <i>haroset</i> is not an obligation.” Romaine lettuce is an obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This question implies that in his version of the Mishnah, “vegetables” were not mentioned. Since <i>haroset</i> is singled out as optional, it follows that bitter herbs are not optional even for the first dipping, and that a benediction for non-bitter vegetables as food would not be valid for bitter herbs.</i>. He said to him, since Rav dips with beets<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore it is preferable to use some other vegetable for the benediction over food and reserve bitter herbs for the biblically ordained ceremony. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 115a" href="/Pesachim.115a">Babli 115a</a>.</i>.
The traders of Jerusalem used to say, “come and buy spices for the meritorious deed.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>haroset.</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 116a" href="/Pesachim.116a">Babli 116a</a>.</i>” The family of Issy in the same of Issy: Why is it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>haroset.</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 116a" href="/Pesachim.116a">Babli 116a</a>.</i> called “pounded”? Because one pounds<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The spices and fruits are pounded to produce <i>haroset.</i></i> for it. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, it has to be thick. His word implies that it is a remembrance of the mortar<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The mortar used by the Israelite slaves in Egypt.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, it has to be soft<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">It needs added wine. (In Medieval sources, such as <i>Or Zaruaˋ</i>2, §256, the text is very different.)</i>. This word implies that it is a remembrance of the blood.
It was stated: Outside Jerusalem one needs two dishes<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The common usage is a roasted bone and an egg. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 114b" href="/Pesachim.114b">Babli 114b</a>.</i>, one as remembrance of the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> and one as remembrance of the holiday offering.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One mixes him the second cup, and here the son asks<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">He is supposed spontaneously to ask why this dinner is different from the usual set-up.</i>. If the son does not know how to ask, his father instructs him<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The following catalogue is an example of the questions with which the instruction may start. The order of the questions is the same in Maimonides’s text, different in the Babli.</i>: What is the difference between this night and all other nights? For every night we dip once<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.3.2">Note 52</a>.</i>, but this night we dip twice. For every night we eat leavened and unleavened bread, but this night only unleavened. For in all other nights we eat meat roasted, preserved<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">By extended cooking. This always is mentioned as a separate category of preparation of food.</i>, or cooked, but in this night only roasted. According to the son’s understanding the father teaches him<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Along the lines explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.4.1">Mishnah 5</a>. If children or women do not understand Hebrew, this implies the duty to translate the Haggadah text.</i>. He starts with ignominy<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.5.2">Halakhah 5</a>.</i> and ends with praise and explains from “a lost Aramean was my father” until he finishes the paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:5-8" href="/Deuteronomy.26.5-8"><i>Deut.</i> 26:5–8</a>. The Babli text, “until he finishes the paragraph completely”, would instruct to include also v. 9, not included in the Haggadah text identical with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:5-8" href="/Deuteronomy.26.5-8"><i>Sifry Deut.</i> on <i>Deut.</i> 26:5–8</a>.</i>.<br>Rabban Gamliel used to say, anybody who did not teach these three words on Passover did not fulfill his duty, <i>Pesaḥ, mazzah,</i> and bitter herbs. <i>Pesaḥ</i> because the Omnipresent passed over the houses of our forefathers in Egypt, <i>mazzah</i> because our forefathers were freed from Egypt<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A reference to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:39" href="/Exodus.12.39"><i>Ex.</i> 12:39</a> is understood, but is not included in any reliable Mishnah ms.</i>. Bitter herbs because the Egyptians embittered the lives of our forefathers in Egypt. In every generation a person is required to see himself as if he himself had gone out of Egypt, as it was said, <i>you shall tell your son on that day, this is on account of what the Eternal did for me when I left Egypt.</i> Therefore we are required to thank, sing His praise, laud, glorify, exalt, praise in perpetuity, to glorify, Him who did all these miracles for us<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since everybody has to consider himself as if he left Egypt, it is appropriate that here “our forefathers” are not mentioned, in contrast to the Mishnah in the Babli.</i> and led us from slavery to freedom; let us say before Him <i>Hallelujah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Hallel, Pss.</i> 113–118.</i><i>.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian parallel, used in the common Haggadah, is in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Bo, Pisqa</i> 18. There the answers to the wise and stupid children are switched.</i> “The Torah spoke about Four Children, a wise child, a wicked child, a stupid child, and a child who does not know how to ask. What does the wise child say? <i>What are the testimonials, the ordinances, and the laws, that the Eternal, our God, commanded us</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:20" href="/Deuteronomy.6.20"><i>Deut.</i> 6:20</a>. The text of R. Ḥiyya and the Mekhilta is that underlying the LXX; the masoretic text has the child asking, <i>what are the testimonials, … commanded you.</i> This is a natural formulation for a child born later, asking a member of the generation which first received the Torah. The formulation of the Yerushalmi/Mekhilta was common in all Haggadot until corrected by pedantic editors who copied the masoretic text, disregarding talmudic tradition in Biblical quotes.<br>Following Nahmanides, the “testimonials” are obligations in remembrance of His miracles, the “ordinances” are those commandments for which no reason is hinted at in the Torah, and the “laws” are the legal instructions to the court.</i><i>?</i> Also you shall tell him, <i>with a strong hand did the Eternal lead us out of Egypt, the house of slaves</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:14" href="/Exodus.13.14"><i>Ex.</i> 13:14</a>, the biblical answer to the question of the stupid child. The answer addresses the reason why one is obligated to follow the rules of Jewish worship, which is much more expensive than gentile rites.</i><i>.</i> What does the wicked son say? <i>What does this service mean to you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:26" href="/Exodus.12.26"><i>Ex.</i> 12:26</a>.</i><i>?</i> What is this exertion which you impose on us every (moment) [year]? Since he excluded himself from the community, also you shall tell him, <i>because of this did the Eternal do for me when I left Egypt</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:8" href="/Exodus.13.8"><i>Ex.</i> 13:8</a>.</i>. For me, He did it, for that man He did not do it. If that man had been in Egypt, he would not have been worthy ever to be redeemed. What does the stupid child say? <i>What is this</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:14" href="/Exodus.13.14"><i>Ex.</i> 13:14</a>. The question is not about Passover but about the obligation of sacrificing the firstlings of the flock. In order that the child should understand the answer given to the wise child, he first has to learn by rote all the rules, up to the last Mishnah dealing with the rules of the Passover celebration.</i><i>?</i> Tell him the rules of Passover, that one may not follow the <i>Pesaḥ</i>ἐπὶ κῶμον<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A (frequently drunken) revelry, characteristic of Greek celebrations and most objectionable to Jews.</i>. What means ἐπὶ κῶμον? That one not leave one company and join another company. With the child who does not know how to ask<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The child who does not know how to ask is alluded to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:8" href="/Exodus.13.8"><i>Ex.</i> 13:8</a>, <i>you must tell your child on that day as follows: …,</i> without a question preceding.</i>, you have to begin and initiate with him.” Rebbi Yose said, that is what the Mishnah says, “if the son does not know how to ask, his father instructs him.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How far does he say? The House of Shammai say, up to <i>a happy mother of children</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113" href="/Psalms.113"><i>Ps.</i> 113</a> only.</i><i>.</i> But the House of Hillel are saying, up to <i>the pebble into a source of water</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 114" href="/Psalms.114"><i>Ps.</i> 114</a>, starting with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113" href="/Psalms.113"><i>Ps.</i> 113</a>.</i><i>,</i> and he finishes with “redemption.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Text of the benediction for redemption prescribed at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.5.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>”<br>Rebbi Tarphon says, <sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Text of the benediction for redemption prescribed at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.5.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>“Who freed us and freed our forefathers from Egypt and let us reach this night”, and does not seal<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">For him the required benediction is “short”, starting with the formula “Praise to You, Eternal, our God, King of the Universe,” followed by a single sentence.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, <sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">This text is in addition to R. Tarphon’s. It is “long”, closing with a separate sentence starting with “Praise to You, Eternal”.</i>“so Eternal, our God, let us reach other festivals coming towards us in peace, happy in the rebuilding of Your city, enjoying Your service, and the renewal of Your Temple. There may we eat from the <i>Pesaḥ</i> and well-being offerings whose blood will reach to the wall of Your altar for pleasure and we shall thank You for our redemption. Praise to You, Eternal, Who redeems Israel.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This explains the expression in the Mishnah, “he starts with ignominy.” The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 116a" href="/Pesachim.116a">Babli 116a</a> reports that this is the Galilean version; the autochthonous Babylonian version was different. (The name tradition in modern prints of the Babli is unreliable; it is discussed in detail in <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i> pp. 252–255.)</i> Rav said, a priori he has to start with<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 24:2-4" href="/Joshua.24.2-4"><i>Jos.</i> 24:2–4</a>.</i> <i>your forefathers always lived on the far side of the River,</i> etc. <i>But I took your father Abraham from the far side of the River,</i> etc <i>And I increased.</i> Rebbi Aḥa said, the <i>ketib</i> is וארב. How may quarrels I made for him before I gave him Isaac<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The word is explained not from the root רבה “to increase” but ריב “to quarrel”.</i>. Another explanation, I prepared an ambush for him<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Root ארב “to ambush”.</i>. If he would sin, to repay him, if he would acquire merit, to reward him.
<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 10:9" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).10.9">Tosephta 10:9</a>.</i>“The House of Shammai said to them, did Israel leave Egypt that he should mention the Exodus from Egypt<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The first verse of <i>Ps.</i> 114 mentions the Exodus; the entire Psalm is more about the crossing of the Red Sea.</i>? The House of Hillel answered them, even if he would wait until the cock crows<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since the House of Shammai also agree that after the meal one finishes the <i>Hallel</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:5:5-6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.5.5-6.1">Mishnah 8</a>), even if this is said close to dawn it would be much too early.</i>, they did not reach half the redemption,” since they left only at noontime, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:41" href="/Exodus.12.41"><i>Ex.</i> 12:41</a>; the remainder of the verse is misquoted.</i>, <i>in the middle of this day</i> did the Eternal lead out the Children of Israel, etc. But since one started fulfilling the obligation, one tells him to clean it up. Rebbi Abunah bar Sehora objected, did he not already mention it for the cup<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since the House of Shammai agree that in the <i>Qiddush</i> spoken over the first cup one mentions the Exodus, the objection of the House of Shammai seems without base.</i>?
It was stated: One says “time”<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the benediction “Praise to You, Eternal, our God, King of the universe, Who has kept us alive, maintained us, and enabled us to reach this festive time.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 40b" href="/Eruvin.40b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 40b</a>. Since the benediction is said with <i>Qiddush,</i> this paragraph belongs with Halakhah 1.</i> only at the three festivals of pilgrimage. Rebbi Mana said, a <i>baraita</i> says so, <i>on the festival of mazzot, the festival of weeks, and the festival of booths</i><sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:16" href="/Deuteronomy.16.16"><i>Deut</i>. 16:16</a>. It seems that the <i>baraita</i> notes that the verse singles out these three festivals at times one is obligated to enjoy.</i><i>.</i> It was stated: For any {day} where it is stated “declaration as holy” one has to say ”time”<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">This includes the day following the festival of booths, New Year’s Day, and the Day of Atonement.</i>. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, this is correct. If somebody saw a fig as first fruit, does he not have to recite “time”<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The benediction is for all festive occasions which happen not more than once a year.</i>? This applies to New Year’s Day and the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parenthesis is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.1.13">Chapter 4:1, Notes 57–60</a>, it is the continuation of the preceding half sentence, which also is the start of this text, but has no meaning in this context.</i>
(where in Judea one used to follow Rebbi Aqiba and in Galilee Rebbi Joḥanan  ben Nuri. If he changed and in Judea acted as in Galilee or in Galilee as in Judea he discharged his obligation. But is there not Purim, where these read on the fourteenth and those on the fifteenth? There is a difference since it is written, <i>family and family, country and country, and town and town.)</i>

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One mixes him the third cup, he says Grace<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Which requires a cup of wine anyhow.</i>. The fourth, over which he finishes the Hallel and recites the benediction of the song<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The benediction after the recitation of the Hallel in the synagogue.</i>. Between these cups<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">After the first cup up to the conclusion of the meal.</i>, he may drink if he wants to drink; between the third and the fourth he may not drink. After the <i>Pesaḥ</i> one may not serve <i>afiqoman</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">While in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.4.2">Halakhah 4</a> this was interpreted as ἐπὶ κῶμον, the Halakhah here gives other interpretations.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.4.3"><i>Sotah</i>5:6, Notes 138–141</a>.</i> it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 5:2" href="/Judges.5.2"><i>Jud.</i> 5:2</a>.</i>: <i>When retribution is retributed for Israel, when the people volunteered, praise the Eternal;</i> the heads of the people volunteered. When the Holy One, praise to Him, does wonders for Israel, they should sing a song. They objected: There is the redemption from Egypt<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning that the Song of the Sea also covers the Exodus, since the latter was confirmed only at the Sea. In the Babli and midrashic literature, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113" href="/Psalms.113"><i>Ps.</i> 113</a> is the song of the Israelites during Passover night <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 117a" href="/Pesachim.117a"><i>[Pesaḥim</i> 117a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113" href="/Psalms.113"><i>Midrash Tehillim</i> 113</a>; cf. <i>Cant. rabba</i> 1(37), <i>Mekhilta deR. Ismael, Shirah</i> 1; <i>deR. Simeon ben Yoḥai</i> p. 71]. Cf. <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah,</i> pp. 314–319.</i>! That is something else since it was the beginning of their redemption. They objected. There is redemption of Mardocai and Esther! That is something else since they were outside the Land; some want to say that Mardocai and Esther were freed of their enemies, they were not freed from [Gentile] government.
Why? That he should not get drunk. Is he not already drunk? What is the difference between wine with the meal and wine after the meal? Wine after the meal intoxicates, wine with the meal does not intoxicate<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Tosaphot (in Babli 117b s.v. רביעי deduce from here that drinking water is permitted between the third and the fourth cups.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon in the name of Rebbi Inainy bar Sisay: kinds of songs<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the word as ἐπικῶμιον “drinking song”, one of the more objectionable aspects of the κῶμος. The other explanations do not seem to be based on any Greek word. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 119b" href="/Pesachim.119b">Babli 119b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan says, sweets. Samuel says, truffles and chick pigeons prepared by Ḥanania bar Shilat.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If part of them slept, they may eat; if all of them, they may not eat<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices become disqualified if they are not permanently guarded. If the entire company sleeps before the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is consumed, the meat automatically is disqualified and impure.</i> Rebbi Yose says, if they<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The entire company subscribing to one <i>Pesaḥ</i>.</i> were sleepy, they may eat, if they slept they may not eat. The <i>Pesaḥ</i> after midnight<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">When rabbinically it may no longer be eaten (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 5:8" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.5.8">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 5:8</a>), it is treated as leftover.</i> makes hands impure. <i>Piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">A sacrifice slaughtered with the intention of consuming it after its allotted time or at an unauthorized place. While it is a deadly sin to consume <i>piggul,</i> the impurity imposed on it is purely rabbinical and weaker than biblical impurity.</i> and leftover<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">A qualified sacrifice left after the time allotted to its consumption.</i> make the hands impure. If he recited the benediction for the <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">“Praise to You, Eternal, our God, King of the universe, Who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us to eat <i>Pesaḥ”</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 10:13" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).10.13">Tosephta 10:13</a>).</i> it exempted that of the family sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">“Praise to You, Eternal, our God, King of the universe, Who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us to eat sacrifice.” For R. Ismael, the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is the main dish, the family sacrifice is a filler and does not need a separate benediction (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.7.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 6:7</a>). For R. Aqiba the two are coordinate, neither is subordinate to the other.</i>; the one for the family sacrifice does not exempt the one for the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> the words of Rebbi Ismael. Rebbi Aqiba said, neither one exempts the other.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Why? Because they did not think of it or because midnight had passed? Think of it, if there was another group and midnight had not passed? Therefore the reason only is because they did not think of it<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this paragraph does not refer to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.7.1">Mishnah 9</a> but to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:5:5-6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.5.5-6.1">Mishnah 8</a>. Why is the <i>Pesaḥ</i> disqualified if the participants were sleeping? Is it rabbinically, for they may miss the deadline of midnight, or biblically because nobody guards the sacrifice? Since the Mishnah implies that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is disqualified even if there is another group in the house which would see to it that the first group would not miss the deadline, it follows that the reason must be the lack of attention given to the <i>Pesaḥ.</i></i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.7">Halakhah 7:11, Notes 300–309</a>.</i> Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: <i>Piggul</i> and leftovers add together to make hands impure in the amount that exposes to punishment, the volume of an olive. Do they disqualify heave? It is a argument <i>de minore ad maius.</i> If they make hands impure to disqualify heave, they themselves not so much more? This which was left, what are you doing with it? Does it make hands impure or does it not make hands impure? If you are saying that this which was left makes hands impure, would <i>piggul</i> and leftovers not disqualify heave? About that which was left they did not decide anything<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 99b" href="/Pesachim.99b">Babli 99b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i> 5:1</a>. The Babli denies that in the special case of Passover Eve R. Yose disagrees.</i>. For if it were not so, the outer part would make the inner part impure<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 100a" href="/Pesachim.100a">Babli 100a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abbin said, who holds that something can become impure by touching itself? Rebbi Meïr. Did not Rebbi Joḥanan say, everything becomes pure by its greater part? When he cut off the greater part, is it not separated? Then it would be touching and be disqualified. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, if he cuts off minimal pieces and throws away.
Can you say, because midnight has passed? No, because they did not think of it. Therefore the reason only is because they did not think of it<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Since the preceding paragraph is about the <i>Pesaḥ</i> taken out from its place, long before midnight, it follows that also in that case it is disqualified and makes hands impure because one no longer guards it.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira said, should not making the benediction for <i>Pesaḥ</i> not exempt the sacrifice, but for the sacrifice exempt the <i>Pesaḥ,</i> since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is included in the notion of sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since the formulation of the benediction for the festival sacrifice is general, and making a general benediction in general makes it impossible to recite the more appropriate specific benediction (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.2.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 6:2</a>), one can understand the position neither of R. Ismael nor that of R. Aqiba.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is the main item, the {family} sacrifice a minor part<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The principle of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.2.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i>6:2</a> is not applicable here for either R. Ismael or R. Aqiba.</i>.
